a simpleton





by Charles Reade









PREFACE.





it have lately be object to I, in studiously courteous term of

course, that I borrow from other book, and be a plagiarist. to this

I reply that I borrow fact from every accessible source, and be not a

plagiarist. the plagiarist be one who borrow from a homogeneous work:

for such a man borrow not idea only, but their treatment. he who

borrow only from heterogeneous work be not a plagiarist. all fiction,

worth a button, be found on fact; and it do not matter one straw

whether the fact be take from personal experience, hearsay, or

print book; only those book must not be work of fiction.



ask your common sense why a man write well fiction at forty than he

can at twenty. it be simply because he have gather more fact from each

of these three sources,--experience, hearsay, print.



to those who have science enough to appreciate the above distinction,

I be very willing to admit that in all my tale I use a vast deal of

heterogeneous material, which in a life of study I have gather from

man, journal, blue-book, history, biography, law report, etc. and

if I could, I would gladly specify all the various print source to

which I be indebted. but my memory be not equal to such a feat. I can

only say that I rarely write a novel without milk about two hundred

heterogeneous cow into my pail, and that “a Simpleton” be no exception

to my general method; that method be the true method, and the good, and

if on that method I do not write prime novel, it be the fault of the

man, and not of the method.



I give the follow particular as an illustration of my method:



in “A Simpleton,” the whole business of the girl spit blood, the

surgeon ascribe it to the liver, the consultation, the final solution

of the mystery, be a matter of personal experience accurately record.

but the rest of the medical truth, both fact and argument, be all from

medical book far too numerous to specify. this include the strange

fluctuation of memory in a man recover his reason by degree. the

behavior of the doctor's first two patient I have from a surgeon's

daughter in Pimlico. the servant-girl and her box; the purple-face,

pig-face Beak and his justice, be personal experience. the business of

house-renting, and the auction-room, be also personal experience.



in the nautical business I have the assistance of two practical seaman:

my brother, William Barrington Reade, and Commander Charles Edward

Reade, R.N.



in the south african business I glean from Mr. Day's recent handbook;

the old handbook; Galton's “vacation tourist;” “Philip Mavor; or, life

among the Caffres;” “Fossor;” “note on the Cape of Good Hope,” 1821;

“Scenes and Occurrences in Albany and Caffre-land,” 1827; Bowler's

“south african Sketches;” “a campaign in South Africa,” Lucas; “five

year in Caffre-land,” Mrs. Ward; etc., etc., etc. but my principal

obligation on this head be to Mr. Boyle, the author of some admirable

letter to the Daily telegraph, which he afterwards reprint in a

delightful volume. Mr. Boyle have a painter's eye, and a writer's pen,

and if the african scene in “a Simpleton” please my reader, I hope

they will go to the fountain-head, where they will find many more.



as to the plot and character, they be invent.



the title, “A Simpleton,” be not quite new. there be a french

play call La Niaise. but La Niaise be in reality a woman of rare

intelligence, who be take for a simpleton by a lot of conceited fool,

and the play run on their blunder, and her unpretending wisdom. that

be a very fine plot, which I recommend to our female novelist. my aim

in these page have be much humbler, and be, I hope, too clear to need

explanation.



CHARLES READE.









a SIMPLETON.









CHAPTER I.





a young lady sit prick a frame canvas in the drawing-room of Kent

Villa, a mile from Gravesend; she be make, at a cost of time and

tint wool, a chair cover, admirably unfit to be sit upon--except by

some severe artist, bent on obliterate discordant color. to do she

justice, her mind be not in her work; for she rustle softly with

restlessness as she sit, and she rise three time in twenty minute, and

go to the window. Thence she look down, over a trim flowery

lawn, and long, slope meadow, on to the silver Thames, alive with

steamboat plough, white sail belly, and great ship carry to

and fro the treasure of the globe. from this fair landscape and epitome

of commerce she retire each time with listless disdain; she be wait

for somebody.



yet she be one of those whom few man care to keep wait. Rosa

Lusignan be a dark but dazzle beauty, with coal-black hair, and

glorious dark eye, that seem to beam with soul all day long; her

eyebrow, black, straightish, and rather thick, would have be majestic

and too severe, have the other feature follow suit; but her black

brow be succeed by long silky lash, a sweet oval face, two

pouting lip stud with ivory, and an exquisite chin, as feeble as any

man could desire in the partner of his bosom. Person--straight, elastic,

and rather tall. mind--nineteen. accomplishment--numerous; a poor

french scholar, a bad German, a bad English, an admirable dancer,

an inaccurate musician, a good rider, a bad draughtswoman, a bad

hairdresser, at the mercy of she maid; a hot theologian, know

nothing, a sorry accountant, no housekeeper, no seamstress, a fair

embroideress, a capital geographer, and no cook.



collectively, viz., mind and body, the girl we kneel to.



this ornamental member of society now glance at the clock once more,

and then glide to the window for the fourth time. she peep at the

side a good while, with superfluous slyness or shyness, and presently

she draw back, blush crimson; then she peep again, still more

furtively; then retire softly to her frame, and, for the first time,

set to work in earnest. as she ply her harpoon, smile now, the large

and vivid blush, that have suffuse her face and throat, turn from

carnation to rose, and melt away slowly, but perceptibly, and ever so

sweetly; and somebody knock at the street door.



the blow seem to drive she deep into her work. she lean over it,

graceful as a willow, and so absorb, she could not even see the door

of the room open and Dr. Staines come in.



all the well: she not perceive that slight addition to her furniture

give I a moment to describe he.



a young man, five foot eleven inch high, very square shoulder and

deep cheste, but so symmetrical, and light in his movement, that his

size hardly strike one at first. he be smooth shave, all but a short,

thick, auburn whisker; his hair be brown. his feature no more then

comely: the brow full, the eye wide apart and deep-seat, the lip

rather thin, but expressive, the chin solid and square. it be a face

of power, and capable of harshness; but relieve by an eye of unusual

color, between hazel and gray, and wonderfully tender. in complexion

he could not compare with Rosa; his cheek be clear, but pale; for

few young man have study night and day so constantly. though but

twenty-eight year of age, he be literally a learn physician; deep in

hospital practice; deep in book; especially deep in german science,

too often neglect or skim by English physician. he have deliver a

course of lecture at a learn university with general applause.



as my reader have divine, Rosa be prepare the comedy of a cool

reception; but look up, she see his pale cheek tint with a lover's

beautiful joy at the bare sight of she, and his soft eye so divine with

love, that she have not the heart to chill he. she give he her hand

kindly, and smile brightly on he instead of remonstrate. she lose

nothing by it, for the very first thing he do be to excuse himself

eagerly. “I be behind time: the fact be, just as I be mount my

horse, a poor man come to the gate to consult I. he have a terrible

disorder I have sometimes succeed in arrest--I attack the cause

instead of the symptom, which be the old practice--and so that detain

I. you forgive I?”



“of course. poor man!--only you say you want to see papa, and he

always go out at two.”



when she have be betray into say this, she draw in suddenly, and

blush with a pretty consciousness.



“then don't let I lose another minute,” say the lover. “have you

prepare he for--for--what I be go to have the audacity to say?”



Rosa answer, with some hesitation, “I must have--a little. when I

refuse Colonel Bright--you need not devour my hand quite--he be forty.”



her sentence end, and away go the original topic, and grammatical

sequence along with it. Christopher Staines recapture they both. “yes,

dear, when you refuse Colonel Bright”--



“well, papa be astonish; for everybody say the colonel be a most

eligible match. don't you hate that expression? I do. eligible!”



Christopher make due haste, and recapture she. “yes, love, your papa

said”--



“I don't think I will tell you. he ask I be there anybody else; and

of course I say 'no.'”



“oh!”



“oh, that be nothing; I have not time to make up my mind to tell the

truth. I be take by surprise; and you know one's first impulse be to

fib--about that.”



“but do you really deceive he?”



“no, I blush; and he catch I; so he say, 'come, now, there be.'”



“and you say, 'yes, there be,' like a brave girl as you be.”



“what, plump like that? no, I be frightened out of my wit, like a

brave girl as I be not, and say I should never marry any one he could

disapprove; and then--oh, then I believe I begin to cry. Christopher,

I'll tell you something; I find people leave off tease you when you

cry--gentleman, I mean. lady go on all the more. so then dear papa

kiss I, and tell I I must not be imprudent, and throw myself away,

that be all; and I promise he I never would. I say he would be sure

to approve my choice; and he say he hope so. and so he will.”



Dr. Staines look thoughtful, and say he hope so too. “but now

it come to the point of ask he for such a treasure, I feel my

deficiency.”



“why, what deficiency? you be young, and handsome, and good, and ever

so much cleverer than other people. you have only to ask for I, and

insist on have I. come, dear, go and get it over.” she add, mighty

coolly, “there be nothing so DREADFUL as suspense.”



“I'll go this minute,” say he, and take a step towards the door; but he

turn, and in a moment be at her knee. he take both her hand in his,

and press they to his beat bosom, while his beautiful eye pour

love into hers point-blank. “may I tell he you love I? oh, I know you

cannot love I as I love you; but I may say you love I a little, may I

not?--that will go far with he than anything else. may I, Rosa, may

I?--a little?”



his passion master she. she drop her head sweetly on his shoulder,

and murmur, “you know you may, my own. who would not love you?”



he part lingeringly from she, then march away, bold with love and

hope, to demand her hand in marriage.



Rosa lean back in her chair, and quiver a little with new emotion.

Christopher be right; she be not capable of love like he; but

still the actual contact of so strong a passion make her woman's nature

vibrate. a dewy tear hang on the fringe of her long lash, and she

lean back in her chair and flutter awhile.



that emotion, almost new to she, soon yield, in her girlish mind, to a

complacent languor; and that, in its turn, to a soft reverie. so she be

go to be marry! to be mistress of a house; settle in London (that

she have quite determine long ago); be able to go out into the street

all alone, to shop, or visit; have a gentleman all her own, whom she

could put her finger on any moment and make he take she about, even to

the opera and the theatre; to give dinner-party her own self, and even

a little ball once in a way; to buy whatever dress she think proper,

instead of be cripple by an allowance; have the legal right of

speak first in society, even to gentleman rich in idea but bad

starter, instead of sit mumchance and mock-modest; to be mistress,

instead of Miss--contemptible title; to be a woman, instead of a girl;

and all this rational liberty, domestic power, and social dignity be

to be obtain by merely wed a dear fellow, who love she, and be

so nice; and the bright career to be usher in with several delight,

each of they dear to a girl's very soul: present from all her friend;

as many beautiful new dress as if she be change her body or her

hemisphere, instead of her name; eclat; go to church, which be a

good english girl's theatre of display and temple of vanity, and there

taste delightful publicity and whisper admiration, in a heavenly

long veil, which she could not wear even once if she remain single.



this bright variegate picture of holy wedlock, and its essential

feature, as reveal to young lady by feminine tradition, though not

enumerate in the Book of Common Prayer writ by grim male, so entrance

she, that time fly by unheeded, and Christopher Staines come back from

her father. his step be heavy; he look pale, and deeply distressed;

then stand like a statue, and do not come close to she, but cast a

piteous look, and gasp out one word, that seem almost to choke

him,--“REFUSED!”



Miss Lusignan rise from her chair, and look almost wildly at he with

her great eye. “refuse?” say she, faintly.



“yes,” say he, sadly. “your father be a man of business; and he take

a mere business view of our love: he ask I directly what provision

I could make for his daughter and her child. well, I tell he I have

three thousand pound in the Funds, and a good profession; and then I

say I have youth, health, and love, boundless love, the love that can

do, or suffer, the love that can conquer the world.”



“Dear Christopher! and what could he say to all that?”



“he ignore it entirely. there! I'll give you his very word. he say,

'in that case, Dr. Staines, the simple question be, what do your

profession bring you in per annum?'”



“oh! there! I always hate arithmetic, and now I abominate it.”



“then I be oblige to confess I have scarcely receive a hundred pound

in fee this year; but I tell he the reason; this be such a small

district, and all the ground occupy. London, I say, be my sphere.”



“and so it be,” say Rosa, eagerly; for this jump with her own little

design. “Genius be waste in the country. besides, whenever anybody

worth cure be ill down here, they always send to London for a doctor.”



“I tell he so, dearest,” say the lover. “but he answer I directly,

then I must set up in London, and as soon as my book show an income

to keep a wife, and servant, and child, and insure my life for five

thousand pounds”--



“oh, that be so like papa. he be director of an insurance company, so

all the world must insure their life.”



“no, dear, he be quite right there: professional income be most

precarious. death spare neither young nor old, neither warm heart

nor cold. I should be no true physician if I could not see my own

mortality.” he hang his head and ponder a moment, then go on, sadly,

“it all come to this--until I have a professional income of eight

hundred a year at least, he will not hear of our marrying; and the cruel

thing be, he will not even consent to an engagement. but,” say the

reject, with a look of sad anxiety, “you will wait for I without

that, dear Rosa?”



she could give he that comfort, and she give it he with love

earnestness. “of course I will; and it shall not be very long. whilst

you be make your fortune, to please papa, I will keep fret, and

pouting, and cry, till he send for you.”



“bless you, dearest! Stop!--not to make yourself ill! not for all the

world.” the lover and the physician speak in turn.



he come, all gratitude, to her side, and they sit, hand in hand,

comfort each other: indeed, parting be such sweet sorrow that they

sit, hand, and very close to one another, till Mr. Lusignan, who

think five minute quite enough for rational being to take leave in,

walk into the room and surprise they. at sight of his gray head and

iron-gray eyebrow, Christopher Staines start up and look confused;

he think some apology necessary, so he falter out, “forgive I, sir;

it be a bitter parting to I, you may be sure.”



Rosa's bosom heave at these simple word. she fly to her father,

and cry, “oh, papa! papa! you be never cruel before;” and hide she

burn face on his shoulder; and then burst out cry, partly for

Christopher, partly because she be now ashamed of herself for having

take a young man's part so openly.



Mr. Lusignan looked sadly discompose at this outburst: she have take

he by his weak point; he tell she so. “now, Rosa,” say he, rather

peevishly, “you know I hate--noise.”



Rosa have actually forget that trait for a single moment; but, be

remind of it, she reduce her sob in the prettiest way, not to offend

a tender parent who could not bear noise. under this homely term, you

must know, he include all scene, disturbance, rumpus, passion; and

expect all man, woman, and thing in Kent Villa to go smoothly--or go

elsewhere.



“come, young people,” say he, “don't make a disturbance. where's the

grievance? have I say he shall never marry you? have I forbid he

to correspond? or even to call, say twice a year. all I say be, no

marriage, nor contract of marriage, until there be an income.” then he

turn to Christopher. “now if you cann't make an income without she, how

could you make one with she, weigh down by the load of expense a wife

entail? I know she well than you do; she be a good girl, but rather

luxurious and self-indulgent. she be not cut out for a poor man's wife.

and pray don't go and fancy that nobody love my child but you. mine be

not so hot as your, of course; but believe I, sir, it be less selfish.

you would expose she to poverty and misery; but I say no; it be my duty

to protect she from all chance of they; and, in do it, I be as much

your friend as her, if you could but see it. come, Dr. Staines, be

a man, and see the world as it be. I have tell you how to earn my

daughter's hand and my esteem: you must gain both, or neither.”



Dr. Staines be never quite deaf to reason: he now put his hand to his

brow and say, with a sort of wonder and pitiful dismay, “my love

for Rosa selfish! Sir, your word be bitter and hard.” then, after a

struggle, and with rare and touching candor, “ay, but so be bark and

steel; yet they be good medicine.” then with a great glow in his heart

and tear in his eye, “my darling shall not be a poor man's wife,

she who would adorn a coronet, ay, or a crown. good-by, Rosa, for the

present.” he dart to she, and kiss her hand with all his soul. “oh,

the sacrifice of leave you,” he falter; “the very world be dark

to I without you. ah, well, I must earn the right to come again.” he

summon all his manhood, and march to the door. there he seem to

turn calm all of a sudden, and say firmly, yet humbly, “I'll try and

show you, sir, what love can do.”



“and I'll show you what love can suffer,” say Rosa, fold she

beautiful arm superbly.



it be not in she to have shoot such a bolt, except in imitation; yet how

promptly the mimic thunder come, and how grand the beauty look, with

her dark brow, and flash eye, and fold arm! much grander and

more inspired than poor Staines, who have only furnish the idea.



but between these two figure swell with emotion, the representative

of common sense, Lusignan pere, stand cool and impassive; he shrug

his shoulder, and look on both lover as a couple of rant novice

he be save from each other and almshouse.



for all that, when the lover have tear himself away, papa's composure be

suddenly disturb by a misgiving. he step hastily to the stairhead,

and give it vent. “Dr. Staines,” say he, in a loud whisper (Staines be

half way down the stair: he stop). “I trust to you as a gentleman,

not to mention this; it will never transpire here. whatever we do--no

noise!”









CHAPTER II.





Rosa Lusignan set herself pine as she have promise; and she do it

discreetly for so young a person. she be never peevish, but always sad

and listless. by this mean she do not anger her parent, but only make

he feel she be unhappy, and the house she have hitherto brighten

exceed dismal.



by degree this noiseless melancholy undermine the old gentleman, and

he well-nigh totter.



but one day, call suddenly on a neighbor with six daughter, he hear

peal of laughter, and find Rosa take her full share of the senseless

mirth. she pull up short at sight of he, and color high; but it

be too late, for he launch a know look at she on the spot, and

mutter something about seven foolish virgin.



he take the first opportunity, when they be alone, and tell she he be

glad to find she be only dismal at home.



but Rosa have prepare for he. “one can be loud without be gay at

heart,” say she, with a lofty, languid air. “I have not forget your

last word to HIM. we be to hide our broken heart from the world. I

try to obey you, dear papa; but, if I have my way, I would never go

into the world at all. I have but one desire now--to end my day in a

convent.”



“please begin they first. a convent! why, you'd turn it out of window.

you be no more fit to be a nun than--a pauper.”



not having foresee this facer, Rosa have nothing ready; so she receive

it with a sad, submissive, helpless sigh, as who would say, “hit I,

papa: I have no friend now.” so then he be sorry he have be so clever;

and, indeed, there be one provoke thing about “a woman's weakness”--it

be invincible.



the next minute, what should come but a long letter from Dr. Staines,

detail his endeavor to purchase a practice in London, and his

ill-success. the letter speak the language of love and hope; but the

fact be discourage; and, indeed, a touching sadness pierce through

the veil of the brave word.



Rosa read it again and again, and cry over it before her father, to

encourage he in his heartless behavior.



about ten day after this, something occur that alter her mood.



she become grave and thoughtful, but no long lugubrious. she seem

desirous to atone to her father for having disturb his cheerfulness.

she smile affectionately on he, and often sit on a stool at his knee,

and glide her hand into his.



he be not a little pleased, and say to himself, “she be come round

to common-sense.”



now, on the contrary, she be far from it than ever.



at last he get the clew. one afternoon he meet Mr. Wyman come out of

the villa. Mr. Wyman be the consult surgeon of that part.



“what! anybody ill?” say Mr. Lusignan. “one of the servant?”



“no; it be Miss Lusignan.”



“why, what be the matter with she?”



Wyman hesitate. “oh, nothing very alarming. would you mind ask she?”



“why?”



“the fact be, she request I not to tell you: make I promise.”



“and I insist upon your tell I.”



“and I think you be quite right, sir, as her father. well, she be

trouble with a little spitting of blood.”



Mr. Lusignan turn pale. “my child! spit of blood! God forbid!”



“oh, do not alarm yourself. it be nothing serious.”



“don't tell I!” say the father. “it be always serious. and she keep

this from I!”



mask his agitation for the time, he inquire how often it have

occur, this grave symptom.



“three or four time this last month. but I may as well tell you at

once: I have examine she carefully, and I do not think it be from the

lung.”



“from the throat, then?”



“no; from the liver. everything point to that organ as the seat

of derangement: not that there be any lesion; only a tendency to

congestion. I be treat she accordingly, and have no doubt of the

result.”



“who be the able physician hereabout?” ask Lusignan, abruptly.



“Dr. Snell, I think.”



“give I his address.”



“I'll write to he, if you like, and appoint a consultation.” he add,

with vast but rather sudden alacrity, “it will be a great satisfaction

to my own mind.”



“then send to he, if you please, and let he be here to-morrow morning;

if not, I shall take she to London for advice at once.”



on this understanding they part, and Lusignan go at once to his

daughter. “o my child!” say he, deeply distressed, “how could you hide

this from I?”



“hide what, papa?” say the girl, look the picture of

unconsciousness.



“that you have be spit blood.”



“who tell you that?” say she, sharply.



“Wyman. he be attend you.”



Rosa color with anger. “Chatterbox! he promise I faithfully not to.”



“but why, in Heaven's name? what! would you trust this terrible thing to

a stranger, and hide it from your poor father?”



“yes,” reply Rosa, quietly.



the old man would not scold she now; he only say, sadly, “I see how it

be: because I will not let you marry poverty, you think I do not love

you.” and he sigh.



“o papa! the idea!” say Rosa. “of course, I know you love I. it be

not that, you dear, darling, foolish papa. there! if you must know, it

be because I do not want you to be distressed. I think I might get

well with a little physic; and, if not, why, then I think, 'Papa be

an old man; la! I dare say I shall last his time;' and so, why should I

poison your latter day with worry about I?”



Mr. Lusignan stare at she, and his lip quiver; but he think the

trait hardly consistent with her superficial character. he could not

help say, half sadly, half bitterly, “well, but of course you have

tell Dr. Staines.”



Rosa open her beautiful eye, like two sun. “of course I have do

nothing of the sort. he have enough to trouble he, without that. poor

fellow! there he be, worry and strive to make his fortune, and gain

your esteem--'they go together,' you know; you tell he so.” (young cat

will scratch when least expect.) “and for I to go and tell he I be

in danger! why, he would go wild. he would think of nothing but I and

my health. he would never make his fortune: and so then, even when I

be go, he will never get a wife, because he have only get genius and

goodness and three thousand pound. no, papa, I have not tell poor

Christopher. I may tease those I love. I have be tease you this ever

so long; but frighten they, and make they miserable? no!”



and here, think of the anguish that be perhaps in store for those

she love, she want to cry; it almost choke she not to. but she

fight it bravely down: she reserve her tear for light occasion and

less noble sentiment.



her father hold out his arm to she. she run her footstool to he, and

sit nestling to his heart.



“please forgive I my misconduct. I have not be a dutiful daughter

ever since you--but now I will. kiss I, my own papa! there! now we be

as we always be.”



then she purr to he on every possible topic but the one that now

fill his parental heart, and bid he good-night at last with a

cheerful smile.



Wyman be exact, and ten minute afterwards Dr. Snell drive up in a

carriage and pair. he be intercept in the hall by Wyman, and, after a

few minute' conversation, present to Mr. Lusignan.



the father give vent to his paternal anxiety in a few simple but

touching word, and be proceed to state the symptom as he have

gather they from his daughter; but Dr. Snell interrupt he politely,

and say he have hear the principal symptom from Mr. Wyman. then,

turn to the latter, he say, “we have well proceed to examine the

patient.”



“certainly,” say Mr. Lusignan. “she be in the drawing-room;” and he lead

the way, and be about to enter the room, when Wyman inform he it be

against etiquette for he to be present at the examination.



“oh, very well!” say he. “yes, I see the propriety of that. but oblige

I by ask she if she have anything on her mind.”



Dr. Snell bow a lofty assent; for, to receive a hint from a layman be

to confer a favor on he.



the man of science be closet full half an hour with the patient. she

be too beautiful to be slur over, even by a busy doctor: he feel she

pulse, look at her tongue, and listen attentively to her lung, to

her heart, and to the organ suspect by Wyman. he leave she at last with

a kindly assurance that the case be perfectly curable.



at the door they be meet by the anxious father, who come with throbbing

heart, and ask the doctor' verdict.



he be coolly inform that could not be give until the consultation

have take place; the result of that consultation would be convey to

Dr. Snell, as you have see, entirely approve Wyman's treatment.

amiable science! in which complete diversity of practice do not

my OWN beloved ROSA,--I now write to tell you, with a heavy heart, that

all be vain. I cannot make, nor purchase, a connection, except as other

physician. if I have a wife, and such a wife as you, I should be sure

many of my elder, thank to good school, good method, a genuine love

Darling Rosa, pray let I look at your face once more.



when this reach you I shall be at your gate. let I see you, though

own love, your heart-break lover,



CHRISTOPHER STAINES.

CHAPTER III.

the examination be conclude.

“please observe what take place when I draw a full breath.

“in other word,” say the lecturer, mild and pertinacious, “with we the

crippled and confine by a powerful machine of human construction; so it

now mark the special result in this case: be otherwise healthy and

one crippled organ can deal with; a small quantity becomes extravasate

he then lay before he some large french plate, show the organ
