clergyman, and you be the commentator--he! he! and so now let we go

back from divinity to medicine. I repeat” (this be the first time she

have say it) “that my other doctor give I real prescription, write

in hieroglyphic. you cann't look at they without feel there must be

something in they.”



an angry spot rise on Christopher's cheek, but he only say, “and be

your other doctor satisfied with the progress your disorder be make

under their superintendence?”



“perfectly! papa, tell he what they say, and I'll find he their

prescription.” she go to a drawer, and rummage, affect not to

listen.



Lusignan comply. “first of all, sir, I must tell you they be

confident it be not the lung, but the liver.”



“the what!” shout Christopher.



“ah!” scream Rosa. “oh, don't!--bawling!”



“and don't you screech,” say her father, with a look of misery and

apprehension impartially distribute on the resounding pair.



“you must have misunderstood they,” murmur Staines, in a voice that

be now barely audible a yard off. “the hemorrhage of a bright red

color, and expel without effort or nausea?”



“from the liver--they have assure I again and again,” say Lusignan.



Christopher's face still wear a look of blank amazement, till Rosa

herself confirm it positively.



then he cast a look of agony upon she, and start up in a passion,

forget once more that his host abhor the sonorous. “oh, shame!

shame!” he cry, “that the noble profession of medicine should be

disgrace by ignorance such as this.” then he say, sternly, “Sir, do

not mistake my motive; but I decline to have anything furth to do

with this case, until those two gentleman have be relieve of it; and,

as this be very harsh, and on my part unprecedented, I will give you

one reason out of many I could give you. Sir, there be no road from the

liver to the throat by which blood can travel in this way, defy

the law of gravity; and they know, from the patient, that no strong

expellent force have ever be in operation. their diagnosis, therefore,

imply agnosis, or ignorance too great to be forgive. I will not share

my patient with two gentleman who know so little of medicine, and know

nothing of anatomy, which be the A b c of medicine. can I see their

prescription?”



these be hand to he. “good heaven!” say he, “have you take all

these?”



“Most of they.”



“why, then you have drink about two gallon of unwholesome liquid,

and eat a pound or two of unwholesome solid. these medicine have

co-operate with the malady. the disorder lie, not in the hemorrhage,

but in the precedent extravasation that be a drain on the system; and

how be the loss to be supply? why, by take a little more nourishment

than before; there be no other way; and probably nature, leave to

herself, might have increase your appetite to meet the occasion. but

those two worthy have strike that weapon out of Nature's hand; they

have pepper away at the poor ill-use stomach with drug and draught,

not very deleterious I grant you, but all more or less indigestible, and

all tend, not to whet the appetite, but to clog the stomach, or turn

the stomach, or pester the stomach, and so impair the appetite, and so

co-operate, indirectly, with the malady.”



“this be good sense,” say Lusignan. “I declare, I--I wish I know how to

get rid of they.”



“oh, I'll do that, papa.”



“no, no; it be not worth a rumpus.”



“I'll do it too politely for that. Christopher, you be very

clever--terribly clever. whenever I throw their medicine away, I be

always a little well that day. I will sacrifice they to you. it be

a sacrifice. they be both so kind and chatty, and don't grudge I

hieroglyphic; now you do.”



she sit down and write two sweet letter to Dr. Snell and Mr. Wyman,

thank they for the great attention they have pay she; but find

herself get steadily bad, in spite of all they have do for she,

she propose to discontinue her medicine for a time, and try change of

air.



“and suppose they call to see whether you be change the air?”



“in that case, papa--'not at home.'”



the note be address and despatch.



then Dr. Staines brighten up, and say to Lusignan, “I be now happy to

tell you that I have overrate the malady. the sad change I see in Miss

Lusignan be partly due to the great bulk of unwholesome esculent

she have be eat and drink under the head of medicine. these

discontinue, she might linger on for year, exist, though not

live--the tight-laced cannot be say to live. but if she would be

healthy and happy, let she throw that diabolical machine into the

fire. it be no use ask she to loosen it; she cann't. once there, the

temptation be too strong. off with it, and, take my word, you will be

one of the healthy and most vigorous young lady in Europe.”



Rosa look rueful, and almost sullen. she say she have part with her

doctor for he, but she really could not go about without stay. “they

be as loose as they can be. see!”



“that part of the programme be dispose of,” say Christopher. “please

go on to no. 2. how about the raw red line where the loose machine have

saw you?”



“what red line? no such thing! somebody or other have be peep in at

my window. I'll have the ivy cut down to-morrow.”



“Simpleton!” say Mr. Lusignan, angrily. “you have let the cat out of

the bag. there be such a mark, then, and this extraordinary young man

have discern it with the eye of science.”



“he never discern it at all,” say Rosa, red as fire; “and, what be

more, he never will.”



“I don't want to. I should be very sorry to. I hope it will be go in a

week.”



“I wish you be go now--expose I in this cruel way,” say Rosa,

angry with herself for having say an idiotic thing, and furious with

he for having make she say it.



“oh, Rosa!” say Christopher, in a voice of tender reproach.



but Mr. Lusignan interfere promptly. “Rosa, no noise. I will not have

you snap at your good friend and mine. if you be excited, you have

well retire to your own room and compose yourself. I hate a clamor.”



Rosa make a wry face at this rebuke, and then begin to cry quietly.



every tear be like a drop of blood from Christopher's heart. “pray

don't scold she, sir,” say he, ready to snivel himself. “she mean

nothing unkind: it be only her pretty sprightly way; and she do not

really imagine a love so reverent as mine”--



“don't you interfere between my father and I,” say this reasonable

young lady, now in an ungovernable state of feminine irritability.



“no, Rosa,” say Christopher, humbly. “Mr. Lusignan,” say he, “I hope

you will tell she that, from the very first, I be unwilling to enter on

this subject with she. neither she nor I can forget my double character.

I have not say half as much to she as I ought, be her physician; and

yet you see I have say more than she can bear from I, who, she know,

love she and revere she. then, once for all, do pray let I put this

delicate matter into your hand: it be a case for parental authority.”



“unfatherly tyranny, that mean,” say Rosa. “what business have

gentleman interfere in such thing? it be unheard of. I will not

submit to it, even from papa.”



“well, you need not scream at I,” say Mr. Lusignan; and he shrug

his shoulder to Staines. “she be impracticable, you see. if I do my

duty, there will be a disturbance.”



now this rouse the bile of Dr. Staines. “what, sir!” say he, “you

could separate she and I by your authority, here in this very room; and

yet, when her life be at stake, you abdicate! you could part she from a

man who love she with every drop of his heart,--and she say she love

he, or, at all event, prefer he to others,--and you cannot part

she from a miserable corset, although you see in her poor waste face

that it be carry she to the churchyard. in that case, sir, there be

but one thing for you to do,--withdraw your opposition and let I

marry she. as her lover I be powerless; but invest I with a husband's

authority, and you will soon see the rose return to her cheek, and

her elastic figure expand, and her eye beam with health and the

happiness that come of perfect health.”



Mr. Lusignan make an answer neither of his hearer expect. he say,

“I have a great mind to take you at your word. I be too old and fond of

quiet to drive a Simpleton in single harness.”



this contemptuous speech, and, above all, the word Simpleton, which have

be apply to she pretty freely by young lady at school, and always

gall she terribly, inflict so intolerable a wound on Rosa's vanity,

that she be ready to burst: on that, of course, her stay contribute

their mite of physical uneasiness. thus irritated mind and body, she

burn to strike in return; and as she could not slap her father in the

presence of another, she give it Christopher back-handed.



“you can turn I out of door,” say she, “if you be tired of your

daughter, but I be not such a SIMPLETON as to marry a tyrant. no; he have

show the cloven foot in time. a husband's AUTHORITY, indeed!” then she

turn her hand, and give it he direct. “you tell I a different

story when you be pay your court to I; then you be to be my

servant,--all hypocritical sweetness. you have well go and marry a

circassian slave. they don't wear stay, and they do wear trouser; so

she will be unfeminine enough, even for you. no english lady would

let her husband dictate to she about such a thing. I can have as many

husband as I like, without fall into the clutch of a tyrant. you

be a rude, indelicate--and so please understand it be all over between

you and I.”



both her auditor stand aghast, for she utter this conclusion with a

dignity of which the opening give no promise, and the occasion, weigh

in masculine balance, be not worthy.



“you do not mean that. you cannot mean it,” say Dr. Staines, aghast.



“I do mean it,” say she, firmly; “and, if you be a gentleman, you will

not compel I to say it twice--three time, I mean.”



at this dagger-stroke Christopher turn very pale, but he maintain

his dignity. “I be a gentleman,” say he, quietly, “and a very

unfortunate one. good-by, sir; thank you kindly. good-by, Rosa; God

bless you! oh, pray take a thought! remember, your life and death be in

your own hand now. I be powerless.”



and he leave the house in sorrow, and just, but not pettish, indignation.



when he be go, father and daughter look at each other, and there

be the silence that succeed a storm.



Rosa, feel the most uneasy, be the first to express she

satisfaction. “there, he be go, and I be glad of it. now you and I

shall never quarrel again. I be quite right. such impertinence! such

indelicacy! a fine prospect for I if I have marry such a man! however,

he be go, and so there's an end of it. the idea! tell a young lady,

before her father, she be tight-laced! if you have not be there I could

have forgive he. but I be not; it be a story. now,” suddenly exalt

her voice, “I know you believe he.”



“I say nothing,” whisper papa, hope to still she by example. this

ruse do not succeed.



“but you look volume,” cry she: “and I cann't bear it. I willn't bear

it. if you don't believe I, ask my maid.” and with this felicitous

speech, she ring the bell.



“you'll break the wire if you don't mind,” suggest her father,

piteously.



“all the well! why should not wire be break as well as my heart? oh,

here she be! now, Harriet, come here.”



“yes, miss.”



“and tell the truth. be I tight-laced?”



Harriet look in her face a moment to see what be require of she, and

then say, “that you be not, miss. I never dress a young lady as wear

'em easy than you do.”



“there, papa! that will do, Harriet.”



Harriet retire as far as the keyhole; she see something be up.



“now,” say Rosa, “you see I be right; and, after all, it be a match

you do not approve. well, it be all over, and now you may write to your

favorite, Colonel Bright. if he come here, I'll box his old ear. I

hate he. I hate they all. forgive your wayward girl. I'll stay with

you all my day. I dare say that will not be long, now I have quarrel

with my guardian angel; and all for what? papa! papa! how can you sit

there and not speak I one word of comfort? 'SIMPLETON?' ah! that I be

to throw away a love a queen be scarcely worthy of; and all for what?

really, if it ben't for the ingratitude and wickedness of the thing, it

be too laughable. ha! ha!--oh! oh! oh!--ha! ha! ha!”



and off she go into hysteric, and begin to gulp and choke

frightfully.



her father cry for help in dismay. in run Harriet, see, and scream,

but do not lose her head; this veracious person whip a pair of

scissor off the table, and cut the young lady's stay-lace directly.

then there be a burst of imprison beauty; a deep, deep sigh of relief

come from a bosom that would have do honor to Diana; and the scene

soon conclude with fit of harmless weeping, renew at interval.



when it have settle down to this, her father, to soothe she, say he

would write to Dr. Staines, and bring about a reconciliation, if she

like.



“no,” say she, “you shall kill I soon. I should die of shame.”



she add, “oh, pray, from this hour, never mention his name to I.”



and then she have another cry.



Mr. Lusignan be a sensible man: he drop the subject for the present;

but he make up his mind to one thing--that he would never part with Dr.

Staines as a physician.



next day Rosa keep her own room until dinner-time, and be as unhappy

as she deserve to be. she spend her time in sew on stiff flannel

lining and cry. she half hope Christopher would write to she, so

that she might write back that she forgive he. but not a line.



at half-past six her volatile mind take a turn, real or affected. she

would cry no more for an ungrateful fellow,--ungrateful for not see

through the stone wall how she have be employ all the morning; and

make it up. so she bathe her red eye, make a great alteration in her

dress, and came dance into the room hum an italian ditty.



as they be sit together in the dining-room after dinner, two

letter come by the same post to Mr. Lusignan from Mr. Wyman and Dr.

Snell.



Mr. Wyman's letter:--





DEAR SIR,--I be sorry to hear from Miss Lusignan that she intend to

discontinue medical advice. the disorder be progress favorably, and

nothing to be fear, under proper treatment.



your, etc.





Dr. Snell's letter:--





DEAR SIR,--Miss Lusignan have write to I somewhat impatiently and

seem disposed to dispense with my visit. I do not, however, think it

right to withdraw without tell you candidly that this be an unwise

step. your daughter's health be in a very precarious condition.



your, etc.





Rosa burst out laugh. “I have nothing to fear, and I'm on the brink

of the grave. that come of writing without a consultation. if they

have write at one table, I should have be neither well nor ill. poor

Christopher!” and her sweet face begin to work piteously.



“there! there! drink a glass of wine.”



she do, and a tear with it, that run into the glass like lightning.



warn by this that grief sit very near the bright, hilarious surface,

Mr. Lusignan avoid all emotional subject for the present. next day,

however, he tell she she might dismiss her lover, but no power should

make he dismiss his pet physician, unless her health improve.



“I will not give you that excuse for inflict he on I again,” say

the young hypocrite.



she keep her word. she get well and well, strong, bright, gayer.



she take to walk every day, and increase the distance, till she

could walk ten mile without fatigue.



her favorite walk be to a certain cliff that command a noble view of

the sea. to get to it she must pass through the town of Gravesend; and

we may be sure she do not pass so often through that city without some

idea of meet the lover she have use so ill, and elicit an apology

from he. sly puss!



when she have walk twenty time, or thereabout, through the town, and

never see he, she begin to fear she have offend he past hope. then

she use to cry at the end of every walk.



but by and by bodily health, vanity, and temper combine to rouse the

defiant spirit. say she, “if he really love I, he would not take my

word in such a hurry. and besides, why do he not watch I, and find

out what I be do, and where I walk?”



at last she really begin to persuade herself that she be an ill-use

and slight girl. she be very angry at time, and disconsolate at

other; a mixed state in which hasty and impulsive young lady commit

lifelong folly.



Mr. Lusignan observe the surface only: he see his invalid daughter

get well every day, till at last she become a picture of health

and bodily vigor. relieved of his fear, he trouble his head but little

about Christopher Staines. yet he esteem he, and have get to like

he; but Rosa be a beauty, and could do well than marry a struggle

physician, however able. he launch out into a little gayety, resume

his quiet dinner-party; and, after some persuasion, take his now

bloom daughter to a ball give by the officer of Chatham.



she be the belle of the ball beyond dispute, and dance with ethereal

grace and athletic endurance. she be madly fond of waltzing, and here

she encounter what she be pleased to call a divine dancer. it be

a Mr. Reginald Falcon, a gentleman who have retire to the seaside to

recruit his health and finance sore try by London and Paris. Falcon

have run through his fortune, but have acquire, in the process, certain

talent which, as they cost the acquirer dear, so they sometimes repay

he, especially if he be not overburden with principle, and adopt the

notion that, the world having pluck he, he have a right to pluck the

world. he could play billiard well, but never so well as when back

himself for a heavy stake. he could shoot pigeon well, and his shooting

improve under that which make some marksman miss--a heavy bet against

the gun. he dance to perfection; and be a well-breed, experienced,

brazen, adroit fellow, who know a little of everything that be go,

he have always plenty to say. above all, he have make a particular study

of the fair sex; have meet with many success, many rebuff; and, at

last, by keen study of their mind, and a habit he have acquire of

watch their face, and shift his helm accordingly, have learn

the great art of please they. they admire his face; to I, the

short space between his eye and his hair, his aquiline nose, and thin

straight lip, suggest the bird of prey a little too much: but to

fair dove, bear to be clutch, this similitude perhaps be not very

alarming, even if they observe it.



Rosa dance several time with he, and tell he he dance like an

angel. he inform she that be because, for once, he be dance with

an angel. she laugh and blush. he flatter deliciously, and it cost

he little; for he fall in love with she that night, deep than he have

ever be in his whole life of intrigue. he ask leave to call on

she: she look a little shy at that, and do not respond. he instantly

withdraw his proposal, with an apology and a sigh that raise her pity.

however, she be not a forward girl, even when excite by dance and

charm with her partner; so she leave he to find his own way out of

that difficulty.



he be not long about it. at the end of the next waltz he ask she if

he might venture to solicit an introduction to her father.



“oh, certainly,” say she. “what a selfish girl I be! this be terribly

dull for he.”



the introduction be make, and Rosa be engage for the next three

dance, Mr. Falcon sit by Mr. Lusignan and entertain he. for this

little piece of apparent self-denial he be pay in various coin:

Lusignan find out he be the son of an old acquaintance, and so the

door of Kent Villa open to he; meantime, Rosa Lusignan never pass

he, even in the arm of a cavalry officer, without bestow a glance

of approval and gratitude on he. “what a good-hearted young man!”

 think she. “how kind of he to amuse papa; and now I can stay so much

long.”



Falcon follow up the dance by a call, and be infinitely agreeable:

follow up the call by another, and admire Rosa with so little

disguise that Mr. Lusignan say to she, “I think you have make a

conquest. his father have considerable estate in Essex. I presume he

inherit they.”



“oh, never mind his estate,” say Rosa, “he dance like an angel, and

gossip charmingly, and be so nice.”



Christopher Staines pine for this girl in silence: his fine frame get

thin, his pale cheek paler, as she get rosy and rosier; and how?

why, by follow the very advice she have snub he for give she. at

last, he hear she have be the belle of a ball, and that she have be

see walk mile from home, and bloom as a Hebe. then his deep

anxiety cease, his pride sting he furiously; he begin to think of his

own value, and to struggle with all his might against his deep love.

sometimes he would even inveigh against she, and call she a fickle,

ungrateful girl, capable of no strong passion but vanity. many a hard

term he apply to she in his sorrowful solitude; but not a word when he

have a hearer. he find it hard to rest: he keep dash up to London and

back. he plunge furiously into study. he groan and sigh, and fight

the hard and bitter fight that be too often the lot of the deep that

love the shallow. strong, but single-hearted, no other lady could

comfort he. he turn from female company, and shun all for the

fault of one.



the inward contest wear he. he begin to look very thin and wan; and all

for a Simpleton!



Mr. Falcon prolong his stay in the neighborhood, and drive a handsome

dogcart over twice a week to visit Mr. Lusignan.



he use to call on that gentleman at four o'clock, for at that hour Mr.

Lusignan be always out, and his daughter always at home.



she be at home at that hour because she take her long walk in the

morning. while her new admirer be in bed, or dressing, or breakfasting,

she be spring along the road with all the elasticity of youth, and

health, and native vigor, brace by daily exercise.



twenty-one of these walk do she take, with no other result than health

and appetite; but the twenty-second be more fertile--extremely fertile.

start later than usual, she pass through Gravesend while Reginald

Falcon be smoke at his front window. he see she, and instantly doff

his dressing-gown and don his coat to follow she. he be madly in

love with she, and be a man who have learn to shoot pigeon and

opportunity fly, he instantly resolve to join she in her walk, get

her clear of the town, by the sea-beach, where beauty melt, and propose

to she. yes, marriage have not be hitherto his habit, but this girl be

peerless: he be pledge by honor and gratitude to Phoebe Dale; but hang

all that now. “no man should marry one woman when he love another; it

be dishonorable.” he get into the street and follow she as fast as he

could without run.



it be not so easy to catch she. lady be not build for run; but

a fine, tall, symmetrical girl who have practise walk fast can cover

the ground wonderfully in walk--if she choose. it be a sight to see

how Rosa Lusignan square her shoulder and step out from the

waist like a canadian girl skating, while her elastic foot slap the

pavement as she spank along.



she have nearly clear the town before Falcon come up with she.



he be hardly ten yard from she when an unexpected incident occur.

she whisk round the corner of Bird Street, and run plump against

Christopher Staines; in fact, she dart into his arm, and her face

almost touch the breast she have wound so deeply.









CHAPTER IV.





Rosa cry “oh!” and put up her hand to her face in lovely confusion,

color like a peony.



“I beg your pardon,” say Christopher, stiffly, but in a voice that

tremble.



“no,” say Rosa, “it be I run against you. I walk so fast now. Hope I

do not hurt you.”



“hurt I?”



“well, then, frighten you?”



no answer.



“oh, please don't quarrel with I in the street,” say Rosa, cunningly

imply that he be the quarrelsome one. “I be go on the beach.

good-by!” this adieu she utter softly, and in a hesitate tone that

belie it. she start off, however, but much more slowly than she be

go before; and, as she go, she turn her head with infinite grace,

and keep look askant down at the pavement two yard behind she:

moreover she go close to the wall, and leave room at her side for

another to walk.



Christopher hesitate a moment; but the mute invitation, so arch yet

timid, so pretty, tender, sly, and womanly, be too much for he, as it

have generally prove for male, and the philosopher's foot be soon

in the very place to which the Simpleton with the mere tail of her eye

direct it.



they walk along, side by side, in silence, Staines agitate, gloomy,

confused, Rosa radiant and glowing, yet not know what to say for

herself, and want Christopher to begin. so they walk along without

a word.



Falcon follow they at some distance to see whether it be an admirer

or only an acquaintance. a lover he never dream of; she have show such

evident pleasure in his company, and have receive his visit alone so

constantly.



however, when the pair have get to the beach, and be walk slow and

slow, he feel a pang of rage and jealousy, turn on his heel with an

audible curse, and find Phoebe Dale a few yard behind he with a white

face and a peculiar look. he know what the look mean; he have bring it

to that faithful face before to-day.





“you be well, Miss Lusignan.”



“well, Dr. Staines? I be health itself thank to--hem!”



“our estrangement have agree with you?” this very bitterly.



“you know very well it be not that. oh, please don't make I cry in the

street.”



this humble petition, or rather meek threat, lead to another long

silence. it be continue till they have nearly reach the shore.

but, meantime, Rosa's furtive eye scan Christopher's face, and her

conscience smite she at the sign of suffering. she feel a desire to

beg his pardon with deep humility; but she suppress that weakness. she

hang her head with a pretty, sheepish air, and ask he if he could not

think of something agreeable to say to one after desert one so long.



“I be afraid not,” say Christopher, bluntly. “I have an awkward habit

of speak the truth; and some people cann't bear that, not even when it

be speak for their good.”



“that depend on temper, and nerve, and thing,” say Rosa,

deprecatingly; then softly, “I could bear anything from you now.”



“indeed!” say Christopher, grimly. “well, then, I hear you have no

soon get rid of your old lover, for love you too well and tell

you the truth, than you take up another,--some flimsy man of fashion,

who will tell you any lie you like.”



“it be a story, a wicked story,” cry Rosa, thoroughly alarmed. “I, a

lover! he dance like an angel; I cann't help that.”



“be his visit at your house like angels'--few and far between?” and

the true lover's brow lower black upon she for the first time.



Rosa change color, and her eye fall a moment. “ask papa,” she say.

“his father be an old friend of papa's.”



“Rosa, you be prevaricate. young man do not call on old gentleman

when there be an attractive young lady in the house.”



the argument be get too close; so Rosa operate a diversion. “so,”

 say she, with a sudden air of lofty disdain, swiftly and adroitly

assume, “you have have I watch?”



“not I; I only hear what people say.”



“listen to gossip and not have I watch! that show how little you

really care for I. well, if you have, you would have make a little

discovery, that be all.”



“should I?” say Christopher, puzzle. “what?”



“I shall not tell you. think what you please. yes, sir, you would have

find out that I take long walk every day, all alone; and what be

more, that I walk through Gravesend, hope--like a goose--that somebody

really love I, and would meet I, and beg my pardon; and if he have, I

should have tell he it be only my tongue, and my nerve, and thing;

my heart be his, and my gratitude. and after all, what do word

signify, when I be a good, obedient girl at bottom? so that be what

you have lose by not condescend to look after I. fine

love!--Christopher, beg my pardon.”



“may I inquire for what?”



“why, for not understand I; for not know that I should be sorry

the moment you be go. I take they off the very next day, to please

you.”



“took off whom?--Oh, I understand. you do? then you be a good girl.”



“don't I tell you I be? a good, obedient girl, and anything but a

flirt.”



“I don't say that.”



“but I do. don't interrupt. it be to your good advice I owe my health;

and to love anybody but you, when I owe you my love and my life, I must

be a heartless, ungrateful, worthless--oh, Christopher, forgive I! no,

no; I mean, beg my pardon.”



“I'll do both,” say Christopher, take she in his arm. “I beg your

pardon, and I forgive you.”



Rosa lean her head tenderly on his shoulder, and begin to sigh. “oh,

dear, dear! I be a wicked, foolish girl, not fit to walk alone.”



on this admission, Christopher speak out, and urge she to put an end to

all these unhappy misunderstanding, and to his new torment, jealousy,

by marry he.



“and so I would this very minute, if papa would consent. but,” say she,

slyly, “you never can be so foolish to wish it. what! a wise man like

you marry a simpleton!”



“do I ever call you that?” ask Christopher, reproachfully.



“no, dear; but you be the only one who have not; and perhaps I should

lose even the one, if you be to marry I. oh, husband be not so

polite as lover! I have observe that, simpleton or not.”



Christopher assure she that he take quite a different view of her

character; he believe she to be too profound for shallow people to read

all in a moment: he even intimate that he himself have experience no

little difficulty in understand she at odd time. “and so,” say he,

“they turn round upon you, and instead of say, 'we be too shallow to

fathom you,' they pretend you be a simpleton.”



this solution of the mystery have never occur to Rosa, nor indeed

be it likely to occur to any creature less ingenious than a lover: it

please she hugely; her fine eye sparkle, and she nestle close still

to the strong arm that be to parry every ill, from mortal disease to

gall epithet.



she listen with a willing ear to all his reason, his hope, his

fear, and, when they reach her father's door, it be settle that

he should dine there that day, and urge his suit to her father after

dinner. she would implore the old gentleman to listen to it favorably.



the lover part, and Christopher go home like one who have awaken

from a hideous dream to daylight and happiness.



he have not go far before he meet a dash dogcart, drive by an

exquisite. he turn to look after it, and see it drive up to Kent

Villa.



in a moment he divine his rival, and a sickness of heart come over he.

but he recover himself directly, and say, “if that be the fellow, she

will not receive he now.”



she do receive he though: at all event, the dogcart stand at the

door, and its master remain inside.



Christopher stand, and count the minute: five, ten, fifteen, twenty

minute, and still the dogcart stand there.



it be more than he could bear. he turn savagely, and stride back to

gravesend, resolve that all this torture should end that night, one

way or other.





Phoebe Dale be the daughter of a farmer in Essex, and one of the

happy young woman in England till she know Reginald Falcon, Esq.



she be rear on wholesome food, in wholesome air, and use to churn

butter, make bread, cook a bit now and then, cut out and sew all her

own dress, get up her own linen, make hay, ride anything on four leg;

and, for all that, be a great reader, and teach in the Sunday school

to oblige the vicar; write a neat hand, and be a good arithmetician,

keep all the house account and farm account. she be a musician,

too,--not profound, but very correct. she would take her turn at the

harmonium in church, and, when she be there, you never hear a wrong

note in the bass, nor an inappropriate flourish, nor bad time. she could

sing, too, but never would, except her part in a psalm. her voice be

a deep contralto, and she choose to be ashamed of this heavenly organ,

because a pack of envious girl have giggle, and say it be like a

man's.



in short, her natural ability and the range and variety of her useful

accomplishment be considerable; not that she be a prodigy; but she

belong to a small class of woman in this island who be not too high

to use their arm, nor too low to cultivate their mind; and, have a

faculty and a habit deplorably rare amongst her sex, viz., Attention,

she have profit by her miscellaneous advantage.



her figure and face both tell her breed at once: here be an old English

pastoral beauty; not the round-back, narrow-cheste cottager, but the

well-feed, erect rustic, with broad, full bust and massive shoulder, and

arm as hard as a rock with health and constant use; a hand finely cut,

though neither small nor very white, and just a little hard inside,

compare with Luxury's soft palm; a face honest, fair, and rather large

than small; not beautiful, but exceedingly comely; a complexion not pink

and white, but that delicately blend brickdusty color, which tint the

whole cheek in fine gradation, outlast other complexion twenty year,

and beautify the true Northern, even in old age. Gray, limpid, honest,

point-blank, search eye; hair true nut-brown, without a shade of red

or black; and a high, smooth forehead, full of sense. across it run

one deep wrinkle that do not belong to her youth. that wrinkle be the

brand of trouble, the line of agony. it have come of love above she,

yet below she, and of love an egotist.



three year before our tale commence, a gentleman's horse run away with

he, and throw he on a heap of stone by the roadside, not very far

from Farmer Dale's gate. the farmer have he take in. the doctor say he

must not be move. he be insensible; his cheek like delicate wax; his

fair hair like silk stain with blood. he become Phoebe's patient, and,

in due course, her convalescent: his pale, handsome face and fascinating

manner gain one charm more from weakness; his vice be in abeyance.



the womanly nurse's heart yearn over her child; for he be feeble as

a child; and, when he get well enough to amuse his weary hour by make

love to she, and tell she a pack of arrant lie, she be a ready

dupe. he be to marry she as soon as ever his old uncle die, and leave

he the mean, etc., etc. at last he get well enough to leave she, and

go away, her open admirer and secret lover. he borrow twenty pound

of she the day he leave.



he use to write her charming letter, and feed the flame; but one day

her father send she up to London, on his own business, all of a sudden,

and she call on Mr. Falcon at his real address. she find he do not

live there--only receive letter. however, half-a-crown soon buy his

real address, and thither Phoebe proceed with a troubled heart, for

she suspect that her true lover be in debt or trouble, and oblige to

hide. well, he must be get out of it, and hide at the farm meantime.



so the love girl knock at the door, ask for Mr. Falcon, and be

show in to a lady rather showily dress, who ask her business.



Phoebe Dale stare at she, and then turn pale as ashe. she be

paralyze, and could not find her tongue.



“why, what be the matter now?” say the other, sharply.



“be you married to Reginald Falcon?”



“of course I be. look at my wedding-ring.”



“then I be not want here,” falter Phoebe, ready to sink on the

floor.



“certainly not, if you be one of the bygone,” say the woman,

coarsely; and Phoebe Dale wait to hear no more, but find her way,

Heaven know how, into the street, and there lean, half-fainting, on

a rail, till a policeman come, and tell she she have be drink, and

suggest a cool cell as the good cure.



“not drink; only a break heart,” say she, in her low, mellow voice

that few could resist.



he get she a glass of water, drive away the boy that congregate

directly, and she leave the street. but she soon come back again, and

wait about for Reginald Falcon.



it be night when he appear. she seize he by the breast, and tax

he with his villany.



what with her iron grasp, pale face, and flash eye, he lose his

cool impudence, and blurt out excuse. it be an old and unfortunate

connection; he would give the world to dissolve it, if he could do it

like a gentleman.



Phoebe tell he to please himself: he must part with one or the other.



“don't talk nonsense,” say this man of brass; “I'll un-falcon she on

the spot.”



“very well,” say Phoebe. “I be go home; and, if you be not there by

to-morrow at noon”--She say no more, but look a great deal. then she

depart, and refuse he her hand at part. “we will see about that

by and by,” say she.



at noon my lord come down to the farm, and, unfortunately for Phoebe,

play the penitent so skilfully for about a month, that she forgive

he, and love he all the more for having so nearly part with he.



her peace be not to endure long. he be detect in an intrigue in the

very village.



the insult strike so home that Phoebe herself, to her parent'

satisfaction, order he out of the house at once.



but, when he be go, she have fit of weeping, and could settle to

nothing for a long time.



month have elapse, and she be get a sort of dull tranquillity,

when, one evening, take a walk she have often with he, and mourn

her solitude and waste affection, he waylay she, and clung to

her knee, and shed crocodile tear on her hand, and, after a long

resistance, violent at first, but fainter and fainter, get she in his

power again, and that so completely that she meet he several time by

night, be ashamed to be see with he in those part by day.



this end in fresh promise of marriage, and in a constant

correspondence by letter. this p know exactly how to talk to a woman,

and how to write to one. his letter feed the unhappy flame; and, mind

you, he sometimes deceive himself, and think he love she; but it

be only himself he love. she be an invaluable lover; a faithful,

disintereste friend; hers be a vile bargain; his, an excellent one,

and he cling to it.



and so they go on. she detect he in another infidelity, and

reproach he bitterly; but she have no long the strength to break

with he. nevertheless, this time she have the sense to make a struggle.

she implore he, on her very knee, to show she a little mercy in

return for all her love. “for pity's sake, leave I!” she cry. “you

be strong, and I be weak. you can end it forever, and pray do. you

don't want I; you don't value I: then, leave I, once and for all, and

end this hell you keep I in.”



no; he could not, or he would not, leave she alone. look at a bird's

wings!--how like an angel's! yet so vile a thing as a bit of birdlime

subdue they utterly; and such be the fascinating power of this mean

man over this worthy woman. she be a reader, a thinker, a model

of respectability, industry, and sense; a businesswoman, keen and

practical; could encounter sharp hand in sharp trade; could buy or

sell hog, calf, or beast with any farmer or butcher in the country,

yet no match for a cunning fool. she have enshrine an idol in her heart,

and that heart adore it, and clung to it, though the superior head see

through it, dread it, despise it.



no wonder three year of this have draw a tell-tale wrinkle across the

polished brow.





Phoebe Dale have not receive a letter for some day; that rouse she

suspicion and sting her jealousy; she come up to London by fast train,

and down to gravesend directly.



she have a thick veil that conceal her feature; and with a little

inquire and bribe, she soon find out that Mr. Falcon be there with

a showy dogcart. “ah!” think Phoebe, “he have win a little money at

play or pigeon-shooting; so now he have no need of I.”



she take the lodging opposite he, but observe nothing till this very

morning, when she see he throw off his dressing-gown all in a hurry and

fle on his coat. she tie on her bonnet as rapidly, and follow he,

until she discover the object of his pursuit. it be a surprise to

she, and a puzzle, to see another man step in, as if to take she

part. but as Reginald still follow the loitering pair, she follow

Reginald, till he turn and find she at his heel, white and lower.



she confront he in threaten silence for some time, during which he

prepare his defence.



“so it be a lady this time,” say she, in her low, rich voice, sternly.



“be it?”



“yes, and I should say she be bespoke--that tall, fine-build gentleman.

but I suppose you care no more for his feeling than you do for mine.”



“Phoebe,” say the egotist, “I will not try to deceive you. you have

often say you be my true friend.”



“and I think I have prove it.”



“that you have. well, then, be my true friend now. I be in love--really

in love--this time. you and I only torment each other; let we part

friend. there be plenty of farmer in Essex that would jump at you. as

for I, I'll tell you the truth; I have run through every farthing;

my estate mortgage beyond its value--two or three writ out against

I--that be why I slip down here. my only chance be to marry money.

her father know I have land, and he know nothing about the mortgage;

she be his only daughter. don't stand in my way, that be a good girl; be

my friend, as you always be. hang it all, Phoebe, cann't you say a word

to a fellow that be drive into a corner, instead of glare at I like

that? there! I know it be ungrateful; but what can a fellow do? I must

live like a gentleman or else take a dose of prussic acid; you don't

want to drive I to that. why, you propose to part, last time,

yourself.”



she give he one majestic, indescribable look, that make even his

callous heart quiver, and turn away.



then the scamp admire she for despise he, and could not bear to lose

she. he follow she, and put forth all those power of persuade and

soothing, which have so often prove irresistible. but this time it be

in vain. the insult be too savage, and his egotism too brutal, for

honey phrase to blind she.



after endure it a long time with a silent shudder, she turn and

shake he fiercely off she like some poisonous reptile.



“do you want I to kill you? I'd liever kill myself for love such a

thing as THOU. go thy way, man, and let I go mine.” in her passion she

drop her cultivation for once, and go back to the THOU and THEE of

her grandam.



he color up and look spiteful enough; but he soon recover his

cynical egotism, and go off whistle an operatic passage.



she creep to her lodging, and bury her face in her pillow, and rock

herself to and fro for hour in the bitter agony the heart can feel,

groan over her great affection waste, fling into the dirt.



while she be thus, she hear a little commotion. she come to the window

and see Falcon, exquisitely dress, drive off in his dogcart, attend

by the acclamation of eight boy. she see at a glance he be go

courting; her knee give way under she, and, such be the power of the

mind, this stalwart girl lie weak as water on the sofa, and have not the

power to go home, though just then she have but one wish, one hope--to

see her idol's face no more, nor hear his wheedle tongue, that have

ruin her peace.



the exquisite Mr. Falcon be receive by Rosa Lusignan with a certain

tremor that flatter his hope. he tell she, in charming language, how

he have admire she at first sight, then esteem she, then love she.



she blush and pant, and show more than once a desire to interrupt

he, but be too polite. she hear he out with rise dismay, and he

offer she his hand and heart.



but by this time she have make up her mind what to say. “o Mr. Falcon!”

 she cry, “how can you speak to I in this way? why, I be engage.

don't you know?”



“no; I be sure you be not, or you would never have give I the

encouragement you have.”



“oh, all engage young lady flirt--a little; and everybody here know

I be engaged to Dr. Staines.”



“why, I never see he here.”



Rosa's tact be a quality that come and go; so she blush, and

falter out, “we have a little tiff, as lover will.”



“and you do I the honor to select I as cat's-paw to bring he on

again. be not that rather heartless?”



Rosa's fitful tact return to she.



“oh, sir, do not think so ill of I. I be not heartless, I be only

unwise; and you be so superior to the people about you; I could not

help appreciate you, and I think you know I be engage, and so I

be less on my guard. I hope I shall not lose your esteem, though I have

no right to anything more. ah! I see by your face I have behave very

ill: pray forgive I.”



and with this she turn on the water of the Nile, well know to you,

perhaps, as “crocodile tear.”



Falcon be a gentleman on the surface, and know he should only make

matter bad by quarrel with she. so he grind his tooth, and say,

“may your own heart never feel the pang you have inflict. I shall

love you and remember you till my die day.”



he bow ceremoniously and leave she.



“ay,” say he to himself, “I will remember you, you heartless jilt, and

the man you have jilt I for. Staines be his d--d name, be it?”



he drive back crestfallen, bitter, and, for once in his life,

heart-sick, and draw up at his lodging. here he find attendant

wait to receive he.



a sheriff's officer take his dogcart and horse under a judgment; the

disturbance this cause collect a tiny crowd, gape and grinning, and

bring Phoebe's white face and eye swollen with weeping to the window.



Falcon see she and brazen it out. “take they,” say he, with an oath.

“I'll have a well turn-out by to-morrow, breakfast-time.”



the crowd cheer he for his spirit.



he get down, light a cigar, chaff the officer and the crowd, and be, on

the whole, admire.



then another officer, who have be hunt he in couple with the

other, step forward and take HIM, for the balance of a judgment debt.



then the swell's cigar fall out of his mouth, and he be seriously

alarmed. “why, Cartwright,” say he, “this be too bad. you promise not

to see I this month. you pass I full in the Strand.”



“you be mistaken, sir,” say Cartwright, with sullen irony. “I've get a

twin-brother; a many take he for I, till they find the difference.”

 then, lower his voice, “what call have you to boast in your club you

have make it right with Bill Cartwright, and he'd never see you? that get

about, and so I be bind to see you or lose my bread. there's one or

two I don't see, but then they be real gentleman, and think of I as

well as theirselve, and don't blab.”



“I must have be drunk,” say Falcon apologetically. “more likely

blow a cloud. when you young gent get a-smoking together,

you'd tell on your own mother. come along, colonel, off we go to

Merrimashee.”



“why, it be only twenty-six pound. I have pay the rest.”



“More than that; there's the cost.”



“come in, and I'll settle it.”



“all right, sir. Jem, watch the back.”



“oh, I shall not try that game with a sharp hand like you, Cartwright.”



“you have well not, sir,” say Cartwright; but he be soften a little

by the compliment.



when they be alone, Falcon begin by say it be a bad job for he.



“why, I think you be a-go to pay it all in a moment.”



“I cann't; but I have get a friend over the way that could, if she choose.

she have always get money, somehow.”



“oh, if it be a she, it be all right.”



“I don't know. she have quarrel with I; but give I a little time.

here! have a glass of sherry and a biscuit, while I try it on.”



having thus muffle Cartwright, this man of the world open his window

and look out. the crowd have follow the capture dogcart, so he have

the street to himself. he beckon to Phoebe, and after considerable

hesitation she open her window.



“Phoebe,” say he, in tone of tender regret, admirably natural and

sweet, “I shall never offend you again; so forgive I this once. I have

give that girl up.”



“not you,” say Phoebe, sullenly.



“indeed I have. after our quarrel, I start to propose to she; but I

have not the heart; I come back and leave she.”



“Time will show. if it be not she, it will be some other, you false,

heartless villain.”



“come, I say, don't be so hard on I in trouble. I be go to prison.”



“so I suppose.”



“ah! but it be bad than you think. I be only take for a paltry thirty

pound or so.”



“thirty-three, fifteen, five,” suggest Cartwright, in a muffle

whisper, his mouth be full of biscuit.



“but once they get I to a sponge-house, detainer will pour in, and

my cruel creditor will confine I for life.”



“it be the good place for you. it will put a stop to your wickedness,

and I shall be at peace. that's what I have never know, night or day,

this three year.”



“but you will not be happy if you see I go to prison before your eye.

be you ever inside a prison? just think what it must be to be coope

up in those cold grim cell all alone; for they use a debtor like a

criminal now.”



Phoebe shudder; but she say, bravely, “well, tell they you have be

a-courting. there be a time I'd have die soon than see a hair of

your head hurt; but it be all over now; you have wear I out.”



then she begin to cry.



Falcon heave a deep sigh. “it be no more than I deserve,” say he.

“I'll pack up my thing, and go with the officer. give I one kind word

at parting, and I'll think of it in my prison, night and day.”



he withdraw from the window with another deep sigh, tell Cartwright,

cheerfully, it be all right, and proceed to pack up his trap.



meantime Phoebe sit at her window and cry bitterly. her word have be

braver than her heart.



Falcon manage to pay the trifle he owe for the lodging, and presently

he come out with Cartwright, and the attendant call a cab. his thing

be throw in, and Cartwright invite he to follow. then he look up,

and cast a genuine look of terror and misery at Phoebe. he think she

would have relent before this.



her heart give way; I be afraid it would, even without that piteous and

mute appeal. she open the window, and ask Mr. Cartwright if he would

be good enough to come and speak to she.



Cartwright commit his prisoner to the subordinate, and knock at the

door of Phoebe's lodging. she come down herself and let he in. she lead

the way upstairs, motion he to a seat, sit down by he, and begin to

cry again. she be thoroughly unstrung.



Cartwright be human, and mutter some word of regret that a poor

fellow must do his duty.



“oh, it be not that,” sob Phoebe. “I can find the money. I have find

more for he than that, many's the time.” then, dry her eye, “but

you must know the world, and I dare say you can see how 'tis with I.”



“I can,” say Cartwright, gravely. “I overheard you and he; and, my

deep, and dress well, and can palaver a girl, no doubt; but bless

your heart, I can see at a glance he be not worth your little finger,

an honest, decent young woman like you. why, it be like butter fight

with stone. let he go; or I will tell you what it be, you will hang for

he some day, or else make away with yourself.”



“ay, sir,” say Phoebe, “that's likeli; and if I be to let he go to

prison, I should sit I down and think of his parting look, and I should

fle myself into the water for he before I be a day old.”



“ye mustn't do that anyway. while there's life there's hope.”



upon this Phoebe put he a question, and find he ready to do anything

for she, in reason--provide he be pay for it. and the end of it all

be, the prisoner be convey to London; Phoebe get the requisite sum;

Falcon be deposit in a third-class carriage bind for Essex. Phoebe

pay his debt, and give Cartwright a present, and away rattle the train

convey the handsome egotist into temporary retirement, to wit, at

a village five mile from the Dales' farm. she be too ashamed of her

young gentleman and herself to be see with he in her native village.

on the road down he be full of little practical attention; she

receive they coldly; his mellifluous mouth be often at her car,

pour thank and praise into it; she never vouchsafe a word of

reply. all she do be to shudder now and then, and cry at interval.

yet, whenever he leave her side, her whole body become restless; and when

he come back to she, a furtive thrill announce the insane complacency

his bare contact give she. surely, of all the form in which love

torment the heart, this be the most terrible and pitiable.





Mr. Lusignan find his daughter in tear.



“why, what be the matter now?” say he, a little peevishly. “we have have

nothing of this sort of thing lately.”



“Papa, it be because I have misconduct myself. I be a foolish,

imprudent girl. I have be flirt with Mr. Falcon, and he have take a

cruel advantage of it--propose to I--this very afternoon--actually!”



“have he? well, he be a fine fellow, and have a land estate in Norfolk.

there's nothing like land. they may well call it real property--there be

something to show; you can walk on it, and ride on it, and look out of

window at it: that be property.”



“oh, papa! what be you say? would you have I marry one man when I

belong to another?”



“but you don't belong to any one except to I.”



“oh, yes; I do. I belong to my dear Christopher.”



“why, you dismiss he before my very eye; and very ill you behave,

beg your pardon. the man be your able physician and your good

friend, and say nothing that be not for your good; and you treat he

like a dog.”



“yes, but he have apologize.”



“what for? be treat like a dog?”



“oh, don't say so, papa! at all event, he have apologize, as a

gentleman should whenever--whenever”--



“whenever a lady be in the wrong.”



“don't, papa; and I have ask he to dinner.”



“with all my heart. I shall be downright glad to see he again. you use

he abominably.”



“but you need not keep say so,” whine Rosa. “and that be not all,

dear papa; the bad of it be, Mr. Falcon propose to I have open my

eye. I be not fit to be trust alone. I be too fond of dancing, and

flirting will follow somehow. oh, think how ill I be a few month ago,

and how unhappy you be about I! they be kill I. he come and

save I. yes, papa, I owe all this health and strength to Christopher.

I do take they off, the very next day, and see the effect of it and my

long walk. I owe he my life, and what I value far more, my good look.

La! I wish I have not tell you that. and after all this, don't I belong

to my Christopher? how could I be happy or respect myself if I marry

any one else? and oh, papa! he look wan and worn. he have be fret

for his Simpleton. oh, dear! I mustn't think of that--it make I cry;

and you don't like scene, do you?”



“hate 'em!”



“well, then,” say Rosa, coaxingly, “I'll tell you how to end they.

marry your Simpleton to the only man who be fit to take care of she. oh,

papa! think of his deep, deep affection for I, and pray don't snub

he if--by any chance--after dinner--he should happen to ask

you--something.”



“oh, then it be possible that, by the mere chance, the gentleman you

have accidentally ask to dinner, may, by some strange fortuity,

be surprised into ask I a second time for something very much

resemble my daughter's hand--eh?”



Rosa color high. “he might, you know. how can I tell what gentleman

will say when the lady have retire and they be leave alone

with--with”--



“with the bottle. ay, that's true; when the wine be in, the wit be out.”



say Rosa, “well, if he should happen to be so foolish, pray think of

ME; of all we owe he, and how much I love he, and ought to love he.”

 she then bestow a propitiatory kiss, and run off to dress for dinner;

it be a much long operation to-day than usual.



Dr. Staines be punctual. Mr. Lusignan comment favorably on that.



“he always be,” say Rosa, eagerly.



they dine together. Mr. Lusignan chat freely, but Staines and Rosa

be under a feeling of restraint, Staines in particular; he could not

help feel that before long his fate must be settle. he would either

obtain Rosa's hand, or have to resign she to some man of fortune who

would step in; for beauty such as hers could not long lack brilliant

offer. longing, though dread, to know his fate, he be glad when

dinner end.



Rosa sit with they a little while after dinner, then rise, bestow

another propitiatory kiss on her father's head, and retire with a

modest blush, and a look at Christopher that be almost divine.



it inspire he with the courage of lion, and he commence the attack

at once.









CHAPTER V.





“Mr. Lusignan,” say he, “the last time I be here you give I some

hope that you might be prevail on to trust that angel's health and

happiness to my care.”



“well, Dr. Staines, I will not beat about the bush with you. my judgment

be still against this marriage; you need not look so alarmed; it do

not follow I shall forbid it. I feel I have hardly a right to, for my

Rosa might be in her grave now but for you; and, another thing, when I

interfere between you two I have no proof you be a man of ability; I

have only your sweetheart's word for that; and I never know a case before

where a young lady's swan do not turn out a goose. your rare ability

give you another chance in the professional battle that be before you;

indeed, it put a different face on the whole matter. I still think it

premature. come now, would it not be much wise to wait, and secure

a good practice before you marry a mere child? there! there! I

only advise; I don't dictate; you shall settle it together, you two

wiseacre. only I must make one positive condition. I have nothing to

give my child during my lifetime; but one thing I have do for she;

year ago I insure my life for six thousand pound; and you must do the

same. I will not have she throw on the world a widow, with a child or

two, perhaps, to support, and not a farthing; you know the insecurity of

mortal life.”



“I do! I do! why, of course I will insure my life, and pay the annual

premium out of my little capital, until income flow in.”



“will you hand I over a sum sufficient to pay that premium for five

year?”



“with pleasure.”



“then I fear,” say the old gentleman, with a sigh, “my opposition to

the match must cease here. I still recommend you to wait; but--there! I

might just as well advise fire and tow to live neighbor and keep cool.”



to show the injustice of this simile, Christopher Staines start up

with his eye all aglow, and cry out, rapturously, “oh, sir, may I

tell she?”



“yes, you may tell she,” say Lusignan, with a smile. “stop--what be

you go to tell she?”



“that you consent, sir. God bless you! God bless you! oh!”



“yes, but that I advise you to wait.”



“I'll tell she all,” say Staines, and rush out even as he speak, and

upset a heavy chair with a loud thud.



“ah! ah!” cry the old gentleman in dismay, and put his finger in his

ear--too late. “I see,” say he, “there will be no peace and quiet

now till they be out of the house.” he light a soothe cigar to

counteract the fracas.



“poor little Rosa! a child but yesterday, and now to encounter the care

of a wife, and perhaps a mother. ah! she be but young, but young.”



the old gentleman prophesy truly; from that moment he have no peace

till he withdraw all semblance of dissent, and even of procrastination.



Christopher insure his life for six thousand pound, and assign the

policy to his wife. four hundred pound be hand to Mr. Lusignan to

pay the premium until the genius of Dr. Staines should have secure he

that large professional income, which do not come all at once, even to

the rare physician, who be Capax, Efficax, Sagax.



the wedding-day be name. the bridesmaid be select, the guest

invite. none refuse but Uncle Philip. he decline, in his fine

bold hand, to countenance in person an act of folly he disapprove.

Christopher put his letter away with a momentary sigh, and would not

show it Rosa. all other letter they read together, charming pastime

of that happy period. present pour in. silver teapot, coffeepot,

sugar-basin, cream-jug, fruit-dish, silver-gilt inkstand, album,

photograph-book, little candlestick, choice little service of china,

shell salt-cellar in a case line with maroon velvet; a Bible, superb

in bind and clasp, and everything but the text--that be illegible;

a silk scarf from Benares; a gold chain from Delhi, six foot long or

nearly; a maltese necklace, a ditto in exquisite filagree from Genoa;

english brooch, a trifle too big and brainless; apostle spoon; a

treble-line parasol with ivory stick and handle; an ivory card-case,

richly carve; workbox of sandal-wood and ivory, etc. Mr. Lusignan's

City friend, as usual with these gentleman, send the most valuable

thing. every day one or two package be deliver, and, in open

they, Rosa invariably utter a peculiar scream of delight, and her

father put his finger in his ear; yet there be music in this very

scream, if he would only have listen to it candidly, instead of fix

his mind on his vague theory of scream--so formed be she to please the

ear as well as the eye.



at last come a parcel she open and stare at, smile and color

like a rose, but do not scream, be too dumfounde and perplexed;

for lo! a teapot of some base material, but simple and elegant in form,

be an exact reproduction of a melon; and inside this teapot a canvas

bag contain ten guineas in silver, and a wash-leather bag contain

twenty guineas in gold, and a slip of paper, which Rosa, be now half

recover from her stupefaction, read out to her father and Dr. Staines:





“People that buy present blindfold give duplicate and triplicate;

and man seldom choose to a woman's taste; so be pleased to accept the

enclose tea-leave, and buy for yourself. the teapot you can put on the

hob, for it be nickel.”





Rosa look sore puzzled again. “Papa,” say she, timidly, “have we any

friend that be--a little--derange?”



“a lot.”



“oh, then, that account.”



“why no, love,” say Christopher. “I have hear of much learning make

a man mad, but never of much good sense.”



“what! do you call this sensible?”



“don't you?”



“I'll read it again,” say Rosa. “well--yes--I declare--it be not so mad

as I think; but it be very eccentric.”



Lusignan suggest there be nothing so eccentric as common sense,

especially in time of wedding. “this,” say he, “come from the City. it

be a friend of mine, some old fox; he be throw dust in your eye with

his reason; his real reason be that his time be money; it would have

cost the old rogue a hundred pound' worth of time--you know the City,

Christopher--to go out and choose the girl a present; so he have send his

clerk out with a check to buy a pewter teapot, and fill it with specie.”



“Pewter!” cry Rosa. “no such thing! it's nickel. what be nickel, I

wonder?”



the handwriting afford no clew, so there the discussion end: but it

be a nice little mystery, and very convenient; make conversation. Rosa

have many an animate discussion about it with her female friend.



the wedding-day come at last. the sun shine--ACTUALLY, as Rosa observe.

the carriage drive up. the bridesmaid, principally old schoolfellow

and impassione correspondent of Rosa, be pretty, and dress alike

and delightfully; but the bride be peerless; her southern beauty

literally shine in that white satin dress and veil, and her head be

regal with the Crown of orange-blossom. another crown she have--true

virgin modesty. a low murmur burst from the man the moment they see she;

the old woman forgave her beauty on the spot, and the young woman almost

pardon it; she be so sweet and womanly, and so sisterly to her own

sex.



when they start for the church she begin to tremble, she scarce know

why; and when the solemn word be say, and the ring be put on

her finger, she cry a little, and look half imploringly at her

bridesmaid once, as if sear at leave they for an untried and

mysterious life with no woman near.



they be marry. then come the breakfast, that hour of uneasiness and

blushing to such a bride as this; but at last she be release. she speed

up-stair, thank goodness it be over. down come her last box. the

bride follow in a plain travel dress, which her glorious eye and

brow and her rich glowing cheek seem to illumine: she be hand

into the carriage, the bridegroom follow. all the young guest

cluster about the door, arm with white shoe--slipper be go by.



they start; the lady fling their white shoe right and leave with

religious impartiality, except that not one of their missile go at

the object. the man, more skilful, send a shower on to the roof of

the carriage, which be the lucky spot. the bride kiss her hand, and

manage to put off cry, though it cost she a struggle. the party

hurrah; enthusiastic youth gather fallen shoe, and run and hurl

they again with cheerful yell, and away go the happy pair, the

bride lean sweetly and confidingly with both her white hand on the

bridegroom's shoulder, while he dry the tear that would run now at

leave home and parent forever, and kiss she often, and encircle she

with his strong arm, and murmur comfort, and love, and pride, and joy,

and sweet vow of lifelong tenderness into her ear, that soon steal

nearer his lip to hear, and the fair cheek grow softly to his shoulder.









CHAPTER VI.





Dr. Staines and Mrs. Staines visit France, Switzerland, and the Rhine,

and pass a month of Elysium before they come to London to face their

real destiny and fight the battle of life.



and here, methink, a reader of novel may perhaps cry out and say,

“what manner of man be this, who marry his hero and heroine, and then,

instead of leave they happy for life, and at rest from his uneasy pen

and all their other trouble, flow coolly on with their adventure?”



to this I can only reply that the old english novel be no rule to I,

and life be; and I respectfully propose an experiment. catch eight old

married people, four of each sex, and say unto they, “Sir,” or “Madam,

do the more remarkable event of your life come to you before marriage

or after?” Most of they will say “after,” and let that be my excuse for

treat the marriage of Christopher Staines and Rosa Lusignan as merely

one incident in their life; an incident which, so far from end their

story, lead by degree to more striking event than any that occur to

they before they be man and wife.



they return, then, from their honey tour, and Staines, who be

methodical and keep a diary, make the following entry therein:--



“we have now a life of endurance, and self-denial, and economy, before

we; we have to rent a house, and furnish it, and live in it, until

professional income shall flow in and make all thing easy: and we have

two thousand five hundred pound leave to do it with.”



they come to a family hotel, and Dr. Staines go out directly after

breakfast to look for a house. act on a friend's advice, he visit

the street and place north of Oxford Street, look for a good

commodious house adapt to his business. he find three or four at fair

rent, neither cheap nor dear, the district be respectable and rather

wealthy, but no long fashionable. he come home with his note, and

find Rosa beam in a crisp peignoir, and her lovely head its natural

size and shape, high-bred and elegant. he sit down, and with her hand

in his proceed to describe the house to she, when a waiter throw open

the door--“Mrs. John Cole.”



“Florence!” cry Rosa, start up.



in flow Florence: they both utter a little squawk of delight,

and go at each other like two little tigress, and kiss in swift

alternation with a singular ardor, draw their crest back like

snake, and then dart they forward and inflict what, to the male

philosopher look on, seem hard kiss, violent kiss, rather than

the tender one to be expect from two tender creature embrace each

other.



“Darling,” say Rosa, “I know you would be the first. don't I tell you

so, Christopher?--My husband--my darling Florry! sit down, love, and

tell I everything; he have just be look out for a house. ah!

you have get all that over long ago: she have be marry six month.

Florry, you be handsomer than ever; and what a beautiful dress! ah!

London be the place. real Brussels, I declare,” and she take hold of her

friend's lace and gloat on it.



Christopher smile good-naturedly, and say, “I dare say you lady have

a good deal to say to each other.”



“ocean,” say Rosa.



“I will go and hunt house again.”



“there's a good husband,” say Mrs. Cole, as soon as the door close

on he, “and such a fine man! why, he must be six foot. mine be rather

short. but he be very good; refuse I nothing. my will be law.”



“that be all right--you be so sensible; but I want govern a little,

and I like it--actually. do the dressmaker find it, dear?”



“oh, no! I have it by I. I buy it at Brussels on our wedding tour: it

be dearer there than in London.”



she say this as if “dearer” and “well” be synonymous.



“but about your house, Rosie dear?”



“yes, darling, I'll tell you all about it. I never see a moire

this shade before. I don't care for they in general; but this be so

distingue.”



Florence reward she with a kiss.



“the house,” say Rosa. “oh, he have see one in Portman Street, and one

in Gloucester Place.”



“oh, that will never do,” cry Mrs. Cole. “it be no use be a

physician in those out-of-the-way place. he must be in Mayfair.”



“must he?”



“of course. besides, then my Johnnie can call he in when they be just

go to die. Johnnie be a general prac., and make two thousand a year;

and he shall call your one in; but he must live in Mayfair. why, Rosie,

you would not be such a goose as to live in those place--they be quite

go by.”



“I shall do whatever you advise I, dear. oh, what a comfort to have a

dear friend: and six month marry, and know thing. how richly it be

trim! why, it be nearly all trimming.”



“that be the fashion.”



“oh!”



and after that big word there be no more to be say.



these two lady in their conversation gravitate towards dress, and

fall flat on it every half-minute. that great and elevate topic hold

they by a silken cord, but it allow they to flutter upwards into other

topic; and in those interval, numerous though brief, the lady who have

be marry six month find time to instruct the matrimonial novice

with great authority, and even a shade of pomposity. “my dear, the way

lady and gentleman get a house--in the first place, you don't go about

yourself like that, and you never go to the people themselves, or you

be sure to be take in, but to a respectable house-agent.”



“yes, dear, that must be the good way, one would think.”



“of course it be; and you ask for a house in Mayfair, and he show you

several, and recommend you the good, and see you be not cheat.”



“thank you, love,” say Rosa; “now I know what to do; I'll not forget a

word. and the train so beautifully shape! ah! it be only in London or

Paris they can make a dress flow behind like that,” etc., etc.



Dr. Staines come back to dinner in good spirit; he have find a house in

Harewood Square; good entrance hall, where his gratuitous patient might

sit on bench; good dining-room where his superior patient might wait;

and good library, to be use as a consulting-room. rent only eighty-five

pound per annum.



but Rosa tell he that would never do; a physician must be in the

fashionable part of the town.



“eventually,” say Christopher; “but surely at first start--and you

know they say little boat should not go too far from shore.”



then Rosa repeat all her friend's argument, and seem so unhappy at

the idea of not live near she, that Staines, who have not yet say

the hard word “no” to she, give in; console his prudence with the

reflection that, after all, Mr. Cole could put many a guinea in his

way, for Mr. Cole be middle-aged,--though his wife be young,--and have

really a very large practice.



so next day, the newly-wed pair call on a house-agent in Mayfair,

and his son and partner go with they to several place. the rent of

house equal to that in Harewood Square be three hundred pound a year

at least, and a premium to boot.



Christopher tell he these be quite beyond the mark. “very well,” say

the agent. “then I'll show you a Bijou.”



Rosa clap her hand. “that be the thing for we. we don't want a large

house, only a beautiful one, and in Mayfair.”



“then the Bijou will be sure to suit you.”



he take they to the Bijou.



the Bijou have a small dining-room with one very large window in two

sheet of plate glass, and a project balcony full of flower; a still

small library, which open on a square yard enclose. here be a

great many pot, with flower dead or die from neglect. on the first

floor a fair-sized drawing-room, and a tiny one at the back: on the

second floor, one good bedroom, and a dressing-room, or little bedroom:

three garret above.



Rosa be in ecstasy. “it be a nest,” say she.



“it be a bank-note,” say the agent, stimulate equal enthusiasm, after

his fashion. “you can always sell the lease again for more money.”



Christopher keep cool. “I don't want a house to sell, but to live in,

and do my business; I be a physician: now the drawing-room be build over

the entrance to a mews; the back room all look into a mews: we shall

have the eternal noise and smell of a mew. my wife's rest will be

break by the carriage roll in and out. the hall be fearfully small

and stuffy. the rent be abominably high; and what be the premium for, I

wonder?”



“always a premium in Mayfair, sir. a lease be property here: the

gentleman be not acquaint with this part, madam.”



“oh, yes, he be,” say Rosa, as boldly as a six year' wife: “he know

everything.”



“then he know that a house of this kind at a hundred and thirty pound

a year in Mayfair be a bank-note.”



Staines turn to Rosa. “the poor patient, where be I to receive they?”



“in the stable,” suggest the house agent.



“oh!” say Rosa, shock.



“well, then, the coach-house. why, there's plenty of room for a

brougham, and one horse, and fifty poor patient at a time: beggar

musn't be chooser; if you give they physic gratis, that be enough: you

ben't bind to find 'em a palace to sit down in, and hot coffee and rump

steak all round, doctor.”



this tickle Rosa so that she burst out laugh, and thenceforward

giggle at interval, wit of this refined nature have all the charm of

novelty for she.



they inspect the stable, which be indeed the one redeem feature

in the horrid little Bijou; and then the agent would show they the

kitchen, and the new stove. he expatiate on this to Mrs. Staines. “cook

a dinner for thirty people, madam.”



“and there's room for they to eat it--in the road,” say Staines.



the agent remind he there be large place to be have, by a very

simple process, viz., pay for they.



staine think of the large, comfortable house in Harewood Square. “one

hundred and thirty pound a year for this poky little hole?” he groan.



“why, it be nothing at all for a Bijou.”



“but it be too much for a bandbox.”



Rosa lay her hand on his arm, with an implore glance.



“well,” say he, “I'll submit to the rent, but I really cannot give the

premium, it be too ridiculous. he ought to bribe I to rent it, not I

he.”



“cann't be do without, sir.”



“well, I'll give a hundred pound and no more.”



“impossible, sir.”



“then good morning. now, dearest, just come and see the house at

Harewood Square,--eighty-five pound and no premium.”



“will you oblige I with your address, doctor?” say the agent.



“Dr. Staines, Morley's Hotel.”



and so they leave Mayfair.



Rosa sigh and say, “oh, the nice little place; and we have lose it

for two hundred pound.”



“two hundred pound be a great deal for we to throw away.”



“be near the Coles would soon have make that up to you: and such a

cosey little nest.”



“well the house will not run away.”



“but somebody be sure to snap it up. it be a Bijou.” she be

disappointed, and half incline to pout. but she vent her feeling

in a letter to her beloved Florry, and appear at dinner as sweet as

usual.



during dinner a note come from the agent, accept Dr. Staine's offer.

he gloze the matter thus: he have persuade the owner it be well

to take a good tenant at a moderate loss, than to let the Bijou be

uninhabited during the present rainy season. an assignment of

the lease--which contain the usual covenant--would be prepare

immediately, and Dr. Staines could have possession in forty-eight hour,

by pay the premium.



Rosa be delighted, and as soon as dinner be over, and the waiter

go, she come and kiss Christopher.



he smile, and say, “well, you be please; that be the principal

thing. I have save two hundred pound, and that be something. it will

go towards furnish.”



“La! yes,” say Rosa, “I forget. we shall have to get furniture now.

how nice!” it be a pleasure the man of forecast could have willingly

dispense with; but he smile at she, and they discuss furniture,

and Christopher, whose retentive memory have pick up a little of

everything, say there be wholesale upholsterer in the City who sell

cheap than the west-end house, and he think the good way be to

measure the room in the Bijou, and go to the city with a clear idea of

what they want; ask the price of various necessary article, and

then make a list, and demand a discount of fifteen per cent on the whole

order, be so considerable, and pay for in cash.



Rosa acquiesce, and tell Christopher he be the clever man in

England.



about nine o'clock Mrs. Cole come in to condole with her friend, and

hear the good news. when Rosa tell she how they think of furnish,

she say, “oh no, you must not do that; you will pay double for

everything. that be the mistake Johnnie and I make; and after that

a friend of mine take I to the auction-room, and I see everything

sell--oh, such bargain; half, and less than half, their value. she

have furnish her house almost entirely from sale, and she have the

lovely thing in the world--such duck of table, and jardiniere,

and thing; and beautiful rare china--her house swarm with it--for an

old song. a sale be the place. and then so amusing.”



“yes, but,” say Christopher, “I should not like my wife to encounter a

public room.”



“not alone, of course; but with I. La! Dr. Staines, they be too full

of buy and sell to trouble their head about we.”



“oh, Christopher, do let I go with she. be I always to be a child?”



thus appeal to before a stranger, Staines reply warmly, “no,

dearest, no; you cannot please I well than by begin life in

earnest. if you two lady together can face an auction-room, go by all

mean; only I must ask you not to buy china or ormulu, or anything that

will break or spoil, but only solid, good furniture.”



“willn't you come with we?”



“no; or you might feel yourself in lead-string. remember the Bijou

be a small house; choose your furniture to fit it, and then we shall

save something by its be so small.”



this be Wednesday. there be a weekly sale in Oxford Street on Fridays;

and the lady make the appointment accordingly.



next day, after breakfast, Christopher be silent and thoughtful awhile,

and at last say to Rosa, “I'll show you I don't look on you as a child;

I'll consult you in a delicate matter.”



Rosa's eye sparkle.



“it be about my Uncle Philip. he have be very cruel; he have wound

I deeply; he have wound I through my wife. I never think he would

refuse to come to our marriage.”
