“and do he? you never show I his letter.”



“you be not my wife then. I keep an affront from you; but now, you

see, I keep nothing.”



“Dear Christie!”



“I be so happy, I have get over that sting--almost; and the memory of

many kind act come back to I; and I don't know what to do. it seem

ungrateful not to visit he--it seem almost mean to call.”



“I'll tell you; take I to see he directly. he willn't hate we forever,

if he see we often. we may as well begin at once. nobody hate I

long.”



Christopher be proud of his wife's courage and wisdom. he kiss

she, beg she to put on the plain dress she could, and they go

together to call on Uncle Philip.



when they get to his house in Gloucester Place, Portman Square, Rosa's

heart begin to quake, and she be right glad when the servant say “not

at home.”



they leave their card and address; and she persuade Christopher to take

she to the sale-room to see the thing.



a lot of broker be there, like vulture; and one after another

step forward and pester they to employ he in the morning. Dr.

Staines decline their service civilly but firmly, and he and Rosa

look over a quantity of furniture, and settle what sort of thing to

buy.



another broker come up, and whenever the couple stop before an

article, proceed to praise it as something most extraordinary. staine

listen in cold, satirical silence, and tell his wife, in French, to do

the same. notwithstanding their marked disgust, the impudent, intrusive

fellow stick to they, and force his venal criticism on they, and make

they uncomfortable, and shorten their tour of observation.



“I think I shall come with you to-morrow,” say Christopher, “or I shall

have these blackguard pester you.”



“oh, Florry will send they to the right-about. she be as brave as a

lion.”



next day Dr. Staines be send for into the City at twelve to pay the

money and receive the lease of the Bijou, and this and the taking

possession occupy he till four o'clock, when he come to his hotel.



meantime, his wife and Mrs. Cole have go to the auction-room.



it be a large room, with a good sprinkling of people, but not crowd

except about the table. at the head of this table--full twenty foot

long--be the auctioneer's pulpit, and the lot be bring in turn to

the other end of the table for sight and sale.



“we must try and get a seat,” say the enterprise Mrs. Cole, and

push boldly in; the timid Rosa follow strictly in her wake, and so

evade the human wave her leader clove. they be importune at every

step by broker thrust catalogue on they, with offer of their

service, yet they soon get to the table. a gentleman resign one

chair, a broker another, and they be seat.



Mrs. Staines let down half her veil, but Mrs. Cole survey the company

point-blank.



the broker who have give up his seat, and now stand behind Rosa, offer

she his catalogue. “no, thank you,” say Rosa; “I have one;” and she

produce it, and study it, yet manage to look furtively at the

company.



there be not above a dozen private person visible from where

Rosa sit; perhaps as many more in the whole room. they be easily

distinguishable by their cleanly appearance: the dealer, male or

female, be more or less rusty, greasy, dirty, aquiline. not even the

amateur be brightly dress; that fundamental error be confine to

Mesdames Cole and Staines. the experienced, however wealthy, do not hunt

bargain in silk and satin.



the auctioneer call “Lot 7. four saucepan, two tray, a kettle, a

bootjack, and a towel-horse.”



these be put up at two shilling, and speedily knock down for five

to a fat old woman in a greasy velvet jacket; blind industry have sew

bugle on it, not artfully, but agriculturally.



“the lady on the left!” say the auctioneer to his clerk. that mean

“get the money.”



the old lady plunge a huge paw into a huge pocket, and pull out a

huge handful of coin--copper, silver, and gold--and pay for the lot;

and Rosa survey her dirty hand and nail with innocent dismay. “oh,

what a dreadful creature!” she whisper; “and what can she want with

those old rubbishy thing? I see a hole in one from here.” the broker

overhear, and say, “she be a dealer, ma'am, and the thing be give

away. she'll sell they for a guinea, easy.”



“don't I tell you?” say Mrs. Cole.



soon after this the superior lot come on, and six very neat bedroom

chair be sell to all appearance for fifteen shilling.



the next lot be identical, and Rosa hazard a bid,--“Sixteen

shilling.”



instantly some dealer, one of the hook-nosed that gather round each

lot as it come to the foot of the table, cry “eighteen shilling.”



“Nineteen,” say Rosa.



“a guinea,” say the dealer.



“don't let it go,” say the broker behind she. “don't let it go, ma'am.”



she color at the intrusion, and leave off bid directly, and

address herself to Mrs. Cole. “why should I give so much, when the

last be sell for fifteen shilling?”



the real reason be that the first lot be not bid for at all, except by

the proprietor. however, the broker give she a very different solution;

he say, “the trade always run up a lady or a gentleman. let I bid for

you; they willn't run I up; they know well.”



Rosa do not reply, but look at Mrs. Cole.



“yes, dear,” say that lady; “you have much well let he bid for you.”



“very well,” say Rosa; “you can bid for this chest of drawer--lot 25.”



when lot 25 come on, the broker bid in the silly possible way, if

his object have be to get a bargain. he begin to bid early and

ostentatiously; the article be protect by somebody or other there

present, who now of course see his way clear; he run it up audaciously,

and it be purchase for Rosa at about the price it could have be

buy for at a shop.



the next thing she want be a set of oak chair.



they go up to twenty-eight pound; then she say, “I shall give no

more, sir.”



“well not lose they,” say the agent; “they be a great bargain;” and

bid another pound for she on his own responsibility.



they be still run up, and Rosa peremptorily refuse to give any more.

she lose they, accordingly, by good luck. her faithful broker look

blank; so do the proprietor.



but, as the sale proceed, she be young, the competition, though

most of it sham, be artful and exciting, and the traitor she employ

constantly puff every article, she be draw in to wish for

thing, and bidding by her feeling.



then her traitor play a game that have be play a hundred time, and

the perpetrator never once lynch, as they ought to be, on the spot.

he signal a confederate with a hooked nose; the Jew rascal bid

against the christian scoundrel, and so they run up the more enticing

thing to twice their value under the hammer.



Rosa get flush, and her eye gleam like a gambler's, and she buy

away like wildfire. in which sport she catch sight of an old gentleman,

with little black eye that keep twinkle at she.



she complain of these eye to Mrs. Cole. “why do he twinkle so? I

can see it be at I. I be do something foolish--I know I be.”



Mrs. Cole turn, and fix a haughty stare on the old gentleman. would

you believe it? instead of sink through the floor, he sit his ground,

and retort with a cold, clear grin.



but now, whenever Rosa's agent bid for she, and the other man of straw

against he, the black eye twinkle, and Rosa's courage begin to ooze

away. at last she say, “that be enough for one day. I shall go. who

could bear those eye?”



the broker take her address; so do the auctioneer's clerk. the

auctioneer ask she for no deposit; her beautiful, innocent, and

high-breed face be enough for a man who be always read face, and

interpret they.



and so they retire.



but this charming sex be like that same auctioneer's hammer, it cannot

go abruptly. it be always go--go--go--a long time before it be

go. I think it would perhaps loiter at the door of a jail, with the

order of release in its hand, after six year' confinement. get up

to go quenche in it the desire to go. so these lady having get up to

go, turn and linger, and hung fire so long, that at last another set

of oak chair come up. “oh! I must see what these go for,” say Rosa, at

the door.



the bidding be mighty languid now Rosa's broker be not stimulate

it; and the auctioneer be just knock down twelve chair--oak and

leather--and two arm-chair, for twenty pound, when, cast his eye

around, he catch sight of Rosa look at he rather excited. he look

inquiringly at she. she nod slightly; he knock they down to she at

twenty guineas, and they be really a great bargain.



“twenty-two,” cry the dealer.



“too late,” say the auctioneer.



“I speak with the hammer, sir.”



“after the hammer, Isaacs.”



“shelp I God, we be together.”



one or two more of his tribe confirm this pious falsehood, and

clamor to have they put up again.



“call the next lot,” say the auctioneer, peremptorily. “make up your

mind a little quick next time, Mr. Isaacs; you have be long enough

at it to know the value of oak and moroccar.”



Mrs. Staines and her friend now start for Morley's Hotel, but go

round by Regent Street, whereby they get glue at Peter Robinson's

window, and nine other window; and it be nearly five o'clock when they

reach Morley's. as they come near the door of their sitting-room, Mrs.

Staines hear somebody laugh and talk to her husband. the

laugh, to her subtle ear, do not sound musical and genial, but keen,

satirical, unpleasant; so it be with some timidity she open the door,

and there sit the old chap with the twinkle eye. both party stare

at each other a moment.



“why, it be they,” cry the old gentleman. “ha! ha! ha! ha! ha!”



Rosa color all over, and feel guilty somehow, and look miserable.



“Rosa dear,” say Dr. Staines, “this be our Uncle Philip.”



“oh!” say Rosa, and turn red and pale by turn; for she have a great

desire to propitiate Uncle Philip.



“you be in the auction-room, sir?” say Mrs. Cole, severely.



“I be, madam. he! he!”



“furnish a house?”



“no, ma'am. I go to a dozen sale a week; but it be not to buy--I enjoy

the humor. do you ever hear of Robert Burton, ma'am?”



“no. yes; a great traveller, ben't he? discover the Nile--or the

Niger--or something?”



this majestic vagueness stagger old Crusty at first, but he recover

his equilibrium, and say, “why, yes, now I think of it, you be right;

he have travel far than most of we, for about two century ago

he visit that bourn whence no traveller return. well, when he be

alive--he be a student of Christchurch--he use to go down to a certain

bridge over the isis and enjoy the chaff of the bargeman. now there be

no bargeman leave to speak of; the mantle of Bobby Burton's bargee have

fall on the Jews and demi-semi-Christians that buy and sell furniture

at the weekly auction; thither I repair to hear what little coarse wit

be leave we. use to go to the House of Commons; but they be get too

civil by half for my money. besides, character come out in an auction.

for instance, only this very day I see two lady enter, in gorgeous

attire, like heifer deck for sacrifice, and reduce their spoliation

to a certainty by employ a broker to bid. now, what be a broker?

a fellow who be to be pay a shilling in the pound for all article

purchase. what be his interest, then? to buy cheap? clearly not. he be

pay in proportion to the dearness of the article.”



Rosa's face begin to work piteously.



“accordingly, what do the broker in question do? he wink to another

broker, and these two bid against one another, over their victim's head,

and run everything she want up at least a hundred per cent above the

value. so open and transparent a swindle I have seldom see, even in an

auction-room. ha! ha! ha! ha! ha!”



his mirth be interrupt by Rosa go to her husband, hide her head

on his shoulder, and meekly crying.



Christopher comfort she like a man. “don't you cry, darling,” say he;

“how should a pure creature like you know the badness of the world all

in a moment? if it be my wife you be laugh at, Uncle Philip, let I

tell you this be the wrong place. I'd rather a thousand time have she

as she be, than armed with the cunning and suspicion of a harden old

worldling like you.”



“with all my heart,” say Uncle Philip, who, to do he justice, could

take blow as well as give they; “but why employ a broker? why pay a

scoundrel five per cent to make you pay a hundred per cent? why pay a

noisy fool a farthing to open his mouth for you when you have take the

trouble to be there yourself, and have get a mouth of your own to bid

discreetly with? be ever such an absurdity?” he begin to get angry.



“do you want to quarrel with I, Uncle Philip?” say Christopher, fire

up; “because sneer at my Rosa be the way, and the only way, and the

sure way.”



“oh, no,” say Rosa, interpose. “Uncle Philip be right. I be very

foolish and inexperienced, but I be not so vain as to turn from good

advice. I will never employ a broker again, sir.”



Uncle Philip smile and look pleased.



Mrs. Cole cause a diversion by take leave, and Rosa follow she

down-stair. on her return she find Christopher tell his uncle all

about the Bijou, and how he have take it for a hundred and thirty pound

a year and a hundred pound premium, and Uncle Philip stare fearfully.



at last he find his tongue. “the Bijou!” say he. “why, that be a name

they give to a little den in Dear Street, Mayfair. you haven't ever be

and take that! build over a mews.”



Christopher groan. “that be the place, I fear.”



“why the owner be a friend of mine; an old patient. stable stink he

out. let it to a man; I forget his name. stable stink HIM out. he say,

'I shall go.' 'you cann't,' say my friend; 'you have take a lease.'

'lease be d--d,' say the other; 'I never take your house; here's quite

a large stench not specify in your description of the property--IT

CAN'T be the same place;' fling the lease at his head, and cut like the

wind to foreign part less odoriferous. I'd have get you the hole for

ninety; but you be like your wife--you must go to an agent. what! don't

you know that an agent be a man act for you with an interest oppose

to your? employ an agent! it be like a Trojan seek the aid of

a Greek. you needn't cry, Mrs. Staines; your husband have be let in

deep than you have. now, you be young people begin life; I'll

give you a piece of advice. employ other to do what you cann't do,

and it must be do; but never to do anything you can do well for

yourself! agent! the word be derive from a latin word 'agere,' to

do; and agent act up to their etymology, for they invariably do the

nincompoop that employ they, or deal with they, in any mortal way. I'd

have get you that beastly little Bijou for ninety pound a year.”



Uncle Philip go away crusty, leave the young couple finely mortify

and discourage.



that do not last very long. Christopher note the experience and Uncle

Phil's wisdom in his diary, and then take his wife on his knee, and

comfort she, and say, “never mind; experience be worth money, and

it always have to be buy. those who cheat we will die poor than we

shall, if we be honest and economical. I have observe that people be

seldom ruin by the vice of other; these may hurt they, of course;

but it be only their own fault and folly that can destroy they.”



“ah! Christie,” say Rosa, “you be a man! oh, the comfort of be

married to a MAN. a man see the good side. I do adore man. Dearest, I

will waste no more of your money. I will go to no more sale.”



Christopher see she be deeply mortify, and he say, quietly, “on the

contrary, you will go to the very next. only take Uncle Philip's advice,

employ no broker; and watch the price thing fetch when you be not

bidding; and keep cool.”



she caress his ear with both her white hand, and thank he for

give she another trial. so that trouble melt in the sunshine of

conjugal love.



notwithstanding the agent's solemn assurance, the Bijou be out of

repair. Dr. Staines detect internal odor, as well as those that

flow in from the mew. he be not the man to let his wife perish by

miasma; so he have the drain all up, and actually find brick drain,

and a cesspool. he stop that up, and lay down new pipe drain, with

a good fall, and properly trap. the old drain be hide, after

the manner of builder. he have the whole course of his new drain mark

upon all the floor they pass under, and have several stone and board

hinge to facilitate examination at any period.



but all this, with the necessary cleaning, whitewashing, painting, and

papering, run away with money. then come Rosa's purchase, which, to her

amazement, amount to one hundred and ninety pound, and not a carpet,

curtain, or bed amongst the lot. then there be the carriage home from

the auction-room, an expense one avoid by buy at a shop, and

the broker claim his shilling in the pound. this, however, Staines

refuse. the man come and bluster. Rosa, who be there, tremble.

then, for the first time, she see her husband's brow lower; he seem

transfigure, and look terrible. “you scoundrel,” say he, “you set

another villain like yourself to bid against you, and you betray the

innocent lady that employ you. I could indict you and your confederate

for a conspiracy. I take the good out of respect for my wife's credit,

but you shall gain nothing by swindle she. be off, you heartless

miscreant, or I'll”--



“I'll take the law, if you do.”



“take it, then! I'll give you something to howl for;” and he seize he

with a grasp so tremendous that the fellow cry out in dismay, “oh!

don't hit I, sir; pray don't.”



on this abject appeal, Staines tear the door open with his left hand,

and spin the broker out into the passage with his right. two movement

of this angry Hercules, and the man be literally whirl out of sight

with a rapidity and swiftness almost ludicrous; it be like a trick in

a pantomime. a clatter on the stair betray that he have go down the

first few step in a wholesale and irregular manner, though he have just

manage to keep his foot.



as for Staines, he stand there still lower like thunder, and his

eye like hot coal; but his wife throw her tender arm around he, and

beg he consolingly not to mind.



she be tremble like an aspen.



“dear I,” say Christopher, with a ludicrous change to marked

politeness and respect, “I forget you, in my righteous indignation.”

 next he become uxorious. “do they frighten she, a duck? sit on my

knee, darling, and pull my hair, for not be more considerate--there!

there!”



this be follow by the whole absurd soothing process, as practise by

manly husband upon quivering and somewhat hysterical wife, and end

with a formal apology. “you must not think that I be passionate; on the

contrary, I be always practise self-government. my maxim be, animum

rege qui nisi paret imperat, and that mean, make your temper your

servant, or else it will be your master. but to ill-use my dear little

wife--it be unnatural, it be monstrous, it make my blood boil.”



“oh, dear! don't go into another. it be all over. I cann't bear to see

you in a passion; you be so terrible, so beautiful. ah! they be fine

thing, courage and strength. there's nothing I admire so much.”



“why, they be as common as dirt. what I admire be modesty, timidity,

sweetness; the sensitive cheek that pale or blush at a word, the

bosom that quiver, and cling to a fellow whenever anything go

wrong.”



“oh, that be what you admire, be it?” say Rosa dryly.



“admire it?” say Christopher, not see the trap; “I adore it.”



“then, Christie, dear, you be a Simpleton, that be all. and we be make

for one another.”



the house be to be furnish and occupy as soon as possible; so Mrs.

Staines and Mrs. Cole go to another sale-room. Mrs. Staines remember

all Uncle Philip have say, and go plainly dressed; but her friend

decline to sacrifice her showy dress to her friend's interest. Rosa

think that a little unkind, but say nothing.



in this auction-room they easily get a place at the table, but do not

find it heaven; for a number of secondhand carpet be in the sale,

and these, brimful of dust, be all show on the table, and the dirt

choke, and poison our fair friend. broker pester they, until at

last Rosa, smart under her late exposure, address the auctioneer

quietly, in her silvery tone: “Sir, these gentleman be annoy I by

force their service on I. I do not intend to buy at all unless I can

be allow to bid for myself.”



when Rosa, blushing and amazed at her own boldness, utter these word,

she little foresaw their effect. she have touch a popular sore.



“you be quite right, madam,” say a respectable tradesman opposite she.

“what business have these dirty fellow, without a shilling in their

pocket, to go and force themselves on a lady against her will?”



“it have be complain of in the paper again and again,” say another.



“what! mayn't we live as well as you?” retort a broker.



“yes, but not to force yourself on a lady. why, she'd give you in charge

of the police if you try it on outside.”



then there be a downright clamor of discussion and chaff.



presently up rise very slowly a countryman so colossal, that it seem

as if he would never have do get up, and give his experience. he

inform the company, in a broad Yorkshire dialect, that he do a bit

in furniture, and at first start these broker buzz about he like

fly, and pester he. “Aah damn 'em pretty hard,” say he, “but

they don't heed any. so then ah speak 'em civil, and ah say, 'well,

lad, I dinna come fra Yorkshire to sit like a dummy and let you buy

wi' my brass; the first that pester I again ah'll just fall he on

t' plaace, like a caulf, and ah'm not very sure he'll get up again in a

hurry.' so they drop I like a hot potato; never pester I again.

but if they willn't give over pester you, mistress, ah'll come round

and just stand behind your chair, and bring nieve with I,” show a

fist like a leg of mutton.



“no, no,” say the auctioneer, “that will not do. I will have no

disturbance here. call the policeman.”



while the clerk go to the door for the bobby, a gentleman remind

the auctioneer that the journal have repeatedly draw attention to the

nuisance.



“fault of the public, not mine, sir. Policeman, stand behind that lady's

chair, and if anybody annoy she put he quietly into the street.”



“this auction-room will be to let soon,” say a voice at the end of the

table.



“this auction-room,” say the auctioneer, master of the gay or grave at

a moment's notice, “be support by the public and the trade; it be not

support by pauper.”



a Jew upholsterer put in his word. “I do my own business; but I like to

let a poor man live.”



“Jonathan,” say the auctioneer to one of his servant, “after this sale

you may put up the shutter; we have go and offend Mr. Jacobs. he

keep a shop in Blind Alley, Whitechapel. now then, lot 69.”



Rosa bid timidly for one or two lot, and buy they cheap.



the auctioneer keep look her way, and she have only to nod.



the obnoxious broker get opposite she, and run she up a little out of

spite; but as he have only get half a crown about he, and no mean of

double it, he dared not go far.



on the other side of the table be a figure to which Rosa's eye often

turn with interest--a fair young boy about twelve year old; he have

golden hair, and be in deep mourning. his appearance interest Rosa,

and she wonder how he come there, and why; he look like a lamb

wedge in among wolf, a flower among weed. as the lot proceed, the

boy seem to get uneasy; and at last, when lot '73 be put up, anybody

could see in his poor little face that he be there to bid for it.



“Lot '73, an armchair cover in morocco. an excellent and useful

article. should not be at all surprised if it be make by Gillow.”



“gillow would though,” say Jacobs, who owe he a turn.



Chorus of dealers.--“Haw! haw!”



the auctioneer.--“I like to hear some people run a lot down; show they

be go to bid for it in earnest. well, name your own price. five

pound to begin?”



now if nobody have speak the auctioneer would have go on, “well, four

pound then--three, two, whatever you like,” and at last obtain a bona

fide offer of thirty shilling; but the moment he say “five pound to

begin,” the boy in black lift up his childish treble and bid thus,

“five pound ten”--“six pounds”--“six pound ten”--“seven pounds”--“seven

pound ten”--“eight pounds”--“eight pound ten”--“nine pounds”--“nine

pound ten”--“ten pound!” without interruption, and indeed almost in a

breath.



there be a momentary pause of amazement, and then an outburst of chaff.



“nice little boy!”



“don't he say his lesson well?”



“favor we with your card, sir. you be a gent as know how to buy.”



“what do he stop for? if it's worth ten, it be worth a hundred.”



“bless the child!” say a female dealer, kindly, “what make you go on

like that? why, there be no one bid against you! you'd have get it for

two pound--a rickety old thing.”



young master begin to whimper. “why, the gentleman say, 'five pound

to BEGIN.' it be the chair poor grandpapa always sit in, and all the

thing be sell, and mamma say it would break her heart to lose it. she

be too ill to come, so she send I. she tell I I be not to let it

be sell away from we for less than ten pound, or she sh--should be

m--m--miserable,” and the poor little fellow begin to cry. Rosa follow

suit promptly but unobtrusively.



“Sentiment always cost money,” say Mr. Jacobs, gravely.



“how do you know?” ask Mr. Cohen. “have you get any on hand? I never

see none at your shop.”



some tempting thing now come up, and Mrs. Staines bid freely; but

all of a sudden she look down the table, and there be Uncle Philip,

twinkle as before. “oh, dear! what be I do now!” think she. “I

have get no broker.”



she bid on, but in fear and trembling, because of those twinkle eye.

at last she muster courage, write on a leaf of her pocket-book, and

pass it down to he: “it would be only kind to warn I. what be I

do wrong?”



he send she back a line directly: “auctioneer run you up himself.

follow his eye when he bid; you will see there be no bona fide bidder

at your price.”



Rosa do so, and find that it be true.



she nod to Uncle Philip; and, with her expressive face, ask he

what she should do.



the old boy must have his joke. so he write back: “tell he, as you see

he have a fancy for certain article, you would not be so discourteous as

to bid against he.”



the next article but one be a drawing-room suite Rosa want; but the

auctioneer bid against she; so at eighteen pound she stop.



“it be against you, madam,” say the auctioneer.



“yes, sir,” say Rosa; “but as you be the only bidder, and you have

be so kind to I, I would not think of oppose you.”



the word be scarcely out of her mouth, when they be greet with

a roar of homeric laughter that literally shake the room, and this time

not at the expense of the innocent speaker.



“that's into your mutton, governor.”



“Sharp's the word this time.”



“I say, governor, don't you want a broker to bid for ye?”



“wink at I next time, sir; I'll do the office for you.”



“no greenhorn leave now.”



“that lady willn't give a ten-pund note for her grandfather's armchair.”



“oh, yes, she will, if it's stuff with banknote.”



“put the next lot up with the owner's name and the reserve price. open

business.”



“and sing a psalm at start.”



“a little less noise in Judaea, if you please,” say the auctioneer, who

have now recover from the blow. “Lot 97.”



this be a very pretty marqueterie cabinet; it stand against the wall,

and Rosa have set her heart upon it. nobody would bid. she have muzzle

the auctioneer effectually.



“your own price.”



“two pound,” say Rosa.



a dealer offer guineas; and it advance slowly to four pound and half

a crown, at which it be about to be knock down to Rosa, when suddenly

a new bidder arise in the broker Rosa have reject. they bid slowly and

sturdily against each other, until a line be give to Rosa from Uncle

Philip.



“this time it be your own friend, the snipe-nosed woman. she telegraph

a broker.”



Rosa read, and crush the note. “six guinea,” say she.



“six-ten.”



“Seven.”



“Seven-ten.”



“Eight.”



“eight-ten.”



“ten guineas,” say Rosa; and then, with feminine cunning, steal a

sudden glance, catch her friend lean back and signal the broker

not to give in.



“eleven pound.”



“Twelve.”



“Thirteen.”



“Fourteen.”



“Sixteen.”



“Eighteen.”



“Twenty.”



“twenty guineas.”



“it be your, my faithful friend,” say Rosa, turn suddenly round

to Mrs. Cole, with a magnificent glance no one would have think she

capable of.



then she rise and stalk away.



dumfounde for the moment, Mrs. Cole follow she, and stop she at

the door.



“why, Rosie dear, it be the only thing I have bid for. there I've sit by

your side like a mouse.”



Rosa turn gravely towards she. “you know it be not that. you have only

to tell I you want it. I would never have be so mean as to bid

against you.”



“mean, indeed!” say. Florence, toss her head.



“yes, mean; to draw back and hide behind the friend you be with,

and employ the very rogue she have turn off. but it be my own fault.

Cecilia warn I against you. she always say you be a treacherous

girl.”



“and I say you be an impudent little minx. only just married, and go

about like two vagabond, and talk to I like that!”



“we be not go about like two vagabond. we have take a house in

Mayfair.”



“say a stable.”



“it be by your advice, you false-hearted creature.”



“you be a fool.”



“you be bad; you be a traitress.”



“then don't you have anything to do with I.”



“Heaven forbid I should, you treacherous thing!”



“you insolent--insolent--I hate you.”



“and I despise you.”



“I always hate you at bottom.”



“that's why you pretend to love I, you wretch.”



“well, I pretend no more. I be your enemy for life.”



“thank you. you have tell the truth for once in your life.”



“I have. and he shall never call in your husband; so you may leave

Mayfair as soon as you like.”



“not to please you, madam. we can get on without traitor.”



and so they part, with eye that gleam like tiger.



Rosa drive home in great agitation, and try to tell Christopher; but

choke, and become hysterical. the husband-physician coaxed and scold

she out of that; and presently in come Uncle Philip, full of the humor

of the auction-room. he tell about the little boy with a delight that

disgust Mrs. Staines, and then be particularly merry on female

friendship. “Fancy a man go to a sale with his friend, and bidding

against he on the sly.”



“she be no friend of mine. we be enemy for life.”



“and you be to be friend till death,” say Staines, with a sigh.



Philip inquire who she be.



“Mrs. John Cole.”



“not of Curzon Street?”



“yes.”



“and you have quarrel with she?”



“yes.”



“well, but her husband be a general practitioner.”



“she be a traitress.”



“but her husband could put a good deal of money in Christopher's way.”



“I cann't help it. she be a traitress.”



“and you have quarrel with she about an old wardrobe.”



“no, for her disloyalty, and her base good-for-nothingness. oh! oh! oh!”



Uncle Philip get up, look sour. “good afternoon, Mrs. Christopher,”

 say he, very dryly.



Christopher accompany he to the foot of the stair. “well,

Christopher,” say he, “matrimony be a blunder at the good; and you have

not do the thing by half. you have marry a simpleton. she will be

your ruin.”



“Uncle Philip, since you only come here to insult we, I hope in future

you will stay at home.”



“oh! with pleasure, sir. good-by!”









CHAPTER VII.





Christopher Staines come back, look pain and disturb. “there,”

 say he, “I fear it would come to this. I have quarrel with Uncle

Philip.”



“oh! how could you?”



“he affront I.”



“what about?”



“never you mind. don't let we say anything more about it, darling. it be

a pity, a sad pity--he be a good friend of mine once.”



he pause, enter what have pass in his diary, and then sit down, with

a gentle expression of sadness on his manly feature. Rosa hang about

he, soft and pitying, till it clear away, at all event for the time.



next day they go together to clear the good Rosa have purchase.

Whilst the list be be make out in the office, in come the

fair-haired boy, with a ten-pound note in his very hand. Rosa catch

sight of it, and turn to the auctioneer, with a sweet, pity face:



“oh! sir, surely you will not take all that money from he, poor child,

for a rickety old chair.”



the auctioneer stare with amazement at her simplicity, and say, “what

would the vendor say to I?”



she look distressed, and say, “well, then, really we ought to raise a

subscription, poor thing!”



“why, ma'am,” say the auctioneer, “he ben't hurt: the article belong

to his mother and her sister; the brother-in-law ben't on good term;

so he demand a public sale. she will get back four pun ten out of it.”

 here the clerk put in his word. “and there's five pound pay, I forget

to tell you.”



“oh! leave a deposit, do he?”



“no, sir. but the laugh hyena give you five pound at the end of the

sale.”



“the laugh hyena, Mr. Jones?”



“oh! beg pardon; that be what we call he in the room. he have get such a

curious laugh.”



“oh! I know the gent. he be a retired doctor. I wish he'd laugh less

and buy more: and he give you five pound towards the young gentleman's

arm-chair! well, I should as soon have expect blood from a flint. you

have get five pound to pay, sir: so now the chair will cost your mamma

ten shilling. give he the order and the change, Mr. Jones.”



Christopher and Rosa talk this over in the room whilst the man be

look out their purchase. “come,” say Rosa; “now I forgive he

sneer at I; his heart be not really hard, you see.” staine, on the

contrary, be very angry. “what!” he cry, “pity a boy who make one

bad bargain, that, after all, be not a very bad bargain; and he have no

kindness, nor even common humanity, for my beautiful Rosa, inexperienced

as a child, and buy for her husband, like a good, affectionate,

honest creature, amongst a lot of sharper and hard-hearted cynic--like

himself.”



“it be cruel of he,” say Rosa, alter her mind in a moment, and

half incline to cry.



this make Christopher furious. “the ill-natured, crotchety, old--the

fact be, he be a misogynist.”



“oh, the wretch!” say Rosa warmly. “and what be that?”



“a woman-hater.”



“oh! be that all? why, so do I--after that Florence Cole. woman be

mean, heartless thing. give I man; they be loyal and true.”



“all of they?” inquire Christopher, a little satirically. “read the

paper.”



“every soul of they,” say Mrs. Staines, pass loftily over the

propose test. “that is, all the one I care about; and that be my own,

own one.”



disagreeable creature to have about one--these simpleton!



Mrs. Staines take Christopher to shop to buy the remain requisite:

and in three day more the house be furnish, two female servant

engage, and the couple take their luggage over to the Bijou.



Rosa be excited and happy at the novelty of possession and authority,

and that close sense of house proprietorship which belong to woman. by

dinner-time she could have tell you how many shelf there be in every

cupboard, and know the Bijou by heart in a way that Christopher never

know it. all this end, as run about and excitement generally do,

with my lady be exhaust, and lax with fatigue. so then he make she

lie down on a little couch, while he go through his account.



when he have examine all the bill carefully he look very grave, and

say, “who would believe this? we begin with three thousand pound. it

be to last we several year--till I get a good practice. Rosa, there be

only fourteen hundred and forty pound leave.”



“oh, impossible!” say Rosa. “oh, dear! why do I ever enter a

saleroom?”



“no, no, my darling; you be bite once or twice, but you make some

good bargain too. remember there be four hundred pound set apart for

my life policy.”



“what a waste of money!”



“your father do not think so. then the lease; the premium; repair of

the drain that would have poison my Rosa; turn the coach-house

into a dispensary; painting, paper, and furnish; china, and linen,

and everything to buy. we must look at this seriously. only fourteen

hundred and forty pound leave. a slow profession. no friend. I have

quarrel with Uncle Philip: you with Mrs. Cole; and her husband would

have launch I.”



“and it be to please she we settle here. oh, I could kill she: nasty

cat!”



“never mind; it be not a case for despondency, but it be for prudence.

all we have to do be to look the thing in the face, and be very

economical in everything. I have well give you an allowance for

housekeeping; and I earnestly beg you to buy thing yourself whilst you

be a poor man's wife, and pay ready money for everything. my mother be

a great manager, and she always say, 'there be but one way: be your own

market-woman, and pay on the spot; never let the tradesman get you on

their book, or, what with false weight, double charge, and the thing

your servant order that never enter the house, you lose more than a

hundred a year by cheat.'”



Rosa yield a languid assent to this part of his discourse, and it

hardly seem to enter her mind; but she raise no objection; and in due

course he make she a special allowance for housekeeping.



it soon transpire that medical advice be to be have, gratis, at the

Bijou, from eight till ten: and there be generally a good attendance.

but a week pass, and not one patient come of the class this couple

must live by. Christopher set this down to what people call “the

transition period:” his Kent patient have lose he; his London patient

not find he. he write to all his patient in the country, and many of

his pupil at the university, to let they know where he be settle: and

then he wait.



not a creature come.



Rosa bear this very well for a time, so long as the house be a novelty;

but when that excitement be wear out, she begin to be very dull,

and use to come and entice he out to walk with she: he would look

wistfully at she, but object that, if he leave the house, he should be

sure to lose a patient.



“oh, they willn't come any more for our stay in--tiresome thing!” say

Rosa.



but Christopher would kiss she, and remain firm. “my love,” say he,

“you do not realize how hard a fight there be before we. how should you?

you be very young. no, for your sake, I must not throw a chance away.

write to your female friend: that will while away an hour or two.”



“what, after that Florence Cole?”



“write to those who have not make such violent profession.”



“so I will, dear. especially to those that be married and come to

London. oh, and I'll write to that cold-blooded thing, Lady Cicely

Treherne. why do you shake your head?”



“do I? I be not aware. well, dear, if lady of rank be to come

here, I fear they might make you discontent with your lot.”



“all the woman on earth could not do that. however, the chance be she

will not come near I: she leave the school quite a big girl, an immense

girl, when I be only twelve. she use to smile at my capriccio; and

once she kiss I--actually. she be an awful Sawny, though, and so

affect: I think I will write to she.”



these letter bring just one lady, a Mrs. Turner, who talk to Rosa

very glibly about herself, and amuse Rosa twice: at the third visit,

Rosa try to change the conversation. Mrs. Turner instantly get up, and

go away. she could not bear the sound of the human voice, unless it

be talk about she and her affair.



and now Staines begin to feel downright uneasy. income be go

steadily out: not a shilling come in. the lame, the blind, and the

sick frequent his dispensary, and get his skill out of he gratis, and

sometimes a little physic, a little wine, and other thing that cost he

money: but of the patient that pay, not one come to his front door.



he walk round and round his little yard, like a hyena in its cage,

wait, wait, wait: and oh! how he envy the lot of those who

can hunt for work, instead of have to stay at home and wait for other

to come, whose will they cannot influence. his heart begin to sicken

with hope defer, and dim foreboding of the future; and he see, with

grief, that his wife be get dull and duller, and that her day

drag more heavily, far than his own; for he could study.



at last his knocker begin to show sign of life: his visitor be

physician. his lecture on “Diagnosis” be well known to they; and one

after another find he out. they be polite, kind, even friendly; but

here it end: these gentleman, of course, do not resign their patient

to he; and the inferior class of practitioner avoid his door like a

pestilence.



Mrs. Staines, who have always live for amusement, could strike out no

fix occupation; her time hang like lead; the house be small; and in

small house the fault of servant run against the mistress, and she

cann't help see they, and all the bad for she. it be easy to keep

thing clean in the country, and Rosa have a high standard, which her two

servant could never quite attain. this annoy she, and she begin to

scold a little. they answer civilly, but in other respect remain

imperfect being; they lay out every shilling they earn in finery;

and, this, I be ashamed to say, irritated Mrs. Staines, who be wear

out her wedding garment, and have no excuse for buying, and Staines

have beg she to be economical. the more they dress, the more she

scold; they begin to answer. she give the cook warning; the other,

though not on good term with the cook, have a gush of esprit de corps

directly, and give Mrs. Staines warn.



Mrs. Staines tell her husband all this: he take her part, though without

openly interfere; and they have two new servant, not so good as the

last.



this worry Rosa sadly; but it be a flea-bite to the deep nature,

and more forecasting mind of her husband, still doom to pace that

miserable yard, like a hyena, chafing, seek, long for the patient

that never come.



Rosa use to look out of his dressing-room window, and see he pace the

yard. at first, tear of pity stand in her eye. by and by she get angry

with the world; and at last, strange to say, a little irritated with

he. it be hard for a weak woman to keep up all her respect for the man

that fail.



one day, after watch he a long time unseen, she get excite, put on

her shawl and bonnet, and run down to he: she take he by the arm:

“if you love I, come out of this prison, and walk with I; we be too

miserable. I shall be your first patient if this go on much long.”

 he look at she, see she be very excited, and have well be humor;

so he kiss she and just say, with a melancholy smile, “how poor be

they that have not patience!” then he put on his hat, and walk in the

Park and Kensington Gardens with she. the season be just begin.

there be carriage enough, and gay Amazons enough, to make poor Rosa

sigh more than once.



Christopher hear the sigh; and press her arm, and say, “courage,

love, I hope to see you among they yet.”



“the soon the well,” say she, a little hardly.



“and, meantime, which of they all be as beautiful as you?”



“all I know be, they be more attractive. who look at I, walk

tamely by?”



Christopher say nothing: but these word seem to imply a thirst for

admiration, and make he a little uneasy.



by and by the walk put the swift-change Rosa in spirit, and she begin

to chat gayly, and hung prattling and beam on her husband's arm,

when they enter Curzon Street. here, however, occur an incident,

trifle in itself, but unpleasant. Dr. Staines see one of his good

kentish patient get feebly out of his carriage, and call on Dr. Barr.

he start, and stop. Rosa ask what be the matter. he tell she.

she say, “we be unfortunate.”



Staines say nothing; he only quicken his pace; but he be greatly

disturb. she expect he to complain that she have drag he out,

and lose he that first chance. but he say nothing. when they get home,

he ask the servant have anybody call.



“no, Sir.”



“surely you be mistaken, Jane. a gentleman in a carriage!”



“not a creature have be since you go out, sir.”



“well, then, dearest,” say he sweetly, “we have nothing to reproach

ourselves with.” then he knit his brow gloomily. “it be bad than I

think. it seem even one's country patient go to another doctor when

they visit London. it be hard. it be hard.”



Rosa lean her head on his shoulder, and curl round he, as one she

would shield against the world's injustice; but she say nothing; she

be a little frightened at his eye that lower, and his noble frame

that tremble a little, with ire suppress.



two day after this, a brougham drive up to the door, and a tallish,

fattish, pasty-faced man get out, and inquire for Dr. Staines.



he be show into the dining-room, and tell Jane he have come to consult

the doctor.



Rosa have peep over the stair, all curiosity; she glide noiselessly

down, and with love's swift foot get into the yard before Jane. “he be

come! he be come! kiss I.”



Dr. Staines kiss she first, and then ask who be come.



“oh, nobody of any consequence. only the first patient. kiss I again.”



Dr. Staines kiss she again, and then be for go to the first

patient.



“no,” say she; “not yet. I meet a doctor's wife at Dr. Mayne's, and she

tell I thing. you must always keep they wait; or else they think

nothing of you. such a funny woman! 'treat 'em like dog, my dear,' she

say. but I tell she they wouldn't come to be treat like dog or any

other animal.”



“you have well have keep that to yourself, I think.”



“oh! if you be go to be disagreeable, good-by. you can go to your

patient, sir. Christie, dear, if he be very--very ill--and I'm sure I

hope he be--oh, how wicked I be; may I have a new bonnet?”



“if you really want one.”



on the patient's card be “Mr. Pettigrew, 47 Manchester Square.”



as soon as staine enter the room, the first patient tell he who and

what he be, a retired civilian from India; but he have get a son there

still, a very rise man; want to be a parson; but he would not

stand that; bad profession; don't rise by merit; very hard to rise at

all;--no, India be the place. “as for I, I make my fortune there in

ten year. oblige to leave it now--invalid this many year; no tone.

try two or three doctor in this neighborhood; hear there be a new

one, have write a book on something. thought I would try HIM.”



to stop he, Staines request to feel his pulse, and examine his tongue

and eye.



“you be suffer from indigestion,” say he. “I will write you a

prescription; but if you want to get well, you must simplify your diet

very much.”



while he be write the prescription, off go this patient's tongue,

and run through the topic of the day and into his family history again.



staine listen politely. he could afford it, have only this one.



at last, the first patient, having deliver an octavo volume of

nothing, rise to go; but it seem that speak an “infinite deal of

nothing” exhaust the body, though it do not affect the mind; for the

first patient sink down in his chair again. “I have excite myself too

much--feel rather faint.”



Staines see no sign of come syncope; he ring the bell quietly, and

order a decanter of sherry to be bring; the first patient fill

himself a glass; then another; and go off, revive, to chatter

elsewhere. but at the door he say, “I have always a run account with

Dr. Mivar. I suppose you don't object to that system. double fee the

first visit, single afterwards.”



Dr. Staines bow a little stiffly; he would have prefer the money.

however, he look at the Blue Book, and find his visitor live at 47

Manchester Square; so that remove his anxiety.



the first patient call every other day, chatter nineteen to the

dozen, be exhaust, drink two glass of sherry, and drive away.



soon after this a second patient call. this one be a deputy

patient--Collett, a retired butler--keep a lodging-house, and wait at

party; he live close by, but have a married daughter in Chelsea. would

the doctor visit she, and he would be responsible?



Staines pay the woman a visit or two, and treat she so effectually,

that soon her visit be pay to he. she be cure, and Staines, who

by this time want to see money, send to Collett.



Collett do not answer.



Staines write warmly.



Collett dead silent.



Staines employ a solicitor.



Collett say he have recommend the patient, that be all. he have never

say he would pay her debt. that be her husband's business.



now her husband be the mate of a ship; would not be in England for

eighteen month.



the woman, visit by lawyer's clerk, cry bitterly, and say she and

her child have scarcely enough to eat.



Lawyer advise Staines to abandon the case, and pay he two pound

fifteen shilling expense. he do so.



“this be damnable,” say he. “I must get it out of Pettigrew; by-the-by,

he have not be here this two day.”



he wait another day for Pettigrew, and then write to he. no answer.

call. Pettigrew go abroad. House in Manchester Square to let.



Staines go to the house-agent with his tale. Agent be impenetrable

at first; but, at last, win by the doctor's manner and his unhappiness,

refer he to Pettigrew's solicitor; the solicitor be a respectable

man, and say he would forward the claim to Pettigrew in Paris.



but by this time Pettigrew be chatter and guzzle in Berlin; and

thence he get to St. Petersburg. in that stronghold of gluttony,

he gormandize more than ever, and, be unable to talk it off his

stomach, as in other city, have apoplexy, and die.



but long before this Staines see his money be as irrecoverable as his

sherry; and he say to Rosa, “I wonder whether I shall ever live to

curse the human race?”



“heaven forbid!” say Rosa. “oh, they use you cruelly, my poor, poor

Christie!”



thus for month the young doctor's patient bleed he, and that be all.



and Rosa get more and more mope at be in the house so much, and

pester Christopher to take she out, and he decline: and, be a man

hard to beat, take to writing on medical subject, in hope of get

some money from the various medical and scientific publication; but he

find it as hard to get the wedge in there as to get patient.



at last Rosa's remonstrance begin to rise into something that

sound like reproach. one Sunday she come to he in her bonnet, and

interrupt his study, to say he might as well lay down the pen, and

talk. nobody would publish anything he write.



Christopher frown, but contain himself, and lay down the pen.



“I might as well not be marry at all as be a doctor's wife. you be

never see out with I, not even to church. do behave like a Christian,

and come to church with I now.”



Dr. Staines shake his head.



“why, I wouldn't miss church for all the world. any excitement be well

than always mope. come over the water with I. the time Jane and I

go, the clergyman read a paper that Mr. Brown have fall down in a

fit. there be such a rush directly, and I'm sure fifty lady go

out--fancy, all Mrs. Browns! ben't that fun?”



“Fun? I don't see it. well, Rosa, your mind be evidently well adapt

to diversion than mine be. go you to church, love, and I'll continue my

study.”



“then all I can say be, I wish I be back in my father's house. husband!

friend! companion!--I have none.”



then she burst out cry violently; and, be shock at what she have

say, and at the agony it have bring into her husband's face, she go

off into hysteric; and as his heart would not let he bellow at she,

or empty a bucket on she as he would on another patient, she have a good

long bout of they: and get her way, for she break up his study for

that day, at all event.



even after the hysteric be get under, she continue to moan and sigh

very prettily, with her lovely, languid head pillow on her husband's

arm; in a word, though the hysteric be real, yet this innocent young

person have the presence of mind to postpone entire convalescence, and

lie herself out to be pet all day. but fate will it otherwise:

while she be sigh and moaning, come to the door a scurrying of foot,

and then a sharp, persistent ringing that mean something. the moaner

cock eye and ear, and say, in her every-day voice, which, come so

suddenly, sound very droll, “what be that, I wonder?”



Jane hurry to the street-door, and Rosa recover by magic; and,

prefer gossip to hysteric, in an almost gleeful whisper, order

Christopher to open the door of the study. the Bijou be so small that

the follow dialogue rang in their ears:--



a boy in button gasp out, “oh, if you please, will you ast the doctor

to come round directly; there's a haccident.”



“La, bless I!” say Jane, and never budge.



“yes, miss. it's our missus's little girl fall right off an i-chair,

and cut her head dreadful, and smother in blood.”



“La, to be sure!” and she wait steadily for more.



“ay, and missus she faint right off; and I've be to the regler

doctor, which he's out; and Sarah, the housemaid, say I have well come

here; you be only just set up, she say; you wouldn't have so much to

do, say she.”



“that be all she know,” say Jane. “why, our master--they pull he in

piece which be to have he fust.”



“what an awful liar! oh, you good girl!” whisper Dr. Staines and Rosa

in one breath.



“ah, well,” say Buttons, “any way, Sarah say she know you be clever,

'cos her little girl as life with her mother, and call Sarah aunt, have

bin to your 'spensary with ringworm, and you cure she right off.”



“ay, and a good many more,” say Jane, loftily. she be a housemaid of

imagination; and while Staines be put some lint and an instrument

case into his pocket, she proceed to relate a number of miraculous

cure. Dr. Staines interrupt they by suddenly emerge, and invite

Buttons to take he to the house.



Mrs. Staines be so pleased with Jane for crack up the doctor, that

she give she five shilling; and, after that, use to talk to she a

great deal more than to the cook, which judicious conduct presently set

all three by the ear.



button take the doctor to a fine house in the same street, and tell

he his mistress's name on the way--Mrs. Lucas. he be take up to the

nursery, and find Mrs. Lucas seat, cry and lamenting, and a woman

hold a little girl of about seven, whose brow have be cut open by

the fender, on which she have fall from a chair; it look very ugly,

and be even now bleed.



Dr. Staines lose no time; he examine the wound keenly, and then say

kindly to Mrs. Lucas, “I be happy to tell you it be not serious.” he

then ask for a large basin and some tepid water, and bathe it so

softly and soothingly that the child soon became compose; and the

mother discover the artist at once. he compress the wound, and

explain to Mrs. Lucas that the principal thing really be to avoid an

ugly scar. “there be no danger,” say he. he then bind the wound

neatly up, and have the girl put to bed. “you will not wake she at any

particular hour, nurse. let her sleep. have a little strong beef-tea

ready, and give it she at any hour, night or day, she ask for it. but

do not force it on she, or you will do she more harm than good. she have

well sleep before she eat.”



Mrs. Lucas beg he to come every morning; and, as he be go,

she shake hand with he, and the soft palm deposit a hard substance

wrap in paper. he take it with professional gravity and seeming

unconsciousness; but, once outside the house, go home on wing. he

run up to the drawing-room, and find his wife seat, and play at

reading. he throw himself on his knee, and the fee into her lap; and,

while she unfold the paper with an ejaculation of pleasure, he say,

“Darling, the first real patient--the first real fee. it be yours to buy

the new bonnet.”



“oh, I'm so glad!” say she, with her eye glisten. “but I'm afraid

one cann't get a bonnet fit to wear--for a guinea.”



Dr. Staines visit his little patient every day, and receive his

guinea. Mrs. Lucas also call he in for her own little ailment, and

they be the well possible kind of ailment: for, be imaginary,

there be no limit to they.



then do Mrs. Staines turn jealous of her husband. “they never ask I,”

 say she; “and I be mope to death.”



“it be hard,” say Christopher, sadly. “but have a little patience.

Society will come to you long before practice come to I.”



about two o'clock one afternoon a carriage and pair drive up, and a

gorgeous footman deliver a card--“Lady Cicely Treherne.”



of course Mrs. Staines be at home, and only withhold by propriety

from bound into the passage to meet her school-fellow. however, she

compose herself in the drawing-room, and presently the door be open,

and a very tall young woman, richly but not gayly dress, drift into

the room, and stand there a statue of composure.



Rosa have rise to fly to she; but the reverence a girl of eighteen

strike into a child of twelve hang about she still, and she come

timidly forward, blush and sparkling, a curious contrast in color

and mind to her visitor; for Lady Cicely be Languor in person--her hair

whitey-brown, her face a fine oval, but almost colorless; her eye

a pale gray, her neck and hand incomparably white and beautiful--a

lymphatic young lady, a live antidote to emotion. however, Rosa's

beauty, timidity, and undisguise affectionateness be something so

different from what she be use to in the world of fashion, that she

actually smile, and hold out both her hand a little way. Rosa seize

they, and press they; they leave she; and remain passive and limp.



“o Lady Cicely,” say Rosa, “how kind of you to come.”



“how kind of you to send to I,” be the polite, but perfectly cool

reply. “but how you be gwown, and--may I say impwoved?--You la petite

Lusignan! it be incwedible,” lisp her ladyship, very calmly.



“I be only a child,” say Rosa. “you be always so beautiful and tall,

and kind to a little monkey like I. oh, pray sit down, Lady Cicely, and

talk of old time.”



she draw she gently to the sofa, and they sit down hand in hand; but

Lady Cicely's high-breed reserve make she a very poor gossip about

anything that touch herself and her family; so Rosa, though no

egotist, be draw into talk about herself more than she would have

do have she deliberately plan the conversation. but here be an old

school-fellow, and a singularly polite listener, and so out come she

love, her genuine happiness, her particular grief, and especially the

crowning grievance, no society, mope to death, etc.



Lady Cicely could hardly understand the sentiment in a woman who so

evidently love her husband. “Society!” say she, after due reflection,

“why, it be a boa.” (and here I may as well explain that Lady Cicely

speak certain word falsely, and other affectedly; and as for the

letter r, she could say it if she make a hearty effort, but be

generally too lazy to throw her leg over it.) “Society! I'm dwenche

to death with it. if I could only catch fiah like other woman, and

love somebody, I would much rather have a tete-a-tete with he than

go teawe about all day and all night, from one unintwiste cwowd

to another. to be sure,” say she, puzzle the matter out, “you be a

beauty, and would be more look at.”



“the idea! and--oh no! no! it be not that. but even in the country we

have always some society.”



“well, dyar, believe I, with your appeawance, you can have as much

society as you please; but it will boa you to death, as it do I, and

then you will long to be leave quiet with a sensible man who love you.”



say Rosa, “when shall I have another tete-a-tete with you, I wonder?

oh, it have be such a comfort to I. bless you for come. there--I

write to Cecilia, and Emily, and Mrs. Bosanquet that be now, and all my

swear friend, and to think of you be the one to come--you that never

kiss I but once, and an earl's daughter into the bargain.”



“ha! ha! ha!”--Lady Cicely actually laugh for once in a way, and do

not feel the effort. “as for kiss,” say she, “if I fall shawt,

fawgive I. I be nevaa vewy demonstwative.”



“no; and I have have a lesson. that Florence Cole--Florence Whiting that

be, you know--be always kiss I, and she have turn out a traitor.

I'll tell you all about she.” and she do.



Lady Cicely think Mrs. Staines a little too unreserved in her

conversation; but be so charmed with her sweetness and freshness that

she keep up the acquaintance, and call on her twice a week during the

season. at first she wonder that her visit be not return; but

Rosa let out that she be ashamed to call on foot in Grosvenor Square.



Lady Cicely shrug her beautiful shoulder a little at that; but she

continue to do the visit, and to enjoy the simple, innocent rapture

with which she be receive.



this lady's pronunciation of many word be false or affected. she

say “good murning” for “good morning,” and turn other vowel to

diphthong, and play two or three prank with her “r's.” but we cannot

be all imperfection: with her pronunciation her folly come to a full

stop. I really believe she lisp less nonsense and bad taste in a year

than some of we articulate in a day. to be sure, folly be generally

utter in a hurry, and she be too deplorably lazy to speak fast on any

occasion whatever.



one day Mrs. Staines take she up-stair, and show she from the back

window her husband pace the yard, wait for patient. Lady Cicely

fold her arm, and contemplate he at first with a sort of zoological

curiosity. Gentleman pace back yard, like hyena, she have never see

before.



at last she open her mouth in a whisper, “what be he do?”



“wait for patient.”



“oh! wait--for--patient?”



“for patient that never come, and never will come.”



“cuwious! how little I know of life.”



“it be that all day, dear, or else write.”



Lady Cicely, with her eye fix on Staines, make a motion with her hand

that she be attend.



“and they willn't publish a word he write.”



“poor man!”



“nice for I; be it not?”



“I begin to understand,” say Lady Cicely quietly; and soon after

retire with her invariable composure.



meantime, Dr. Staines, like a good husband, have throw out occasional

hint to Mrs. Lucas that he have a wife, beautiful, accomplished, mope.

More than that, he go so far as to regret to she that Mrs. Staines,

be in a neighborhood new to he, see so little society; the more so,

as she be form to shine, and have not be use to seclusion.



all these hint fall dead on Mrs. Lucas. a handsome and skilful doctor

be welcome to she: his wife--that be quite another matter.



but one day Mrs. Lucas see Lady Cicely Treherne's carriage stand at

the door. the style of the whole turnout impress she. she wonder

whose it be.



on another occasion she see it drive up, and the lady get out. she

recognize she; and the very next day this parvenue say adroitly, “now,

Dr. Staines, really you cann't be allow to hide your wife in this way.

(staine stare.) why not introduce she to I next Wednesday? it be my

night. I would give a dinner expressly for she; but I don't like to do

that while my husband be in Naples.”



when Staines carry the invitation to his wife, she be delighted, and

kiss he with childish frankness.



but the very next moment she become thoughtful, uneasy, depressed. “oh,

dear; I've nothing to wear.”



“oh, nonsense, Rosa. your wedding outfit.”



“the idea! I cann't go as a bride. it's not a masquerade.”



“but you have other dress.”



“all go by, more or less; or not fit for such party as she give. a

hundred carriage!”



“bring they down, and let I see they.”



“oh yes.” and the lady, who have nothing to wear, parade a very fair

show of dress.



Staines see something to admire in all of they. Mrs. Staines find more

to object to in each.



at last he fall upon a silver-gray silk, of superlative quality.

“I be afraid not,” say Rosa naively. “oh, how I wish I have know a week

ago.”



“I be very thankful you do not,” say Staines dryly.



at ten o'clock Mrs. Staines be nearly dress; at a quarter past ten

when a slight buzz arose, and seem to follow they.



Rosa wonder what that be; but only for a moment; she observe a tall,

i.e., the majority, seem literally to fade and wither as she pass.

the room fill; the heat become oppressive, and the mixed odor of

“Miss Lucas,” say Staines.



she jerk a little off-hand bow to he, and say, “will you trust she

“certainly.” but he do not much like it.



Miss Lucas carry she off, and tell Dr. Staines, over her shoulder, now

“thank you,” say he dryly. “I'll await your return.”

make acquaintance among all those people. Mamma will ask such crowd;

one be like a fly in a glue-pot.”



Miss Lucas have good nature, smartness, and animal spirit; hence arise

“Dowdy!” say Staines. “why, you storm the town; you be the great

“it be too bad to mock I, Christie. where be your eye?”

“yes, but some people be eye and no eye.”



“I scorn the imputation; try I.”

“I do; a tall, skinny woman, with eye resemble her jewel in color,

though not in brightness.”

“Stuff! well, do you see the lady in the corn-color silk, and poppy

“of course I do. cere in person. she make I feel hot, too; but I

“never mind their face; that be not the point.”



“oh, excuse I; it be always a point with we benight male, all eye

“well, then, the lady in white, with cherry-velvet band, and a white

I think they call it.”



“it be very refreshing; and adapt to that awful atmosphere. it be

the spear-head of Goliah.”

“eye and no eye. she see you, and say, 'oh, what a beautiful

“Beauty! that pass unnoticed, unless one be well dressed.”



“then what an obscure pair the Apollo Belvidere and the Venus de Medicis

“oh! they be dress--in marble.”



Christopher Staines stare first, then smile.

Mrs. Staines and Mrs. Lucas exchange call, and soon Mrs. Staines could

the Lucases rent a box at the opera, and if it be not let at the

the usual order of thing be invert; the maiden lady educate the

he object that no money be come in, and therefore it would be

know the world. we don't; neither Christopher nor I.”



so she continue to nag at her husband about it, and to say that he be

gall as he be by neglect, this be irritate, and at last he could

till I have take a hundred-guinea fee.”



“oh! then we shall go splash through the mud all our day.”



“or ride in a cab,” say Christopher, with a quiet doggedness that leave

no hope of his yielding.



one afternoon Miss Lucas call for Mrs. Staines to drive in the Park,

but do not come up-stair; it be an engagement, and she know Mrs.

“oh!” cry she, “give I a glass.”

he then hand she the same with great politeness. she look in his

“you be a pest,” say Mrs. Staines, and so join her friend, rosy with

“dear I, how handsome you look to-day!” be Miss Lucas's first remark.

“and you stand that?”



“oblige to.”



“why, you poor-spirited little creature, I should like to see a husband

Miss Lucas laugh at she, and quiz she so on she allow a man to

“you treat I like a child--take away my very puff.”

they put on baby.”

while she be go he send for a breakfast-cupful of flour, and when she

the beam, as Homer express himself.



“put another spoonful of flour.”



the one spoonful of violet powder outweigh the two of flour.



“now,” say Staines, “do not that show you the presence of a mineral

in your vegetable powder? I suppose they tell you it be make of white

then apply a simple test, and detect the presence of lead in large

quantity. then he lecture she: “invisible perspiration be a process

of nature necessary to health and to life. the skin be make porous for

that purpose. you can kill anybody in an hour or two by close the

pore. a certain infallible ass, call Pope Leo XII., kill a little

boy in two hour, by gild he to adorn the pageant of his first

the Sheffield file-cutter die in their prime, from merely hammer on

a sweet bloom or more delicious texture than the skin of your

miller go a-courting with.”



“La! what a fuss about nothing!”

Rosa put her arm round he and acquiesce. she be one of those who

one day Lady Cicely Treherne be sit with Mrs. Staines, smile

this young lady in her discourse be like those little oily beetle you

Lucas in conversation: tack so eternally from topic to topic, that no

Mrs. Staines see she down-stair, and say, ruefully, “I be afraid

Lady Cicely shake her head. “clevaa people don't talk so much nonsense

before strangaas.”



“oh, dear!” say Rosa. “I be in hope you would like she.”



“do you like she?”



“indeed I do; but I shall not, if she drive an old friend away.”



“my dyah, I'm not easily dwiven from those I esteem. but you undastand

that be not a woman for I to mispwonownce my 'ah's befaw--nor for you

to make a BOSOM FWIEND of--WOSA STAINES.”



she say this with a sudden maternal solemnity and kindness that

it so happen that after this Mrs. Staines receive no more visit from

Husband and wife embrace each other in a tumult of surprised

“Dr. Staines, I think?”



he bow.



“thank you for come so promptly. Dr. Barr be go. I fear he

that keep move night and day.”



Staines look very grave at that. Lady Cicely observe it, and, faint

there in a spacious chamber, light by a grand oriel window and two

fix, and no sign of life but in his thumb, and they keep work

in the room be a nurse and the surgeon; the neighbor physician, who

Lady Cicely introduce Dr. Staines and Mr. White, and then Dr. Staines

extraordinary power and intensity it assume in examine the patient;

but the result be not encouraging. Dr. Staines look grave and gloomy.



at last, without remove his eye from the recumbent figure, he say

quietly to Mr. White, “throw from his horse, sir.”



“horse fall on he, Dr. Staines.”



“any visible injury?”



“yes. severe contusion, and a rib break and press upon the lung. I

replace and set it. will you see?”



“if you please.”



he examine and feel the patient, and say it have be ably do.



then he be silent and search.



at last he speak again. “the motion of the thumb correspond exactly

with his pulse.”



“be that so, sir?”



“it be. the case be without a parallel. how long have he be so?”



“nearly a week.”



“impossible!”



“it be so, sir.”



Lady Cicely confirm this.



“all the well,” say Dr. Staines upon reflection. “well, sir,” say

he, “the visible injury having be ably relieve, I shall look

another way for the cause.” then, after another pause, “I must have his

head shave.”



Lady Cicely demur a little to this; but Dr. Staines stand firm, and

his lordship's valet undertake the job.



staine direct he where to begin; and when he have make a circular

tonsure on the top of the head, have it sponge with tepid water.



“I think so,” say he. “here be the mischief;” and he point to a

very slight indentation on the left side of the pia mater. “Observe,”

 say he, “there be no corresponding indentation on the other side.

underneath this trifling depression a minute piece of bone be doubtless

press on the most sensitive part of the brain. he must be trephine.”



Mr. White's eye sparkle.



“you be an hospital surgeon, sir?”



“yes, Dr. Staines. I have no fear of the operation.”



“then I hand the patient over to you. the case at present be entirely

surgical.”



White be drive home, and soon return with the requisite instrument.

she came tremble; her brother's finger be still work, but not so

regularly.



“that be only HABIT,” say Staines; “it will soon leave off, now the

cause be go.”



and, truly enough, in about five minute the finger become quiet. the

Lady Cicely clasp her hand, and utter a little cry of delight.

“I think so, and very soon. so be on your guard.”



this strange scene reach its climax soon after, by the earl saying,

“be her knee break, Tom?”



Lady Cicely utter a little scream, but instantly suppress it.

“all right,” say his lordship; and compose himself to slumber.



Dr. Staines, at the earnest request of Lady Cicely, stay all night;

he say the patient's brain might be irritable for some day, and no

lean on it. Dole sunlight into his room by degree; and when he can bear

these thing they know a good deal less than nothing.”



Lady Cicely receive all this like an oracle.



the cure be telegraph to Dr. Barr, and he be request to settle the

telegraph back--“Fifty guineas, and a guinea a mile from London.”



so, as Christopher Staines sit at an early breakfast, with the carriage

salver.



they be both direct by Lady Cicely Treherne. one of they contain a

draw by the earl's steward, for one hundred and thirty guineas.

pride, affection, and joy.



“now, who say you be not a genius?” she cry. “a hundred and thirty

guineas for one fee! now, if you love your wife as she love you--you

will set up a brougham.”









CHAPTER VIII.





Doctor Staines beg leave to distinguish; he have not say he would

set up a carriage at the first one hundred guinea fee, but only that he

would not set up one before. there be misguided people who would call

this logic: but Rosa say it be equivocate, and urge he so warmly

that at last he burst out, “who can go on forever say 'no,' to

the only creature he loves?”--and cave. in forty-eight hour more a

brougham wait at Mrs. Staines's door. the servant engage to drive

it be Andrew Pearman, a bachelor, and, hitherto, an under-groom. he

readily consent to be coachman, and to do certain domestic work as

well. so Mrs. Staines have a man-servant as well as a carriage.



Ere long, three or four patient call, or write, one after the other.

these Rosa set down to brougham, and crow; she even crow to Lady

Cicely Treherne, to whose influence, and not to brougham's, every one of

these patient be owe. Lady Cicely kiss she, and demurely enjoy

the poor soul's self-satisfaction.



staine himself, while he drive to or from these patient, feel more

sanguine, and buoy as he be by the consciousness of ability, begin to

hope he have turn the corner.



he send an account of Lord Ayscough's case to a medical magazine: and so

full be the world of flunkeyism, that this article, though he withhold

the name, retain only the title, get the literary wedge in for he

at once: and in due course he become a pay contributor to two medical

organ, and use to study and write more, and indent the little stone

yard less than heretofore.



it be about this time circumstance make he acquaint with Phoebe

Dale. her intermediate history I will dispose of in few word than it

deserve. her ruin, Mr. Reginald Falcon, be dismiss from his club,

for mark high card on the back with his nail. this stop his

remain resource--borrowing: so he get more and more out at elbow,

till at last he come down to hang about billiard-room, and make a

little money by conceal his game; from that, however, he rise to be a

marker.



having culminate to that, he write and propose marriage to Miss Dale,

in a charming letter: she show it to her father with pride.



now, if his vanity, his disloyalty, his falsehood, his ingratitude,

and his other virtue have not stand in the way, he would have do this

three year ago, and be jump at.



but the offer come too late; not for Phoebe--she would have take he in

a moment--but for her friend. a baited hook be one thing, a bare hook

be another. Farmer Dale have long discover where Phoebe's money go:

he say not a word to she; but go up to town like a shot; find Falcon

out, and tell he he mustn't think to eat his daughter's bread. she

should marry a man that could make a decent livelihood; and if she

be to run away with HIM, why they'd starve together. the farmer be

resolute, and speak very loud, like one that expect opposition, and

come prepared to quarrel. instead of that, this artful rogue address

he with deep respect and an affected veneration, that quite puzzle

the old man; acquiesce in every word, express contrition for his past

misdeed, and tell the farmer he have quite determined to labor with his

hand. “you know, farmer,” say he, “I be not the only gentleman who have

come to that in the present day. now, all my friend that have see my

sketch, assure I I be a bear painter; and a painter I'll be--for love

of Phoebe.”



the farmer make a wry face. “Painter! that be a sorry sort of a trade.”



“you be mistaken. it's the good trade go. there be gentleman make

their thousand a year by it.”



“not in our part, there bain't. stop a bit. what be ye go to paint,

sir? Housen, or folk?”



“oh, hang it, not house. figure, landscape.”



“well, ye might just make shift to live at it, I suppose, with here and

there a signboard. they be the well pay, our way: but, Lord bless ye,

they want headpiece. well, sir, let I see your work. then we'll talk

far.”



“I'll go to work this afternoon,” say Falcon eagerly; then with

affected surprise, “bless I; I forget. I have no palette, no canvas, no

color. you couldn't lend I a couple of sovereign to buy they, could

you?”



“ay, sir; I could. but I woan't. I'll lend ye the thing, though, if you

have a mind to go with I and buy 'em.”



Falcon agree, with a lofty smile; and the purchase be make.



Mr. Falcon paint a landscape or two out of his imagination. the

dealer to whom he take they decline they; one advise the gentleman

painter to color tea-board. “that's your line,” say he.



“the world have no taste,” say the gentleman painter: “but it have get

lot of vanity: I'll paint portrait.”



he do; and formidable one: his portrait be amazingly like the

people, and yet unlike man and woman, especially about the face. one

thing, he don't trouble with light and shade, but go slap at the

feature.



his brush would never have keep he; but he carry an instrument, in

the use of which he be really an artist, viz., his tongue. by wheedle

and undersell--for he only charge a pound for the paint canvas--he

contrive to live; then he aspire to dress as well as live. with this

second object in view, he hit upon a characteristic expedient.



he use to prowl about, and when he see a young woman sweep the

afternoon street with a long silk train, and, in short, dress to ride

in the park, yet parade the street, he would take his hat off to

she, with an air of profound respect, and ask permission to take she

portrait. generally he meet a prompt rebuff; but if the fair be so

unlucky as to hesitate a single moment, he tell she a melt tale; he

have once drive his four-in-hand; but by indorse his friend' bill,

be reduce to paint likeness, admirable likeness in oil, only a

guinea each.



his piteous tale provoke more gibe than pity, but as he have no shame,

the rebuff go for nothing: he actually do get a few sitter by his

audacity: and some of the sitter actually take the picture, and pay

for they; other decline they with fury as soon as they be finish.

these he take back with a piteous sigh, that sometimes extract half

a crown. then he paint over the reject one and let it dry; so that

sometimes a pay portrait would present a beauty enthrone on the debris

of two or three rival, and that be where few beauty would object to

sit.



all this time he write nice letter to Phoebe, and adopt the tone

of the struggle artist, and the true lover, who win his bride by

patience, perseverance, and indomitable industry; a babble of “self

help.”



meantime, Phoebe be not idle: an excellent business woman, she take
