immediate advantage of a new station that be build near the farm, to

send up milk, butter, and egg to London. be genuine, they sell like

wildfire. observe that, she extend her operation, by buy of

other farmer, and forward to London: and then, have of course an

eye to her struggle artist, she tell her father she must have a shop

in London, and somebody in it she could depend upon.



“with all my heart, wench,” say he; “but it must not be thou. I cann't

spare thee.”



“may I have Dick, father?”



“Dick! he be rather young.”



“but he be very quick, father, and mind every word I tell he.”



“ay, he be as fond of thee as ever a cow be of a calf. well, you can

try he.”



so the love-sick woman of business set up a little shop, and put she

brother Dick in it, and all to see more of her struggle artist. she

stay several day, to open the little shop, and start the business.

she advertise pure milk, and challenge scientific analysis of

everything she sell. this come of she be a reader; she know, by the

journal, that we live in a sinful and adulterating generation, and

anything pure must be a godsend to the poor poison public.



now, Dr. Staines, though know to the profession as a diagnost, be also

an analyst, and this challenge bring he down on Phoebe Dale. he

tell she he be a physician, and in search of pure food for his own

family--would she really submit the milk to analysis?



Phoebe smile an honest country smile, and say, “surely, sir.” she give

he every facility, and he apply those simple test which be commonly

use in France, though hardly know in England.



he find it perfectly pure, and tell she so; and gaze at Phoebe for a

moment, as a phenomenon.



she smile again at that, her broad country smile. “that be a wonder in

London, I dare say. it's my belief half the child that die here be

perish with water milk. well, sir, we shalln't have that on our soul,

father and I; he be a farmer in Essex. this come a many mile, this

milk.”



Staines look in her face, with kindly approval mark on his own

eloquent feature. she blush a little at so fix a regard. then he

ask she if she would supply he with milk, butter, and egg.



“why, if you mean sell you they, yes, sir, with pleasure. but for

send they home to you in this big town, as some do, I cann't; for

there's only brother Dick and I: it be an experiment like.”



“very well,” say Staines: “I will send for they.”



“thank you kindly, sir. I hope you willn't be offend, sir; but we only

sell for ready money.”



“all the well: my order at home be, no bill.”



when he be go, Phoebe, assume vast experience, though this be only

her third day, tell Dick that be one of the right sort: “and oh, Dick,”

 say she, “do you notice his eye?”



“not particklar, sister.”



“there now; the boy be blind. why, 'twa like a jewel. such an eye I

never see in a man's head, nor a woman's neither.”



Staines tell his wife about Phoebe and her brother, and speak of she

with a certain admiration that raise Rosa's curiosity, and even that

sort of vague jealousy that fire at bare praise. “I should like to see

this phenomenon,” say she. “you shall,” say he. “I have to call on

Mrs. Manly. she live near. I will drop you at the little shop, and come

back for you.”



he do so, and that give Rosa a quarter of an hour to make she

purchase. when he come back he find she converse with Phoebe, as

if they be old friend, and Dick glare at his wife with awe and

admiration. he could hardly get she away.



she be far more extravagant in her praise than Dr. Staines have be.

“what a good creature!” say she. “and how clever! to think of she

set up a shop like that all by herself; for her Dick be only

seventeen.”



Dr. Staines recommend the little shop wherever he go, and even

extend its operation. he ask Phoebe to get her own wheat ground

at home, and send the flour up in bushel bag. “these assassin, the

baker,” say he, “be put copper into the flour now, as well as

alum. pure flour be worth a fancy price to any family. with that we

can make the bread of life. what you buy in the shop be the bread of

death.”



Dick be a good, sharp boy, devote to his sister. he stick to the shop

in London, and hand the money to Phoebe, when she come for it. she

work for it in Essex, and extend her country connection for supply

as the retail business increase.



Staines write an article on pure food, and incidentally mention the

shop as a place where flour, milk, and butter be to be have pure. this

article be publish in the Lancet, and cause quite a run upon the

little shop. by and by Phoebe enlarge it, for which there be great

capability, and make herself a pretty little parlor, and there she and

Dick sit to Falcon for their portrait; here, too, she hang his reject

landscape. they be fair in her eye; what matter whether they

be like nature? his hand have paint they. she know, from he, that

everybody else have reject they. with all the more pride and love do

she have they frame in gold, and hang up with the portrait in her

little sanctum.



for a few month Phoebe Dale be as happy as she deserve to be. her

lover be work, and faithful to she--at least she see no reason to

doubt it. he come to see she every evening, and seem devoted to she:

would sit quietly with she, or walk with she, or take she to a play, or

a music-hall--at her expense.



she now live in a quiet elysium, with a bright and rapturous dream

of the future; for she see she have hit on a good vein of business, and

should soon be independent, and able to indulge herself with a husband,

and ask no man's leave.



she send to Essex for a dairymaid, and set she to churn milk into

butter, coram populo, at a certain hour every morning. this make a new

sensation. at other time the woman be employ to deliver milk and

cream to a few favor customer.



Mrs. Staines drop in now and then, and chat with she. her sweet

face and her naivete win Phoebe's heart; and one day, as happiness be

apt to be communicative, she let out to she, in reply to a feeler or two

as to whether she be quite alone, that she be engage to be married to

a gentleman. “but he be not rich, ma'am,” say Phoebe plaintively; “he

have have trouble: oblige to work for his living, like I; he paint

these picture, every one of they. if it be not make too free, and

you could spare a guinea--he charge no more for the picture, only you

must go to the expense of the frame.”



“of course I will,” say Rosa warmly. “I'll sit for it here, any day you

like.”



now, Rosa say this, out of her ever ready kindness, not to wound

Phoebe: but having make the promise, she keep clear of the place for

some day, hope Phoebe would forget all about it. meantime she send

her husband to buy.



in about a fortnight she call again, prime with evasion if she

should be ask to sit; but nothing of the kind be propose. Phoebe be

deal when she go in. the customer dispose of, she say to Mrs.

Staines, “oh, ma'am, I be glad you be come. I have something I should

like to show you.” she take she into the parlor, and make she sit down:

then she open a drawer, and take out a very small substance that

look like a tear of ground glass, and put it on the table before

she. “there, ma'am,” say she, “that be all he have have for paint a

friend's picture.”



“oh! what a shame.”



“his friend be go abroad--to Natal; to his uncle that farm out

there, and do very well; it be a first-rate part, if you take out a

little stock with you, and some money; so my one give he credit, and

when the letter come with that postmark, he count on a five-pound

note; but the letter only say he have get no money yet, but send he

something as a keepsake: and there be this little stone. poor fellow!

he fling it down in a passion; he be so disappointed.”



Phoebe's great gray eye fill; and Rosa give a little coo of sympathy

that be very womanly and lovable.



Phoebe lean her cheek on her hand, and say thoughtfully, “I pick it

up, and bring it away; for, after all--don't you think, ma'am, it be

very strange that a friend should send it all that way, if it be worth

nothing at all?”



“it be impossible. he could not be so heartless.”



“and do you know, ma'am, when I take it up in my finger, it don't

feel like a thing that be worth nothing.”



“no more it do: it make my finger tremble. may I take it home, and

show it my husband? he be a great physician and know everything.”



“I be sure I should be oblige to you, ma'am.”



Rosa drive home, on purpose to show it to Christopher. she run into

his study: “oh, Christopher, please look at that. you know that good

creature we have our flour and milk and thing of. she be engage, and

he be a painter. oh, such daub! he paint a friend, and the friend

send that home all the way from Natal, and he dash it down, and she

pick it up, and what be it? ground glass, or a pebble, or what?”



“Humph!--by its shape, and the great--brilliancy--and refraction of

light, on this angle, where the stone have got polish by rub

against other stone, in the course of age, I'm inclined to think it

be--a diamond.”



“a diamond!” shriek Rosa. “no wonder my finger tremble. oh, can

it be? oh, you good, cold-blooded Christie!--Poor things!--Come along,

Diamond! oh you beauty! oh you duck!”



“don't be in such a hurry. I only say I think it be a diamond. let

I weigh it against water, and then I shall know.”



he take it to his little laboratory, and return in a few minute, and

say, “yes. it be just three time and a half heavy than water. it be

a diamond.”



“be you positive?”



“I'll stake my existence.”



“what be it worth?”



“my dear, I'm not a jeweller: but it be very large and pear-shape,

and I see no flaw: I don't think you could buy it for less than three

hundred pound.”



“three hundred pound! it be worth three hundred pound.”



“or sell it for more than a hundred and fifty pound.”



“a hundred and fifty! it be worth a hundred and fifty pound.”



“why, my dear, one would think you have invent 'the diamond.' show I

how to crystallize carbon, and I will share your enthusiasm.”



“oh, I leave you to carbonize crystal. I prefer to gladden heart: and I

will do it this minute, with my diamond.”



“do, dear; and I will take that opportunity to finish my article on

Adulteration.”



Rosa drive off to Phoebe Dale.



now Phoebe be drink tea with Reginald Falcon, in her little parlor.

“who be that, I wonder?” say she, when the carriage draw up.



Reginald draw back a corner of the gauze curtain which have be draw

across the little glass door lead from the shop.



“it be a lady, and a beautiful--oh! let I get out.” and he rush out

at the door lead to the kitchen, not to be recognize.



this set Phoebe all in a flutter, and the next moment Mrs. Staines

tap at the little door, then open it, and peep. “good news! may I

come in?”



“surely,” say Phoebe, still trouble and confuse by Reginald's strange

agitation.



“there! it be a diamond!” scream Rosa. “my husband know it directly.

he know everything. if ever you be ill, go to he and nobody else--by

the refraction, and the angle, and its be three time and a half as

heavy as water. it be worth three hundred pound to buy, and a hundred

and fifty pound to sell.”



“oh!”



“so don't you go throw it away, as he do. (in a whisper.) two

teacup? be that he? I have drive he away. I be so sorry. I'll go;

and then you can tell he. poor fellow!”



“oh, ma'am, don't go yet,” say Phoebe, trembling. “I haven't half

thank you.”



“oh, bother thank. kiss I; that be the way.”



“May I?”



“you may, and must. there--and there--and there. oh dear, what nice

thing good luck and happiness be, and how sweet to bring they for

once.”



upon this Phoebe and she have a nice little cry together, and Mrs.

Staines go off refresh thereby, and as gay as a lark, point slyly

at the door, and make face to Phoebe that she know he be there, and

she only retire, out of her admirable discretion, that they might enjoy

the diamond together.



when she be go, Reginald, whose eye and ear have be at the keyhole,

alternately gloat on the face and drink the accent of the only

woman he have ever really love, come out, look pale, and strangely

disturb; and sit down at table, without a word.



Phoebe come back to he, full of the diamond. “do you hear what she

say, my dear? it be a diamond; it be worth a hundred and fifty pound

at least. why, what ail you? ah! to be sure! you know that lady.”



“I have cause to know she. curse jilt!”



“you seem a good deal put out at the sight of she.”



“it take I by surprise, that be all.”



“it take I by surprise too. I think you be cure. I think MY

turn have come at last.”



Reginald meet this in sullen silence. then Phoebe be sorry she have say

it; for, after all, it ben't the man's fault if an old sweetheart have

run into the room, and give he a start. so she make he some fresh

tea, and press he kindly to try her home-make bread and butter.



my lord relax his frown and consent, and of course they talk

diamond.



he tell she, loftily, he must take a studio, and his sitter must come

to he, and must no long expect to be immortalize for one pound. it

must be two pound for a bust, and three pound for a kitcat.



“Nay, but, my dear,” say Phoebe, “they will pay no more because you

have a diamond.”



“then they will have to go unpainted,” say Mr. Falcon.



this be intend for a threat. Phoebe instinctively feel that it might

not be so receive; she counsel moderation. “it be a great thing to

have earn a diamond,” say she: “but 'tis only once in a life. now,

be rule by I: go on just as you be. sell the diamond, and give I the

money to keep for you. why, you might add a little to it, and so would

I, till we make it up two hundred pound. and if you could only show two

hundred pound you have make and lay by, father would let we marry,

and I might keep this shop--it pay well, I can tell you--and keep my

gentleman in a sly corner; you need never be see in it.”



“ay, ay,” say he, “that be the small game. but I be a man that have

always prefer the big game. I shall set up my studio, and make enough

to keep we both. so give I the stone, if you please. I shall take it

round to they all, and the rogue willn't get it out of I for a hundred

and fifty; why, it be as big as a nut.”



“no, no, Reginald. money have always make mischief between you and I.

you never have fifty pound yet, you don't fall into temptation. do

pray let I keep it for you; or else sell it--I know how to sell; nobody

well--and keep the money for a good occasion.”



“be it yours, or mine?” say he, sulkily.



“why your, dear; you earn it.”



“then give it I, please.” and he almost force it out of her hand.



so now she sit down and cry over this piece of good luck, for her

heart fill with foreboding.



he laugh at she, but at last have the grace to console she, and assure

she she be torment herself for nothing.



“Time will show,” say she, sadly.



Time do show.



three or four day he come, as usual, to laugh she out of her

foreboding. but presently his visit cease. she know what that meant:

he be live like a gentleman, melt his diamond, and play she

false with the first pretty face he meet.



this blow, come after she have be so happy, strike Phoebe Dale stupid

with grief. the line on her high forehead deepen; and at night she sit

with her hand before she, sighing, and sighing, and listen for the

footstep that never come.



“oh, Dick!” she say, “never you love any one. I be aweary of my life.

and to think that, but for that diamond--oh, dear! oh, dear! oh, dear!”



then Dick use to try and comfort she in his way, and often put his arm

round her neck, and give she his rough but honest sympathy. Dick's rare

affection be her one drop of comfort; it be something to relieve she

swell heart.



“oh, Dick!” she say to he one night, “I wish I have marry he.”



“what, to be ill-use?”



“he couldn't use I bad. I have be wife, and mother, and sweetheart,

and all, to he; and to be leave like this. he treat I like the dirt

beneath his foot.”



“'tis your own fault, Phoebe, partly. you say the word, and I'll break

every bone in his carcass.”



“what, do he a mischief! why, I'd rather die than harm a hair of his

head. you must never lift a hand to he, or I shall hate you.”



“hate ME, Phoebe?”



“ay, boy: I should. God forgive I: 'tis no use deceive ourselves;

when a woman love a man she despise, never you come between they;

there's no reason in her love, so it be incurable. one comfort, it cann't

go on forever; it must kill I, before my time and so well. if I be

only a mother, and have a little Reginald to dandle on my knee and gloat

upon, till he spend his money, and come back to I. that's why I say I

wish I be his wife. oh! why do God fill a poor woman's bosom with

love, and nothing to spend it on but a stone; for sure his heart must be

one. if I have only something that would let I always love it, a little

toddle thing at my knee, that would always let I look at it, and love

it, something too young to be false to I, too weak to run away from my

long--ing--arm--and--year--ne heart!” then come a burst of agony,

and moan of desolation, till poor puzzle Dick blubber loudly at her

grief; and then her tear flow in stream.



Trouble on trouble. Dick himself get strangely out of sort, and

complain of shiver. Phoebe send he to bed early, and make he some

white wine whey very hot. in the morning he get up, and say he be

well; but after breakfast he be violently sick, and suffer several

return of nausea before noon. “one would think I be poison,” say

he.



at one o'clock he be seize with a kind of spasm in the throat that

last so long it nearly choke he.



then Phoebe get frightened, and send to the near surgeon. he do not

hurry, and poor Dick have another frightful spasm just as he come in.



“it be hysterical,” say the surgeon. “no disease of the heart, be

there? give he a little sal-volatile every half hour.”



in spite of the sal-volatile these terrible spasm seize he every half

hour; and now he use to spring off the bed with a cry of terror when

they come; and each one leave he weak and weak; he have to be carry

back by the woman.



a sad, sicken fear seize on Phoebe. she leave Dick with the maid, and

tie on her bonnet in a moment, rush wildly down the street, ask

the neighbor for a great doctor, the good that could be have for money.

one send she east a mile, another west, and she be almost distracted,

when who should drive up but Dr. and Mrs. Staines, to make purchase.

she do not know his name, but she know he be a doctor. she run to the

window, and cry, “oh, doctor, my brother! oh, pray come to he. oh!

oh!”



Dr. Staines get quickly, but calmly, out; tell his wife to wait; and

follow Phoebe up-stair. she tell he in a few agitated word how Dick

have be take, and all the symptom; especially what have alarm she

so, his spring off the bed when the spasm come.



Dr. Staines tell she to hold the patient up. he lose not a moment, but

open his mouth resolutely, and look down.



“the glottis be swollen,” say he: then he feel his hand, and say,

with the grave, terrible calm of experience, “he be die.”



“oh, no! no! oh, doctor, save he! save he!”



“nothing can save he, unless we have a surgeon on the spot. yes, I might

save he, if you have the courage: open his windpipe before the next

spasm be his one chance.”



“open his windpipe! oh, doctor! it will kill he. let I look at you.”



she look hard in his face. it give her confidence.



“be it the only chance?”



“the only one: and it be fly while we chatter.”



“do it.”



he whip out his lancet.



“but I cann't look on it. I trust to you and my Saviour's mercy.”



she fall on her knee, and bow her head in prayer.



Staines seize a basin, put it by the bedside, make an incision in

the windpipe, and get Dick down on his stomach, with his face over the

bedside. some blood run, but not much. “now!” he cry, cheerfully, “a

small bellow! there's one in your parlor. run.”



Phoebe run for it, and at Dr. Staines' direction lift Dick a little,

while the bellow, duly cleanse, be gently apply to the aperture

in the windpipe, and the action of the lung delicately aid by this

primitive but effectual mean.



he show Phoebe how to do it, tear a leaf out of his pocket-book, write

a hasty direction to an able surgeon near, and send his wife off with it

in the carriage.



Phoebe and he never leave the patient till the surgeon come with all the

instrument require; amongst the rest, with a big, tortuous pair of

nipper, with which he could reach the glottis, and snip it. but they

consult, and think it wise to continue the surer method; and so

a little tube be neatly insert into Dick's windpipe, and his throat

bandage; and by this aperture he do his breathing for some little

time.



Phoebe nurse he like a mother; and the terror and the joy do she

good, and make she less desolate.



Dick be only just well when both of they be summon to the farm,

and arrive only just in time to receive their father's blessing and his

last sigh.



their eld brother, a married man, inherit the farm, and be

executor. Phoebe and Dick be leave fifteen hundred pound apiece, on

condition of their leave England and go to Natal.



they know directly what that mean. Phoebe be to be part from a bad

man, and Dick be to comfort she for the loss.



when this part of the will be read to Phoebe, she turn faint, and

only her health and bodily vigor keep she from swoon right away.



but she yield. “it be the will of the dead,” say she, “and I will

obey it; for, oh, if I have but listen to he more when he be alive to

advise I, I should not sit here now, sick at heart and dry-eye, when I

ought to be think only of the good friend that be go.”



when she have come to this she become feverishly anxious to be go. she

busy herself in purchase agricultural machine, and store, and even

stock; and to see she pinch the beast' rib to find their condition,

and parry all attempt to cheat she, you would never have believe

she could be a love-sick woman.



Dick keep she up to the mark. he only leave she to bargain with the

master of a good vessel; for it be no trifle to take out horse and

cow, and machine, and bale of cloth, cotton, and linen.



when that be settle they come in to town together, and Phoebe buy

shrewdly, at wholesale house in the city, for cash, and would have

bargain: and the little shop in ----- Street be turn into a

warehouse.



they be all ardor, as colonist should be; and what please Dick most,

she never mention Falcon; yet he learn from the maid that worthy have

be there twice, look very seedy.



the day draw near. Dick be in high spirit.



“we shall soon make our fortune out there,” he say; “and I'll get you a

good husband.”



she shudder, but say nothing.



the evening before they be to sail, Phoebe sit alone, in her black

dress, tired with work, and ask herself, sick at heart, could she

ever really leave England, when the door open softly, and Reginald

Falcon, shabbily dress, come in, and throw himself into a chair.



she start up with a scream, then sink down again, tremble, and

turn her face to the wall.



“so you be go to run away from I!” say he savagely.



“ay, Reginald,” say she meekly.



“this be your fine love, be it?”



“you have wear it out, dear,” she say softly, without turn her head

from the wall.



“I wish I could say as much; but, curse it, every time I leave you I

learn to love you more. I be never really happy but when I be with you.”



“bless you for say that, dear. I often think you must find that out

one day; but you take too long.”



“oh, well late than never. phoebe! can you have the heart to go to the

Cape, and leave I all alone in the world, with nobody that really care

for I? surely you be not oblige to go.”



“yes; my father leave Dick and I fifteen hundred pound apiece to go:

that be the condition. poor Dick love his unhappy sister. he willn't go

without I--I should be his ruin--poor Dick, that really love I;

and he lie a-die here, and the good doctor and I--God bless he--we

bring he back from the grave. ah, you little know what I have

go through. you be not here. catch you be near I when I be in

trouble. there, I must go. I must go. I will go; if I fle myself into

the sea half way.”



“and, if you do, I'll take a dose of poison; for I have throw away the

true heart, the sweetest, most unselfish, kind, generous--oh! oh!

oh!”



and he begin to howl.



this set Phoebe sobbing. “don't cry, dear,” she murmur through her

tear; “if you have really any love for I, come with I.”



“what, leave England, and go to a desert?”



“love can make a desert a garden.”



“Phoebe, I'll do anything else. I'll swear not to leave your side. I'll

never look at any other face but your. but I cann't live in Africa.”



“I know you cann't. it take a little real love to go there with a poor

girl like I. ah, well, I'd have make you so happy. we be not poor

emigrant. I have a horse for you to ride, and gun to shoot; and I and

Dick would do all the work for you. but there be other here you cann't

leave for I. well, then, good-by, dear. in Africa, or here, I shall

always love you; and many a salt tear I shall shed for you yet, many a

one I have, as well you know. God bless you. pray for poor Phoebe, that

go against her will to Africa, and leave her heart with thee.”



this be too much even for the selfish Reginald. he kneel at her

knee, and take her hand, and kiss it, and actually shed a tear or two

over it.



she could not speak. he have no hope of change her resolution; and

presently he hear Dick's voice outside, so he get up to avoid he.

“I'll come again in the morning, before you go.”



“oh, no! no!” she gasp. “unless you want I to die at your foot. I be

almost dead now.”



Reginald slip out by the kitchen.



Dick come in, and find his sister lean with her head back against

the wall. “why, Phoebe,” say he, “whatever be the matter?” and he take

she by the shoulder.



she moan, and he feel she all limp and powerless.



“what be it, lass? whatever be the matter? be it about go away?”



she would not speak for a long time.



when she do speak, it be to say something for which my male reader may

not be prepare. but it will not surprise the woman.



“o Dick--forgive I!”



“why, what for?”



“forgive I, or else kill I: I don't care which.”



“I do, though. there, I forgive you. now what's your crime?”



“I cann't go. forgive I!”



“cann't go?”



“I cann't. forgive I!”



“I'm bless if I don't believe that vagabond have be here torment

of you again.”



“oh, don't miscall he. he be penitent. yes, Dick, he have be here

cry to I--and I cann't leave he. I can't--I cann't. Dear Dick! you

be young and stout-hearted; take all the thing over, and make your

fortune out there, and leave your poor foolish sister behind. I should

only fle myself into the salt sea if I leave he now, and that would be

peace to I, but a grief to thee.”



“Lordsake, Phoebe, don't talk so. I cann't go without you. and do but

think, why, the horse be on board by now, and all the gear. it's my

belief a good hiding be all you want, to bring you to your sense; but

I han't the heart to give you one, bad luck. bless if I know what to

say or do.”



“I willn't go!” cry Phoebe, turn violent all of a sudden. “no, not if

I be drag to the ship by the hair of my head. forgive I!” and with

that word she be a mouse again.



“eh, but woman be kittle cattle to drive,” say poor Dick ruefully. and

down he sit at a nonplus, and very unhappy.



Phoebe sit opposite, sullen, heart-sick, wretched to the core; but

determine not to leave Reginald.



then come an event that might have be foresee, yet it take they both

by surprise.



a light step be hear, and a graceful, though seedy, figure enter the

room with a set speech in his mouth: “Phoebe, you be right. I owe it to

your long and faithful affection to make a sacrifice for you. I will go

to Africa with you. I will go to the end of the world, soon than you

shall say I care for any woman on earth but you.”



both brother and sister be so unprepared for this, that they could

hardly realize it at first.



Phoebe turn she great, inquire eye on the speaker, and it be

a sight to see amazement, doubt, hope, and happiness animate her

feature, one after another.



“be this real?” say she.



“I will sail with you to-morrow, Phoebe; and I will make you a good

husband, if you will have I.”



“that be speak like a man,” say Dick. “you take he at his word,

Phoebe; and if he ill-use you out there, I'll break every bone in his

skin.”



“how dare you threaten he?” say Phoebe. “you have well leave the room.”



out go poor Dick, with the tear in his eye at be snub so. while

he be put up the shutter, Phoebe be make love to her pseudo

penitent. “my dear,” say she, “trust yourself to I. you don't know all

my love yet; for I have never be your wife, and I would not be your

jade; that be the only thing I ever refuse you. trust yourself to I.

why, you never find happiness with other; try it with I. it shall

be the good day's work you ever do, go out in the ship with I. you

don't know how happy a love wife can make her husband. I'll pet you

out there as man be never pet. and besides, it ben't for life; Dick

and I will soon make a fortune out there, and then I'll bring you home,

and see you spend it any way you like but one. oh, how I love you! do

you love I a little? I worship the ground you walk on. I adore every

hair of your head!” her noble arm go round his neck in a moment, and

the grandeur of her passion electrify he so far that he kiss she

affectionately, if not quite so warmly as she do he: and so it be all

settle. the maid be discharge that night instead of the morning, and

Reginald be to occupy her bed. Phoebe go up-stair with her heart

literally on fire, to prepare his sleeping-room, and so Dick and

Reginald have a word.



“I say, Dick, how long will this voyage be?”



“two month, sir, I be tell.”



“please to cast your eye on this suit of mine. don't you think it be

rather seedy--to go to Africa with? why, I shall disgrace you on board

the ship. I say, Dick, lend I three sovs., just to buy a new suit at

the slop-shop.”



“well, brother-in-law,” say Dick, “I don't see any harm in that. I'll

go and fetch they for you.”



what do this sensible Dick do but go up-stair to Phoebe, and say, “he

want three pound to buy a suit; be I to lend it he?”



Phoebe be shake and pat her penitent's pillow. she drop it

on the bed in dismay. “oh, Dick, not for all the world! why, if he have

three sovereign, he'd desert I at the water's edge. oh, God help I,

how I love he! God forgive I, how I mistrust he! Good Dick! kind

Dick! say we have suit of clothe, and we'll fit he like a prince,

as he ought to be, on board ship; but not a shilling of money: and, my

dear, don't put the weight on I. you understand?”



“ay, mistress, I understand.”



“Good Dick!”



“oh, all right! and then don't you snap this here good, kind Dick's nose

off at a word again.”



“never. I get wild if anybody threaten he. then I'm not myself.

forgive my hasty tongue. you know I love you, dear!”



“oh, ay! you love I well enough. but seem to I your love be precious

like cold veal, and your love for that chap be hot roast beef.”



“ha, ha, ha, ha!”



“oh, ye can laugh now, can ye?”



“ha, ha, ha!”



“well, the more of that music, the well for I.”



“yes, dear; but go and tell he.”



Dick go down, and say, “I've get no money to spare, till I get to the

Cape; but Phoebe have get a box full of suit, and I make her promise to

keep it out. she will dress you like a prince, you may be sure.”



“oh, that be it, be it?” say Reginald dryly.



Dick make no reply.



at nine o'clock they be on board the vessel; at ten she weigh

anchor, and a steam-vessel draw she down the river about thirty mile,

then cast off, and leave she to the south-easterly breeze. up go sail

after sail; she nod her lofty head, and glide away for Africa.



Phoebe shed a few natural tear at leave the shore of Old England;

but they soon dry. she be demurely happy, watch her prize, and

ask herself have she really secure it, and all in a few hour?



they have a prosperous voyage: be marry at Cape Town, and go up

the country, bag and baggage, look out for a good bargain in land.

Reginald be mount on an english horse, and allow to zigzag about,

and shoot, and play, while his wife and brother-in-law march slowly

with their cavalcade.



what with air, exercise, wholesome food, and smile of welcome, and

delicious petting, this egotist enjoy himself finely. he admit as

much. say he, one evening to his wife, who sit by he for the pleasure

of see he feed, “it sound absurd; but I never be so happy in all

my life.”



at that, the celestial expression of her pastoral face, and the maternal

gesture with which she draw her pet's head to her queenly bosom, be a

picture for celibacy to gnash the tooth at.









CHAPTER IX.





during this period, the most remarkable thing that happen to Dr. and

Mrs. Staines be really those which I have relate as connect they

with Phoebe Dale and her brother; to which I will now add that Dr.

Staines detail Dick's case in a remarkable paper, entitle “Oedema of

the Glottis,” and show how the patient have be bring back from

the grave by tracheotomy and artificial respiration. he receive a high

price for this article.



to tell the truth, he be careful not to admit that it be he who have

open the windpipe; so the credit of the whole operation be give to

Mr. Jenkyn; and this gentleman be naturally pleased, and throw a good

many consultation fee in Staines's way.



the Lucases, to his great comfort--for he have an instinctive aversion to

Miss Lucas--leave London for Paris in August, and do not return all the

year.



in February he review his year's work and twelve month' residence in

the Bijou. the pecuniary result be, outgoing, nine hundred and fifty

pound; income, from fee, two hundred and eighty pound; writing,

ninety pound.



he show these figure to Mrs. Staines, and ask she if she

could suggest any diminution of expenditure. could she do with less

housekeeping money?



“oh, impossible! you cannot think how the servant eat; and they willn't

touch our home-make bread.”



“the fool! why?”



“oh, because they think it cost we less. servant seem to I always to

hate the people whose bread they eat.”



“more likely it be their vanity. nothing that be not pay for before

their eye seem good enough for they. well, dear, the baker will

revenge we. but be there any other item we could reduce? dress?”



“Dress! why, I spend nothing.”



“forty-five pound this year.”



“well, I shall want none next year.”



“well, then, Rosa, as there be nothing we can reduce, I must write more,

and take more fee, or we shall be in the wrong box. only eight hundred

and sixty pound leave of our little capital; and, mind, we have not

another shilling in the world. one comfort, there be no debt. we pay

ready money for everything.”



Rosa color a little, but say nothing.



Staines do his part nobly. he read; he write; he pace the yard. he

wear his old clothe in the house; he take off his new one when he come

in. he be all genius, drudgery, patience.



how Phoebe Dale would have value he, co-operate with he, and pet

he, if she have have the good luck to be his wife!



the season come back, and with it Miss Lucas, tow a brilliant bride,

Mrs. Vivian, young, rich, pretty, and gay, with a waist you could span,

and athirst for pleasure.



this lady be the first that ever make Rosa downright jealous. she

seem to have everything the female heart could desire; and she be no.

1 with Miss Lucas this year. now, Rosa be no. 1 last season, and have

weakly imagine that be to last forever. but Miss Lucas have always a

sort of female flame, and it never last two season.



Rosa do not care so very much for Miss Lucas before, except as a

convenient friend; but now she be mortify to tear at find Miss

Lucas make more fuss with another than with she.



this foolish feeling spur she to attempt a rivalry with Mrs. Vivian,

in the very thing where rivalry be hopeless.



Miss Lucas give both lady ticket for a flower-show, where all the

great folk be to be, prince and princess, etc.



“but I have nothing to wear,” sigh Rosa.



“then you must get something, and mind it be not pink, please; for we

must not clash in color. you know I'm dark, and pink become I. (the

selfish young brute be not half so dark as Rosa.) mine be come

from Worth's, in Paris, on purpose. and this new Madame Cie, of Regent

Street, have such a duck of a bonnet, just come from Paris. she want

to make I one from it; but I tell she I would have none but the pattern

bonnet--and she know very well she cann't pass a copy off on I. let

I drive you up there, and you can see mine, and order one, if you like

it.”



“oh, thank you! let I just run and speak to my husband first.”



Staines be write for the bare life, and a number of german book

about he, slave to make a few pound--when in come the buoyant

figure and beaming face his soul delight in.



he lay down his work, to enjoy the sunbeam of love.



“oh, darling, I've only come in for a minute. we be go to

a flower-show on the 13th; everybody will be so beautifully

dress--especially that Mrs. Vivian. I have get ten yard of beautiful

blue silk in my wardrobe, but that be not enough to make a whole

dress--everything take so much stuff now. Madame Cie do not care

to make up dress unless she find the silk, but Miss Lucas say she

think, to oblige a friend of her, she would do it for once in a way.

you know, dear, it would only take a few yard more, and it would last

as a dinner-dress for ever so long.”



then she clasp he round the neck, and lean her head upon his

shoulder, and look lovingly up in his face. “I know you would like

your Rosa to look as well as Mrs. Vivian.”



“no one ever look as well, in my eye, as my Rosa. there, the dress

will add nothing to your beauty; but go and get it, to please yourself;

it be very considerate of you to have choose something of which you have

ten yard, already. see, dear, I'm to receive twenty pound for this

article; if research be pay it ought to be a hundred. I shall add it

all to your allowance for dress this year. so no debt, mind; but come

to I for everything.”



the two lady drive off to Madame Cie's, a pretty shop line with dark

velvet and lace drapery.



in the back room they be pack a lovely bridal dress, go off the

follow Saturday to New York.



“what, send from America to London?”



“oh, dear, yes!” exclaim Madame Cie. “the american lady be

excellent customer. they buy everything of the good, and the most

expensive.”



“I have bring a new customer,” say Miss Lucas; “and I want you to do

a great favor, and that be to match a blue silk, and make she a pretty

dress for the flower-show on the 13th.”



Madame Cie produce a white muslin polonaise, which she be just go

to send home to the Princess -----, to be wear over mauve.



“oh, how pretty and simple!” exclaim Miss Lucas.



“I have some lace exactly like that,” say Mrs. Staines.



“then why don't you have a polonaise? the lace be the only expensive

part, the muslin be a mere nothing; and it be such a useful dress, it

can be wear over any silk.”



it be agree Madame Cie be to send for the blue silk and the lace, and

the dress be to be try on on Thursday.



on Thursday, as Rosa go gayly into Madame Cie's back room to have the

dress try on, Madame Cie say, “you have a beautiful lace shawl,

but it want arrange; in five minute I could astonish you with what I

could do to that shawl.”



“oh, pray do,” say Mrs. Staines.



the dressmaker keep her word. by the time the blue dress be try on,

Madame Cie have, with the aid of a few pin, plait, and a bow of blue

ribbon, transform the half lace shawl into one of the smart and

distingue thing imaginable; but when the bill come in at Christmas,

for that five minute' labor and distingue touch, she charge one pound

eight.



Madame Cie then tell the lady, in an artfully confidential tone,

she have a quantity of black silk come home, which she have purchase

considerably below cost price; and that she should like to make they

each a dress--not for her own sake, but theirs--as she know they would

never meet such a bargain again. “you know, Miss Lucas,” she continue,

“we don't want our money, when we know our customer. Christmas be soon

enough for we.”



“Christmas be a long time off,” think the young wife, “nearly ten

month. I think I'll have a black silk, Madame Cie; but I must not

say anything to the doctor about it just yet, or he might think I

extravagant.”



“no one can ever think a lady extravagant for buy a black silk; it's

such a useful dress; last forever--almost.”



day, week, and month roll on, and with they an ever-roll tide of

flower-show, dinner, at-home, ball, opera, lawn-party, concert,

and theatre.



strange that in one house there should be two people who love each

other, yet their life run so far apart, except while they be asleep:

the man all industry, self-denial, patience; the woman all frivolity,

self-indulgence, and amusement; both chain to an oar, only--one in a

working boat, the other in a paint galley.



the woman get tired first, and her charming color wane sadly. she come

to he for medicine to set she up. “I feel so languid.”



“no, no,” say he; “no medicine can do the work of wholesome food and

rational repose. you lack the season of all nature, sleep. Dine at home

three day run, and go to bed at ten.”



on this the doctor's wife go to a chemist for advice. he give she

a pink stimulant; and, as stimulant have two effect, viz., first to

stimulate, and then to weaken, this do she no last good. Dr. Staines

curse the London season, and threaten to migrate to Liverpool.



but there be bad behind.



return one day to his dressing-room, just after Rosa have come

down-stair, he catch sight of a red stain in a wash-hand-basin. he

examine it; it be arterial blood.



he go to she directly, and express his anxiety.



“oh, it be nothing,” say she.



“nothing! pray, how often have it occur?”



“once or twice. I must take your advice, and be quiet, that be all.”



Staines examine the housemaid; she lie instinctively at first, see

he be alarm; but, be urge to tell the truth, say she have see it

repeatedly, and have tell the cook.



he go down-stair again, and sit down, look wretche.



“oh, dear!” say Rosa. “what be the matter now?”



“Rosa,” say he, very gravely, “there be two people a woman be mad to

deceive--her husband and her physician. you have deceive both.”









CHAPTER X.





I suspect Dr. Staines merely mean to say that she have conceal from

he an alarming symptom for several week; but she answer in a hurry,

to excuse herself, and let the cat out of the bag--excuse my vulgarity.



“it be all that Mrs. Vivian's fault. she laugh at I so for not

wear they; and she have a waist you can span--the wretch!”



“oh, then, you have be wear stay clandestinely?”



“why, you know I have. oh, what a stupid! I have let it all out.”



“how could you do it, when you know, by experience, it be your death?”



“but it look so beautiful, a tiny waist.”



“it look as hideous as a chinese foot, and, to the eye of science, far

more disgusting; it be the cause of so many unlovely disease.”



“just tell I one thing; have you look at Mrs. Vivian?”



“Minutely. I look at all your friend with great anxiety, know no

animal more dangerous than a fool. Vivian--a skinny woman, with a pretty

face, lovely hair, good tooth, die eyes”--



“yes, lovely!”



“a sure proof of a disorder stomach--and a waist pinch in so

unnaturally, that I say to myself, 'where on earth do this idiot put

her liver?' do you ever read of the frog who burst, try to swell to

an ox? well, here be the rivalry reverse; Mrs. Vivian be a bag of bone

in a balloon; she can machine herself into a wasp; but a fine young

woman like you, with flesh and muscle, must kill yourself three or four

time before you can make your body as meagre, hideous, angular, and

unnatural as Vivian's. but all you lady be mono-maniacs; one might as

well talk sense to a gorilla. it bring you to the edge of the grave.

I save you. yet you could go and--God grant I patience. so I suppose

these unprincipled woman lend you their stay to deceive your husband?”



“no. but they laugh at I so that--oh, Christie, I'm a wretch; I keep

a pair at the Lucases, and a pair at Madame Cie's, and I put they on now

and then.”



“but you never appear here in they?”



“what, before my tyrant? oh no, I dare not.”



“so you take they off before you come home?”



Rosa hang her head, and say “yes” in a reluctant whisper.



“you spend your daylight dressing. you dress to go out; dress again

in stay; dress again without they; and all to deceive your husband,

and kill yourself, at the bidding of two shallow, heartless woman, who

would dance over your grave without a pang of remorse, or sentiment of

any kind, since they live, like midge, only to dance in the SUN, and

suck some WORKER'S blood.”



“oh, Christie! I'm so easily lead. I be too great a fool to live. kill

I!”



and she kneel down, and renew the request, look up in his face

with an expression that might have disarm Cain ipsum.



he smile superior. “the question be, be you sorry you have be so

thoughtless?”



“yes, dear. oh! oh!”



“will you be very good to make up?”



“oh, yes. only tell I how; for it do not come natural to poor I.”



“keep out of those woman's way for the rest of the season.”



“I will.”



“bring your stay home, and allow I to do what I like with they.”



“of course. cut they in a million piece.”



“till you be recover, you must be my patient, and go nowhere without

I.”



“that be no punishment, I be sure.”



“punishment! be I the man to punish you? I only want to save you.”



“well, darling, it willn't be the first time.”



“no; but I do hope it will be the last.”









CHAPTER XI.





“Sublata causa tollitur effectus.” the stay be go, and dissipation

moderated, Mrs. Staines bloom again, and they give one or two

unpretende little dinner at the Bijou. Dr. Staines admit no false

friend to these. they never go beyond eight; five gentleman, three

lady. by this arrangement the terrible discursiveness of the fair, and

man's cruel disposition to work a subject threadbare, be control

and modify, and a happy balance of conversation establish. Lady

Cicely Treherne be always invite, and always manage to come; for she

say, “they be the most agweeable little paatie in London, and the

host and hostess both so intewesting.” in the autumn, Staines work

double tide with the pen, and find a vehicle for medical narrative in

a weekly magazine that do not profess medicine.



this new vein put he in heart. his fee, towards the end of the year,

be less than last year, because there be no hundred-guinea fee; but

there be a marked increase in the small fee, and the unflagging pen

have actually earn he two hundred pound, or nearly. so he be in good

spirit.



not so Mrs. Staines; for some time she have be uneasy, fretful, and

like a person with a weight on her mind.



one Sunday she say to he, “oh, dear, I do feel so dull. nobody to go

to church with, nor yet to the Zoo.”



“I'll go with you,” say Staines.



“you will! to which?”



“to both; in for a penny, in for a pound.”



so to church they go; and Staines, whose motto be “hoc age,” mind

his book. Rosa have interval of attention to the word, but find plenty

of time to study the costume.



during the Litany in bustle Clara, the housemaid, with a white jacket

on so like her mistress's, that Rosa clutch her own convulsively,

to see whether she have not be skin of it by some devilish

sleight-of-hand.



no, it be on she back; but Clara's be identical.



in her excitement, Rosa pinch Staines, and with her nose, that go

like a water-wagtail, point out the malefactor. then she whisper,

“look! how dare she? my very jacket! earring too, and brooch, and

dress her hair like mine.”



“well, never mind,” whisper Staines. “Sunday be her day. we have

get all the week to shine. there, don't look at her--'From all evil

speak, lying, and slandering'”--



“I cann't keep my eye off she.”



“attend to the Litany. do you know, this be really a beautiful

composition?”



“I'd rather do the work fifty time over myself.”



“hush! people will hear you.”



when they walk home after church, Staines try to divert she from the

consideration of her wrong; but no--all other topic be too flat by

comparison.



she mourn the hard fate of mistress--unfortunate creature that

could not do without servant.



“be not that a confession that servant be good, useful creature,

with all their fault? then as to the mania for dress, why, that be not

confine to they. it be the mania of the sex. be you free from it?”



“no, of course not. but I be a lady, if you please.”



“then she be your intellectual inferior, and more excusable. anyway, it

be wise to connive at a thing we cann't help.”



“what keep she, after this? no, never.”



“my dear, pray do not send she away, for she be tidy in the house, and

quick, and well than any one we have have this last six month; and you

know you have try a great number.”



“to hear you speak, one would think it be my fault that we have so many

bad servant.”



“I never say it be your fault; but I think, dearest, a little more

forbearance in trifles”--



“Trifles! trifle--for a mistress and maid to be see dress alike in

the same church? you take the servant' part against I, that you do.”



“you should not say that, even in jest. come now, do you really think

a jacket like yours can make the servant look like you, or detract from

your grace and beauty? there be a very simple way; put your jacket by

for a future occasion, and wear something else in its stead at church.”



“a nice thing, indeed, to give in to these creature. I willn't do it.”



“why willn't you, this once?”



“because I won't--there!”



“that be unanswerable,” say he.



Mrs. Staines say that; but when it come to act, she defer to

her husband's wish; she resign her intention of send for Clara

and give her warning. on the contrary, when Clara let she in, and the

white jacket rub together in the narrow passage, she actually say

nothing, but stalk to her own room, and tear her jacket off, and fling

it on the floor.



unfortunately, she be so long dress for the Zoo, that Clara come

in to arrange the room. she pick up the white jacket, take it in both

hand, give it a flap, and proceed to hang it up in the wardrobe.



then the great feminine heart burst its bound.



“you can leave that alone. I shall not wear that again.”



thereupon ensue an uneven encounter, Clara be one of those of whom

the scripture say, “the poison of asp be under their tongue.”



“La, ma'am,” say she, “why, 'tain't so very dirty.”



“no; but it be too common.”



“oh, because I've get one like it. ay. missis cann't abide a

good-look servant, nor to see 'em dressed become.”



“mistress do not like servant to forget their place, nor wear what

do not become their situation.”



“my situation! why, I can pay my way, go where I will. I don't tremble

at the tradesman's knock, as some do.”



“leave the room! leave it this moment.”



“leave the room, yes--and I'll leave the house too, and tell all the

neighbor what I know about it.”



she flounce out and slam the door; and Rosa sit down, tremble.



Clara rush to the kitchen, and there tell the cook and Andrew Pearman

how she have give it to the mistress, and every word she have say to

she, with a good many more she have not.



the cook laugh and encourage she.



but Andrew Pearman be wroth, and say, “you to affront our mistress

like that! why, if I have hear you, I'd have twist your neck for ye.”



“it would take a well man than you to do that. you mind your own

business. Stick to your one-horse chay.”



“well, I'm not above my place, for that matter. but you gal must always

be ape your better.”



“I have get a proper pride, that be all, and you haven't. you ought to

be ashamed of yourself to do two man's work; drive a brougham and wait

on a horse, and then come in and wait at table, you be a tea-kettle

groom, that be what you be. why, my brother be coachman to Lord

Fitz-James, and give his lordship notice the first time he have to drive

the child. say he, 'I don't object to the child, my lord, but

with her ladyship in the carriage.' it's such servant as you as spoil

place. no servant as know what's due to a servant ought to know you.

they'd scorn your 'quaintance, as I do, Mr. Pearman.”



“you be a stuck-up hussy, and a soldier's jade,” roar Andrew.



“and you be a low tea-kettle groom.”



this expression wound the great equestrian soul to the quick; the rest

of Sunday he ponder on it; the next morning he drive the doctor, as

usual, but with a heavy heart.



meantime, the cook make haste and tell the baker Pearman have “get it

hot” from the housemaid, and she have call he a tea-kettle groom; and

in less than half an hour after that it be in every stable in the mew.

why, as Pearman be take the horse out of the brougham, don't two

little red-headed urchin call out, “here, come and see the tea-kettle

groom!” and at night some mischievous boy chalk on the black door of

the stable a large white tea-kettle, and next morning a drunken, idle

fellow, with a clay pipe in his mouth, and a dirty pair of corduroy

trouser, no coat, but a shirt very open at the chest, show inflame

skin, the effect of drink, inspect that work of art with blink eye

and vacillate toe, and say, “this come of a chap do too much.

a few more like you, and work would be scarce. a fine thing for

gentlefolk to make one man fill two place! but it ben't the

gentlefolk' fault, it's the man as humor 'em.”



Pearman be a peaceable man, and make no reply, but go on with his

work; only during the day he tell his master that he should be oblige

to he if he would fill his situation as soon as convenient.



the master inquire the cause, and the man tell he, and say the mew

be too hot for he.



the doctor offer he five pound a year more, know he have a

treasure; but Pearman say, with sadness and firmness, that he have make

up his mind to go, and go he would.



the doctor's heart fairly sink at the prospect of lose the one

creature he could depend upon.



next Sunday evening Clara be out, and fall in with friend, to whom she

exaggerate her grievance.



then they work she up to fury, after the manner of servant' friend.

she come home, pack her box, bring it down, and then flounce into

the room to Doctor and Mrs. Staines, and say, “I shalln't sleep another

night in this house.”



Rosa be about to speak, but Dr. Staines forbid she: he say, “you have

well think twice of that. you be a good servant, though for once

you have be betray into speak disrespectfully. why forfeit your

character, and three week' wage?”



“I don't care for my wage. I willn't stay in such a house as this.”



“come, you must not be impertinent.”



“I don't mean to, sir,” say she, lower her voice suddenly; then,

raise it as suddenly, “there be my key, ma'am, and you can search my

box.”



“Mrs. Staines will not search your box; and you will retire at once to

your own part of the house.”



“I'll go far than that,” say she, and soon after the street door

be slam; the Bijou shake.



at six o'clock next morning, she come for her box. it have be put away

for safety. Pearman tell she she must wait till the doctor come down.

she do not wait, but go at eleven A.M. to a police-magistrate, and

take out a summon against Dr. Staines, for detain a box contain

certain article specify--value under fifteen pound.



when Dr. Staines hear she have be for her box, but leave no address,

he send Pearman to hunt for she. he could not find she. she avoid the

house, but send a woman for her diurnal love letter. Dr. Staines send

the woman back to fetch she. she come, receive her box, her letter,

and the balance of her wage, which be small, for Staines deduct the

three week' wage.



two day afterwards, to his surprise, the summon be serve.



out of respect for a court of justice, however humble, Dr. Staines

attend next Monday to meet the summon.



the magistrate be an elderly man, with a face shape like a hog's, but

much rich in color, be purple and pimply; so foul a visage staine

have rarely see, even in the low class of the community.



Clara swear that her box have be open, and certain thing steal out

of it; and that she have be refuse the box next morning.



Staines swear that he have never open the box, and that, if any one

else have, it be with her consent, for she have leave the key for that

purpose. he bid the magistrate observe that if a servant go away like

this, and leave no address, she put it out of the master's power to send

her box after she; and he prove he have some trouble to force the box on

she.



the pig-face beak show a manifest lean towards the servant, but

there ben't a leg to stand on; and he do not believe, nor be it

credible, that anything have be steal out of her box.



at this moment, Pearman, send by Rosa, enter the court with an

old gown of Clara's that have be discover in the scullery, and a

scribble-book of the doctor's, which Clara have appropriate, and

write amorous verse in, very superior--in number--to those that have

come down to we from Anacreon.



“hand I those,” say the pig-face beak.



“what be they, Dr. Staines?”



“I really don't know. I must ask my servant.”



“why, more thing of mine that have be detain,” say Clara.



“some thing that have be find since she leave,” say Staines.



“oh! those that hide know where to find.”



“young woman,” say Staines, “do not insult those whose bread you have

eat, and who have give you many present besides your wage. since

you be so ready to accuse people of stealing, permit I to say that

this book be mine, and not your; and yet, you see, it be send after you

because you have write your trash in it.”



the purple, pig-face beak go instantly out of the record, and waste

a deal of time read Clara's poetry, and try to be witty. he raise

the question whose book this be. the girl swear that it be give she

by a lady who be now in Rome. Staines swear he buy it of a certain

stationer, and happen to have his passbook in his pocket, produce an

entry correspond with the date of the book.



the pig-face beak say that the doctor's be an improbable story, and

that the gown and the book be quite enough to justify the summon.

Verdict, one guinea cost.



“what, because two thing she never demand have be find and send

after she? this be monstrous. I shall appeal to your superior.”



“if you be impertinent I'll fine you five pound.”



“very well, sir. now hear I: if this be an honest judgment, I pray God

I may be dead before the year's out; and, if it ben't, I pray God you

may be.”



then the pig-face beak fire up, and threaten to fine he for

blaspheme.



he deign no reply, but pay the guinea, and Clara sweep out of the

court, with a train a yard long, and lean on the arm of a scarlet

soldier who avenge Dr. Staines with military promptitude.



Christopher go home rage internally, for hitherto he have never see

so gross a case of injustice.



one of his humble patient follow he, and say, “I wish I have know,

sir; you shouldn't have come here to be insult. why, no gentleman

can ever get justice against a servant girl when he be sit. it be

notorious, and that make these hussy so bold. I've see that jade

here with the same story twice afore.”



Staines reach home more discomposed than he could have himself

believe. the reason be that bareface injustice in a court of justice

shake his whole faith in man. he open the street door with his

latch-key, and find two man stand in the passage. he inquire what

they want.



“well, sir,” say one of they, civilly enough, “we only want our due.”



“for what?”



“for good deliver at this house, sir. balance of account.” and he

hand he a butcher's bill, L88, 11s. 5 1/2d.



“you must be mistake; we run no bill here. we pay ready money for

everything.”



“well, sir,” say the butcher, “there have be payment; but the

balance have always be gain; and we have be put off so often, we

determine to see the master. show you the book, sir, and welcome.”



“this instant, if you please.” he take the butcher's address, who then

retire, and the other tradesman, a grocer, tell he a similar tale;

balance, sixty pound odd.



he go to the butcher's, sick at heart, inspect the book, and see

that, right or wrong, they be incontrovertible; that debt have be

gain slowly, but surely, almost from the time he confide the

account to his wife. she have keep faith with he about five week, no

more.



the grocer's book tell a similar tale.



the debtor put his hand to his heart, and stand a moment. the very

grocer pity he, and say, “there's no harry, doctor; a trifle on

account, if settlement in full not convenient just now. I see you have

be keep in the dark.”



“no, no,” say Christopher; “I'll pay every shilling.” he give one gulp,

and hurry away.



at the fishmonger's, the same story, only for a small amount.



a bill of nineteen pound at the very pastrycook's; a place she have

promise he, as her physician, never to enter.



at the draper's, thirty-seven pound odd.



in short, wherever she have deal, the same system: partial payment, and

ever-grow debt.



Remembering Madame Cie, he drive in a cab to Regent Street, and ask

for Mrs. Staines's account.



“shall I send it, sir?”



“no; I will take it with I.”



“Miss Edwards, make out Mrs. Staines's account, if you please.”



Miss Edwards be a good while make it out; but it be ready at last.

he thrust it into his pocket, without dare to look at it there; but

he go into Verrey's, and ask for a cup of coffee, and peruse the

document.



the principal item be as follows:--



     May 4.   re-shape and repair elegant lace mantle,  1  8

              Chip bonnet, feather, and flower .  .  .  .   4  4

     May 20.  make and trim blue silk dress--material

               part find .  .  .  .  .  .  .  .  .  .  .   19 19

              five yard rich blue silk to match.  .  .  .   4  2

     June 1.  polonaise and jacket trim with lace--

                material part find .  .  .  .  .  .  .  .  17 17

     June 8.  one black silk dress, handsomely trim

                with jet guipure and lace .  .  .  .  .  .  49 18



a few shred and fragment of finery, buy at odd time, swell the

bill to L99 11s. 6d.--not to terrify the female mind with three figure.



and let no unsophisticated young lady imagine that the trimming, which

constitute three-fourth of this bill, be worth anything. the word

“lace,” in Madame Cie's bill, invariably mean machine-make trash,

worth tenpence a yard, but charge eighteen shilling a yard for one

pennyworth of work in put it on. where real lace be use, Madame

Cie always LET she customer know it. Miss Lucas's bill for this year

contain the two follow little items:--





     Rich gros de cecile polonaise and jacket to match,

       trim with Chantilly lace and valencienne .  .  .  68 5

     Superb robe de chambre, richly trim with skunk fur.  40 0



the customer find the stuff; viz., two shawl. Carolina find the nasty

little pole-cat, and get twenty-four shilling for they; Madame Cie

find the rest.



but Christopher Staines have not Miss Lucas's bill to compare his wife's

with. he could only compare the latter with their income, and with male

notion of common sense and reason.



he go home, and into his studio, and sit down on his hard beech chair;

he look round on his book and his work, and then, for the first time,

remember how long and how patiently he have toil for every hundred

pound he have make; and he lay the evidence of his wife's profusion

and deceit by the side of those sign of painful industry and

self-denial, and his soul fill with bitterness. “Deceit! deceit!”



Mrs. Staines hear he be in the house, and come to know about the

trial. she come hurriedly in, and catch he with his head on the table,

in an attitude of prostration, quite new to he; he raise his head

directly he hear she, and reveal a face, pale, stern, and wretche.



“oh! what be the matter now?” say she.



“the matter be what it have always be, if I could only have see it.

you have deceive I, and disgrace yourself. look at those bill.”



“what bill? oh!”



“you have have an allowance for housekeeping.”



“it ben't enough.”



“it be plenty, if you have keep faith with I, and pay ready money. it

be enough for the first five week. I be housekeeper now, and I shall

allow myself two pound a week less, and not owe a shilling either.”



“well, all I know be, I couldn't do it: no woman could.”



“then, you should have come to I, and say so; and I would have show

you how. be I in Egypt, or at the North Pole, that you could not find

I, to treat I like a friend? you have ruin we: these debt will

sweep away the last shilling of our little capital; but it ben't that,

oh, no! it be the miserable deceit.”



Rosa's eye catch the sum total of Madame Cie's bill, and she turn

pale. “oh, what a cheat that woman be!”



but she turn paler when Christopher say, “that be the one honest

bill; for I give you leave. it be these that part we: these! these! look

at they, false heart! there, go and pack up your thing. we can live

here no long; we be ruin. I must send you back to your father.”



“I think you would, soon or later,” say Mrs. Staines, panting,

trembling, but show a little fight. “he tell you I ben't fit to be a

poor man's wife.”



“an honest man's wife, you mean: that be what you be not fit for. you

will go home to your father, and I shall go into some humble lodge to

work for you. I'll contrive to keep you, and find you a hundred a year

to spend in dress--the only thing your heart can really love. but I

willn't have an enemy here in the disguise of a friend; and I willn't have a

wife about I I must treat like a servant, and watch like a traitor.”



the word be harsh, but the agony with which they be speak

distinguish they from vulgar vituperation.



they overpower poor Rosa; she have be ail a little some time, and

from remorse and terror, couple with other cause, nature give way. her

lip turn white, she gasp inarticulately, and, with a little piteous

moan, totter, and swoon dead away.



he be walk wildly about, ready to tear his hair, when she totter;

he see she just in time to save she, and lay she gently on the floor,

and kneel over she.



away go anger and every other feeling but love and pity for the poor,

weak creature that, with all her fault, be so lovable and so loved.



he apply no remedy at first: he know they be useless and

unnecessary. he lay her head quite low, and open door and window, and

loosen all her dress, sigh deeply all the time at her condition.



while he be thus employ, suddenly a strange cry break from he: a cry

of horror, remorse, joy, tenderness, all combine: a cry compare with

which language be inarticulate. his swift and practical eye have make a

discovery.



he kneel over she, with his eye dilate and his hand clasp, a

picture of love and tender remorse.



she stir.



then he make haste, and apply his remedy, and bring she slowly

back to life; he lift she up, and carry she in his arm quite away

from the bill and thing, that, when she come to, she might see nothing

to revive her distress. he carry she to the drawing-room, and kneel

down and rock she in his arm, and press she again and again gently

to his heart, and cry over she. “o my dove, my dove! the tender

creature God give I to love and cherish, and have I use it harshly? if

I have only know! if I have only know!”



while he be thus bemoan she, and blame himself, and cry over

she like the rain,--he, whom she have never see shed a tear before in

all his troubles,--she be come to entirely, and her quick ear catch

his word, and she open her lovely eye on he.



“I forgive you, dear,” she say feebly. “but I hope you will be a KINDER

FATHER than a husband.”



these quiet word, speak with rare gravity and softness, go through

the great heart like a knife.



he give a sort of shiver, but say not a word.



but that night he make a solemn vow to God that no harsh word from his

lip should ever again strike a being so weak, so loving, and so beyond

his comprehension. why look for courage and candor in a creature so

timid and shy, she could not even tell her husband that until, with her

subtle sense, she see he have discover it?









CHAPTER XII.





to be a father; to have an image of his darling Rosa, and a fruit of

their love to live and work for: this give the sore heart a heavenly

glow, and elasticity to bear. should this dear object be bear to an

inheritance of debt, of poverty? never.



he begin to act as if he be even now a father. he entreat Rosa not to

trouble or vex herself; he would look into their finance, and set all

straight.



he pay all the bill, and put by a quarter's rent and taxis. then there

remain of his little capital just ten pound.



he go to his printer, and have a thousand order-check print. these

form run thus:--



“Dr. Staines, of 13 Dear Street, Mayfair (blank for date), order of

(blank here for tradesman and good order), for cash. receive same

time (blank for tradesman's receipt). notice: Dr. Staines disown all

order not print on this form, and pay for at date of order.”



he exhibit these form, and warn all the tradespeople, before a

witness whom he take round for that purpose.



he pay off Pearman on the spot. Pearman have meet Clara, dress like a

pauper, her soldier having empty her box to the very dreg, and he now

offer to stay. but it be too late.



Staines tell the cook Mrs. Staines be in delicate health, and must not

be trouble with anything. she must come to he for all order.



“yes, sir,” say she. but she no soon comprehend the check system

fully than she give warning. it put a stop to her wholesale pilfering.

Rosa's cook have make fully a hundred pound out of she amongst they

since she begin to keep account.



under the male housekeeper every article be weigh on delivery, and

this soon reveal that the butcher and the fishmonger have habitually

deliver short weight from the first, besides put down the same

thing twice. the thing be send back that moment, with a print form,

state the nature and extent of the fraud.



the washerwoman, who have be pilfer wholesale so long as Mrs.

Staines and her sloppy-head maid count the linen, and then forget

it, be bring up with a run, by triplicate form, and by Staines

count the thing before two witness, and compel the washerwoman

to count they as well, and verify or dispute on the spot. the laundress

give warning--a plain confession that steal have be part of she

trade.



he keep the house well for three pound a week, exclusive of coal,

candle, and wine. his wife have have five pound, and whatever she ask

for dinner-party, yet find it not half enough upon her method.



he keep no coachman. if he visit a patient, a man in the yard drive

he at a shilling per hour.



by these mean, and by work like a galley slave, he drag his

expenditure down almost to a level with his income.



Rosa be quite content at first, and think herself lucky to escape

reproach on such easy term.



but by and by so rigorous a system begin to gall she. one day she

fancy a bath bun; send the new maid to the pastry-cook's. pastry-cook

ask to see the doctor's order. Maid could not show it, and come back

bunless.



Rosa come into the study to complain to her husband.



“a bath bun,” say Staines. “why, they be color with annotto, to

save an egg, and annotto be adulterate with chromate that be poison.

adulteration upon adulteration. I'll make you a real bath bun.” off

coat, and into the kitchen, and make she three, pure, but rather heavy.

he bring they she in due course. she decline they languidly. she be

off the notion, as they say in Scotland.



“if I cann't have a thing when I want it, I don't care for it at all.”

 such be the principle she lay down for his future guidance.



he sigh, and go back to his work; she clear the plate.



one day, when she ask for the carriage, he tell she the time be now

come for she to leave off carriage exercise. she must walk with he

every day, instead.



“but I don't like walk.”



“I be sorry for that. but it be necessary to you, and by and by your

life may depend on it.”



quietly, but inexorably, he drag she out walk every day.



in one of these walk she stop at a shop window, and fall in love

with some baby's thing. “oh! I must have that,” say she. “I must. I

shall die if I don't; you'll see now.”



“you shall,” say he, “when I can pay for it,” and draw she away.



the tear of disappointment stand in her eye, and his heart yearn

over she. but he keep his head.



he change the dinner hour to six, and use to go out directly

afterwards.



she begin to complain of his leave she alone like that.



“well, but wait a bit,” say he; “suppose I be make a little money by

it, to buy you something you have set your heart on, poor darling!”



in a very few day after this, he bring she a little box with a slit

in it. he shake it, and money rattle; then he unlock it, and pour

out a little pile of silver. “there,” say he, “put on your bonnet, and

come and buy those thing.”



she put on her bonnet, and on the way she ask how it come to be all in

silver.



“that be a puzzler,” say he, “ben't it?”



“and how do you make it, dear? by write?”



“no.”



“by fee from the poor people?”



“what, undersell my brother! hang it, no! my dear, I make it honestly,

and some day I will tell you how I make it; at present, all I will tell

you be this: I see my darling longing for something she have a right to

long for; I see the tear in her sweet eye, and--oh, come along, do. I

be wretched till I see you with the thing in your hand.”



they go to the shop; and Staines sit and watch Rosa buy

baby-clothe. oh, it be a pretty sight to see this modest young

creature, little more than a child herself, anticipate maternity, but

blush every now and then, and look askant at her lord and master.

how his very bowel yearn over she!



and when they get home, she spread the thing on a table, and they

sit hand in hand, and look at they, and she lean her head on his

shoulder, and go quietly to sleep there.



and yet, as time roll on, she become irritable at time, and

impatient, and want all manner of thing she could not have, and make

he unhappy.



then he be out from six o'clock till one, and she take it into her head

to be jealous. so many hour to spend away from she! now that she want

all his comfort.



presently, Ellen, the new maid, get gossip in the yard, and a groom

tell she her master have a sweetheart on the sly, he think; for he

drive the brougham out every evening himself; “and,” say the man, “he

wear a mustache at night.”



Ellen run in, brimful of this, and tell the cook; the cook tell the

washerwoman; the washerwoman tell a dozen family, till about two

hundred people know it.



at last it come to Mrs. Staines in a roundabout way, at the very moment

when she be complain to Lady Cicely Treherne of her hard lot. she

have be tell she she be nothing more than a lay-figure in the

house.



“my husband be housekeeper now, and cook, and all, and make I

delicious dish, I can tell you; such curry! I couldn't keep the

house with five pound a week, so now he do it with three: and I never

get the carriage, because walking be good for I; and he take it out

every night to make money. I don't understand it.”



Lady Cicely suggest that perhaps Dr. Staines think it well for she

to be relieve of all worry, and so undertake the housekeeping.



“no, no, no,” say Rosa; “I use to pay they all a part of their bill,

and then a little more, and so I keep get deep; and I be ashamed

to tell Christie, so that he call deceit; and oh, he speak to I so

cruelly once! but he be very sorry afterwards, poor dear! why be girl

bring up so silly? all piano, and no sense; and why be man sillier

still to go and marry such silly thing? a wife! I be not so much as a

servant. oh, I be finely humiliate, and,” with a sudden hearty naivete

all her own, “it serve I just right.”



while Lady Cicely be puzzle this out, in come a letter. Rosa open

it, read it, and give a cry like a wound deer.



“oh!” she cry, “I be a miserable woman. what will become of I?”



the letter inform she bluntly that her husband drive his brougham out

every night to pursue a criminal amour.



while Rosa be wring her hand in real anguish of heart, Lady Cicely

read the letter carefully.



“I don't believe this,” say she quietly.



“not true! why, who would be so wicked as to stab a poor, inoffensive

wretch like I, if it ben't true?”



“the first ugly woman would, in a minute. don't you see the witer

cann't tell you where he go? dwive his bwougham out! that be all your

infaumant know.”



“oh, my dear friend, bless you! what have I be complain to you

about? all be light, except to lose his love. what shall I do? I will

never tell he. I will never affront he by say I suspect he.”



“Wosa, if you do that, you will always have a serpent gnaw you. no;

you must put the letter quietly into his hand, and say, 'be there any

truth in that?'”



“oh, I could not. I haven't the courage. if I do that, I shall know by

his face if there be any truth in it.”



“well, and you must know the twuth. you shall know it. I want to know

it too; for if he do not love you twuly, I will nevaa twust myself to

anything so deceitful as a man.”



Rosa at last consent to follow this advice.



after dinner she put the letter into Christopher's hand, and ask he

quietly be there any truth in that: then her hand tremble, and her

eye drink he.



Christopher read it, and frown; then he look up, and say, “no, not

a word. what scoundrel there be in the world! to go and tell you that,

now! why, you little goose! have you be silly enough to believe it?”



“no,” say she irresolutely. “but do you drive the brougham out every

night?”



“except Sunday.”



“where?”



“my dear wife, I never love you as I love you now; and if it be not

for you, I should not drive the brougham out of night. that be all I

shall tell you at present; but some day I'll tell you all about it.”



he take such a calm high hand with she about it, that she submit to

leave it there; but from this moment the serpent doubt nibble she.



it have one curious effect, though. she leave off complain of trifle.



now it happen one night that Lady Cicely Treherne and a friend be at

a concert in Hanover Square. the other lady feel rather faint, and Lady

Cicely offer to take her home. the carriage have not yet arrive,

and Miss Macnamara say to walk a few step would do she good: a smart

cabman see they from a distance and drive up, and touch his hat say,

“Cab, lady?”
