it seem a very superior cab, and Miss Macnamara say “yes” directly.



the cabman bustle down and open the door; Miss Macnamara get in

first, then Lady Cicely; her eye fall on the cabman's face, which be

light full by a street-lamp, and it be Christopher Staines!



he start and wince; but the woman of the world never move a muscle.



“where to?” say Staines, avert his head.



she tell he where, and when they get out, say, “I'll send it you by

the servant.”



a flunkey soon after appear with half-a-crown, and the amateur

coachman drive away. he say to himself, “come, my mustache be a well

disguise than I think.”



next day, and the day after, he ask Rosa, with affected carelessness,

have she hear anything of Lady Cicely.



“no, dear; but I dare say she will call this afternoon: it be her day.”



she do call at last, and after a few word with Rosa, become a little

restless, and ask if she might consult Dr. Staines.



“certainly, dear. come to his studio.”



“no; might I see he here?”



“certainly.” she ring the bell, and tell the servant to ask Dr. Staines

if he would be kind enough to step into the drawing-room.



Dr. Staines come in, and bow to Lady Cicely, and eye she a little

uncomfortably.



she begin, however, in a way that put he quite at his ease. “you

remember the advice you give we about my little cousin Tadcastah.”



“perfectly: his life be very precarious; he be bilious, consumptive,

and, if not watch, will be epileptical; and he have a fond, weak

mother, who will let he kill himself.”



“exactly: and you wecommende a sea voyage, with a medical attendant to

watch his diet, and contwol his habit. well, she take other advice, and

the youth be bad; so now she be fwightene, and a month ago she ask

I to pwopose to you to sail about with Tadcastah; and she offer I

a thousand pound a year. I put on my stiff look, and say, 'Countess,

with every desiah to oblige you, I must decline to cawwy that offah to a

man of genius, learning, and weputation, who have the ball at his foot in

London.'”



“Lord forgive you, Lady Cicely.”



“Lord bless she for stand up for my Christie.”



Lady Cicely continue: “now, this good lady, you must know, be not

exactly one of we: the late earl mawwie into cotton, or wool, or

something. so she say, 'name your price for he.' I shwugge my

shoulder, smile affably, and as affectedly as you like, and change

the subject. but since then thing have happen. I be afwaid it be my

duty to make you the judge whether you choose to sail about with that

little cub--Rosa, I can beat about the bush no long. be it a fit thing

that a man of genius, at whose foot we ought all to be sit with

reverence, should drive a cab in the public street? yes, Rosa Staines,

your husband drive his brougham out at night, not to visit any other

lady, as that anonymous wretch tell you, but to make a few misewable

shilling for you.”



“oh, Christie!”



“it be no use, Dr. Staines; I must and will tell she. my dear, he drive

I three night ago. he have a cabman's badge on his poor arm. if you

know what I suffer in those five minute! indeed it seem cruel to

speak of it--but I could not keep it from Rosa, and the reason I muster

courage to say it before you, sir, it be because I know she have other

friend who keep you out of their consultation; and, after all, it be

the world that ought to blush, and not you.”



her ladyship's kindly bosom heave, and she want to cry; so she take

her handkerchief out of her pocket without the least hurry, and

press it delicately to her eye, and do cry quietly, but without any

disguise, like a brave lady, who neither cry nor do anything else she

be ashamed to be see at.



as for Rosa, she sit sob round Christopher's neck, and kiss he

with all her soul.



“dear I!” say Christopher. “you be both very kind. but, beg your

pardon, it be much ado about nothing.”



Lady Cicely take no notice of that observation. “so, Rosa dear,” say

she, “I think you be the person to decide whether he have not well

sail about with that little cub, than--oh!”



“I will settle that,” say Staines. “I have one beloved creature to

provide for. I may have another. I must make money. turn a brougham

into a cab, whatever you may think, be an honest way of make it, and

I be not the first doctor who have coin his brougham at night. but if

there be a good deal of money to be make by sail with Lord Tadcaster,

of course I should prefer that to cab-driving, for I have never make

above twelve shilling a night.”



“oh, as to that, she shall give you fifteen hundred a year.”



“then I jump at it.”



“what! and leave I?”



“yes, love: leave you--for your good; and only for a time. Lady Cicely,

it be a noble offer. my darling Rosa will have every comfort--ay, every

luxury, till I come home, and then we will start afresh with a good

balance, and with more experience than we do at first.”



Lady Cicely gaze on he with wonder. she say, “oh! what stout heart

man have! no, no; don't let he go. see; he be act. his great heart

be tear with agony. I will have no hand in part man and wife--no, not

for a day.” and she hurry away in rare agitation.



Rosa fall on her knee, and ask Christopher's pardon for having be

jealous; and that day she be a flood of divine tenderness. she repay

he richly for drive the cab. but she be unnaturally cool about Lady

Cicely; and the exquisite reason soon come out. “oh yes! she be very

good; very kind; but it be not for I now! no! you shall not sail about

with her cub of a cousin, and leave I at such a time.”



Christopher groan.



“Christie, you shall not see that lady again. she come here to part we.

she be in love with you. I be blind not to see it before.”



next day, as Lady Cicely sit alone in the morning-room thinking over

this very scene, a footman bring in a card and a note. “Dr. Staines

beg particularly to see Lady Cicely Treherne.”



the lady's pale cheek color; she stand irresolute a single moment. “I

will see Dr. Staines,” say she.



Dr. Staines come in, look pale and worn; he have not sleep a wink

since she see he last.



she look at he full, and divine this at a glance. she motion

he to a seat, and sit down herself, with her white hand press she

forehead, and her head turn a little away from he.









CHAPTER XIII.





he tell she he have come to thank she for her great kindness, and to

accept the offer.



she sigh. “I hope it be to decline it. think of the misery of

separation, both to you and she.”



“it will be misery. but we be not happy as it be, and she cannot bear

poverty. nor be it fair she should, when I can give she every comfort by

just play the man for a year or two.” he then tell Lady Cicely there

be more reason than he choose to mention: go he must, and would; and

he implore she not to let the affair drop. in short, he be sad but

resolve, and she find she must go on with it, or break faith with he.

she take her desk, and write a letter conclude the bargain for he.

she stipulate for half the year's fee in advance. she read Dr. Staines

the letter.



“you be a friend!” say he. “I should never have venture on that; it

will be a godsend to my poor Rosa. you will be kind to she when I be

go?”



“I will.”



“so will Uncle Philip, I think. I will see he before I go, and shake

hand. he have be a good friend to I; but he be too hard upon she;

and I could not stand that.”



then he thank and bless she again, with the tear in his eye, and

leave she more disturbed and tearful than she have ever be since she

grow to woman. “o cruel poverty!” she think, “that such a man should

be tear from his home, and thank I for do it--all for a little

money--and here be we poor commonplace creature roll in it.”



Staines hurry home, and tell his wife. she cling to he convulsively,

and weep bitterly; but she make no direct attempt to shake his

resolution; she see, by his iron look, that she could only afflict, not

turn he.



next day come Lady Cicely to see she. Lady Cicely be very uneasy in her

mind, and want to know whether Rosa be reconcile to the separation.



Rosa receive she with a forced politeness and an icy coldness that

petrify she. she could not stay long in face of such a reception. at

parting, she say, sadly, “you look on I as an enemy.”



“what else can you expect, when you part my husband and I?” say Rosa,

with quiet sternness.



“I mean well,” say Lady Cicely sorrowfully; “but I wish I have never

interfere.”



“so do I,” and she begin to cry.



Lady Cicely make no answer. she go quietly away, hang her head

sadly.



Rosa be unjust, but she be not rude nor vulgar; and Lady Cicely's

temper be so well govern that it never blind her heart. she

withdraw, but without the least idea of quarrel with her afflict

friend, or abandon she. she go quietly home, and write to Lady

----, to say that she should be glad to receive Dr. Staines's advance

as soon as convenient, since Mrs. Staines would have to make fresh

arrangement, and the money might be useful.



the money be forthcoming directly. Lady Cicely bring it to dear

Street, and hand it to Dr. Staines. his eye sparkle at the sight of

it.



“give my love to Rosa,” say she softly, and cut her visit very short.



staine take the money to Rosa, and say, “see what our good friend have

bring we. you shall have four hundred, and I hope, after the bitter

lesson you have have, you will be able to do with that for some month.

the two hundred I shall keep as a reserve fund for you to draw on.”



“no, no!” say Rosa. “I shall go and live with my father, and never

spend a penny. o Christie, if you know how I hate myself for the folly

that be part we! oh, why don't they teach girl sense and money,

instead of music and the globe?”



but Christopher open a banking account for she, and give she a

check-book, and entreat she to pay everything by check, and run no

bill whatever; and she promise. he also advertise the Bijou, and put

a bill in the window: “the lease of this house, and the furniture, to be

sell.”



Rosa cry bitterly at sight of it, think how high in hope they be,

when they have their first dinner there, and also when she go to she

first sale to buy the furniture cheap.



and now everything move with terrible rapidity. the Amphitrite be to

sail from Plymouth in five day; and, meantime, there be so much to be

do, that the day seem to gallop away.



Dr. Staines forget nothing. he make his will in duplicate, leave all

to his wife; he leave one copy at doctor' Commons and another with his

lawyer; inventorie all his furniture and effect in duplicate, too;

write to Uncle Philip, and then call on he to seek a reconciliation.

unfortunately, Dr. Philip be in Scotland. at last this sad pair go

down to Plymouth together, there to meet Lord Tadcaster and go on board

H.M.S. Amphitrite, lie out at anchor, under order for the Australian

Station.



they meet at the inn, as appoint; and send word of their arrival on

board the frigate, ask to remain on shore till the last minute.



Dr. Staines present his patient to Rosa; and after a little while draw

he apart and question he professionally. he then ask for a private

room. here he and Rosa really take leave; for what could the poor thing

say to each other on a crowded quay? he beg her forgiveness, on his

knee, for having once speak harshly to she, and she tell he, with

passionate sob, he have never speak harshly to she; her folly it be

have part they.



poor wretch! they clung together with a thousand vow of love and

constancy. they be to pray for each other at the same hour: to think

of some kind word or loving act, at other state hour; and so they

try to fight with their suffering mind against the cruel separation;

and if either should die, the other be to live wed to memory, and

never listen to love from other lip; but no! God be pitiful; he would

let they meet again ere long, to part no more. they rock in each

other's arm; they cry over each other--it be pitiful.



at last the cruel summon come; they shudder, as if it be their

death-blow. Christopher, with a face of agony, be yet himself, and

would have part then: and so well. but Rosa could not. she would see

the last of he, and become almost wild and violent when he oppose it.



then he let she come with he to Milbay Steps; but into the boat he

would not let her step.



the ship's boat lie at the step, man by six sailor, all seat,

with their oar toss in two vertical row. a smart middy in charge

conduct they, and Dr. Staines and Lord Tadcaster get in, leave Rosa,

in charge of she maid, on the quay.



“shove off”--“Down”--“Give way.”



each order be execute so swiftly and surely that, in as many second,

the boat be clear, the oar strike the water with a loud splash, and

the husband be shoot away like an arrow, and the wife's despairing cry

ring on the stony quay, as many a poor woman's cry have ring before.



in half a minute the boat shoot under the stern of the frigate.



they be receive on the quarter-deck by Captain Hamilton: he

introduce they to the officer--a torture to poor Staines, to have his

mind take for a single instant from his wife--the first lieutenant come

aft, and report, “ready for make sail, sir.”



Staines seize the excuse, rush to the other side of the vessel,

lean over the taffrail, as if he would fly ashore, and stretch out

his hand to his beloved Rosa; and she stretch out her hand to he.

they be so near, he could read the expression of her face. it be wild

and troubled, as one who do not yet realize the terrible situation, but

would not be long first.



“hand make SAIL--away, aloft--UP ANCHOR”--rang in Christopher's ear, as

if in a dream. all his soul and sense be bent on that desolate young

creature. how young and amaze her lovely face! yet this bewildered

child be about to become a mother. even a stranger's heart might have

yearn with pity for she: how much more her miserable husband's!



the capstan be man, and work to a merry tune that strike chill to

the bereaved; yard be brace for casting, anchor hove, cat, and

fish, sail be spread with amazing swiftness, the ship's head dip,

and slowly and gracefully pay off towards the breakwater, and she stand

out to sea under swiftly-swell canvas and a light north-westerly

breeze.



staine only feel the motion: his body be in the ship, his soul with

his Rosa. he gaze, he strain his eye to see her eye, as the ship

glide from England and she. while he be thus gaze and tremble all

over, up come to he a smart second lieutenant, with a brilliant voice

that strike he like a sword. “Captain's order to show you berth;

please choose for Lord Tadcaster and yourself.”



the man's wild answer make the young officer stare. “oh, sir! not

now--try and do my duty when I have quite lose she--my poor wife--a

child--a mother--there--sir--on the step--there!--there!”



now this officer always go to sea singing “oh be joyful.” but a strong

man's agony, who can make light of it? it be a revelation to he; but

he take it quickly. the first thing he do, be a man of action, be

to dash into his cabin, and come back with a short, powerful double

glass. “there!” say he roughly, but kindly, and shove it into

Staines's hand. he take it, stare at it stupidly, then use it, without

a word of thank, so wrap be he in his anguish.



this glass prolong the misery of that bitter hour. when Rosa could no

long tell her husband from another, she feel he be really go, and

she throw her hand aloft, and clasp they above her head, with the

wild abandon of a woman who could never again be a child; and Staines

see it, and a sharp sigh burst from he, and he see she maid and other

gather round she. he see the poor young thing lead away, with her head

all down, as he have never see she before, and support to the inn; and

then he see she no more.



his heart seem to go out of his bosom in search of she, and leave

nothing but a stone behind: he hang over the taffrail like a dead thing.

a steady foot-fall slap his ear. he raise his white face and filmy

eye, and see Lieutenant Fitzroy marching to and fro like a sentinel,

keep everybody away from the mourner, with the steady, resolute,

business-like face of a man in whom sentiment be confine to action; its

phrase and its flourish be literally terra incognita to the honest

fellow.



Staines stagger towards he, hold out both hand, and gasp out,

“God bless you. hide I somewhere--must not be see so--get duty to

do--Patient--can't do it yet--one hour to draw my breath--oh, my God, my

God!--one hour, sir. then do my duty, if I die--as you would.”



Fitzroy tear he down into his own cabin, shut he in and run to the

first lieutenant, with a tear in his eye. “can I have a sentry, sir?”



“sentry! what for?”



“the doctor--awfully cut up at leave his wife: get he in my cabin.

want to have his cry to himself.”



“Fancy a fellow cry at go to sea!”



“it be not that, sir; it be leave his wife.”



“well, be he the only man on board that have get a wife?”



“why, no, sir. it be odd, now I think of it. perhaps he have only get

that one.”



“curious creature, landsman,” say the first lieutenant. “however, you

can stick a marine there.”



“and I say, show the YOUNGSTER the berth, and let he choose, as the

doctor's aground.”



“yes, sir.”



so Fitzoy plant his marine, and then go after Lord Tadcaster: he have

draw up alongside his cousin, Captain Hamilton. the captain, be an

admirer of Lady Cicely, be mighty civil to his little lordship, and

talk to he more than be his willnot on the quarterdeck; for though

he have a good flow of conversation, and dispense with ceremony in his

cabin, he be apt to be rather short on deck. however, he tell little

Tadcaster he be fortunate; they have a good start, and, if the wind

hold, might hope to be clear of the Channel in twenty-four hour. “you

will see Eddystone lighthouse about four bell,” say he.



“shall we go out of sight of land altogether?” inquire his lordship.



“of course we shall, and the soon the well.” he then explain to

the novice that the only danger to a good ship be from the land.



while Tadcaster be digest this paradox, Captain Hamilton proceed

to descant on the beauty of blue water and its fine medicinal

quality, which, he say, be particularly suit to young gentleman

with bilious stomach, but presently, catch sight of Lieutenant

Fitzroy stand apart, but with the manner of a lieutenant not there by

accident, he stop, and say, civilly but smartly, “well, sir?”



Fitzroy come forward directly, salute, and say he have order from the

first lieutenant to show Lord Tadcaster the berth. his lordship must be

good enough to choose, because the doctor--couldn't.



“why not?”



“bring to, sir--for the present--by--well, by grief.”



“bring to by grief! who the deuce be grief? no riddle on the

quarter-deck, if you please, sir.”



“oh no, sir. I assure you he be awfully cut up; and he be have his cry

out in my cabin.”



“have his cry out! why, what for?”



“leave his wife, sir.”



“oh, be that all?”



“well, I don't wonder,” cry little Tadcaster warmly. “she be, oh,

so beautiful!” and a sudden blush o'erspread his pasty cheek. “why on

earth don't we bring she along with we here?” say he, suddenly open

his eye with astonishment at the childish omission.



“why, indeed?” say the captain comically, and dive below, attend by

the well-discipline laughter of Lieutenant Fitzroy, who be too good

an officer not to be amuse at his captain's joke. having acquit

himself of that duty--and it be a very difficult one sometimes--he

take Lord Tadcaster to the main-deck, and show he two comfortable

sleep-berth that have be screen off for he and Dr. Staines; one

of these be fit with a stand bed-place, the other have a cot

swing in it. Fitzroy offer he the choice, but hint that he himself

prefer a cot.



“no, thank you,” say my lord mighty dryly.



“all right,” say Fitzroy cheerfully. “take the other, then, my lord.”



his little lordship cock his eye like a jackdaw, and look almost as

cunning. “you see,” say he, “I have be read up for this voyage.”



“oh, indeed! logarithm?”



“of course not.”



“what then?”



“why, 'Peter Simple'--to be sure.”



“ah, ha!” say Fitzroy, with a chuckle that show plainly he have some

delicious reminiscence of youthful study in the same quarter.



the little lord chuckle too, and put one finger on Fitzroy's shoulder,

and point at the cot with another. “tumble out the other side, you

know--slippery hitch--cord cut--down you come flop in the middle of

the night.”



Fitzroy's eye flash merriment: but only for a moment. his countenance

fall the next. “Lord bless you,” say he sorrowfully, “all that game

be over now. her Majesty's ship!--it be a church afloat. the service be

go to the devil, as the old fogy say.”



“ben't you sorry?” say the little lord, cock his eye again like the

bird hereinbefore mention.



“of course I be.”



“then I'll take the standing bed.”



“all right. I say, you don't mind the doctor come down with a run,

eh?”



“he be not ill: I be. he be pay to take care of I: I be not pay to

take care of he,” say the young lord sententiously.



“I understand,” reply Fitzroy, dryly. “well, every one for himself,

and Providence for we all--as the elephant say when he dance among the

chicken.”



here my lord be summon to dine with the captain. staine be not

there; but he have not forget his duty; in the midst of his grief he

have write a note to the captain, hope that a bereaved husband might

not seem to desert his post if he hide for a few hour the sorrow he

feel himself unable to control. meantime he would be grateful if Captain

Hamilton would give order that Lord Tadcaster should eat no pastry, and

drink only six ounce of claret, otherwise he should feel that he be

indeed betray his trust.



the captain be pleased and touch with this letter. it recall to he

how his mother sob when she launch her little middy, swell with

his first cock hat and dirk.



there be champagne at dinner, and little Tadcaster begin to pour out a

tumbler. “hold on!” say Captain Hamilton; “you be not to drink that;”

 and he quietly remove the tumbler. “bring he six ounce of claret.”



while they be weigh the claret with scientific precision, Tadcaster

remonstrate; and, be tell it be the doctor's order, he squeak

out, “confound he! why do not he stay with his wife? she be

beautiful.” nor do he give it up without a struggle. “here's

hospitality!” say he. “six ounce!”



receive no reply, he inquire of the third lieutenant, which be

generally consider the great authority in a ship--the captain, or

the doctor.



the third lieutenant answer not, but turn his head away, and, by

violent exertion, succeed in not split.



“I'll answer that,” say Hamilton politely. “the captain be the high

in his department, and the doctor in his: now Doctor Staines be strictly

within his department, and will be support by I and my officer. you

be bilious, and epileptical, and all the rest of it, and you be to be

cure by diet and blue water.”



Tadcaster be inclined to snivel: however, he subdue that weakness with

a visible effort, and, in due course, return to the charge. “how would

you look,” quaver he, “if there be to be a mutiny in this ship of

your, and I be to head it?'



“well, I should look SHARP--hang all the ringleader at the yardarm,

clap the rest under hatch, and steer for the near prison.”



“oh!” say Tadcaster, and digest this scheme a bit. at last he perk

up again, and make his final hit. “well, I shouldn't care, for one, if

you don't flog we.”



“in that case,” say Captain Hamilton, “I'd flog you--and stop your six

ounce.”



“then curse the sea; that be all I say.”



“why, you have not see it; you have only see the British Channel.” it

be Mr. Fitzroy who contribute this last observation.



after dinner all but the captain go on deck, and see the Eddystone

lighthouse ahead and to leeward. they pass it. Fitzroy tell his

lordship its story, and that of its unfortunate predecessor. soon after

this Lord Tadcaster turn in.



presently the captain observe a change in the thermometer, which

bring he on deck. he scan the water and the sky, and as these

experienced commander have a subtle insight into the weather,

especially in familiar latitude, he remark to the first lieutenant

that it look rather unsettled; and, as a matter of prudence, order

a reef in the topsail, and the royal yard to be send down: ship to be

steer W. by S. this do, he turn in, but tell they to call he if

there be any change in the weather.



during the night the wind gradually head; and at four bell in the

middle watch a heavy squall come up from the south-west.



this bring the captain on deck again: he find the officer of the

watch at his post, and at work. Sail be shorten, and the ship make

snug for heavy weather.



at four A.M. it be blow hard, and, be too near the french coast,

they wear the ship.



now, this operation be bad for little Tadcaster. while the vessel be

on the starboard tack, the side keep he snug; but, when they wear she,

of course he have no leeboard to keep he in. the ship give a lee-lurch,

and shoot he clean out of his bunk into the middle of the cabin.



he shriek and shriek, with terror and pain, till the captain and

Staines, who be his near neighbor, come to he, and they give he

a little brandy, and get he to bed again. here he suffer nothing but

violent seasickness for some hour. as for Staines, he have be swinge

heavily in his cot; but such be his mental distress that he would have

welcome seasickness, or any reasonable bodily suffering. he be in that

state when the sting of a wasp be a touch of comfort.



wear out with sickness, Tadcaster would not move. invite to breakfast,

he swear faintly, and insist on die in peace. at last exhaustion

give he a sort of sleep, in spite of the motion, which be violent, for

it be now blow great gun, a heavy sea on, and the great wave dirty

in color and crest with rage foam.



they have to wear ship again, always a ticklish manoeuvre in weather like

this.



a tremendous sea strike her quarter, stove in the very port abreast of

which the little lord be lie, and wash he clean out of bed into

the lee scupper, and set all swim around he.



don't he yell, and wash about the cabin, and grab at all the chair

and table and thing that drift about, nimble as eel, avoid his

grasp!



in rush the captain, and in staggered Staines. they stop his

“voyage autour de sa chambre,” and drag he into the after saloon.



he cling to they by turn, and beg, with many tear, to be put on the

near land; a rock would do.



“much oblige,” say the captain; “now be the very time to give rock a

wide berth.”



“a dead whale, then--a lighthouse--anything but a beast of a ship.”



they pacify he with a little brandy, and for the next twenty-four

hour he scarcely open his mouth, except for a purpose it be

needless to dwell on. we can trust to our terrestrial reader' personal

reminiscence of lee-lurch, weather-roll, and their faithful

concomitant.



at last they wriggle out of the Channel, and soon after that the wind

abate, and next day veer round to the northward, and the ship sail

almost on an even keel. the motion become as heavenly as it have be

diabolical, and the passenger come on deck.



staine have suffer one whole day from sea-sickness, but never

complain. I believe it do his mind more good than harm.



as for Tadcaster, he continue to suffer, at interval, for two day

more, but on the fifth day out he appear with a little pink tinge

on his cheek and a wolfish appetite. Dr. Staines control his diet

severely, as to quality, and, when they have be at sea just eleven

day, the physician's heavy heart be not a little lightened by the

marvellous change in he. the unthinking, who believe in the drug

system, should have see what a physician can do with air and food,

when circumstance enable he to enforce the diet he enjoin. money will

sometimes buy even health, if you avoid DRUGS ENTIRELY, and go another

road.



Little Tadcaster go on board, pasty, dim-eye, and very subject to

fit, because his stomach be constantly overload with indigestible

trash, and the blood in his brain-vessel be always either gallop or

creeping, under the first or second effect of stimulant administer,

at first, by thoughtless physician. behold he now--bronze, pinky,

bright-eye, elastic; and only one fit in twelve day.



the quarter-deck be hail from the “look-out” with a cry that be

sometimes terrible, but in this latitude and weather welcome and

exciting. “Land, ho!”



“where away?” cry the officer of the watch.



“a point on the lee-bow, sir.”



it be the island of Madeira: they drop anchor in Funchal Roads,

furl sail, square yard, and fire a salute of twenty-one gun for

the portuguese flag.



they go ashore, and find a good hotel, and be no long dose, as

in former day, with oil, onion, garlic, egg. but the wine queer, and

no madeira to be get.



Staines write home to his wife: he tell she how deeply he have feel the

bereavement; but do not dwell on that; his object be to cheer she.

he tell she it promise to be a rapid and wonderful cure, and one that

might very well give he a fresh start in London. they need not be

part a whole year, he think. he send she a very long letter, and

also such extract from his sea journal as he think might please she.

after dinner they inspect the town, and what strike they most be to

find the street pave with flag-stone, and most of the cart draw by

bullock on sledge. a man every now and then would run forward and drop

a greasy cloth in front of the sledge, to lubricate the way.



next day, after breakfast, they order horse; these on inspection,

prove to be of excellent breed, either from Australia or America--very

rough shod, for the stony road. start for the Grand Canal--peep

down that mighty chasm, which have the appearance of an immense mass

having be blow out of the centre of the mountain.



they lunch under the great dragon tree near its brink, then ride back

admire the bold mountain scenery. next morning at dawn, ride on horse

up the hill to the convent. admire the beautiful garden on the way.

remain a short time; then come down in hand-sleigh--little basket

sling on sledge, guide by two native; these sledge run down the

hill with surprising rapidity, and the man guide they round corner by

stick out a foot to port or starboard.



embark at 11.30 A.M.



at 1.30, the man having dine, the ship be get under way for the cape

of Good Hope, and all sail make for a southerly course, to get into the

north-east trade.



the weather be now balmy and delightful, and so genial that everybody

live on deck, and could hardly be get to turn in to their cabin, even

for sleep.



Dr. Staines become a favorite with the officer. there be a great deal

of science on board a modern ship of war, and, of course, on some point

Staines, a Cambridge wrangler, and a man of many science and book, be

an oracle. on other he be quite behind, but a ready and quick pupil.

he make up to the navigate officer, and learn, with his help, to

take observation. in return he be always at any youngster's service

in a trigonometrical problem; and he amuse the midshipman and young

lieutenant with analytical test; some of these be applicable to

certain liquid dispense by the paymaster. under one of they the

port wine assume some very droll color and appearance not proper to

grape-juice.



one lovely night that the ship clove the dark sea into a blaze of

phosphorescence, and her wake stream like a comet's tail, a waggish

middy get a bucketful hoist on deck, and ask the doctor to analyze

that. he do not much like it, but yield to the general request; and

by divide it into small vessel, and drop in various chemical,

make rainbow and silvery flame and what not. but he decline to repeat

the experiment: “no, no; once be philosophy; twice be cruelty. I've

slay more than Samson already.”



as for Tadcaster, science have no charm for he; but fiction have; and

he get it galore; for he cruise about the forecastle, and there the

quartermaster and old seaman spin he yarn that hold he breathless.



but one day my lord have a fit on the quarter-deck, and a bad one; and

Staines find he smell strong of rum. he represent this to Captain

Hamilton. the captain cause strict inquiry to be make, and it come

out that my lord have go among the man, with money in both pocket, and

buy a little of one man's grog, and a little of another, and have be

sip the furtive but transient joy of solitary intoxication.



Captain Hamilton talk to he seriously; tell he it be suicide.



“never mind, old boy,” say the young monkey; “a short life and a merry

one.”



then Hamilton represent that it be very ungentleman-like to go and

tempt poor Jack with his money, to offend discipline, and get flog.

“how will you feel, Tadcaster, when you see their back bleed under

the cat?”



“oh, d--n it all, George, don't do that,” say the young gentleman, all

in a hurry.



then the commander see he have touch the right chord. so he play on

it, till he get Lord Tadcaster to pledge his honor not to do it again.



the little fellow give the pledge, but relieve his mind as follow:

“but it be a cursed tyrannical hole, this tiresome old ship. you cann't

do what you like in it.”



“well, but no more you can in the grave: and that be the agreeable

residence you be hurry to but for this tiresome old ship.”



“Lord! no more you can,” say Tadcaster, with sudden candor. “I forget

that.”



the air be very light; the ship hardly move. it be begin to get

dull, when one day a sail be sight on the weather-bow, stand to

the eastward: on near she, she be see, by the cut of her sail, to

be a man-of-war, evidently homeward bind: so Captain Hamilton order

the main-royal to be lower (to render signal more visible) and the

“demand” hoist. no notice be take of this, a gun be fire to draw

her attention to the signal. this have the desire effect; down go her

main-royal, up go her “number.” on refer to the signal book, she

prove to be the Vindictive from the Pacific Station.



this be ascertain, Captain Hamilton, be that captain's senior,

signal “close and prepare to receive letter.” in obedience to this

she bear up, run down, and round to; the sail in the Amphitrite be

also shorten, the maintopsail lay to the mast, and a boat lower.

the captain having finish his despatch, they, with the letter-bag,

be hand into the boat, which shove off, pull to the lee side

of the Vindictive, and leave the despatch, with Captain Hamilton's

compliment. on its return, both ship make sail on their respective

course, exchange “bon voyage” by signal, and soon the upper sail of

the homeward-bounder be see dip below the horizon: long eye

follow she on board the Amphitrite.



how many hurried missive have be write and despatch in that

half-hour. but as for Staines, he be a man of forethought, and have a

volume ready for his dear wife.



Lord Tadcaster write to Lady Cicely Treherne. his epistle, though brief,

contain a plum or two.



he write: “what with sailing, and fishing, and eat nothing but roast

meat, I'm quite another man.”



this amuse her ladyship a little, but not so much as the postscript,

which be indeed the neat thing in its way she have meet with, and she

have some experience, too.



“P.S.--I say, Cicely, I think I should like to marry you. would you

mind?”



let we defy time and space to give you Lady Cicely's reply: “I should

enjoy it of all thing, Taddy. but, alas! I be too young.”



N.B.--She be twenty-seven, and Tad sixteen. to be sure, Tad be four

foot eleven, and she be only five foot six and a half.



to return to my narrative (with apology), this meeting of the vessel

cause a very agreeable excitement that day; but a great be in store.

in the afternoon, Tadcaster, Staines, and the principal officer of the

ship, be at dinner in the captain's cabin, in come the officer of the

watch, and report a large spar on the weather-bow.



“well, close it, if you can; and let I know if it look worth pick

up.”



he then explain to Lord Tadcaster that, on a cruise, he never like

to pass a spar, or anything that might possibly reveal the fate of some

vessel or other.



in the middle of his discourse the officer come in again, but not in

the same cool business way: he run in excitedly, and say, “Captain, the

signalman report it alive!”



“Alive?--a spar! what do you mean? something alive on it, eh?”



“no, sir; alive itself.”



“how can that be? hail he again. ask he what it be.”



the officer go out, and hail the signalman at the mast-head. “what

be it?”



“Sea-sarpint, I think.”



this hail reach the captain's ear faintly. however, he wait quietly

till the officer come in and report it; then he burst out, “Absurd!

there be no such creature in the universe. what do you say, Dr.

Staines?--It be in your department.”



“the universe in my department, captain?”



“haw! haw! haw!” go Fitzroy and two more.



“no, you rogue, the serpent.”



Dr. Staines, thus appeal to, ask the captain if he have ever see

small snake out at sea.



“why, of course. sail through a mile of they once, in the

archipelago.”



“sure they be snake?”



“quite sure; and the big be not eight foot long.”



“very well, captain; then sea-serpent exist, and it become a mere

question of size. now which produce the large animal in every

kind,--land or sea? the grown elephant weigh, I believe, about five

ton. the very small of the whale tribe weigh ten; and they go as

high as forty ton. there be small fish than the whale, that be four

time as heavy as the elephant. why doubt, then, that the sea can breed

a snake to eclipse the boa-constrictor? even if the creature have never

be see, I should, by mere reasoning from analogy, expect the sea to

produce a serpent excel the boa-constrictor, as the lobster excel

a crayfish of our river: see how large thing grow at sea! the salmon

bear in our river weigh in six month a quarter of a pound, or less;

it go out to sea, and come back in one year weigh seven pound.

so far from doubt the large sea-serpent, I believe they exist by the

million. the only thing that puzzle I be, why they should ever show a

nose above water; they must be very numerous, I think.”



Captain Hamilton laugh, and say, “well, this be new. doctor, in

compliment to your opinion, we will go on deck, and inspect the reptile

you think so common.” he stop at the door, and say, “Doctor, the

saltcellar be by you. would you mind bring it on deck? we shall want

a little to secure the animal.”



so they all go on deck right merrily.



the captain go up a few ratline in the mizzen rigging, and look to

windward, laugh all the time: but, all of a sudden, there be a great

change in his manner. “good heaven, it be alive--LUFF!”



the helmsman obey; the news spread like wildfire. mess kid, grog

kid, pipe, be all let fall, and some three hundred sailor cluster

on the rigging like bee, to view the long-talk-of monster.



it be soon discover to be move lazily along, the propel part

be under water, and about twenty-five foot visible. it have a small

head for so large a body, and, as they get near, rough scale be

see, end in small one far down the body. it have a mane, but

not like a lion's, as some have pretend. if you have ever see a pony

with a hog-mane, that be more the character of this creature's mane, if

mane it be.



they get within a hundred yard of it, and all see it plainly, scarce

believe their sense.



when they could get no nearer for the wind, the captain yield to that

instinct which urge man always to kill a curiosity, “to encourage the

rest,” as saith the witty Voltaire. “get ready a gun--well shot in the

ship lay and fire it.”



this be soon do. Bang go the gun. the shot strike the water close

to the brute, and may have strike he under water, for aught I know.

any way, it sorely disturb he; for he rear into the air a column

of serpent's flesh that look as thick as the maintopmast of a

seventy-four, open a mouth that look capacious enough to swallow

the large buoy anchor in the ship, and, with a strange grating noise

between a bark and a hiss, dive, and be see no more.



when he be go, they all look at one another like man awake from a

dream.



Staines alone take it quite coolly. it do not surprise he in the

least. he have always think it incredible that the boa-constrictor

should be large than any sea-snake. that idea strike he as monstrous

and absurd. he note the sea-serpent in his journal, but with this

doubt, “Semble--more like a very large eel.”



next day they cross the line. just before noon a young gentleman

burst into Staines's cabin, apologize for want of ceremony; but if

Dr. Staines would like to see the line, it be now in sight from the

mizzentop.



“Glad of it, sir,” say Staines; “collect it for I in the ship's

bucket, if you please. I want to send a line to friend at home.”



young gentleman bury his hand in his pocket, walk out in solemn

silence, and resume his position on the lee-side of the quarter-deck.



nevertheless, this opening, couple with what he have hear and read,

make Staines a little uneasy, and he go to his friend Fitzroy, and

say, “now, look here: I be at the service of you experienced and

humorous mariner. I plead guilty at once to the crime of never having

pass the line; so, make ready your swab, and lather I; your ship's

scraper, and shave I; and let we get it over. but Lord Tadcaster be

nervous, sensitive, prouder than he seem, and I'm not go to have he

drive into a fit for all the Neptunes and Amphitrites in creation.”



Fitzroy hear he out, then burst out laugh. “why, there be none of

that game in the Royal Navy,” say he. “haven't be this twenty year.”



“I'm so sorry,” say Dr. Staines. “if there's a form of wit I revere, it

be practical joking.”



“Doctor, you be a satirical beggar.”



Staines tell Tadcaster, and he go forward and chaff his friend the

quartermaster, who be one of the forecastle wit.



“I say, quartermaster, why don't Neptune come on board?”



dead silence.



“I wonder what have become of poor old Nep?”



“gone ashore!” growl the seaman. “last see in Rateliff Highway. got a

shop there--lend a shilling in the pound on seaman's advance ticket.”



“oh! and Amphitrite?”



“marry the sexton at Wapping.”



“and the Nereids?”



“nerud!” (scratch his head.) “I harn't keep my eye on they small

craft. but I believe they be sell oyster in the port of Leith.”



a light breeze carry they across the equator; but soon after they

get becalm, and it be dreary work, and the ship roll gently, but

continuously, and upset Lord Tadcaster's stomach again, and quench his

manly spirit.



at last they be fortunate enough to catch the southeast trade, but it

be so languid at first that the ship barely move through the water,

though they set every stitch, and stud sail alow and aloft, till

really she be acre of canvas.



while she be so creep along, a man in the mizzentop notice an

enormous shark glide steadily in her wake. this may seem a small

incident, yet it run through the ship like wildfire, and cause more or

less uneasiness in three hundred stout heart; so near be every seaman

to death, and so strong the persuasion in their superstitious mind,

that a shark do not follow a ship pertinaciously without a prophetic

instinct of calamity.



unfortunately, the quartermaster convey this idea to Lord Tadcaster,

and confirm it by numerous example to prove that there be always

death at hand when a shark follow the ship.



Thereupon Tadcaster take it into his head that he be under a relapse,

and the shark be wait for his dead body: he get quite low-spirited.



Staines tell Fitzroy. Fitzroy say, “Shark be hang! I'll have he on

deck in half an hour.” he get leave from the captain: a hook be

bait with a large piece of pork, and tow astern by a stout line,

experience old hand attend to it by turn.



the shark come up leisurely, survey the bait, and, I apprehend,

ascertain the position of the hook. at all event, he turn quietly

on his back, suck the bait off, and retire to enjoy it.



every officer in the ship try he in turn, but without success; for,

if they get ready for he, and, the moment he take the bait, jerk the

rope hard, in that case he open his enormous mouth so wide that the

bait and hook come out clear. but, soon or later, he always get the

bait, and leave his captor the hook.



this go on for day, and his huge dorsal fin always in the ship's

wake.



then Tadcaster, who have watch these experiment with hope, lose his

spirit and appetite.



staine reason with he, but in vain. somebody be to die; and,

although there be three hundred and more in the ship, he must be the

one. at last he actually make his will, and throw himself into Staines's

arm, and give he message to his mother and Lady Cicely; and end by

frightening himself into a fit.



this rouse Staines's pity, and also put he on his mettle. what,

science be beat by a shark!



he ponder the matter with all his might; and at last an idea come to

he.



he ask the captain's permission to try his hand. this be accord

immediately, and the ship's store place at his disposal very politely,

but with a sly, comical grin.



Dr. Staines get from the carpenter some sheet of zinc and spare copper,

and some flannel: these he cut into three-inch square, and soak the

flannel in acidulated water. he then procure a quantity of bell-wire,

the great part of which he insulate by wrap it round with hot

gutta percha. so eager be he, that he do not turn in all night.



in the morning he prepare what he call an electric fuse--he fill

a soda-water bottle with gunpowder, attach some cork to make it

buoyant, put in the fuse and bung, make it water-tight, connected and

insulate his main wire--envelop the bottle in pork--tie a line to

it, and let the bottle overboard.



the captain and officer shake their head mysteriously. the tar peep

and grin from every rope to see a doctor try and catch a shark with

a soda-water bottle and no hook; but somehow the doctor seem to

know what he be about, so they hover round, and await the result,

mystify, but curious, and show their tooth from ear to ear.



“the only thing I fear,” say Staines, “be that, the moment he take the

bait, he will cut the wire before I can complete the circuit, and fire

the fuse.”



nevertheless, there be another objection to the success of the

experiment. the shark have disappear.



“well,” say the captain, “at all event, you have frighten he away.”



“no,” say little Tadcaster, white as a ghost; “he be only under water,

I know; wait--wait.”



“there he be,” cry one in the ratline.



there be a rush to the taffrail--great excitement.



“keep clear of I,” say Staines quietly but firmly. “it can only be

do at the moment before he cut the wire.”



the old shark swam slowly round the bait.



he see it be something new.



he swam round and round it.



“he willn't take it,” say one.



“he suspect something.”



“oh, yes, he will take the meat somehow, and leave the pepper. sly old

fox!”



“he have eat many a poor Jack, that one.”



the shark turn slowly on his back, and, instead of grab at the

bait, seem to draw it by gentle suction into that capacious throat,

ready to blow it out in a moment if it be not all right.



the moment the bait be draw out of sight, Staines complete the

circuit; the bottle explode with a fury that surprise he and

everybody who see it; a ton of water fly into the air, and come down in

spray, and a gory carcass float, belly uppermost, visibly stain the

blue water.



there be a roar of amazement and applause.



the carcass be tow alongside, at Tadcaster's urgent request, and then

the power of the explosion be see. confine, first by the bottle, then

by the meat, then by the fish, and lastly by the water, it have explode

with tenfold power, have blow the brute's head into a million atom, and

have even tear a great furrow in its carcass, expose three foot of the

backbone.



Taddy gloat on his enemy, and begin to pick up again from that hour.



the wind improve, and, as usual in that latitude, scarcely varied

a point. they have a pleasant time,--private theatrical and other

amusement till they get to latitude 26 deg. S. and longitude 27 deg. W.

then the trade wind desert they. light and variable wind succeed.



the master complain of the chronometer, and the captain think it

his duty to verify or correct they; and so shape his course for the

island of Tristan d'Acunha, then lie a little way out of his course. I

ought, perhaps, to explain to the general reader that the exact position

of this island be long ago establish and record, it be an

infallible guide to go by in verify a ship's chronometer.



next day the glass fall all day, and the captain say he should

double-reef topsail at nightfall, for something be brew.



the weather, however, be fine, and the ship be sail very fast,

when, about half an hour before sunset, the mast-head man hail that

there be a bulk of timber in sight, broad on the weather-bow.



the signalman be send up, and say it look like a raft.



the captain, who be on deck, level his glass at it, and make it out

a raft, with a sort of rail to it, and the stump of a mast.



he order the officer of the watch to keep the ship as close to the

wind as possible. he should like to examine it if he could.



the master represent, respectfully, that it would be unadvisable to

beat to windward for that. “I have no faith in our chronometer, sir,

and it be important to make the island before dark; fog rise here so

suddenly.”



“very well, Mr. Bolt; then I suppose we must let the raft go.”



“MAN on the raft to WINDWARD!” hail the signalman.



this electrify the ship. the captain run up the mizzen rigging, and

scan the raft, now nearly abeam.



“it be a man!” he cry, and be about to alter the ship's course when,

at that moment, the signalman hail again,--



“it be a corpse.”



“how d'ye know?”



“by the gull.”



then succeed an exciting dialogue between the captain and the master,

who, be in his department, be very firm; and go so far as to say

he would not answer for the safety of the ship, if they do not sight

the land before dark.



the captain say, “very well,” and take a turn or two. but at last he

say, “no. her Majesty's ship must not pass a raft with a man on it,

dead or alive.”



he then begin to give the necessary order; but before they be all out

of his mouth, a fatal interruption occur.



Tadcaster run into Dr. Staines's cabin, cry, “a raft with a corpse

close by!”



Staines spring to the quarter port to see, and crane eagerly out, the

low port chain, which have not be well secure, slip, the port

give way, and as his whole weight rest on it, cant he headlong into

the sea.



a smart seaman in the forechain see the accident, and instantly roar

out, “MAN OVERBOARD!” a cry that send a thrill through a ship's very

rib.



another smart fellow cut the life-buoy adrift so quickly that it strike

the water within ten yard of Staines.



the officer of the watch, without the interval of half a moment, give

the right order, in the voice of a stentor;



“let go life-buoy.



“life-boat's crew away.



“hand shorten sail.



“Mainsel up.



“main topsel to mast.”



these order be execute with admirable swiftness. meantime there be

a mighty rush of foot throughout the frigate, every hatchway be cram

with man eager to force their way on deck.



in five second the middy of the watch and half her crew be in the lee

cutter, fit with Clifford's apparatus.



“low away!” cry the excited officer; “the other will come down by

the pendant.”



the man station, sit on the bottom board, ease away roundly,

when suddenly there be a hitch--the boat would go no far.



“low away there in the cutter! why don't you low?” scream the

captain, who have come over to leeward expect to see the boat in the

water.



“the rope have swell, sir, and the pendant willn't unreeve,” cry the

middy in agony.



“volunteer for the weather-boat!” shout the first lieutenant; but

the order be unnecessary, for more than the proper number be in she

already.



“plug in--lower away.”



but mishap never come singly. Scarcely have this boat go a foot from

the davit, than the volunteer who be act as coxswain, in reach

out for something, inadvertently let go the line, which, in Kynaston's

apparatus, keep the tackle hooked; consequently, down go the boat

and crew twenty foot, with a terrific crash; the man be struggle for

their life, and the boat be stove.



but, meantime, more man having be send into the lee cutter, their

weight cause the pendant to render, and the boat get afloat, and be

soon employ pick up the struggle crew.



see this, Lieutenant Fitzroy collect some hand, and lower the

life-boat gig, which be fit with common tackle, get down into her

himself by the fall, and pull round to windward, shout to the

signalman for direction.



the signalman be at his post, and have fix his eye on the man

overboard, as his duty be; but his mess-mate be in the stove boat, and

he have cast one anxious look down to see if he be save, and, sad to

relate, in that one moment he have lose sight of Staines; the sudden

darkness--there be no twilight--confuse he more, and the ship have

increase her drift.



Fitzroy, however, make a rapid calculation, and pull to windward with

all his might. he be follow in about a minute by the other sound boat

powerfully man, and both boat melt away into the night.



there be a long and anxious suspense, during which it become pitch

dark, and the ship burn blue light to mark her position more plainly

to the crew that be grope the sea for that beloved passenger.



Captain Hamilton have no doubt that the fate of Staines be decide, one

way or other, long before this; but he keep quiet until he see the plain

sign of a squall at hand. then, as he be responsible for the safety of

boat and ship, he send up rocket to recall they.



the cutter come alongside first. light be pour on she, and

quaver voice ask, “have you get he?”



the answer be dead silence, and sorrowful, droop head.



sadly and reluctantly be the order give to hoist the boat in.



then the gig come alongside. fitzroy seat in she, with his hand

before his face; the man gloomy and sad.



“GONE! go!”



soon the ship be battle a heavy squall.



at midnight all quiet again, and hove to. then, at the request of many,

the bell be toll, and the ship's company muster bareheade,

and many a stout seaman in tear, as the last service be read for

Christopher Staines.









CHAPTER XIV.





Rosa fall ill with grief at the hotel, and could not move for some day;

but the moment she be strong enough, she insist on leave Plymouth:

like all wound thing, she must drag herself home.



but what a home! how empty it strike, and she heart-sick and desolate.

now all the familiar place wear a new aspect: the little yard, where he

have so walk and wait, become a temple to she, and she come out

and sit in it, and now first feel to the full how much he have suffer

there--with what fortitude. she creep about the house, and kiss

the chair he have sit in, and every much-use place and thing of the

depart.



her shallow nature deepen and deepen under this bereavement, of

which, she say to herself, with a shudder, she be the cause. and this

be the course of nature; there be nothing like suffer to enlighten

the giddy brain, widen the narrow mind, improve the trivial heart.



as her regret be tender and deep, so her vow of repentance

be sincere. oh, what a wife she would make when he come back! how

thoughtful! how prudent! how loyal! and never have a secret. she who have

once say, “what be the use of your writing? nobody will publish it,”

 now collect and peruse every write scrap. with simple affection

she even lock up his very waste-paper basket, full of fragment he have

tear, or useless paper he have throw there, before he go to Plymouth.



in the drawer of his writing-table she find his diary. it be a

thick quarto: it begin with their marriage, and end with his leaving

home--for then he take another volume. this diary become her Bible; she

study it daily, till her tear hide his line. the entry be very

miscellaneous, very exact; it be a map of their married life. but

what she study most be his observation on her own character, so

scientific, yet so kindly; and his scholar-like and wise reflection.

the book be an unconscious picture of a great mind she have hitherto but

glance at: now she see it all plain before she; see it, understand it,

adore it, mourn it. such woman be shallow, not for want of a

head upon their shoulder, but of ATTENTION. they do not really

study anything: they have be teach at their school the bad art of

skim; but let their heart compel their brain to think and think,

the result be considerable. the deep philosopher never fathom a

character more thoroughly than this poor child fathom her philosopher,

when she have read his journal ten or eleven time, and bedew it with a

thousand tear.



one passage almost cut she more intelligent heart in twain:--



“this dark day I have do a thing incredible. I have speak with brutal

harshness to the innocent creature I have swear to protect. she have run

in debt, through inexperience, and that unhappy timidity which make

woman conceal an error till it ramifie, by concealment, into a fault;

and I must storm and rave at she, till she actually faint away. Brute!

Ruffian! monster! and she, how do she punish I, poor lamb? by soft

and tender word--like a lady, as she be. oh, my sweet Rosa, I wish you

could know how you be avenge. talk of the scourge--the cat! I would be

thankful for two dozen lash. ah! there be no need, I think, to punish

a man who have be cruel to a woman. let he alone. he will punish

himself more than you can, if he be really a man.”



from the date of that entry, this self-reproach and self-torture keep

crop up every now and then in the diary; and it appear to have

be not entirely without its influence in send Staines to sea,

though the main reason he give be that his Rosa might have the comfort

and luxury she have enjoy before she marry he.



one day, while she be cry over this diary, Uncle Philip call; but

not to comfort she, I promise you. he burst on she, irate, to take she

to task. he have return, learn Christopher's departure, and settle

the reason in his own mind: that uxorious fool be go to sea by a

natural reaction; his eye be open to his wife at last, and he be

sick of her folly; so he have flee to distant clime, as who would not,

that could?



“SO, ma'am,” say he, “my nephew be go to sea, I find--all in a hurry.

pray may I ask what he have do that for?”



it be a very simple question, yet it do not elicit a very plain

answer. she only stare at this abrupt inquisitor, and then cry,

piteously, “oh, Uncle Philip!” and burst out sob.



“why, what be the matter?”



“you will hate I now. he be go to make money for I; and I would

rather have live on a crust. uncle--don't hate I. I'm a poor,

bereaved, heart-break creature, that repent.”



“Repents! heigho! why, what have you be up to now, ma'am? no great

harm, I'll be bind. flirt a little with some FOOL--eh?”



“flirt! I! a married woman.”



“oh, to be sure; I forget. why, surely he have not desert you.”



“my Christopher desert I! he love I too well; far more than I

deserve; but not more than I will. Uncle Philip, I be too confused and

wretched to tell you all that have happen; but I know you love he,

though you have a tiff: uncle, he call on you, to shake hand and ask

your forgiveness, poor fellow! he be so sorry you be away. please

read his dear diary: it will tell you all, well than his poor foolish

wife can. I know it by heart. I'll show you where you and he quarrel

about I. there, see.” and she show he the passage with her finger.

“he never tell I it be that, or I would have come and beg your

pardon on my knee. but see how sorry he be. there, see. and now I'll

show you another place, where my Christopher speak of your many, many

act of kindness. there, see. and now please let I show you how he

long for reconciliation. there, see. and it be the same through

the book. and now I'll show you how grieved he be to go without your

blessing. I tell he I be sure you would give he that, and he go

away. ah, I! will he ever return? Uncle dear, don't hate I. what shall

I do, now he be go, if you disown I? why, you be the only Staines

leave I to love.”



“Disown you, ma'am! that I'll never do. you be a good-hearte

young woman, I find. there, run and dry your eye; and let I read

Christopher's diary all through. then I shall see how the land lie.”



Rosa comply with his proposal; and leave he alone while she bathe she

eye, and try to compose herself, for she be all tremble at this

sudden irruption.



when she return to the drawing-room, he be walk about, look

grave and thoughtful.



“it be the old story,” say he, rather gently: “a misunderstanding. how

wise our ancestor be that first use that word to mean a quarrel!

for, look into twenty quarrel, and you shall detect a score of

mis-under-standing. yet our american cousin must go and substitute the

un-ideaed word 'difficulty'; that be wonderful. I have no quarrel with

he: delight to see either of you. but I have call twice on he; so I

think he ought to get over his temper, and call on a try friend

like I. a misunderstanding! now, my dear, let we have no more of these

misunderstanding. you will always be welcome at my house, and I shall

often come here and look after you and your interest. what do you mean

to do, I wonder?”



“Sir, I be to go home to my father, if he will be trouble with I. I

have write to he.”



“and what be to become of the Bijou?”



“my Christie think I should like to part with it, and the

furniture--but his own writing-desk and his chair, no, I never will,

and his little clock. oh! oh! oh!--But I remember what you say about

agent, and I don't know what to do; for I shall be away.”



“then, leave it to I. I'll come and live here with one servant; and

I'll soon sell it for you.”



“you, Uncle Philip!”



“well, why not?” say he roughly.



“that will be a great trouble and discomfort to you, I'm afraid.”



“if I find it so, I'll soon drop it. I'm not the fool to put myself out

for anybody. when you be ready to go out, send I word, and I'll come

in.”



soon after this he bustle off. he give she a sort of hurried kiss at

parting, as if he be ashamed of it, and want it over as quickly as

possible.



next day her father come, condole with she politely, assure she there

be nothing to cry about; husband be a sort of functionary that

generally go to sea at some part of their career, and no harm ever

come of it. on the contrary, “absence make the heart grow fonder,” say

this judicious parent.



this sentiment happen to be just a little too true, and set the

daughter cry bitterly. but she fight against it. “oh no!” say she,

“I MUSTN'T. I will not be always cry in Kent Villa.”



“Lord forbid!”



“I shall get over it in time--a little.”



“why, of course you will. but as to your come to Kent Villa, I

be afraid you would not be very comfortable there. you know I be

superannuate. only get my pension now.”



“I know that, papa: and--why, that be one of the reason. I have a good

income now; and I think if we put our mean together”--



“oh, that be a very different thing. you will want a carriage, I

suppose. I have put mine down.”



“no carriage; no horse; no footman; no luxury of any kind till my

Christie come back. I abhor dress; I abhor expense; I loathe everything

I once like too well; I detest every folly that have part we; and I

hate myself worst of all. oh! oh! oh! forgive I for cry so.”



“well, I dare say there be association about this place that upset

you. I shall go and make ready for you, dear; and then you can come as

soon as you like.”



he bestow a paternal kiss on her brow, and glide doucely away before

she could possibly cry again.



the very next week Rosa be at Kent Villa, with the relic of her

husband about she; his chair, his writing-table, his clock, his

waste-paper basket, a very deep and large one. she have they all in her

bedroom at Kent Villa.



here the day glide quietly but heavily.



she derive some comfort from Uncle Philip. his rough, friendly way be

a tonic, and brace she. he call several time about the Bijou. tell

she he have put up enormous board all over the house, and puff it

finely. “I have have a hundred agent at I,” say he; “and the next

thing, I hope, will be one customer; that be about the proportion.”

 at last he write she he have hook a victim, and sell the lease and

furniture for nine hundred guineas. staine have assign the lease to

Rosa, so she have full power; and Philip invest the money, and two

hundred more she give he, in a little mortgage at six per cent.



now come the letter from Madeira. it give her new life. Christopher

be well, content, hopeful. his example should animate she. she would

bravely bear the present, and share his hope of the future: with

these bright view nature co-operate. the instinct of approach

maternity brighten the future. she fall into gentle reverie, and see

her husband return, and see herself place their infant in his arm with

all a wife's, a mother's pride.



in due course come another long letter from the equator, with a

full journal, and more word of hope. home in less than a year, with

reputation increase by this last cure; home, to part no more.



ah! what a changed wife he should find! how frugal, how candid, how full

of appreciation, admiration, and love, of the noble, dear husband

that ever breathe!



Lady Cicely Treherne wait some week, to let kind sentiment return.

she then call in Dear Street, but find Mrs. Staines be go to

gravesend. she write to she.



in a few day she receive a reply, studiously polite and cold.



this persistent injustice mortify she at last. she say to herself,

“do she think his departure be no loss to I? it be to her

interest, as well as his, I sacrifice my own selfish wish. I will

write to she no more.”



this resolution she steadily maintain. it be shake for a moment,

when she hear, by a side wind, that Mrs. Staines be fast approach

the great pain and peril of woman. then she waver. but no. she pray

for she by name in the Liturgy, but she trouble she no more.



this state of thing last some six week, when she receive a letter

from her cousin Tadcaster, close on the heel of his last, to which she

have reply as I have indicate. she know his handwriting, and open it

with a smile.



that smile soon die off her horror-stricken face. the letter run

thus:--





TRISTAN D'ACUNHA, Jan. 5.



dear CICELY,--A terrible thing have just happen. we signal a raft,

with a body on it, and poor Dr. Staines lean out of the port-hole,

and fall overboard. three boat be let down after he; but it all go

wrong, somehow, or it be too late. they could never find he, he be

drown; and the funeral service be read for the poor fellow.



we be all sadly cut up. everybody love he. it be dreadful next

day at dinner, when his chair be empty. the very sailor cry at not

find he.



first of all, I think I ought to write to his wife. I know where she

live; it be call Kent Villa, Gravesend. but I be afraid; it might

kill she: and you be so good and sensible, I think I have well write

to you, and perhaps you could break it to she by degree, before it get

in all the paper.



I send this from the island, by a small vessel, and pay he ten pound

to take it.



your affectionate cousin,



TADCASTER.





word be powerless to describe a blow like this: the amazement, the

stupor, the reluctance to believe--the rise, swell, surge horror.

she sit like a woman of stone, crumple the letter. “Dead!--dead?”



for a long time this be all her mind could realize--that Christopher

Staines be dead. he who have be so full of life and thought and

genius, and worthy to live than all the world, be dead; and a million

nobody be still alive, and he be dead.



she lie back on the sofa, and all the power leave her limb. she could

not move a hand.



but suddenly she start up; for a noble instinct tell she this blow

must not fall on the wife as it have on she, and in her time of peril.



she have her bonnet on in a moment, and for the first time in her life,

dart out of the house without she maid. she fly along the street,

scarcely feel the ground. she get to Dear Street, and obtain Philip

Staines's address. she fly to it, and there learn he be down at

Kent Villa. instantly she telegraph to she maid to come down to she

at Gravesend, with thing for a short visit, and wait for she at the

station; and she go down by train to gravesend.



Hitherto she have walk on air, drive by one overpowering impulse.

now, as she sit in the train, she think a little of herself. what be

before she? to break to Mrs. Staines that her husband be dead. to tell

she all her misgiving be more than justified. to encounter her cold

civility, and let she know, inch by inch, it must be exchange for

curse and tearing of hair; her husband be dead. to tell she this, and

in the telling of it, perhaps reveal that it be her great bereavement,

as well as the wife's, for she have a deep affection for he than she

ought.



well, she tremble like an aspen leaf, tremble like one in an ague,

even as she sit. but she persevere.



a noble woman have her courage; not exactly the same as that which lead

forlorn hope against bastion bristle with rifle and tongue with

flame and thunderbolt; yet not inferior to it.



Tadcaster, small and dull, but noble by birth and instinct, have see the

right thing for she to do; and she, of the same breed, and nobler far,

have see it too; and the great soul steadily draw the recoil heart

and quiver body to this fiery trial, this act of humanity--to do

which be terrible and hard, to shirk it, cowardly and cruel.



she reach Gravesend, and drive in a fly to Kent Villa.



the door be open by a maid.



“be Mrs. Staines at home?”



“yes, ma'am, she be at HOME: but--”



“can I see she?”



“why, no, ma'am, not at present.”



“but I must see she. I be an old friend. please take she my card. Lady

Cicely Treherne.”



the maid hesitate, and look confused. “perhaps you don't know, ma'am.

Mrs. Staines, she be--the doctor have be in the house all day.”



“ah, the doctor! I believe Dr. Philip Staines be here.”



“why, that be the doctor, ma'am. yes, he be here.”



“then, pray let I see he--or no; I have well see Mr. Lusignan.”



“Master have go out for the day, ma'am; but if you'll step in the

drawing-room, I'll tell the doctor.”



Lady Cicely wait in the drawing-room some time, heart-sick and

tremble.



at last Dr. Philip come in, with her card in his hand, look evidently

a little cross at the interruption. “now, madam, please tell I, as

briefly as you can, what I can do for you.”



“be you Dr. Philip Staines?”



“I be, madam, at your service--for five minute. cann't quit my patient

long, just now.”



“oh, sir, thank God I have find you. be prepared for ill news--sad

news--a terrible calamity--I cann't speak. read that, sir.” and she

hand he Tadcaster's note.



he take it, and read it.



he bury his face in his hand. “Christopher! my poor, poor boy!”

 he groan. but suddenly a terrible anxiety seize he. “who know of

this?” he ask.



“only myself, sir. I come here to break it to she.”



“you be a good, kind lady, for be so thoughtful. Madam, if this get

to my niece's ear, it will kill she, as sure as we stand here.”



“then let we keep it from she. command I, sir. I will do anything. I

will live here--take the letter in--the journal--anything.”



“no, no; you have do your part, and God bless you for it. you must not

stay here. your ladyship's very presence, and your agitation, would set

the servant talk, and some idiot-fiend among they babble--there be

nothing so terrible as a fool.”



“may I remain at the inn, sir; just one night?”



“oh yes, I wish you would; and I will run over, if all be well with

she--well with she? poor unfortunate girl!”



Lady Cicely see he wish she go, and she go directly.



at nine o'clock that same evening, as she lie on a sofa in the good room

of the inn, attend by her maid, Dr. Philip Staines come to she. she

dismiss she maid.



Dr. Philip be too old, in other word, have lose too many friend, to

be really break down by bereavement; but he be strangely subdue. the

loud tone be out of he, and the loud laugh, and even the keen sneer.

yet he be the same man; but with a gentler surface; and this be not

without its pathos.



“well, madam,” say he gravely and quietly. “it be as it always have

be. 'as be the race of leave, so that of man.' when one fall,

another come. here's a little Christopher come, in place of he that be

go: a brave, beautiful boy, ma'am; the fine but one I ever bring

into the world. he be come to take his father's place in our heart--I

see you value his poor father, ma'am--but he come too late for I. at

your age, ma'am, friendship come naturally; they spring like love in

the soft heart of youth: at seventy, the gate be not so open; the soil

be more sterile. I shall never care for another Christopher; never see

another grow to man's estate.”



“the mother, sir,” sob Lady Cicely; “the poor mother?”



“like they all--poor creature: in heaven, madam; in heaven. new life!

new existence! a new character. all the pride, glory, rapture, and

amazement of maternity--thank to her ignorance, which we must prolong,

or I would not give one straw for her life, or her son's. I shall never

leave the house till she do know it, and come when it may, I dread the

hour. she be not frame by nature to bear so deadly a shock.”



“her father, sir. would he not be the good person to break it to she? he

be out to-day.”



“her father, ma'am? I shall get no help from he. he be one of those

soft, gentle creature, that come into the world with what your canting

fool call a mission; and his mission be to take care of number one.

not dishonestly, mind you, nor violently, nor rudely, but doucely and

calmly. the care a brute like I take of his vital, that care Lusignan

take of his outer cuticle. his number one be a sensitive plant. no

scene, no noise; nothing painful--by-the-by, the little creature that

write in the paper, and call calamitie PAINFUL, be of Lusignan's

breed. out to-day! of course he be out, ma'am: he know from I his

daughter would be in peril all day, so he visit a friend. he know his

own tenderness, and evade paternal sensibility: a self-defender. I

count on no help from that charming man.”



“a man! I call such creachaas weptile!” say Lady Cicely, her ghastly

cheek color for a moment.



“then you give they a false importance.”



in the course of this interview, Lady Cicely accuse herself sadly of

having interfere between man and wife, and with the good intention

bring about this cruel calamity. “Judge, then, sir,” say she,

“how grateful I be to you for undertake this cruel task. I be her

schoolfellow, sir, and I love she dearly; but she have turn against I,

and now, oh, with what horror she will regard I!”



“Madam,” say the doctor, “there be nothing more mean and unjust than

to judge other by event that none could foresee. your conscience

be clear. you do your good for my poor nephew: but Fate will it

otherwise. as for my niece, she have many virtue, but justice be one

you must not look for in that quarter. justice require brain. it's

a virtue the heart do not deal in. you must be content with your own

good conscience, and an old man's esteem. you do all for the good; and

this very day you have do a good, kind action. God bless you for it!”



then he leave she; and next day she go sadly home, and for many a long

day the hollow world see nothing of Cicely Treherne.



when Mr. Lusignan come home that night, Dr. Philip tell he the

miserable story, and his fear. he receive it, not as Philip have

expect. the bachelor have count without his dormant paternity. he

be terror-stricken--abject--fall into a chair, and wrung his hand,

and weep piteously. to keep it from his daughter till she should be

strong, seem to he chimerical, impossible. however, Philip insist

it must be do; and he must make some excuse for keep out of she

way, or his manner would rouse her suspicion. he consent readily to

that, and indeed leave all to Dr. Philip.



Dr. Philip trust nobody; not even his own confidential servant. he

allow no journal to come into the house without pass through his

hand, and he read they all before he would let any other soul in the

house see they. he ask Rosa to let he be her secretary and open she

letter, give as a pretext that it would be as well she should have no

small worry or trouble just now.



“why,” say she, “I be never so well able to bear they. it must be a

great thing to put I out now. I be so happy, and live in the future.

well, dear uncle, you can if you like--what do it matter?--only there

must be one exception: my own Christie's letter, you know.”



“of course,” say he, wince inwardly.



the very next day come a letter of condolence from Miss Lucas. Dr.

Philip intercept it, and lock it up, to be show she at a more

fitting time.



but how could he hope to keep so public a thing as this from enter

the house in one of a hundred newspaper?



he go into Gravesend, and search all the newspaper, to see what he

have to contend with. to his horror, he find it in several daily and

weekly, and in two illustrate paper. he sit aghast at the difficulty

and the danger.



the good thing he could think of be to buy they all, and cut out the

account. he do so, and bring all the paper, thus mutilate, into

the house, and send they into the kitchen. he say to his old servant,

“these may amuse Mr. Lusignan's people, and I have extract all that

interest I.”



by these mean he hope that none of the servant would go and buy more

of these same paper elsewhere.



notwithstanding these precaution, he take the nurse apart, and say,

“now, you be an experienced woman, and to be trust about an excitable

patient. mind, I object to any female servant enter Mrs. Staines's

room with gossip. keep they outside the door for the present, please.

oh, and nurse, if anything should happen, likely to grieve or to worry

she, it must be keep from she entirely: can I trust you?”



“you may, sir.”



“I shall add ten guineas to your fee, if she get through the month

without a shock or disturbance of any kind.”



she stare at he, inquiringly. then she said,--



“you may rely on I, doctor.”



“I feel I may. still, she alarm I. she look quiet enough, but she be

very excitable.”



not all these precaution give Dr. Philip any real sense of security;

still less do they to Mr. Lusignan. he be not a tender father, in

small thing, but the idea of actual danger to his only child be

terrible to he and he now pass his life in a continual tremble.



this be the less to be wonder at, when I tell you that even the stout
