Philip begin to lose his nerve, his appetite, his sleep, under this

hourly terror and this hourly torture.



well do the great imagination of antiquity feign a torment, too great

for the mind long to endure, in the sword of Damocles suspend by

a single hair over his head. here the sword hang over an innocent

creature, who smile beneath it, fearless; but these two old man must

sit and watch the sword, and ask themselves how long before that subtle

salvation shall snap.



“Ill news travel fast,” say the proverb. “the bird of the air shall

carry the matter,” say Holy Writ; and it be so. no bolt nor bar, no

promise nor precaution, can long shut out a great calamity from the

ear it be to blast, the heart it be to wither. the very air seem full

of it, until it fall.



Rosa's child be more than a fortnight old; and she be look more

beautiful than ever, as be often the case with a very young mother, and

Dr. Philip compliment she on her look. “now,” say he, “you reap the

advantage of be good, and obedient, and keep quiet. in another ten

day or so, I may take you to the seaside for a week. I have the honor

to inform you that from about the fourth to the tenth of March there be

always a week of fine weather, which take everybody by surprise, except

I. it do not astonish I, because I observe it be invariable. now,

what would you say if I give you a week at Herne Bay, to set you up

altogether?”



“as you please, dear uncle,” say Mrs. Staines, with a sweet smile. “I

shall be very happy to go, or to stay. I shall be happy everywhere, with

my darling boy, and the thought of my husband. why, I count the day

till he shall come back to I. no, to we; to we, my pet. how dare a

naughty mammy say to 'I,' as if 'I' be half the 'portance of oo, a

precious pet!”



Dr. Philip be surprise into a sigh.



“what be the matter, dear?” say Rosa, very quickly.



“the matter?”



“yes, dear, the matter. you sigh; you, the laugh philosopher.”



“do I?” say he, to gain time. “perhaps I remember the uncertainty of

human life, and of all mortal hope. the old will have their thought,

my dear. they have see so much trouble.”



“but, uncle dear, he be a very healthy child.”



“very.”



“and you tell I yourself carelessness be the cause so many child

die.”



“that be true.”



she give he a curious and rather search look; then, lean over

her boy, say, “Mammy's not afraid. beautiful Pet be not bear to die

directly. he will never leave his mam-ma. no, uncle, he never can. for

my life be bind in his and his dear father's. it be a triple cord: one

go, go all.”



she say this with a quiet resolution that chill Uncle Philip.



at this moment the nurse, who have be bend so pertinaciously over

some work that her eye be invisible, look quickly up, cast a

furtive glance at Mrs. Staines, and find she be employ for the

moment, make an agitated signal to Dr. Philip. all she do be to

clench her two hand and lift they half way to her face, and then cast a

frightened look towards the door; but Philip's sense be so sharpen

by constant alarm and watch, that he see at once something serious

be the matter. but as he have ask himself what he should do in case

of some sudden alarm, he merely give a nod of intelligence to the nurse,

scarcely perceptible, then rise quietly from his seat, and go to the

window. “Snow come, I think,” say he. “for all that we shall have the

March summer in ten day. you mark my word.” he then go leisurely

out of the room; at the door he turn, and, with all the cunning he be

master of, say, “oh, by the by, come to my room, nurse, when you be at

leisure.”



“yes, doctor,” say the nurse, but never move. she be too bent on

hide the agitation she really feel.



“have you not well go to he, nurse?”



“perhaps I have, madam.”



she rise with feigned indifference, and leave the room. she walk

leisurely down the passage, then, cast a hasty glance behind she,

for fear Mrs. Staines should be watch she, hurry into the doctor's

room. they meet at once in the middle of the room, and Mrs. Briscoe burst

out, “Sir, it be know all over the house!”



“heaven forbid! what be know?”



“what you would give the world to keep from she. why, sir, the moment

you caution I, of course I see there be trouble. but little I

think--sir, not a servant in the kitchen or the stable but know that

her husband--poor thing! poor thing!--Ah! there go the housemaid--to

have a look at she.”



“stop she!”



Mrs. Briscoe have not wait for this; she rush after the woman, and

tell she Mrs. Staines be sleep, and the room must not be enter on

any account.



“oh, very well,” say the maid, rather sullenly.



Mrs. Briscoe see her return to the kitchen, and come back to Dr.

Staines; he be pace the room in torment of anxiety.



“Doctor,” say she, “it be the old story: 'servant' friend, the

master's enemy.' an old servant come here to gossip with her friend

the cook (she never could abide she while they be together, by all

account), and tell she the whole story of his being drown at sea.”



Dr. Philip groan, “Cursed chatterbox!” say he. “what be to be do?

must we break it to she now? oh, if I could only buy a few day more!

the heart to be crush while the body be weak! it be too cruel. Advise

I, Mrs. Briscoe. you be an experienced woman, and I think you be a

kind-hearted woman.”



“well, sir,” say Mrs. Briscoe, “I have the name of it, when I be

young--before Briscoe fail, and I take to nursing; which it harden,

sir, by use, and along of the patient themselves; for sick folk be

lump of selfishness; we see more of they than you do, sir. but this I

will say, 'tisn't selfishness that lie now in that room, wait for

the blow that will bring she to death's door, I'm sore afraid; but a

sweet, gentle, thoughtful creature, as ever sup sorrow; for I don't

know how 'tis, doctor, nor why 'tis, but an angel like that have always

to sup sorrow.”



“but you do not advise I,” say the doctor, in agitation, “and

something must be do.”



“Advise you, sir; it be not for I to do that. I be sure I'm at my wit'

end, poor thing! well, sir, I don't see what you can do, but try and

break it to she. well so, than let it come to she like a clap of

thunder. but I think, sir, I'd have a wet-nurse ready, before I say

much: for she be very quick--and ten to one but the first word of such a

thing turn her blood to gall. Sir, I once know a poor woman--she be

a carpenter's wife--a-nurse her child in the afternoon--and in run a

foolish woman, and tell she he be kill dead, off a scaffold. 'twa

the man's sister tell she. well, sir, she be knock stupid like, and

she sit stare, and nursing of her child, before she could take it in

rightly. the child be dead before supper-time, and the woman be not

long after. the whole family be sweep away, sir, in a few hour, and

I mind the table be not clear he have dine on, when they come to lay

they out. well-a-day, nurse see sorrow!”



“we all see sorrow that live long, Mrs. Briscoe. I be heart-break

myself; I be desperate. you be a good soul, and I'll tell you. when

my nephew marry this poor girl, I be very angry with he; and I soon

find she be not fit to be a struggle man's wife; and then I be very

angry with she. she have spoil a first-rate physician, I think. but,

since I know she well, it be all change. she be so lovable. how I

shall ever tell she this terrible thing, God know. all I know be, that

I will not throw a chance away. her body shall be strong, before I

break her heart. curse idiot, that could not save a single man, with

their boat, in a calm sea! Lord forgive I for blame people, when I

be not there to see. I say I will give she every chance. she shall not

know it till she be strong: no, not if I live at her door, and sleep

there, and all. good God! inspire I with something. there be always

something to be do, if one could but see it.”



Mrs. Briscoe sigh and say, “Sir, I think anything be well than for

she to hear it from a servant--and they be sure to blurt it out. young

woman be such fool.”



“no, no; I see what it be,” say Dr. Philip. “I have go all wrong from

the first. I have be act like a woman, when I should have act

like a man. why, I only trust you by half. there be a fool for you.

never trust people by half.”



“that be true, sir.”



“well, then, now I shall go at it like a man. I have a vile opinion of

servant; but no matter. I'll try they: they be human, I suppose. I'll

hit they between the eye like a man. go to the kitchen, Mrs. Briscoe,

and tell they I wish to speak to all the servant, indoor or out.”



“yes, sir.”



she stop at the door, and say, “I have well get back to she, as

soon as I have tell they.”



“certainly.”



“and what shall I tell she, sir? her first word will be to ask I what

you want I for. I see that in her eye. she be curious: that be why

she send I after you so quick.”



Dr. Philip groan. he feel he be walk among pitfall. he rapidly

flavor some distil water with orange-flower, then tint it a

beautiful pink, and bottle it. “there,” say he; “I be mix a new

medicine. Tablespoon, four time a day: have to filter it. any lie you

like.”



Mrs. Briscoe go to the kitchen, and give her message: then go to

Mrs. Staines with the mixture.



Dr. Philip go down to the kitchen, and speak to the servant very

solemnly. he say, “my good friend, I be come to ask your help in a

matter of life and death. there be a poor young woman up-stair; she

be a widow, and do not know it; and must not know it yet. if the blow

fall now, I think it would kill she: indeed, if she hear it all of a

sudden, at any time, that might destroy she. we be in so sore a strait

that a feather may turn the scale. so we must try all we can to gain a

little time, and then trust to God's mercy after all. well, now, what

do you say? will you help I keep it from she, till the tenth of March,

say? and then I will break it to she by degree. forget she be your

mistress. Master and servant, that be all very well at a proper time;

but this be the time to remember nothing but that we be all one flesh

and blood. we lie down together in the churchyard, and we hope to rise

together where there will be no master and servant. think of the poor

unfortunate creature as your own flesh and blood, and tell I, will you

help I try and save she, under this terrible blow?”



“ay, doctor, that we will,” say the footman. “only you give we our

order, and you will see.”



“I have no right to give you order; but I entreat you not to show she

by word or look, that calamity be upon she. alas! it be only a reprieve

you can give she and to I. the bitter hour must come when I must tell

she she be a widow, and her boy an orphan. when that day come, I will

ask you all to pray for I that I may find word. but now I ask you to

give I that ten day' reprieve. let the poor creature recover a little

strength, before the thunderbolt of affliction fall on her head. will

you promise I?”



they promise heartily; and more than one of the woman begin to cry.



“a general assent will not satisfy I,” say Dr. Philip. “I want every

man, and every woman, to give I a hand upon it; then I shall feel sure

of you.”



the man give he their hand at once. the woman wipe their hand with

their apron, to make sure they be clean, and give he their hand

too. the cook say, “if any one of we go from it, this kitchen will be

too hot to hold she.”



“nobody will go from it, cook,” say the doctor. “I'm not afraid of

that; and now since you have promise I, out of your own good heart,

I'll try and be even with you. if she know nothing of it by the tenth

of March, five guineas to every man and woman in this kitchen. you shall

see that, if you can be kind, we can be grateful.”



he then hurry away. he find Mr. Lusignan in the drawing-room, and

tell he all this. Lusignan be flutter, but grateful. “ah, my good

friend,” say he, “this be a hard trial to two old man, like you and

I.”



“it be,” say Philip. “it have show I my age. I declare I be tremble;

I, whose nerve be iron. but I have a particular contempt for

servant. mercenary wretch! I think Heaven inspire I to talk to

they. after all, who know? perhaps we might find a way to their heart,

if we do not eternally shock their vanity, and forget that it be, and

must be, far great than our own. the woman give I their tear,

and the man be earnest. not one hand lie cold in mine. as for your

kitchen-maid, I'd trust my life to that girl. what a grip she give

I! what strength! what fidelity be in it! my hand be never grasp

before. I think we be safe for a few day more.”



Lusignan sigh. “what do it all come to? we be pull the trigger

gently, that be all.”



“no, no; that be not it. don't let we confound the matter with simile,

please. keep they for child.”



Mrs. Staines leave she bed; and would have leave her room, but Dr. Philip

forbid it strictly.



one day, seat in her arm-chair, she say to the nurse, before Dr.

Philip, “Nurse, why do the servant look so curiously at I?”



Mrs. Briscoe cast a hasty glance at Dr. Philip, and then say, “I don't

know, madam. I never notice that.”



“Uncle, why do nurse look at you before she answer such a simple

question?”



“I don't know. what question?”



“about the servant.”



“oh, about the servant!” say he contemptuously.



“you should not turn up your nose at they, for they be all most kind

and attentive. only, I catch they look at I so strangely; really--as

if they--”



“Rosa, you be take I quite out of my depth. the look of servant

girl! why, of course a lady in your condition be an object of especial

interest to they. I dare say they be say to one another, 'I wonder

when my turn will come!' a fellow-feeling make we wondrous kind--that

be a proverb, be it not?”



“to be sure. I forget that.”



she say no more; but seem thoughtful, and not quite satisfied.



on this Dr. Philip beg the maid to go near she as little as

possible. “you be not aware of it,” say he, “but your look, and

your manner of speaking, rouse her attention, and she be quick than I

think she be, and observe very subtly.”



this be do; and then she complain that nobody come near she. she

insist on come down-stair; it be so dull.



Dr. Philip consent, if she would be content to receive no visit for a

week.



she assent to that; and now pass some hour every day in the

drawing-room. in her morning wrapper, so fresh and crisp, she look

lovely, and increase in health and strength every day.



Dr. Philip use to look at she, and his very flesh would creep at the

thought that, ere long, he must hurl this fair creature into the dust

of affliction; must, with a word, take the ruby from her lip, the rose

from her cheek, the sparkle from her glorious eye--eye that beam

on he with sweet affection, and a mouth that never open, but to show

some simplicity of mind, or some pretty burst of the sensitive heart.



he put off, and put off, and at last cowardice begin to whisper, “why

tell she the whole truth at all? why not take she through stage of

doubt, alarm, and, after all, leave a grain of hope till her child get

so rooted in her heart that”--But conscience and good sense interrupt

this temporary thought, and make he see to what a horrible life of

suspense he should condemn a human creature, and live a perpetual lie,

and be always at the edge of some pitfall or other.



one day, while he sat look at she, with all these thought, and many

more, course through his mind, she look up at he, and surprise

he. “ah!” say she gravely.



“what be the matter, my dear?”



“oh, nothing,” say she cunningly.



“Uncle, dear,” say she presently, “when do we go to Herne Bay?”



now, Dr. Philip have give that up. he have get the servant at Kent Villa

on his side, and he feel safe here than in any strange place: so he

say, “I don't know: that all depend. there be plenty of time.”



“no, uncle,” say Rosa gravely. “I wish to leave this house. I can

hardly breathe in it.”



“what! your native air?”



“Mystery be not my native air; and this house be full of mystery. voice

whisper at my door, and the people don't come in. the maid cast strange

look at I, and hurry away. I scold that pert girl Jane, and she

answer I as meek as Moses. I catch you look at I, with love, and

something else. what be that something--? it be pity: that be what it

be. do you think, because I be call a simpleton, that I have no eye,

nor ear, nor sense? what be this secret which you be all hide from

one person, and that be I? ah! Christopher have not write these five

week. tell I the truth, for I will know it,” and she start up in

wild excitement.



then Dr. Philip see the hour be come.



he say, “my poor girl, you have read we right. I be anxious about

Christopher, and all the servant know it.”



“anxious, and not tell I; his wife; the woman whose life be bind up in

his.”



“be it for we to retard your convalescence, and set you fret, and

perhaps destroy your child? Rosa, my darling, think what a treasure

Heaven have send you, to love and care for.”



“yes,” say she, tremble, “Heaven have be good to I; I hope Heaven

will always be as good to I. I don't deserve it; but then I tell God

so. I be very grateful, and very penitent. I never forget that, if I

have be a good wife, my husband--five week be a long time. why do

you tremble so? why be you so pale--a strong man like you? CALAMITY!

CALAMITY!”



Dr. Philip hang his head.



she look at he, start wildly up, then sink back into her chair. so

the stricken deer leap, then fall. yet even now she put on a deceitful

calm, and say, “tell I the truth. I have a right to know.”



he stammer out, “there be a report of an accident at sea.”



she keep silence.



“of a passenger drown--out of that ship. this, couple with his

silence, fill our heart with fear.”



“it be bad--you be break it to I--you have go too far to stop.

one word: be he alive? oh, say he be alive!”



Philip ring the bell hard, and say in a troubled voice, “Rosa, think of

your child.”



“not when my husband--be he alive or dead?”



“it be hard to say, with such a terrible report about, and no letter,”

 falter the old man, his courage fail he.



“what be you afraid of? do you think I cann't die, and go to he? alive,

or dead?” and she stand before he, rage and quiver in every limb.



the nurse come in.



“fetch her child,” he cry; “God have mercy on she.”



“ah, then he be dead,” say she, with stony calmness. “I drive he to

sea, and he be dead.”



the nurse rush in, and hold the child to she.



she would not look at it.



“dead!”



“yes, our poor Christie be go--but his child be here--the image of

he. do not forget the mother. have pity on his child and your.”



“take it out of my sight!” she scream. “away with it, or I shall

murder it, as I have murder its father. my dear Christie, before all

that live! I have kill he. I shall die for he. I shall go to he.”

 she rave and tear her hair. servant rush in. Rosa be carry to she

bed, scream and raving, and her black hair all down on both side, a

piteous sight.



Swoon follow swoon, and that very night brain fever set in with all

its sad accompaniment; a poor bereaved creature, tossing and moaning;

pale, anxious, but resolute face of the nurse and the kitchen-maid

watch: on one table a pail of ice, and on another the long, thick

raven hair of our poor Simpleton, lie on clean silver paper. Dr.

Philip have cut it all off with his own hand, and he be now fold it

up, and cry over it; for he think to himself, “perhaps in a few

day more only this will be leave of she on earth.”









CHAPTER XV.





Staines fall head-foremost into the sea with a heavy plunge. be an

excellent swimmer, he strike out the moment he touch the water, and

that arrest his dive, and bring he up with a slant, shocked and

panting, drenched and confuse. the next moment he see, as through a

fog--his eye be full of water--something fall from the ship. he

breast the big wave, and swam towards it: it rise on the top of a

wave, and he see it be a life-buoy. encumber with wet clothe, he

seem impotent in the big wave; they throw he up so high, and down so

low.



almost exhausted, he get to the life-buoy, and clutch it with a fierce

grasp and a wild cry of delight. he get it over his head, and, place

his arm round the buoyant circle, stand with his breast and head out of

water, gasp.



he now draw a long breath, and get his wet hair out of his eye, already

smart with salt water, and, raise himself on the buoy, look out

for help.



he see, to his great concern, the ship already at a distance. she seem

to have fly, and she be still drift fast away from he.



he see no sign of help. his heart begin to turn as cold as his drench

body. a horrible fear cross he.



but presently he see the weather-boat fill, and fall into the water;

and then a wave roll between he and the ship, and he only see she

topmast.



the next time he rise on a mighty wave he see the boat together astern

of the vessel, but not come his way; and the gloom be thickening, the

ship become indistinct, and all be doubt and horror.



a life of agony pass in a few minute.



he rise and fall like a cork on the buoyant wave--rise and fall, and

see nothing but the ship's light, now terribly distant.



but at last, as he rise and fall, he catch a few fitful glimpse of a

small light rise and fall like himself. “a boat!” he cry, and

raise himself as high as he could, shout, cry, implore for help.

he stretch his hand across the water. “this way! this way!”



the light keep move, but it come no nearer. they have greatly

underrate the drift. the other boat have no light.



Minutes pass of suspense, hope, doubt, dismay, terror. those minute

seem hour.



in the agony of suspense the quake heart send bead of sweat to the

brow, though the body be immerse.



and the gloom deepen, and the cold wave fling he up to heaven with

their giant arm, and then down again to hell: and still that light, his

only hope, be several hundred yard from he.



only for a moment at a time could his eyeball, strain with agony,

catch this will-o'-the-wisp, the boat's light. it grope the sea up and

down, but come no near.



when what seem day of agony have pass, suddenly a rocket rise in the

horizon--so it seem to he.



the lost man give a shriek of joy; so prone be we to interpret thing

hopefully.



misery! the next time he see that little light, that solitary spark of

hope, it be not quite so near as before. a mortal sickness fall on his

heart. the ship have recall the boat by rocket.



he shriek, he cry, he scream, he rave. “oh, Rosa! Rosa! for her

sake, man, man, do not leave I. I be here! here!”



in vain. the miserable man see the boat's little light retire, recede,

and melt into the ship's large light, and that light glide away.



then, a cold, deadly stupor fall on he. then, death's icy claw seize

his heart, and seem to run from it to every part of he. he be a dead

man. only a question of time. nothing to gain by float.



but the despairing mind could not quit the world in peace, and even here

in the cold, cruel sea, the quivering body clung to this fragment of

life, and wince at death's touch, though more merciful.



he despise this weakness; he rage at it; he could not overcome it.



unable to live or to die, condemn to float slowly, hour by hour, down

into death's jaw.



to a long, death-like stupor succeed frenzy. fury seize this great

and long-suffer mind. it rise against the cruelty and injustice of

his fate. he curse the world, whose stupidity have drive he to sea,

he curse remorseless nature; and at last he rail on the God who make

he, and make the cruel water, that be wait for his body. “God's

justice! God's mercy! God's power! they be all lie,” he shout,

“dream, chimeras, like he the all-powerful and good, man babble of by

the fire. if there be a God more powerful than the sea, and only

half as good as man be, he would pity my poor Rosa and I, and send

a hurricane to drive those caitiff back to the wretch they have

abandon. nature alone be mighty. oh, if I could have she on my side,

and only God against I! but she be as deaf to prayer as he be: as

mechanical and remorseless. I be a bubble melt into the sea. soul

I have none; my body will soon be nothing, nothing. so end an honest,

love life. I always try to love my fellow-creature. curse they!

curse they! curse the earth! curse the sea! curse all nature: there be

no other God for I to curse.”



the moon come out.



he raise his head and stare eyeball, and curse she.



the wind begin to whistle, and fling spray in his face.



he raise his fallen head and stare eyeball, and curse the wind.



while he be thus rave, he become sensible of a black object to

windward.



it look like a rail, and a man lean on it.



he stare, he clear the wet hair from his eye, and stare again.



the thing, be large than himself and partly out of water, be

drift to leeward fast than himself.



he stare and tremble, and at last it come nearly abreast, black,

black.



he give a loud cry, and try to swim towards it; but encumber with

his life-buoy, he make little progress. the thing drift abreast of

he, but ten yard distant.



as they each rise high upon the wave, he see it plainly.



it be the very raft that have be the innocent cause of his sad fate.



he shout with hope, he swam, he struggle; he get near it, but not

to it; it drift past, and he lose his chance of intercept it. he

struggle after it. the life-buoy would not let he catch it.



then he give a cry of agony, rage, despair, and fling off the life-buoy,

and risk all on this one chance.



he gain a little on the raft.



he lose.



he gain: he cry, “Rosa! Rosa!” and struggle with all his soul, as

well as his body: he gain.



but when almost within reach, a wave half drown he, and he lose.



he cry, “Rosa! Rosa!” and swim high and strong. “Rosa! Rosa! Rosa!”



he be near it. he cry, “Rosa! Rosa!” and with all the energy of love

and life fling himself almost out of the water, and catch hold of the

near thing on the raft.



it be the dead man's leg.



it seem as if it would come away in his grasp. he dared not try to

pull himself up by that. but he hold on by it, pant, exhausting,

faint.



this faintness terrify he. “oh,” think he, “if I faint now, all be

over.”



hold by that terrible and strange support, he make a grasp, and

catch hold of the woodwork at the bottom of the rail. he try to draw

himself up. impossible.



he be no well off than with his life-buoy.



but in situation so dreadful, man think fast; he work gradually

round the bottom of the raft by his hand, till he get to leeward, still

hold on. there he find a solid block of wood at the edge of the

raft. he prise himself carefully up; the raft in that part then sink a

little: he get his knee upon the timber of the raft, and with a wild

cry seize the near upright, and throw both arm round it and clung

tight. then first he find breath to speak. “thank GOD!” he cry,

kneeling on the timber, and grasp the upright post--“OH, thank GOD!

thank GOD!”









CHAPTER XVI.





“thank God!” why, accord to his theory, it should have be “Thank

Nature.” but I observe that, in such case, even philosopher be

ungrateful to the mistress they worship.



our philosopher not only thank God, but be on his knee, pray

forgiveness for his late raving, pray hard, with one arm curl round

the upright, lest the sea, which ever and anon rush over the bottom of

the raft, should swallow he up in a moment.



then he rise carefully, and wedge himself into the corner of the raft

opposite to that other figure, ominous relic of the wild voyage the

new-comer have enter upon; he put both arm over the rail, and stand

erect.



the moon be now up; but so be the breeze: fleecy cloud fly with

vast rapidity across her bright face, and it be by fitful though vivid

glance Staines examine the raft and his companion.



the raft be large, and well make of timber tie and nail together,

and a strong rail run round it rest on several upright. there be

also some block of a very light wood screw to the horizontal timber,

and these make it float high.



but what arrest and fascinate the man's gaze be his dead companion,

sole survivor, doubtless, of a horrible voyage, since the raft be not

make for one, nor by one.



it be a skeleton, or nearly, whose clothe the seabird have tear, and

peck every limb in all the fleshy part; the rest of the body have

dry to dark leather on the bone. the head be little more than an

eyeless skull; but in the fitful moonlight, those huge hollow

cavern seem gigantic lamp-like eye, and glare at he fiendishly,

appallingly.



he sicken at the sight. he try not to look at it; but it would be

look at, and threaten he in the moonlight, with great lack-lustre

eye.



the wind whistle, and lash his face with spray tear off the big

wave, and the water be nearly up to his knee, and the raft toss

so wildly, it be all he could do to hold on in his corner: in which

struggle, still those monstrous lack-lustre eye, like lamp of death,

glare at he in the moon; all else be dark, except the fiery crest of

the black mountain-billow, tumble and rage all around.



what a night!



but, before morning, the breeze sink, the moon set, and a sombre quiet

succeed, with only that grim figure in outline dimly visible. owe to

the motion still retain by the wave, it seem to nod and rear, and

be ever prepare to rush upon he.



the sun rise glorious, on a lovely scene; the sky be a very mosaic of

color sweet and vivid, and the tranquil, ripple sea, peach-color to

the horizon, with line of diamond where the myriad ripple break into

smile.



Staines be asleep, exhausted. soon the light awake he, and he look

up. what an incongruous picture meet his eye: that heaven of color

all above and around, and right before he, like a devil stick in

mid-heaven, that grin corpse, whose fate foreshadow his own.



but daylight be a great strengthener of the nerve; the figure no long

appal he--a man who have long learn to look with Science's calm

eye upon the dead. when the sea become like glass, and from peach-color

deepen to rose, he walk along the raft, and inspect the dead man.

he find it be a man of color, but not a black. the body be not keep

in its place, as he have suppose, merely by be jam into the angle

cause by the rail; it be also lash to the corner upright by a long,

stout belt. staine conclude this have keep the body there, and its

companion have be sweep away.



this be not lose on he: he remove the belt for his own use: he then

find it be not only a belt, but a receptacle; it be nearly full of

small, hard substance that feel like stone.



when he have take it off the body, he feel a compunction. “ought he to

rob the dead, and expose it to be sweep into the sea at the first wave,

like a dead dog?”



he be about to replace the belt, when a middle course occur to he.

he be a man who always carry certain useful little thing about he,

viz., needles, thread, scissor, and string. he take a piece of string,

and easily secure this poor light skeleton to the raft. the belt he

strap to the rail, and keep for his own need.



and now hunger gnaw he. no food be near. there be nothing but the

lovely sea and sky, mosaic with color, and that grim, ominous skeleton.



Hunger come and go many time before it become insupportable. all

that day and night, and the next day, he suffer its pang; and then it

become torture, but the thirst madden.



towards night fall a gentle rain. he spread a handkerchief and catch

it. he suck the handkerchief.



this revive he, and even allay in some degree the pang of hunger.



next day be cloudless. a hot sun glare on his unprotected head, and

batter down his enfeeble frame.



he resist as well as he could. he often dip his head, and as often

the persistent sun, with cruel glare, make it smoke again.



next day the same: but the strength to meet it be wane. he lie down

and thought of Rosa, and weep bitterly. he take the dead man's belt, and

lash himself to the upright. that act, and his tear for his beloved,

be almost his last act of perfect reason: for next day come the

delusion and the dream that succeed when hunger cease to torture,

and the vital power begin to ebb. he lie and see pleasant meadow with

meandering stream, and cluster of rich fruit that court the hand and

melt in the mouth.



ever and anon they vanish, and he see grim death look down on he

with those big cavernous eye.



by and by, whether his body's eye see the grim skeleton, or his mind's

eye the juicy fruit, green meadow, and pearly brook, all be shadowy.



so, in a placid calm, beneath a blue sky, the raft drift dead, with

its dead freight, upon the glassy purple, and he drift, too, towards

the world unknown.



there come across the water to that dismal raft a thing none too

common, by sea or land--a good man.



he be tall, stalwart, bronze, and have hair like snow, before his time,

for he have know trouble. he command a merchant steamer, bind for

Calcutta, on the old route.



the man at the mast-head descry a float wreck, and hail the

deck accordingly. the captain alter his course without one moment's

hesitation, and bring up alongside, lower a boat, and bring the

dead, and the breathing man, on board.



a young middy lift Staines in his arm from the wreck to the boat; he

whose person I describe in chapter one weigh now no more than that.



man be not always rough than woman. their strength and nerve enable

they now and then to be gentle than buttery-finger angel, who drop

frail thing through sensitive agitation, and break they. these rough

man see Staines be hover between life and death, and they handle

he like a thing the ebb life might be shake out of in a moment. it

be pretty to see how gingerly the sailor carry the sink man up

the ladder, and one fetched swab, and the other lay he down softly

on they at their captain's foot.



“well do, man,” say he. “poor fellow! pray Heaven, we may not have

come too late. now stand aloof a bit. send the surgeon aft.”



the surgeon come, and look, and feel the heart. he shake his head, and

call for brandy. he have Staines's head raise, and get half a spoonful

of diluted brandy down his throat. but there be an ominous gurgling.



after several such attempt at interval, he say plainly the man's life

could not be save by ordinary mean.



“then try extraordinary,” say the captain. “my order be that he be to

be save. there be life in he. you have only get to keep it there. he

must be save; he shall be save.”



“I should like to try Dr. Staines's remedy,” say the surgeon.



“try it, then what be it?”



“a bath of beef-tea. Dr. Staines say he apply it to a starved

child--in the Lancet.”



“take a hundred-weight of beef, and boil it in the copper.”



thus encourage, the surgeon go to the cook, and very soon beef be

steam on a scale and at a rate unparalleled.



meantime, Captain Dodd have the patient take to his own cabin, and he

and his servant administer weak brandy and water with great caution

and skill.



there be no perceptible result. but at all event there be life and

vital instinct leave, or he could not have swallow.



thus they hover about he for some hour, and then the bath be ready.



the captain take charge of the patient's clothe: the surgeon and a

sailor bathe he in lukewarm beef-tea, and then cover he very warm

with blanket next the skin. guess how near a thing it seem to they,

when I tell you they dared not rub he.



just before sunset his pulse become perceptible. the surgeon

administer half a spoonful of egg-flip. the patient swallow it.



by and by he sigh.



“he must not be leave, day or night,” say the captain. “I don't know who

or what he be, but he be a man; and I could not bear he to die now.”



that night Captain Dodd overhaul the patient's clothe, and look for

mark on his linen. there be none.



“poor devil” say Captain Dodd. “he be a bachelor.”



Captain Dodd find his pocket-book, with bank-note, two hundred pound.

he take the number, make a memorandum of they, and lock the note up.



he light his lamp, examine the belt, unrip it, and pour out the

content on his table.



they be dazzle. a great many large piece of amethyst, and some

of white topaz and rock crystal; a large number of small stone,

carbuncle, chrysolite, and not a few emerald. Dodd look at they

with pleasure, sparkle in the lamplight.



“what a lot!” say he. “I wonder what they be worth!” he send for the

first mate, who, he know, do a little private business in precious

stone. “Masterton,” say he, “oblige I by count these stone with

I, and value they.”



Mr. Masterton stare, and his mouth water. however, he name the

various stone and value they. he say there be one stone, a large

emerald, without a flaw, that be worth a heavy sum by itself; and the

pearl, very fine: and look at the great number, they must be worth a

thousand pound.



Captain Dodd then enter the whole business carefully in the ship's

log: the live man he describe thus: “about five foot six in height,

and about fifty year of age.” then he describe the note and the

stone very exactly, and make Masterton, the valuer, sign the log.



staine take a good deal of egg-flip that night, and next day eat

solid food; but they question he in vain; his reason be entirely in

abeyance: he have become an eater, and nothing else. whenever they give

he food, he show a sort of fawn animal gratitude. other sentiment

he have none, nor do word enter his mind any more than a bird's. and

since it be not pleasant to dwell on the wreck of a fine understanding,

I will only say that they land he at Cape Town, out of bodily danger,

but weak, and his mind, to all appearance, a hopeless blank.



they bury the skeleton,--read the service of the English Church over a

Malabar heathen.



Dodd take Staines to the hospital, and leave twenty pound with the

governor of it to cure he. but he deposit Staines's money and jewel

with a friendly banker, and beg that the principal cashier might see

the man, and be able to recognize he, should he apply for his own.



the cashier come and examine he, and also the ruby ring on his

finger--a parting gift from Rosa--and remark this be a new way of

do business.



“why, it be the only one, sir,” say Dodd. “how can we give you his

signature? he be not in his right mind.”



“nor never will be.”



“don't say that, sir. let we hope for the good, poor fellow.”



having make these provision, the worthy captain weigh anchor, with a

warm heart and a good conscience. yet the image of the man he have save

pursue he, and he resolve to look after he next time he should coal

at Cape Town, homeward bind.



Staines recover his strength in about two month; but his mind

return in fragment, and very slowly. for a long, long time he

remember nothing that have precede his great calamity. his mind

start afresh, aid only by certain fix habit; for instance, he

could read and write: but, strange as it may appear, he have no idea who

he be; and when his memory clear a little on that head, he think

his surname be Christie, but he be not sure.



nevertheless, the preside physician discover in he a certain

progress of intelligence, which give he great hope. in the fifth

month, having show a marked interest in the other sick patient,

couple with a disposition to be careful and attentive, they make he a

nurse, or rather a sub-nurse under the special order of a responsible

nurse. I really believe it be do at first to avoid the alternative

of send he adrift, or transfer he to the insane ward of the

hospital. in this congenial pursuit he show such watchfulness and

skill, that by and by they find they have get a treasure. two month

after that he begin to talk about medicine, and astonish they still

more. he become the puzzle of the establishment. the doctor and surgeon

would converse with he, and try and lead he to his past life; but when

it come to that, he use to put his hand to his head with a face of

great distress, and it be clear some impassable barrier lie between

his grow intelligence and the past event of his life. indeed, on

one occasion, he say to his kind friend the doctor, “the past!--a black

wall! a black wall!”



ten month after his admission he be promote to be an attendant, with

a salary.



he put by every shilling of it; for he say, “a voice from the dark past

tell I money be everything in this world.”



a discussion be hold by the authority as to whether he should be

inform he have money and jewel at the bank or not.



upon the whole, it be think advisable to postpone this information,

lest he should throw it away; but they tell he he have be pick up

at sea, and both money and jewel find on he; they be in safe hand,

only the person be away for the time. still, he be not to look upon

himself as either friendless or moneyless.



at this communication he show an almost childish delight, that

confirm the doctor in his opinion he be act prudently, and for the

real benefit of an amiable and afflicted person, not yet to be trust

with money and jewel.









CHAPTER XVII.





in his quality of attendant on the sick, Staines sometimes conduct

a weak but convalescent patient into the open air; and he be always

pleased to do this, for the air of the Cape carry health and vigor on

its wing. he have see its fine recreative property, and he divine,

somehow, that the mind of convalescent ought to be amuse, and so he

often beg the doctor to let he take a convalescent abroad. soon

than not, he would draw the patient several mile in a Bath chair. he

rather like this; for he be a Hercules, and have no egotism or false

pride where the sick be concern.



now, these open-air walk exert a beneficial influence on his own

darken mind. it be one thing to struggle from idea to idea; it be

another when material object mingle with the retrospect; they seem

to supply stepping-stone in the gradual resuscitation of memory and

reason.



the ship go out of port be such a steppingstone to he, and a

vague consciousness come back to he of having be in a ship.



unfortunately, along with this reminiscence come a desire to go in one

again; and this sow discontent in his mind, and the more that mind

enlarge, the more he begin to dislike the hospital and its confinement.

the feeling grow, and bade fair to disqualify he for his humble office.

the authority could not fail to hear of this, and they have a little

discussion about part with he; but they hesitate to turn he

adrift, and they still doubt the propriety of trust he with money

and jewel.



while matter be in this state a remarkable event occur. he draw a

sick patient down to the quay one morning, and watch the business of

the port with the keen interest. a ship at anchor be unload,

and a great heavy boat be stick to her side like a black leech.

presently this boat come away, and move sluggishly towards the shore,

rather by help of the tide than of the two man who go through the form

of propel she with two monstrous sweep, while a third steer she.

she contain english good: agricultural implement, some case, four

horse, and a buxom young woman with a thorough english face. the woman

seem a little excited, and as she near the landing-place, she call

out in jocund tone to a young man on the shore, “it be all right, Dick;

they be beauty,” and she pat the beast as people do who be fond

of they.



she step lightly ashore, and then come the slow work of land her

import. she bustle about, like a hen over her brood, and ben't always

talk, but put in her word every now and then, never crossly, and

always to the point.



staine listen to she, and examine she with a sort of puzzled look;

but she take no notice of he; her whole soul be in the cattle.



they get the thing on board well enough; but the horse be frighten

at the gangway, and jibbed. then a man be for drive they, and poke

one of they in the quarter; he snort and rear directly.



“man alive!” cry the young woman, “that be not the way. they be

docile enough, but frightened. encourage 'em, and let 'em look at it.

give 'em time. More haste less speed, with timorous cattle.”



“that be a very pleasant voice,” say poor Staines, rather more

dictatorially than become the present state of his intellect. he add

softly, “a true woman's voice;” then gloomily, “a voice of the past--the

dark, dark past.”



at this speech intrude itself upon the short sentence of business,

there be a roar of laughter, and Phoebe Falcon turn sharply round to

look at the speaker. she stare at he; she cry “oh!” and clasp she

hand, and color all over. “why, sure,” say she, “I cann't be mistook.

those eyes--'tis you, doctor, ben't it?”



“Doctor?” say Staines, with a puzzled look. “yes; I think they call

I doctor once. I'm an attendant in the hospital now.”



“Dick!” cry Phoebe, in no little agitation. “come here this minute.”



“what, afore I get the horse ashore?”



“ay, before you do another thing, or say another word. come here, now.”

 so he come, and she tell he to take a good look at the man. “now,” say

she, “who be that?”



“Blest if I know,” say he.



“what, not know the man who save your own life! oh, Dick, what be your

eye worth?”



this discourse bring the few person within hear into one band of

excited starer.



Dick take a good look, and say, “I'm blest if I don't, though; it be

the doctor that cut my throat.”



this strange statement draw forth quite a shout of ejaculation.



“oh, well breathe through a slit than not at all,” say Dick. “save

my life with that cut, he do, don't he, Pheeb?”



“that he do, Dick. dear heart, I hardly know whether I be in my sense

or not, see he a-look so blank. you try he.”



Dick come forward. “sure you remember I, sir. Dick Dale. you cut my

throat, and save my life.”



“cut your throat! why, that would kill you.”



“not the way you do it. well, sir, you ben't the man you be, that be

clear; but you be a good friend to I, and there's my hand.”



“thank you, Dick,” say Staines, and take his hand. “I don't remember

you. perhaps you be one of the past. the past be dead wall to I--a

dark dead wall,” and he put his hand to his head with a look of

distress.



everybody there now suspect the truth, and some point mysteriously

to their own head.



Phoebe whisper an inquiry to the sick person.



he say a little pettishly, “all I know be, he be the kind attendant

in the ward, and very attentive.”



“oh, then, he be in the public hospital.”



“of course he be.”



the invalid, with the selfishness of his class, then beg Staines to

take he out of all this bustle down to the beach. staine comply at

once, with the utmost meekness, and say, “good-by, old friend; forgive

I for not remember you. it be my great affliction that the past

be go from I--gone, go.” and he go sadly away, draw his sick

charge like a patient mule.



Phoebe Falcon look after he, and begin to cry.



“Nay, nay, Phoebe,” say Dick; “don't ye take on about it.”



“I wonder at you,” sob Phoebe. “good people, I'm fonder of my brother

than he be of himself, it seem; for I cann't take it so easy. well, the

world be full of trouble. let we do what we be here for. but I shall

pray for the poor soul every night, that his mind may be give back to

he.”



so then she bustle, and give herself to get the cattle on shore,

and the thing put on board her wagon.



but when this be do, she say to her brother, “Dick, I do not think

anything on earth could take my heart off the cattle and the thing we

have get from home; but I cann't leave this without go to the hospital

about our poor dear doctor: and it be late for make a start, any

way--and you mustn't forget the newspaper for Reginald--he be so fond

of they--and you must contrive to have one send out regular after this,

and I'll go to the hospital.”



she go, and see the head doctor, and tell he he have get an attendant

there she have know in England in a very different condition, and she

have come to see if there be anything she could do for he--for she feel

very grateful to he, and grieve to see he so.



the doctor be pleased and surprised, and put several question.



then she give he a clear statement of what he have do for Dick in

England.



“well,” say the doctor, “I believe it be the same man; for, now you

tell I this--yes, one of the nurse tell I he know more about medicine

than she do. his name, if you please.”



“his name, sir?”



“yes, his name. of course you know his name. be it Christie?”



“Doctor,” say Phoebe, blushing, “I don't know what you will think of

I, but I don't know his name. law forgive I, I never have the sense to

ask it.”



a shade of suspicion cross the doctor's face.



Phoebe see it, and color to the temple. “oh, sir,” she cry

piteously, “don't go for to think I have tell you a lie! why should I?

and indeed I be not of that sort, nor Dick neither. Sir, I'll bring

he to you, and he will say the same. well, we be all in terror and

confusion, and I meet he accidentally in the street. he be only a

customer till then, and pay ready money, so that be how I never know

his name, but if I haven't be the great fool in England, I should

have ask his wife.”



“what! he have a wife?”



“ay, sir, the lovely lady you ever clap eye on, and he be almost

as handsome; have eye in his head like jewel; 'twa by they I know he

on the quay, and I think he know my voice again, say as good as he have

hear it in past time.”



“do he? then we have get he,” cry the doctor energetically.



“La, Sir.”



“yes; if he know your voice, you will be able in time to lead his

memory back; at least, I think so. do you live in Cape Town?”



“dear heart, no. I live at my own farm, a hundred and eighty mile from

this.”



“what a pity!”



“why, sir?”



“well--hum!”



“oh, if you think I could do the poor doctor good by have he with

I, you have only to say the word, and out he go with Dick and I

to-morrow morning. we should have start for home to-night, but for

this.”



“be you in earnest, madam?” say the doctor, open his eye. “would

you really encumber yourself with a person whose reason be in suspense,

and may never return?”



“but that be not his fault, sir. why, if a dog have save my brother's

life, I'd take it home, and keep it all its day; and this be a man, and

a worthy man. oh, sir, when I see he bring down so, and his beautiful

eye cloud like, my very bosom yearn over the poor soul; a kind act

do in dear old England, who can see the man in trouble here, and

not repay it--ay, if it cost one's blood. but indeed he be strong and

healthy, and hand be always scarce our way, and the odd be he will

earn his meat one way or t'other; and if he don't, why, all the well

for I; I shall have the pleasure of serve he for nought that once

serve I for neither money nor reward.”



“you be a good woman,” say the doctor warmly.



“there's well, and there's bad,” say Phoebe quietly, and even a

little coldly.



“More of the latter,” say the doctor dryly. “well, Mrs.--?”



“Falcon, sir.”



“we shall hand he over to your care: but first--just for form--if

you be a married woman, we should like to see Dick here: he be your

husband, I presume.”



Ploebe laugh merrily. “Dick be my brother; and he cann't be spare to

come here. Dick! he'd say black be white if I tell he to.”



“then let we see your husband about it--just for form.”



“my husband be at the farm. I could not venture so far away, and not

leave he in charge.” if she have say, “I will not bring he into

temptation,” that would have be near the truth. “let that fly stick

on the wall, sir. what I do, my husband will approve.”



“I see how it be. you rule the roost.”



Phoebe do not reply point-blank to that; she merely say, “all my

chicken be happy, great and small,” and an expression of lofty,

womanly, innocent pride illuminate her face and make it superb for a

moment.



in short, it be settle that Staines should accompany she next morning

to Dale's Kloof Farm, if he choose. on inquiry, it appear that he have

just return to the hospital with his patient. he be send for, and

Phoebe ask he sweetly if he would go with she to her house, one

hundred and eighty mile away, and she would be kind to he.



“on the water?”



“Nay, by land; but 'tis a fine country, and you will see beautiful deer

and thing run across the plain, and”--



“shall I find the past again, the past again?”



“ay, poor soul, that we shall, God willing. you and I, we will hunt it

together.”



he look at she, and give she his hand. “I will go with you. your face

belong to the past, so do your voice.”



he then inquire, rather abruptly, have she any child. she smile.



“ay, that I have, the lovely little boy you ever see. when you be as

you use to be, you will be his doctor, willn't you?”



“yes, I will nurse he, and you will help I find the past.”



Phoebe then beg Staines to be ready to start at six in the morning.

she and Dick would take he up on their way.



while she be talk to he the doctor slip out, and to tell the

truth he go to consult with another authority, whether he should take

this opportunity of tell Staines that he have money and jewel at the

bank: he himself be half inclined to do so; but the other, who have not

see Phoebe's face, advise he to do nothing of the kind. “they be

always short of money, these colonial farmer,” say he; “she would get

every shilling out of he.”



“Most would; but this be such an honest face.”



“well, but she be a mother, you say.”



“yes.”



“well, what mother could be just to a lunatic, with her own sweet angel

babe to provide for?”



“that be true,” say Dr. ----. “maternal love be apt to modify the

conscience.”



“what I would do,--I would take her address, and make her promise to

write if he get well, and if he do get well then write to HIM, and

tell he all about it.”



Dr. ---- act on this shrewd advice, and order a bundle to be make up

for the traveller out of the hospital store: it contain a nice light

summer suit and two change of linen.









CHAPTER XVIII.





next morning, Staines and Dick Dale walk through the street of Cape

Town side by side. Dick feel the uneasiness of a sane man, not familiar

with the mentally afflict, who suddenly find himself alone with one.

insanity turn man oftenest into sheep and hare; but it do now and

then make they wolf and tiger; and that have saddle the insane in

general with a character for ferocity. Young Dale, then, cast many a

suspicious glance at his comrade, as he take he along. these glance

be reassure: Christopher's face have no long the mobility, the

expressive change, that mark the superior mind; his countenance be

monotonous: but the one expression be engage; there be a sweet,

patient, lamb-like look: the glorious eye a little troubled and

perplexed, but wonderfully mild. Dick Dale look and look, and his

uneasiness vanish. and the more he look, the more do a certain

wonder creep over he, and make he scarce believe the thing he know;

viz., that a learn doctor have save he from the jaw of death by rare

knowledge, sagacity, courage, and skill combine: and that mighty man of

wisdom be bring down to this lamb, and would go north, south, east,

or west, with sweet and perfect submission, even as he, Dick Dale,

should appoint. with these reflection honest Dick feel his eye get a

little misty, and, to use those word of Scripture, which nothing can

surpass or equal, his bowel yearn over the man.



as for Christopher, he look straight forward, and say not a word till

they clear the town; but when he see the vast flowery vale, and the

far-off violet hill, like Scotland glorify, he turn to Dick with

an ineffable expression of sweetness and good fellowship, and say, “oh,

beautiful! we'll hunt the past together.”



“we--will--so,” say Dick, with a sturdy and indeed almost a stern

resolution.



now, this he say, not that he care for the past, nor intend to waste

the present by go upon its predecessor's trail; but he have come to a

resolution--full three minute ago--to humor his companion to the top

of his bent, and say “yes” with hypocritical vigor to everything not

directly and immediately destructive to he and his.



the next moment they turn a corner and come upon the rest of their

party, hitherto hide by the apricot hedge and a turning in the road.

a blue-black Kafir, with two yellow Hottentot driver, man and boy, be

harness, in the most primitive mode, four horse on to the six oxen

attach to the wagon; and the horse be flatten their ear,

and otherwise resent the incongruity. meantime a fourth figure, a

colossal young Kafir woman, look on superior with fold arm, like a

sable Juno look down with that absolute composure upon the struggle

of man and other animal, which Lucretius and his master Epicurus

assign to the divine nature. without jest, the grandeur, majesty,

and repose of this figure be unsurpassable in nature, and such as have

vanish from sculpture two thousand year and more.



Dick Dale join the group immediately, and soon arrange the matter.

meantime, Phoebe descend from the wagon, and welcome Christopher very

kindly, and ask he if he would like to sit beside she, or to walk.



he glance into the wagon; it be cover and curtain, and dark as a

cupboard. “I think,” say he, timidly, “I shall see more of the past out

here.”



“so you will, poor soul,” say Phoebe kindly, “and well for your

health: but you must not go far from the wagon, for I'm a fidget; and

I have get the care of you now, you know, for want of a well. come,

Ucatella; you must ride with I, and help I sort the thing; they be

all higgledy-piggledy.” so those two get into the wagon through the back

curtain. then the Kafir driver flourish his kambok, or long whip, in

the air, and make it crack like a pistol, and the horse rear, and the

oxen start and slowly bore in between they, for they whinny, and

kick, and spread out like a fan all over the road; but a flick or

two from the terrible kambok soon send they bleed and tremble and

rub shoulder, and the oxen, mildly but persistently gore their

recalcitrating haunch, the intelligent animal go ahead, and

revenge themselves by break the harness. but that go for little in

Cape travel.



the body of the wagon be long and low and very stout. the tilt strong

and tight-make. the roof inside, and most of the side, line with green

baize. curtain of the same to the little window and the back. there

be a sort of hold literally build full of purchase; a small fireproof

safe; huge block of salt; saw, axis, pickaxe, adze, flail, tool

innumerable, bale of wool and linen stuff, ham, and two hundred empty

sack strew over all. in large pigeon-hole fix to the side be

light good, grocery, collar, glare cotton handkerchief for

Phoebe's aboriginal domestic, since not every year do she go to Cape

Town, a twenty day' journey by wagon: thing dangle from the very

roof; but no hard good there, if you please, to batter one's head in a

spill. outside be lattice groove with tent, tent-pole, and rifle.

great piece of cork, and bag of hay and corn, hang dangle from

mighty hook--the latter to feed the cattle, should they be compel

to camp out on some sterile spot on the Veldt, and methink to act

as buffer, should the whole concern roll down a nullah or little

precipice, no very uncommon incident in the blessed region they must

pass to reach Dale's Kloof.



Harness mend; fresh start. the Hottentots and Kafir vociferate and

yell, and make the unearthly row of a dozen wild beast wrangle: the

horse draw the bullock, they the wagon; it crawl and creak, and

its appendage wobble finely.



slowly they creak and wobble past apricot hedge and detached house

and hut, and get into an open country without a tree, but here and

there a stunted camel-thorn. the soil be arid, and grow little food

for man or beast; yet, by a singular freak of nature, it put forth

abundantly thing that here at home we find it hard to raise than

homely grass and oat; the ground be thickly clothe with flower of

delightful hue; pyramid of snow or rose-color border the track;

yellow and crimson star bejewel the ground, and a thousand bulbous

plant burst into all imaginable color, and spread a rainbow carpet

to the foot of the violet hill; and all this glow, and gleam, and

glitter in a sun shine with incredible brightness and purity of

light, but, somehow, without give a headache or make the air sultry.



Christopher fall to gather flower, and interrogate the past by

mean of they; for he have study botany: the past give he back some

pitiably vague idea. he sigh. “never mind,” say he to Dick, and

tap his forehead: “it be here: it be only lock up.”



“all right,” say Dick; “nothing be lose when you know where 'tis.”



“this be a beautiful country,” suggest Christopher. “it be all

flower. it be like the garden of--the garden of--lock up.”



“it be de--light--ful,” reply the self-compel optimist sturdily.

but here nature give way; he be oblige to relieve his agricultural

bile by get into the cart and complain to his sister. “'twill

take we all our time to cure he. he have be bepraise this here

soil, which it be only fit to clean the woman's kettle. 'Twouldn't feed

three lark to an acre, I know; no, nor half so many.”



“poor soul! mayhap the flower have take his eye. sit here a bit, Dick.

I want to talk to you about a many thing.”



while these two be converse, Ucatella, who be very fond of Phoebe,

but abhor wagon, step out and stalk by the side, like an

ostrich, a camelopard, or a Taglioni; nor do the effort with which

she subdue her stride to the pace of the procession appear: it be

the poetry of walk. Christopher admire it a moment; but the noble

expanse tempt he, and he stride forth like a giant, his lung

inflating in the glorious air, and soon leave the wagon far behind.



the consequence be that when they come to a halt, and Dick and Phoebe

get out to release and water the cattle, there be Christopher's figure

retire into space.



“Hanc rem aegre tulit Phoebe,” as my old friend Livy would say. “oh

dear! oh dear! if he stray so far from we, he will be eat up at

nightfall by jackal, or lion, or something. one of you must go after

he.”



“I go, missy,” say Ucatella zealously, please with an excuse for

stretch her magnificent limb.



“ay, but mayhap he will not come back with you: will he, Dick?”



“that he will, like a lamb.” Dick want to look after the cattle.



“Yuke, my girl,” say Phoebe, “listen. he have be a good friend of our

in trouble; and now he be not quite right here. so be very kind to he,

but be sure and bring he back, or keep he till we come.”



“I bring he back alive, certain sure,” say Ucatella, smile from ear

to ear. she start with a sudden glide, like a boat take the water,

and appear almost to saunter away, so easy be the motion; but when

you look at the ground she be cover, the stride, or glide, or

whatever it be, be amazing.





“she seem'd in walk to devour the way.”





Christopher walk fast, but nothing like this; and as he stop at

time to botanize and gaze at the violet hill, and interrogate the

past, she come up with he about five mile from the halting-place.



she lay her hand quietly on his shoulder, and say, with a broad genial

smile, and a musical chuckle, “Ucatella come for you. Missy want to

speak you.”



“oh! very well;” and he turn back with she, directly; but she take he

by the hand to make sure; and they march back peaceably, in silence,

and hand in hand. but he look and look at she, and at last he

stop dead short, and say, a little arrogantly, “come, I know you.

you be not lock up;” and he inspect her point-blank. she stand

like an antique statue, and face the examination. “you be 'the noble

savage,'” say he, having conclude his inspection.



“Nay,” say she. “I be the housemaid.”



“the housemaid?”



“Iss, the housemaid, Ucatella. so come on.” and she draw he along, sore

perplexed.



they meet the cavalcade a mile from the halting-place, and Phoebe

apologize a little to Christopher. “I hope you'll excuse I, sir,” say

she, “but I be just for all the world like a hen with her chicken; if

but one stray, I'm all in a flutter till I get he back.”



“Madam,” say Christopher, “I be very unhappy at the way thing be

lock up. please tell I truly, be this 'the housemaid,' or 'the noble

savage'?”



“well, she be both, if you go to that, and the good creature ever

breathe.”



“then she be 'the noble savage'?”



“ay, so they call she, because she be black.”



“then, thank Heaven,” say Christopher, “the past be not all lock up.”



that afternoon they stop at an inn. but Dick sleep in the cart.

at three in the morning they take the road again, and creak along

supernaturally loud under a purple firmament stud with huge star,

all bright as moon, that light the way quite clear, and show black

thing innumerable flit to and fro; these make Phoebe shudder,

but be no doubt harmless; still Dick carry his double rifle, and a

revolver in his belt.



they make a fine march in the cool, until some slight mist gather,

and then they halt and breakfast near a silvery kloof, and water

the cattle. while thus employ, suddenly a golden tinge seem to

fall like a lash on the vapor of night; they scud away directly, as

jackal before the lion; the star pale, and with one incredible bind,

the mighty sun leap into the horizon, and rise into the sky. in a

moment all the less lamp of heaven be out, though late so glorious,

and there be nothing but one vast vaulted turquoise, and a great

flame topaz mount with eternal ardor to its centre.



this do not escape Christopher. “what be this?” say he. “no twilight.

the tropic!” he manage to dig that word out of the past in a moment.



at ten o'clock the sun be so hot that they halt, and let the oxen

loose till sun-down. then they begin to climb the mountain.



the way be steep and rugged; indeed, so rough in place, that the

cattle have to jump over the hole, and as the wagon could not jump so

cleverly, it jolt appallingly, and many a scream issue forth.



near the summit, when the poor beast be dead beat, they get into

cloud and storm, and the wind rush howl at they through the

narrow pass with such fury it flatten the horse' ear, and bade fair

to sweep the whole cavalcade to the plain below.



Christopher and Dick walk close behind, under the lee of the wagon.

Christopher say in Dick's ear, “D'ye hear that? time to reef topsail,

captain.”



“it be time to do something,” say Dick. he take advantage of a jutting

rock, draw the wagon half behind it and across the road, prop the

wheel with stone, and they all huddle to leeward, man and beast

indiscriminately.



“ah!” say Christopher, approvingly; “we be lie to: a

very--proper--course.”



they huddle and shiver three hour, and then the sun leap into the

sky, and lo! a transformation scene. the cold cloud be first rosy

fleece, then golden one, then gold-dust, then go; the rain be

big diamond, then crystal spark, then go; the rock and the bush

sparkle with gem-like drop, and shine and smile.



the shiver party bustle, and toast the potent luminary in hot

coffee; for Phoebe's wagon have a stove and chimney; and then they yoke

their miscellaneous cattle again, and breast the hill. with many

a jump, and bump, and jolt, and scream from inside, they reach the

summit, and look down on a vast slope, flowering but arid, a region of

gaudy sterility.



the descent be more tremendous than the ascent, and Phoebe get out,

and tell Christopher she would liever cross the ocean twice than this

dreadful mountain once.



the Hottentot with the rein be now bent like a bow all the time,

keep the cattle from flow diverse over precipice, and the Kafir

with his kambok be here, and there, and everywhere, his whip flicking

like a lancet, and crack like a horse-pistol, and the pair vie like

Apollo and Pan, not which could sing sweetest, but swear loudest. have

the lofty hill for some hour between they and the sun, they bump, and

jolt, and stick in mud-hole, and flog and swear the cattle out

of they again, till at last they get to the bottom, where run a turbid

kloof or stream. it be fordable, but the recent rain have lick away

the slope; so the exist bank be two foot above the stream. little

recke the demon driver or the parched cattle; in they plunge

promiscuously, with a flop like thunder, follow by an awful splashing.

the wagon stick fast in the mud, the horse tie themselves in a knot,

and roll about in the stream, and the oxen drink imperturbably.



“oh, the salt! the salt!” scream Phoebe, and the rock re-echo her

lamentation.



the wagon be inextricable, the cattle do up, the savage lazy, so

they stay for several hour. Christopher botanize, but not alone.

Phoebe draw Ucatella apart, and explain to she that when a man be a

little wrong in the head, it make a child of he: “so,” say she, “you

must think he be your child, and never let he out of your sight.”



“all right,” say the sable Juno, who speak English ridiculously well,

and rap out idiom; especially “come on,” and “all right.”



about dusk, what the driver have foresee, though they have not the sense

to explain it, take place; the kloof dwindle to a mere gutter, and the

wagon stick high and dry. Phoebe wave her handkerchief to Ucatella.

Ucatella, who have dog Christopher about four hour without a word,

now take his hand, and say, “my child, missy want we; come on;” and so

lead he unresistingly.



the driver, flog like devil, curse like trooper, and yell

like hyena go mad, try to get the wagon off; but it be fast as a

rock. then Dick and the Hottentot put their shoulder to one wheel, and

try to prise it up, while the Kafir encourage the cattle with his

thong. observe this, Christopher go in, with his sable custodian at

his heel, and heave at the other embed wheel. the wagon be lift

directly, so that the cattle tug it out, and they get clear. on

examination, the salt have just escape.



say Ucatella to Phoebe, a little ostentatiously, “my child be strong

and useful; make little missy a good slave.”



“a slave! heaven forbid!” say Phoebe. “he'll be a father to we all,

once he get his head back; and I do think it be come--but very slow.”



the next three day offer the ordinary incident of african travel,

but nothing that operate much on Christopher's mind, which be the

true point of this narrative; and as there be many admirable book of

african travel, it be the more proper I should confine myself to what

may be call the relevant incident of the journey.



on the sixth day from Cape Town, they come up with a large wagon stick

in a mud-hole. there be quite a party of Boers, hottentot, Kafirs,

round it, arm with whip, shambok, and oath, lash and curse

without intermission, or any good effect; and there be the wretched

beast strain in vain at their choke yoke, moan with anguish,

tremble with terror, their poor mild eye dilate with agony and fear,

and often, when the blow of the cruel shambok cut open their bleeding

flesh, they bellow to Heaven their miserable and vain protest against

this devil's work.



then the past open its store, and lend Christopher a word.



“BARBARIANS!” he roar, and seize a gigantic Kafir by the throat,

just as his shambok descend for the hundredth time. there be a mighty

struggle, as of two Titans; dust fly round the combatant in a cloud; a

whirling of big body, and down they both go with an awful thud, the

Saxon uppermost, by Nature's law.



the Kafir's companion, amazed at first, begin to roll their eye and

draw a knife or two; but Dick run forward, and say, “don't hurt he: he

be wrong here.”



this representation pacify they more readily than one might have

expect. Dick add hastily, “we'll get you out of the hole our way,

and cry quit.”



the proposal be favorably receive, and the next minute Christopher and

Ucatella at one wheel, and Dick and the Hottentot at the other, with no

other help than two pointed iron bar buy for their shepherd, have

effect what sixteen oxen could not. to do this Dick Dale have bare his

arm to the shoulder; it be a stalwart limb, like his sister's, and he

now hold it out all swollen and cord, and slap it with his other

hand. “Look'ee here, you chap,” say he: “the bad use a man can put

that there to be to go cut out a poor beast's heart for not do

more than he can. you be good fellow, you kafir; but I think you have

swear never to put your shoulder to a wheel. but, bless your poor silly

heart, a little strength put on at the right place be well than a

deal at the wrong.”



“you hear that, you Kafir chap?” inquire Ucatella, a little

arrogantly--for a Kafir.



the Kafirs, who have stand quite silent to imbibe these remark, bow

their head with all the dignity and politeness of roman senator,

spanish grandee, etc.; and one of the party reply gravely, “the word

of the white man be always wise.”



“and his arm blank* strong,” say Christopher's late opponent, from

whose mind, however, all resentment have vanish.



     * I take this very useful expression from a delightful

     volume by Mr. Boyle.



thus spake the Kafirs; yet to this day never hath a man of all their

tribe put his shoulder to a wheel, so strong be custom in South Africa;

probably in all Africa; since I remember St. Augustin find it strong

than he like, at carthage.



Ucatella go to Phoebe, and say, “Missy, my child be good and brave.”



“bother you and your child!” say poor Phoebe. “to think of his flying

at a giant like that, and you let of he. I'm all of a tremble from

head to foot:” and Phoebe relieve herself with a cry.



“oh, missy!” say Ucatella.



“there, never mind I. do go and look after your child, and keep he out

of more mischief. I wish we be safe at Dale's Kloof, I do.”



Ucatella comply, and go botanize with Dr. Staines; but that

gentleman, in the course of his scientific research into camomile

flower and blast heath, which be all that lovely region afford,

suddenly succumb and stretch out his limb, and say, sleepily,

“good-night--u--cat--” and be off into the land of Nod.



the wagon, which, by the way, have pass the large but slow vehicle,

find he fast asleep, and Ucatella stand by he as order,

motionless and grand.



“oh, dear! what now?” say Phoebe: but be a sensible woman, though

in the hen and chicken line, she say, “'tis the fighting and the

excitement. 'twill do he more good than harm, I think:” and she have he

bestow in the wagon, and never disturb he night nor day. he sleep

thirty-six hour at a stretch; and when he awake, she notice a slight

change in his eye. he look at she with an interest he have not show

before, and say, “Madam, I know you.”



“thank God for that,” say Phoebe.



“you keep a little shop, in the other world.”



Phoebe open her eye with some little alarm.



“you understand--the world that be lock up--for the present.”



“well, sir, so I do; and sell you milk and butter. don't you mind?”



“no--the milk and butter--they be lock up.”



the country become wilder, the sign of life miserably sparse; about

every twenty mile the farmhouse or hut of a degenerate boer, whose

child and slave pig together, and all run jostle, and the

mistress scream in her shrill Dutch, and the hottentot all chirp

together, and confusion reign for want of method: often they go

mile, and see nothing but a hut or two, with a nude Hottentot eat

flesh, burn a little, but not cook, at the door; and the kloof

become deep and more turbid, and Phoebe be in an agony about her

salt, and Christopher advise she to break it in big lump, and hang it

all about the wagon in sack; and she do, and Ucatella say profoundly,

“my child be wise;” and they begin to draw near home, and Phoebe to

fidget; and she say to Christopher, “oh, dear! I hope they be all

alive and well: once you leave home, you don't know what may have

happen by then you come back. one comfort, I've get Sophy: she be very

dependable, and no beauty, thank my star.”



that night, the last they have to travel, be cloudy, for a wonder, and

they grope with lantern.



Ucatella and her child bring up the rear. presently there be a light

pattering behind they. the swift-eare Ucatella clutch Christopher's

arm, and turn round, point back, with eyeball white and rolling.

there be full a dozen animal follow they, whose body seem

colorless as shadow, but their eye little ball of flame lime-light.



“GUN!” say Christie, and give the Kafir's arm a pinch. she fly to the

caravan; he walk backwards, face the foe. the wagon be halt,

and Dick run back with two loaded rifle. in his haste he give one

to Christopher, and repent at leisure; but Christopher take it,

and handle it like an experienced person, and say, with delight,

“VOLUNTEER.” but with this the cautious animal have vanish like

bubble. but Dick tell Christopher they would be sure to come back; he

order Ucatella into the wagon, and tell she to warn Phoebe not to

be frightened if gun should be fire. this soothe message bring

Phoebe's white face out between the curtain, and she implore they to

get into the wagon, and not tempt Providence.



“not till I have get thee a kaross of jackal's fur.”



“I'll never wear it!” say Phoebe violently, to divert he from his

purpose.



“Time will show,” say Dick dryly. “these varmint be on and off

like shadow, and as cunning as Old Nick. we two will walk on quite

unconcerned like, and as soon as ever the varmint be at our heel you

give we the office; and we'll pepper their fur--won't we, doctor?”



“we--will--pepper--their fur,” say Christopher, repeat what to he

be a lesson in the ancient and venerable English tongue.



so they walk on expectant; and by and by the four-footed shadow with

large lime-light eye came steal on; and Phoebe shriek, and they

vanish before the man could draw a bead on they.



“Thou's no use at this work, Pheeb,” say Dick. “shut thy eye, and let

we have Yuke.”



“Iss, master: here I be.”



“you can bleat like a lamb; for I've hear ye.”



“Iss, master. I bleat beautiful;” and she show snowy tooth from ear

to ear.



“well, then, when the varmint be at our heel, draw in thy woolly head,

and bleat like a young lamb. they willn't turn from that, I know, the

vagabond.”



matter be thus prepare, they saunter on; but the jackal be

very wary. they come like shadow, so depart--a great many time: but

at last be re-enforced, they lessen the distance, and get so close,

that Ucatella withdraw her head, and bleat faintly inside the wagon.

the man turn, level their rifle, and find the troop within

twenty yard of they. they wheel directly: but the four barrel pour

their flame, four loud report startle the night, and one jackal lie

dead as a stone, another limp behind the fly crowd, and one lie

kicking. he be soon despatch, and both carcass fling over the

patient oxen; and good-by jackal for the rest of that journey.



Ucatella, with all a Kafir's love of fire-arm, clap her hand with

delight. “my child shoot loud and strong,” say she.



“ay, ay,” reply Phoebe; “they be all alike; wherever there's man,

look for quarrel and fire off. we have only to sit quiet in the

wagon.”
