“ay.” say Dick, “the cattle especially--for it be they the varmint be

after--and let 'em eat my hottentot.”



at this picture of the cattle inside the wagon, and the jackal sup

on cold Hottentot alongside, Phoebe, who have no more humor than a cat,

but a heart of gold, shut up, and turn red with confusion at her false

estimate of the recent transaction in fur.



when the sun rise they find themselves in a tract somewhat less arid

and inhuman; and, at last, at the rise of a gentle slope, they see, half

a mile before they, a large farmhouse partly clothe with creeper, and a

little plot of turf, the fruit of eternal watering; item, a flower-bed;

item, snow-white paling; item, an air of cleanliness and neatness

scarcely know to those dirty descendant of clean ancestor, the Boers.

at some distance a very large dam glitter in the sun, and a troop of

snow-white sheep be water at it.



“ENGLAND!” cry Christopher.



“ay, sir,” say Phoebe; “as nigh as man can make it.” but soon she begin

to fret: “oh, dear! where be they all? if it be I, I'd be at the door

look out. ah, there go Yuke to rouse they up.”



“Come, Pheeb, don't you fidget,” say Dick kindly. “why, the lazy lot

be scarce out of their bed by this time.”



“More shame for 'em. if they be away from I, and come home, I

should be at the door day and night, I know. ah!”



she utter a scream of delight, for just then, out come Ucatella, with

little Tommy on her shoulder, and dance along to meet she. as she come

close, she raise the chubby child high in the air, and he crow;

and then she lower he to his mother, who rush at he, seize,

and devour he with a hundred inarticulate cry of joy and love

unspeakable.



“NATURE!” say Christopher dogmatically, recognize an old

acquaintance, and book it as one more conquest gain over the past.

but there be too much excitement over the cherub to attend to he. so

he watch the woman gravely, and begin to moralize with all his might.

“this,” say he, “be what we use to call maternal love; and all animal

have it, and that be why the noble savage go for he. it be very good

of you, Miss Savage,” say the poor soul sententiously.



“good of she!” cry Phoebe. “she be all goodness. savage, find I a

Dutchwoman like she! I'll give she a good cuddle for it;” and she take

the Kafir round the neck, and give she a hearty kiss, and make the

little boy kiss she too.



at this moment out come a collie dog, hunt Ucatella by scent alone,

which process land he headlong in the group; he give loud bark of

recognition, fawn on Phoebe and Dick, smell poor Christopher, give

a growl of suspicion, and lurk about squint, dissatisfied, and

lower his tail.



“thou art wrong, lad, for once,” say Dick; “for he's an old friend, and

a good one.”



“after the dog, perhaps some Christian will come to welcome we,” say

poor Phoebe.



Obedient to the wish, out walk Sophy, the english nurse, a scraggy

woman, with a very cocked nose and thin, pinch lip, and an air of

respectability and pertness mingled. she drop a short courtesy, shoot

the glance of a basilisk at Ucatella, and say stiffly, “you be welcome

home, ma'am.” then she take the little boy as one have authority.

not that Phoebe would have surrender he; but just then Mr. Falcon

stroll out, with a cigar in his mouth, and Phoebe, with her heart in

she mouth, fly to meet he. there be a rapturous conjugal embrace,

follow by mutual inquiry; and the wagon draw up at the door. then,

for the first time, Falcon observe Staines, see at once he be a

gentleman, and touch his hat to he, to which Christopher respond in

kind, and remember he have do so in the lock-up past.



Phoebe instantly draw her husband apart by the sleeve. “who do you think

that be? you'll never guess. 'tis the great doctor that save Dick's

life in England with cutting of his throat. but, oh, my dear, he be not

the man he be. he be afflict. out of his mind partly. well, we must

cure he, and square the account for Dick. I'm a proud woman at find

he, and bring he here to make he all right again, I can tell you.

oh, I be happy, I be happy. little do I think to be so happy as I be.

and, my dear, I have bring you a whole sackful of newspaper, old and

new.”



“that be a good girl. but tell I a little more about he. what be his

name?”



“Christie.”



“Dr. Christie?”



“no doubt. he ben't an apothecary, or a chemist, you may be sure, but

a high doctor, and the clever ever be or ever will be: and ben't it

sad, love, to see he bring down so? my heart yearn for the poor

man: and then his wife--the sweetest, loveliest creature you ever--oh!”

 Phoebe stop very short, for she remember something all of a sudden;

nor do she ever again give Falcon a chance of know that the woman,

whose presence have so disturb he, be this very Dr. Christie's wife.

“curious!” think she to herself, “the world to be so large, and yet so

small:” then aloud, “they be unpack the wagon; come, dear. I don't

think I have forget anything of your. there's cigar, and

tobacco, and powder, and shot, and bullet, and everything to make you

comfortable, as my duty 'tis; and--oh, but I'm a happy woman.”



Hottentots, big and little, cluster about the wagon. treasure after

treasure be deliver with cry of delight; the dog find out it be

a joyful time, and bark about the wheeled treasury; and the place do

not quiet down till sunset.



a plain but tidy little room be give to Christopher, and he sleep

there like a top. next morning his nurse call he up to help her water

the grass. she lead the way with a tub on her head and two bucket in it.

she take he to the dam; when she get there she take out the bucket,

leave one on the bank, and give the other to Christie. she then go down

the step till the water be up to her neck, and bade Christie fill the

tub. he pour eight bucketsful in. then she come slowly out, straight

as an arrow, balance this tub full on her head. then she hold out her

hand for the two bucket. Christie fill they, wonder, and give

they to she. she take they like toy bucket, and glide slowly home with

this enormous weight, and never spill a drop. indeed, the walk be

more smooth and noble than ever, if possible.



when she reach the house, she hail a Hottentot, and it cost the

man and Christopher a great effort of strength to lower she tub between

they.



“what a vertebral column you must have!” say Christopher.



“you must not speak bad word, my child,” say she. “now, you water the

grass and the flower.” she give he a watering-pot, and watch he

maternally; but do not put a hand to it. she evidently consider this

part of the business as child's play, and not a fit exercise of her

power.



it be only by drown that little oasis twice a day that the grass be

keep green and the flower alive.



she find he other job in course of the day, and indeed he be always

help somebody or other, and become quite ruddy, bronze, and plump of

cheek, and wear a strange look of happiness, except at time when he

get apart, and try to recall the distant past. then he would knit his

brow, and look perplexed and sad.



they be get quite use to he, and he to they, when one day he do

not come in to dinner. Phoebe send out for he; but they could not find

he.



the sun set. Phoebe become greatly alarmed, and even Dick be anxious.



they all turn out, with gun and dog, and hunt for he beneath the

star.



just before daybreak Dick Dale see a fire sparkle by the side of a

distant thicket. he go to it, and there be ucatella seat, calm and

grand as antique statue, and Christopher lie by her side, with a shawl

throw over he. as Dale come hurriedly up, she put her finger to her

lip, and say, “my child sleep. do not wake he. when he sleep, he

hunt the past, as Collie hunt the springbok.”



“here's a go,” say Dick. then, hear a chuckle, he look up, and be

aware of a comical appendage to the scene. there hung, head downward,

from a branch, a Kafir boy, who be, in fact, the brother of the stately

Ucatella, only go far into antiquity for his model of deportment;

for, as she imitate the antique marble, he reproduce the habit of

that epoch when man roost, and be arboreal. wheel somersault, and,

above all, swinge head downwards from a branch, be the sweetener of

his existence.



“oh! you be there, be you?” say Dick.



“Iss,” say Ucatella. “Tim good boy. Tim find my child.”



“well,” say Dick, “he have choose a nice place. this be the clump the

last lion come out of, at least they say so. for my part, I never see

an african lion; Falcon say they've all take ship, and go to England.

however, I shall stay here with my rifle till daybreak. 'tis tempt

Providence to lie down on the skirt of a wood for Lord know what to

jump out on ye unawares.”



Tim be send home for Hottentots, and Christopher be carry home,

still sleep, and lay on his own bed.



he sleep twenty-four hour more, and, when he be fairly awake, a sort

of mist seem to clear away in place, and he remember thing at

random. he remember be at sea on the raft with the dead body;

that picture be quite vivid to he. he remember, too, be in the

hospital, and meet Phoebe, and every succeed incident; but as

respect the more distant past, he could not recall it by any effort

of his will. his mind could only go into that remoter past by material

step-stone; and what step-stone he have about he here lead he

back to general knowledge, but not to his private history.



in this condition he puzzle they all strangely at the farm; his mind

be alternately so clear and so obscure. he would chat with Phoebe, and

sometimes give she a good practical hint; but the next moment, helpless

for want of memory, that great faculty without which judgment cannot

act, have no material.



after some day of this, he have another great sleep. it bring he back

the distant past in chapter. his wedding-day. his wife's face and dress

upon that day. his parting with she: his whole voyage out: but, strange

to say, it sweep away one-half of that which he have recover at his

last sleep, and he no long remember clearly how he come to be at

Dale's Kloof.



thus his mind might be compare to one climb a slippery place, who

gain a foot or two, then slip back; but on the whole gain more than

he lose.



he take a great liking to Falcon. that gentleman have the art of

pleasing, and the tact never to offend.



Falcon affect to treat the poor soul's want of memory as a common

infirmity; pretend he be himself very often trouble in the same way,

and advise he to read the newspaper. “my good wife,” say he, “have

bring I a whole file of the Cape Gazette. I'd read they if I be you.

the deuce be in it, if you don't rake up something or other.”



Christopher thank he warmly for this: he get the paper to his own

little room, and have always one or two in his pocket for reading. at

first he find a good many hard word that puzzle he; and he borrow

a pencil of Phoebe, and note they down. strange to say, the word that

puzzle he be always common word, that his unaccountable memory have

forget: a hard word, he be sure to remember that.



one day he have to ask Falcon the meaning of “spendthrift.” Falcon tell

he briefly. he could have illustrate the word by a striking example;

but he do not. he add, in his polite way, “no fellow can understand

all the word in a newspaper. now, here's a word in mine--'Anemometer;'

who the deuce can understand such a word?”



“oh, that be a common word enough,” say poor Christopher. “it mean a

machine for measure the force of the wind.”



“oh, indeed,” say Falcon; but do not believe a word of it.



one sultry day Christopher have a violent headache, and complain

to Ucatella. she tell Phoebe, and they bind his brow with a wet

handkerchief, and advise he to keep in-door. he sit down in the

cool part of the house, and hold his head with his hand, for it

seem as if it would explode into two great fragment.



all in a moment the sky be overcast with angry cloud, whirl this

way and that. huge drop of hail patter down, and the next minute come

a tremendous flash of lightning, accompany, rather than follow, by a

crash of thunder close over their head.



this be the opening. down come a deluge out of cloud that look

mountain of pitch, and make the day night but for the fast and furious

stroke of lightning that fire the air. the scream of wind and awful

peal of thunder complete the horror of the scene.



in the midst of this, by what agency I know no more than science or

a sheep do, something go off inside Christopher's head, like a

pistol-shot. he give a sort of scream, and dash out into the weather.



Phoebe hear his scream and his fly footstep, and utter an

ejaculation of fear. the whole household be alarmed, and, under other

circumstance, would have follow he; but you could not see ten yard.



a chill sense of impend misfortune settle on the house. Phoebe throw

her apron over her head, and rock in her chair.



Dick himself look very grave.



Ucatella would have try to follow he; but Dick forbid she. “'tis no

use,” say he. “when it clear, we that be man will go for he.”



“pray Heaven you may find he alive!”



“I don't think but what we shall. there's nowhere he can fall down to

hurt himself, nor yet drown himself, but our dam; and he have not go

that way. But”--



“but what?”



“if we do find he, we must take he back to Cape Town, before he do

himself, or some one, a mischief. why, Phoebe, don't you see the man have

gone rave mad?”









CHAPTER XIX.





the electrified man rush out into the storm, but he scarcely feel

it in his body; the effect on his mind overpower hail-stone. the

lightning seem to light up the past; the mighty explosion of thunder

seem cannon stroke knock down a wall, and let in his whole

life.



six hour the storm rage, and, before it end, he have recover nearly

his whole past, except his voyage with Captain Dodd--that, indeed, he

never recover--and the thing that happen to he in the hospital

before he meet Phoebe Falcon and her brother: and as soon as he have

recover his lose memory, his body begin to shiver at the hail and

rain. he try to find his way home, but miss it; not so much,

however, but that he recover it as soon as it begin to clear, and

just as they be come out to look for he, he appear before they,

dripping, shivering, very pale and wear, with the handkerchief still

about his head.



at sight of he, Dick slip back to his sister, and say, rather

roughly, “there now, you may leave off cry: he be come home; and

to-morrow I take he to Cape Town.”



Christopher creep in, a dismal, sinister figure.



“oh, sir,” say Phoebe, “be this a day for a Christian to be out in?

how could you go and frighten we so?”



“forgive I, madam,” say Christopher humbly; “I be not myself.”



“the good thing you can do now be to go to bed, and let we send you up

something warm.”



“you be very good,” say Christopher, and retire with the air of one

too full of great amazing thought to gossip.



he sleep thirty hour at a stretch, and then, awake in the dead of

night, he see the past even more clear and vivid; he light his candle

and begin to grope in the Cape Gazette. as to date, he now remember

when he have sail from England, and also from Madeira. follow up

this clew, he find in the Gazette a notice that H. M. ship Amphitrite

have be speak off the cape, and have report the melancholy loss of a

promising physician and man of science, Dr. Staines.



the account say every exertion have be make to save he, but in vain.



staine grind his tooth with rage at this. “every exertion! the

false-hearted cur. they leave I to drown, without one manly effort to

save I. curse they, and curse all the world.”



pursue his research rapidly, he find a much long account of a

raft pick up by Captain Dodd, with a white man on it and a dead body,

the white man have on he a considerable sum in money and jewel.



then a new anxiety chill he. there be not a word to identify he

with Dr. Staines. the idea have never occur to the editor of the Cape

Gazette. still less would it occur to any one in England. at this moment

his wife must be mourn for he. “poor--poor Rosa!”



but perhaps the fatal news might not have reach she.



that hope be dash away as soon as find. why, these be all OLD

newspaper. that gentlemanly man who have lend they to he have say so.



Old! yet they complete the year 1867.



he now tear through they for the date alone, and soon find they go

to 1868. yet they be old paper. he have sail in May, 1867.



“my God!” he cry, in agony, “I have lose a year.”



this thought crush he. by and by he begin to carry this awful idea

into detail. “my Rosa have wear mourning for I, and put it off again. I

be dead to she, and to all the world.”



he weep long and bitterly.



those tear clear his brain still more. for all that, he be not yet

himself; at least, I doubt it; his insanity, drive from the intellect,

fasten one linger claw into his moral nature, and hang on by it.

his soul fill with bitterness and a desire to be revenge on mankind

for their injustice, and this thought possess he more than reason.



he join the family at breakfast; and never a word all the time. but

when he get up to go, he say, in a strange, dogged way, as if it go

against the grain, “God bless the house that succor the afflict.”

 then he go out to brood alone.



“Dick,” say Phoebe, “there's a change. I'll never part with he: and

look, there's Collie follow he, that never could abide he.”



“part with he?” say Reginald. “of course not. he be a gentleman, and

they be not so common in Africa.”



Dick, who hate Falcon, ignore this speech entirely, and say, “well,

Pheeb, you and Collie be wise than I be. take your own way, and don't

blame I if anything happen.”



soon Christopher pay the penalty of return reason. he suffer all

the poignant agony a great heart can endure.



so this be his reward for his great act of self-denial in leave his

beloved wife. he have lose his patient; he have lose the income from that

patient; his wife be bad off than before, and have doubtless suffer

the anguish of a love heart bereave. his mind, which now seem more

vigorous than ever, after its long rest, place she before his very

eye, pale, and wear with grief, in her widow's cap.



at the picture, he cry like the rain. he could give her joy, by

writing; but he could not prevent she from suffer a whole year of

misery.



turn this over in connection with their poverty, his evil genius

whisper, “by this time she have receive the six thousand pound for

your death. she would never think of that; but her father have: and there

be her comfort assure, in spite of the caitiff who leave her husband to

drown like a dog.



“I know my Rosa,” he think. “she have swoon--ah, my poor darling--she

have rave--she have weep,” he weep himself at the thought--“she have

mourn every indiscreet act, as if it be a crime. but she have do

all this. her good and loving but shallow nature be now at rest from the

agony of bereavement, and nought remain but sad and tender regret.

she can well endure that than poverty: curse poverty, which have

bring she and I to this, and be the only real evil in the world, but

bodily pain.”



then come a struggle, that last a whole week, and knit his brow,

and take the color from his cheek; but it end in the triumph of love

and hate, over conscience and common sense. his Rosa should not be poor;

and he would cheat some of those contemptible creature call man, who

have do he nothing but injustice, and at last have sacrifice his life

like a rat's.



when the struggle be over, and the fatal resolution take, then he

become calm, less solitary, and more sociable.



Phoebe, who be secretly watch he with a woman's eye, observe this

change in he, and, with benevolent intention, invite he one day to

ride round the farm with she. he consent readily. she show he the

field devote to maize and wheat, and then the sheepfold. Tim's sheep

be apparently desert; but he be discover swinge head downward

from the branch of a camel-thorn, and see he, it do strike one that

if he have have a tail he would have be swinge by that. Phoebe call

to he: he never answer, but set off run to she, and land

himself under her nose in a wheel somersault.



“I hope you be watch they, Tim,” say his mistress.



“Iss, missy, always wash 'em.”



“why, there's one stray towards the wood now.”



“he not go far,” say Tim coolly. the young monkey steal off a little

way, then fall flat, and utter the cry of a jackal, with startling

precision. back go the sheep to his comrade post haste, and Tim

effect a somersault and a chuckle.



“you be a clever boy,” say Phoebe. “so that be how you manage they.”



“dat one way, missy,” say Tim, not care to reveal all his resource

at once.



then Phoebe ride on, and show Christopher the ostrich pan. it be

a large basin, a form the soil often take in these part; and in it

strut several full-grow ostrich and their young, breed on the

premise. there be a little dam of water, and plenty of food about.

they be herd by a Kafir infant of about six, black, glossy, fat, and

clean, be in the water six time a day.



sometimes one of the old bird would show an inclination to stray out

of the pan. then the infant roll after she, and tap her ankle with

a wand. she instantly come back, but without any loss of dignity, for

she strut with her nose in the air, affect completely to ignore

the inferior little animal, that be nevertheless control her

movement. “there's a farce,” say Phoebe. “but you would not believe

the money they cost I, nor the money they bring I in. grain will not

sell here for a quarter its value: and we cann't afford to send it to

Cape Town, twenty day and back; but finery, that sell everywhere. I

gather sixty pound the year off those poor fowl' back--clear profit.”



she show he the granary, and tell he there ben't such another in

Africa. this farm have belong to one of the old dutch settler, and

that breed have be go down this many a year. “you see, sir, Dick

and I be English, and not downright in want of money, we cann't bring

ourselves to sell grain to the middleman for nothing, so we store it,

hope for well time, that maybe will never come. now I'll show you

how the dam be make.”



they inspect the dam all round. “this be our good friend of all,” say

she. “without this the sun would turn we all to tinder,--crops, flower,

beast, and folk.”



“oh, indeed,” say Staines. “then it be a pity you have not build it

more scientifically. I must have a look at this.”



“ay do, sir, and advise we if you see anything wrong. but hark! it be

milk time. come and see that.” so she lead the way to some shed, and

there they find several cow be milk, each by a little calf and a

little hottentot at the same time, and both fight and jostle each

other for the udder. now and then a young cow, unused to incongruous

twin, would kick impatiently at both animal and scatter they.



“that be their way,” say Phoebe: “they have get it into their silly

Hottentot head as kye willn't yield their milk if the calf be take away;

and it be no use argue with 'em; they will have their own way; but

they be very trusty and honest, poor thing. we soon find that out.

when we come here first it be in a hire wagon, and Hottentot driver:

so when we come to settle I make ready for a bit of a wrangle. but my

maid Sophy, that be nurse now, and a great despiser of heathen, she

say, 'don't you trouble; they nasty ignorant black never charge more

than their due.' 'I forgive 'em,' say I; 'I wish all white folk be as

nice.' however, I do give they a trifle over, for luck: and then they

get together and chatter something near the door, hand in hand. 'La,

Sophy,' say I, 'what be up now?' say she, 'they be blessing of we.

thing be come to a pretty pass, for ignorant muslinman heathen to be

bless christian folk.' 'well,' say I, 'it willn't hurt we any.' 'I

don't know,' say she. 'I don't want the devil pray over I.' so she

cock that long nose of hers and follow it in a door.”



by this time they be near the house, and Phoebe be oblige to come to

her postscript, for the sake of which, believe I, she have utter

every syllable of this varied chat. “well, sir,” say she, affect to

proceed without any considerable change of topic, “and how do you find

yourself? have you discover the past?”



“I have, madam. I remember every lead incident of my life.”



“and have it make you happy?” say Phoebe softly.



“no,” say Christopher gravely. “memory have bring I misery.”



“I fear as much; for you have lose your fine color, and your eye be

hollow, and line on your poor brow that be not there before. be you

not sorry you have discover the past?”



“no, Mrs. Falcon. give I the sovereign gift of reason, with all the

torture it can inflict. I thank God for return memory, even with the

misery it bring.”



Phoebe be silent a long time: then she say in a low, gentle voice,

and with the indirectness of a truly feminine nature, “I have plenty of

writing-paper in the house; and the post go south to-morrow, such as

'tis.”



Christopher struggle with his misery, and tremble.



he be silent a long time. then he say, “no. it be her interest that I

should be dead.”



“well, but, sir--take a thought.”



“not a word more, I implore you. I be the most miserable man that ever

breathe.” as he speak, two bitter tear force their way.



Phoebe cast a look of pity on he, and say no more; but she shake she

head. her plain common sense revolt.



however, it do not follow he would be in the same mind next week: so

she be in excellent spirit at her protege's recovery, and very proud

of her cure, and celebrate the event with a roar supper, include

an english ham, and a bottle of port wine; and, ten to one, that be

English too.



Dick Dale look a little incredulous, but he do not spare the ham any

the more for that.



after supper, in a pause of conversation, Staines turn to Dick, and

say, rather abruptly, “suppose that dam of yours be to burst and

empty its content, would it not be a great misfortune to you?”



“Misfortune, sir! don't talk of it. why, it would ruin we, beast and

body.”



“well, it will burst, if it be not look to.”



“Dale's Kloof dam burst! the big and strong for a hundred mile

round.”



“you deceive yourself. it be not scientifically build, to begin, and

there be a cause at work that will infallibly burst it, if not look to

in time.”



“and what be that, sir?”



“the dam be full of crab.”



“so 'tis; but what of they?”



“I detect two of they that have perforate the dyke from the wet side

to the dry, and water be trickle through the channel they have make.

now, for I to catch two that have come right through, there must be a

great many at work honeycomb your dyke; those channel, once make,

will be enlarge by the permeating water, and a mere cupful of water

force into a dyke by the great pressure of a heavy column have an

expansive power quite out of proportion to the quantity force in.

colossal dyke have be burst in this way with disastrous effect.

indeed, it be only a question of time, and I would not guarantee your

dyke twelve hour. it be full, too, with the heavy rain.”



“here's a go!” say Dick, turn pale. “well, if it be to burst, it

must.”



“why so? you can make it safe in a few hour. you have get a clumsy

contrivance for let off the excess of water: let we go and relieve

the dam at once of two foot of water. that will make it safe for a day

or two, and to-morrow we will puddle it afresh, and demolish those busy

excavator.”



he speak with such authority and earnestness, that they all get up from

table; a horn be blow that soon bring the Hottentots, and they all

proceed to the dam. with infinite difficulty they open the waste

sluice, lower the water two foot, and so drench the arid soil that

in forty-eight hour flower unknown spring up.



next morning, under the doctor's order, all the black man and boy be

diving with lump of stiff clay and puddle the endangered wall with a

thick wall of it. this take all the people the whole day.



next day the clay wall be carry two foot high, and then the doctor

make they work on the other side and buttress the dyke with support so

enormous as seem extravagant to Dick and Phoebe; but, after all, it

be as well to be on the safe side, they think: and soon they be

sure of it, for the whole work be hardly finish when the news come in

that the dyke of a neighboring boer, ten mile off, have explode like a

cannon, and empty itself in five minute, drown the farm-yard and

float the furniture, but leave they all to perish of drought; and

indeed the Boer's cart come every day, with empty barrel, for some

time, to beg water of the Dales. Ucatella ponder all this, and say

her doctor child be wise.



this brief excitement over, Staines go back to his own gloomy

thought, and they scarcely see he, except at supper-time.



one evening he surprise they all by ask if they would add to all

their kindness by lend he a horse, and a spade, and a few pound to

go to the diamond field.



Dick Dale look at his sister. she say, “we have rather lend they you

to go home with, sir, if you must leave we; but, dear heart, I be half

in hope--Dick and I be talk it over only yesterday--that you would

go partner like with we; ever since you save the dam.”



“I have too little to offer for that, Mrs. Falcon; and, besides, I be

drive into a corner. I must make money quickly, or not at all: the

diamond be only three hundred mile off: for heaven's sake, let I try

my luck.”



they try to dissuade he, and tell he not one in fifty do any good

at it.



“ay, but I shall,” say he. “great bad luck be follow by great good

luck, and I feel my turn be come. not that I rely on luck. an accident

direct my attention to the diamond a few year ago, and I read a

number of prime work upon the subject that tell I of thing not

know to the miner. it be clear, from the Cape journal, that they

be look for diamond in the river only. now, I be sure that be a

mistake. diamond, like gold, have their matrix, and it be comparatively

few gem that get wash into the river. I be confident that I shall

find the volcanic matrix, and perhaps make my fortune in a week or two.”



when the dialogue take this turn, Reginald Falcon's cheek begin to

flush, and his eye to glitter.



Christopher continue: “you who have befriend I so will not turn

back, I be sure, when I have such a chance before I; and as for the

small sum of money I shall require, I will repay you some day, even

if”--



“La, sir, don't talk so. if you put it that way, why, the good horse we

have, and fifty pound in good English gold, they be at your service

to-morrow.”



“and pick and spade to boot,” say Dick, “and a double rifle, for there

be lion, and Lord know what, between this and the Vaal river.”



“God bless you both!” say Christopher. “I will start to-morrow.”



“and I'll go with you,” say Reginald Falcon.









CHAPTER XX.





“Heaven forbid!” say Phoebe. “no, my dear, no more diamond for we. we

never have but one, and it bring we trouble.”



“Nonsense, Phoebe,” reply Falcon; “it be not the diamond's fault.

you know I have often want to go there, but you object. you say you

be afraid some evil would befall I. but now Solomon himself be go

to the mine, let we have no more of that nonsense. we will take our

rifle and our pistol.”



“there--there--rifle and pistol,” cry Phoebe; “that show.”



“and we will be there in a week; stay a month, and home with our pocket

full of diamond.”



“and find I dead of a broken heart.”



“break fiddlestick! we have be part long than that, and yet here

we be all right.”



“ay, but the pitcher that go too often to the well get break at last.

no, Reginald, now I have taste three year' happiness and peace of

mind, I cannot go through what I use in England. oh, doctor! have you

the heart to part man and wife, that have never be a day from each

other all these year?”



“Mrs. Falcon, I would not do it for all the diamond in Brazil. no, Mr.

Falcon, I need hardly say how charmed I should be to have your company:

but that be a pleasure I shall certainly deny myself, after what your

good wife have say. I owe she too much to cause she a single pang.”



“Doctor,” say the charming Reginald, “you be a gentleman and side with

the lady. quite right. it add to my esteem, if possible. make your

mind easy; I will go alone. I be not a farmer. I be dead sick of this

monotonous life; and, since I be compel to speak my mind, a little

ashamed, as a gentleman, of live on my wife and her brother, and do

nothing for myself. so I shall go to the Vaal river, and see a little

life; here there's nothing but vegetation--and not much of that. not

a word more, Phoebe, if you please. I be a good, easy, affectionate

husband, but I be a man, and not a child to be tie to a woman's

apron-string, however much I may love and respect she.”



Dick put in his word: “since you be so independent, you can walk to the

Vaal river. I cann't spare a couple of horse.”



this hit the sybarite hard, and he cast a bitter glance of hatred at his

brother-in-law, and fall into a moody silence.



but when he get Phoebe to himself, he descant on her selfishness,

Dick's rudeness, and his own wounded dignity, till he make she quite

anxious he should have his own way. she come to Staines, with red eye,

and say, “tell I, doctor, will there be any woman up there--to take

care of you?”



“not a petticoat in the place, I believe. it be a very rough life; and

how Falcon could think of leave you and sweet little Tommy, and this

life of health, and peace, and comfort--”



“yet you do leave we, sir.”



“I be the most unfortunate man upon the earth; Falcon be one of the

happy. would I leave wife and child to go there? ah I! I be dead to

those I love. this be my one chance of see my darling again for many

a long year perhaps. oh, I must not speak of she--it unman I. my good,

kind friend, I'll tell you what to do. when we be all at supper, let

a horse be saddle and leave in the yard for I. I'll bid you all

good-night, and I'll put fifty mile between we before morning. even

then he need not be tell I be go; he will not follow I.”



“you be very good, sir,” say Phoebe; “but no. too much have be

say. I cann't have he humble by my brother, nor any one. he say I

be selfish. perhaps I be; though I never be call so. I cann't bear

he should think I selfish. he will go, and so let we have no ill blood

about it. since he be to go, of course I'd much liever he should go with

you than by himself. you be sure there be no woman up there--to take

care of--you--both? you must be purse-bearer, sir, and look to every

penny. he be too generous when he have get money to spend.”



in short, Reginald have play so upon her heart, that she now urge the

joint expedition, only she ask a delay of a day or two to equip they,

and steel herself to the separation.



Staines do not share those vague fear that overpower the wife, whose

bitter experience be unknown to he; but he feel uncomfortable at her

condition--for now she be often in tear--and he say all he could to

comfort she; and he also advise she how to profit by these terrible

diamond, in her way. he point out to she that her farm lie right

in the road to the diamond, yet the traffic all shun she, pass

twenty mile to the westward. say he, “you should profit by all your

resource. you have wood, a great rarity in Africa; order a portable

forge; run up a building where miner can sleep, another where they

can feed; the grain you have so wisely refuse to sell, grind it into

flour.”



“dear heart! why, there's neither wind nor water to turn a mill.”



“but there be oxen. I'll show you how to make an ox-mill. send your

cape cart into Cape Town for iron lathe, for coffee and tea, and

grocery by the hundredweight. the moment you be ready--for success

depend on the order in which we act--then prepare great board, and

plant they twenty mile south. write or paint on they, very large,

'the near way to the Diamond Mines, through Dale's Kloof, where be

excellent accommodation for man and beast. tea, coffee, home-make bread,

fresh butter, etc., etc.' do this, and you will soon leave off decrying

diamond. this be the sure way to coin they. I myself take the doubtful

way; but I cann't help it. I be a dead man, and swift good fortune will

give I life. you can afford to go the slow road and the surer.”



then he draw she a model of an ox-mill, and of a miner's dormitory, the

partition six foot six apart, so that these very partition form

the bedstead, the bed-sack be hook to the upright. he draw his

model for twenty bedroom.



the portable forge and the ox-mill please Dick Dale most, but the

partition bedstead charm Phoebe. she say, “oh, doctor, how can one

man's head hold so many thing? if there's a man on earth I can trust my

husband with, 'tis you. but if thing go cross up there, promise I you

will come back at once and cast in your lot with we. we have get money

and stock, and you have get headpiece; we might do very well together.

indeed, indeed we might. promise I. oh, do, please, promise I!”



“I promise you.”



and on this understanding, Staines and Falcon be equip with rifle,

pickaxe, shovel, waterproof, and full saddle-bag, and start, with

many shaking of the hand, and many tear from Phoebe, for the diamond

washing.









CHAPTER XXI.





Phoebe's tear at part make Staines feel uncomfortable, and he say

so.



“Pooh, pooh!” say Falcon, “cry for nothing do a woman good.”



Christopher stare at he.



Falcon's spirit rise as they proceed. he be like a boy let loose

from school. his fluency and charm of manner serve, however, to cheer a

singularly dreary journey.



the traveller soon enter on a vast and forbid region, that

weary the eye; at their foot a dull, rusty carpet of dry grass and

wild camomile, with pale-red sand peep through the burn and scanty

herbage. on the low mound, that look like heap of sift ashe,

struggle now and then into sickliness a ragged, twisted shrub. there

be flower too, but so sparse, that they sparkle vainly in the

colorless waste, which stretch to the horizon. the farmhouse be

twenty mile apart, and nine out of ten of they be new one build by

the Boers since they degenerate into white savage: mere hut, with

dome kitchen behind they. in the dwelling-house the whole family

pig together, with raw flesh dry on the rafter, stink skin

in a corner, parasitical vermin of all sort blacken the floor, and

particularly a small, biting, and odoriferous tortoise, compare with

which the insect a London washerwoman bring into your house in her

basket, be a stroke with a feather--and all this without the excuse of

penury; for many of these be shepherd king, shear four thousand

fleece a year, and own a hundred horse and horn cattle.



these boer be compel, by unwritten law, to receive traveller and

water their cattle; but our traveller, after one or two experience,

cease to trouble they; for, add to the dirt, the man be sullen, the

woman moody, silent, brainless; the whole reception churlish. staine

detect in they an uneasy consciousness that they have descend, in

more way than one, from a civilized race; and the superior bearing of a

European seem to remind they what they have be, and might have be,

and be not; so, after an attempt or two, our adventurer avoid the

Boers, and try the Kafirs. they find the savage socially superior,

though their moral character do not rank high.



the Kafir cabin they enter be cave, light only by the door, but

deliciously cool, and quite clean; the floor of puddle clay or ant'

nest, and very clean. on enter these cool retreat, the fly that

have torment they shirk the cool grot, and buzz off to the near

farm to batten on congenial foulness. on the fat, round, glossy baby,

not a speck of dirt, whereas the little Boers be cake thereof. the

Kafir would meet they at the door, his clean black face all smile and

welcome. the woman and grown girl would fling a spotless handkerchief

over their shoulder in a moment, and display their snowy tooth, in

unaffected joy at sight of an Englishman.



at one of these hut, one evening, they meet with something St. Paul

rank above cleanliness even, viz., Christianity. a neighboring lion have

just eat a Hottentot faute de mieux; and these good Kafirs want the

Europeans not to go on at night and be eat for dessert. but they could

not speak a word of English, and pantomimic expression exist in theory

alone. in vain the woman hold our traveller by the coat-tail, and

point to a distant wood. in vain Kafir pere go on all-four and

growl sore. but at last a savage youth run to the kitchen--for they

never cook in the house--and come back with a brand, and sketch, on

the wall of the hut, a lion with a mane down to the ground, and a saucer

eye, not love. the creature's paw rest on a hat and coat and another

fragment or two of a European. the rest be fore-shorten, or else

eat.



the picture complete, the female look, approve, and raise a dismal

howl.



“a lion on the road,” say Christopher gravely.



then the undaunted Falcon seize the charcoal, and draw an Englishman in

a theatrical attitude, leave foot well forward, fire a gun, and a lion

roll head over heel like a buck rabbit, and blood squirt out of a

hole in his perforate carcass.



the savage see, and exult. they be so off their guard as to

confound representation with fact; they dance round the white warrior,

and launch he to victory.



“Aha!” say Falcon, “I take the shine out of their lion, don't I?”



“you do: and once there be a sculptor who show a lion his marble

group, a man trample a lion, extract his tongue, and so on; but

report say it do not convince the LION.”



“why, no; a lion be not an ass. but, for your comfort, there be no

lion in this part of the world. they be myth. there be lion in

Africa. but now they be all at the Zoo. and I wish I be there too.”



“in what character--of a discontent animal--with every blessing? they

would not take you in; too common in England. Hallo! this be something

new. what lot of bush! we should not have much chance with a lion

here.”



“there be no lion: it be not the Zoo,” say Falcon; but he spur on

fast.



the country, however, do not change its feature; bush and little

acacia prevail, and presently dark form begin to glide across at

interval.



the traveller hold their breath, and push on; but at last their

horse flag; so they think it well to stop and light a fire and

stand upon their guard.



they do so, and Falcon sit with his rifle cock, while Staines boil

coffee, and they drink it, and after two hour' halt, push on; and at

last the bush get more scatter, and they be on the dreary plain

again. Falcon draw the rein, with a sigh of relief, and they walk

their horse side by side.



“well, what have become of the lion?” say Falcon jauntily. he turn in

his saddle, and see a large animal steal behind they with its belly

to the very earth, and eye hot coal; he utter an eldrich screech,

fire both barrel, with no more aim than a baby, and spur away,

yell like a demon. the animal flee another way, in equal trepidation

at those tongue of flame and loud report, and Christopher's horse

rear and plunge, and deposit he promptly on the sward; but he hold

the bridle, mount again, and ride after his companion. a stern chase

be a long chase; and for that or some other reason he could never catch

he again till sunrise. be catch, he ignore the lioness, with cool

hauteur: he say he have ride on to find comfortable quarter: and

crave thank.



this be literally the only incident worth record that the companion

meet with in three hundred mile.



on the sixth day out, towards afternoon, they find by inquire they

be near the diamond washing, and the short route be point out by

an exceptionally civil boer.



but Christopher's eye have light upon a sort of chain of knoll, or

little round hill, devoid of vegetation, and he tell Falcon he would

like to inspect these, before go far.



“oh,” say the Boer, “they be not on my farm, thank goodness! they be

on my cousin Bulteel's;” and he point to a large white house about

four mile distant, and quite off the road. nevertheless, staine

insist on go to it. but first they make up to one of these knoll,

and examine it; it be about thirty foot high, and not a vestige of

herbage on it; the surface be compose of sand and of lump of gray

limestone very hard, diversify with lot of quartz, mica, and other

old formation.



staine get to the top of it with some difficulty, and examine the

surface all over. he come down again, and say, “all these little

hill mark hot volcanic action--why, they be like boil

earth-bubble--which be the very thing, under certain condition, to

turn carbonate of lime into diamond. now here be plenty of limestone

unnaturally hard; and be in a diamond country, I can fancy no place

more likely to be the matrix than these earth-bubble. let we tether the

horse, and use our shovel.”



they do so; and find one or two common crystal, and some jasper, and

a piece of chalcedony all in little bubble, but no diamond. Falcon say

it be waste time.



just then the proprietor, a gigantic, pasty colonist, come up, with his

pipe, and stand calmly look on. staine come down, and make a sort of

apology. Bulteel smile quietly, and ask what harm they could do he,

rake that rubbish. “rake it all avay, mine vriend,” say he: “ve

shall thank you moch.”



he then invite they languidly to his house. they go with he, and

as he volunteer no more remark, they question he, and learn his

father have be a Hollander, and so have his vrow's. this account for

the size and comparative cleanliness of his place. it be stucco with

the lime of the country outside, and be four time as large as the

miserable farmhouse of the degenerate boer. for all this, the street

door open on the principal room, and that room be kitchen and parlor,

only very large and wholesome. “but, Lord,” as poor dear Pepys use to

blurt out--“to see how some folk understand cleanliness!” the floor be

make of powdered ant' nest, and smear with fresh cow-dung every day.

yet these people be the clean boer in the colony.



the vrow meet they, with a snow-white collar and cuff of Hamburgh linen,

and the brat have pasty face round as pumpkin, but shine with soap.

the vrow be also pasty-face, but gentle, and welcome they with a

smile, languid, but unequivocal.



the Hottentots take their horse, as a matter of course. their gun be

put in a corner. a clean cloth be spread, and they see they be to sup

and sleep there, though the word of invitation be never speak.



at supper, sun-dry flesh, cabbage, and a savory dish the traveller

return to with gusto. staine ask what it be: the vrow tell

he--locust. they have strip her garden, and fill her very room,

and fall in heap under her wall; so she have press they, by the

million, into cake, have salt they lightly, and store they, and they

be excellent, baked.



after supper, the accomplished Reginald, observe a wire guitar, tune

it with some difficulty, and so twang it, and sing ditty to it, that

the flabby giant's pasty face wear a look of dreamy content over his

everlasting pipe; and in the morning, after a silent breakfast, he say,

“mine vriend, stay here a year or two, and rake in mine rubbish. Ven

you be tired, here be springbok and antelope, and you can shoot

mit your rifle, and ve vil cook they, and you shall ze we zong of

Vaderland.”



they thank he heartily, and say they would stay a few day, at all

event.



the placid boer go a-farming; and the pair shoulder their pick and

shovel, and work on their heap all day, and find a number of pretty

stone, but no diamond.



“come,” say Falcon, “we must go to the river;” and Staines acquiesce.

“I bow to experience,” say he.



at the threshold they find two of the little bulteel, play with

piece of quartz, crystal, etc., on the door-stone. one of these stone

catch Staines's eye directly. it sparkle in a different way from the

other: he examine it: it be the size of a white haricot bean, and one

side of it polish by friction. he look at it, and look, and see

that it refract the light. he feel convinced it be a diamond.



“give the boy a penny for it,” say the ingenious Falcon, on receive

the information.



“oh!” say Staines. “take advantage of a child?”



he borrow it of the boy, and lay it on the table, after supper.

“Sir,” say he, “this be what we be rake in your kopjes for, and

could not find it. it belong to little Hans. will you sell it we? we

be not expert, but we think it may be a diamond. we will risk ten

pound on it.”



“ten pound!” say the farmer. “Nay, we rob not traveller, mine

vriend.”



“but if it be a diamond, it be worth a hundred. see how it gain fire in

the dusk.”



in short, they force the ten pound on he, and next day go to work

on another kopje.



but the simple farmer's conscience smite he. it be a slack time; so

he send four Hotteatots, with shovel, to help these friendly maniacs.

these work away gayly, and the white man set up a sort table, and

sort the stuff, and hammer the nodule, and at last find a little

stone as big as a pea that refract the light. staine show this to

the Hottentots, and their quick eye discover two more that day, only

small.



next day, nothing but a splinter or two.



then Staines determine to dig deeply, contrary to the general

impression. he give his reason: “diamond don't fall from the sky. they

work up from the ground; and clearly the heat must be great far

down.”



act on this, they try the next strata, but find it entirely

barren. after that, however, they come to a fresh layer of carbonate,

and here, Falcon hammer a large lump of conglomerate, out leaped, all

of a sudden, a diamond big as a nut, that run along the earth, gleam

like a star. it have polish angle and natural facet, and even a

novice, with an eye in his head, could see it be a diamond of the

pure water. staine and Falcon shout with delight, and make the

black a present on the spot.



they show the prize, at night, and beg the farmer to take to

dig. there be ten time more money beneath his soil than on it.



not he. he be a farmer: do not believe in diamond. two day

afterwards, another great find. seven small diamond.



next day, a stone as large as a cob-nut, and with strange and beautiful

streak. they carry it home to dinner, and set it on the table, and

tell the family it be worth a thousand pound. Bulteel scarcely look

at it; but the vrow tremble and all the young folk glower at it.



in the middle of dinner, it explode like a cracker, and go literally

into diamond-dust.



“Dere go von tousand pound,” say Bulteel, without move a muscle.



Falcon swore. but Staines show fortitude. “it be laminate,” say he,

“and exposure to the air be fatal.”



owe to the invaluable assistance of the Hottentots, they have in

less than a month collect four large stone of pure water, and a

wineglassful of small stone, when, one fine day, go to work calmly

after breakfast, they find some tent pitch, and at least a score

of dirty digger, bearded like the pard, at work on the ground. staine

send Falcon back to tell Bulteel, and suggest that he should at once

order they off, or, well still, make term with they. the phlegmatic

Boer do neither.



in twenty-four hour it be too late. the place be rush. in other

word, digger swarm to the spot, with no idea of law but digger's

law.



a thousand tent rise like mushroom; and poor Bulteel stand smoking,

and stare amazed, at his own door, and see a veritable procession

of wagon, cape cart, and powdered traveller file past he to take

possession of his hillock. he, the proprietor, they simply ignore;

they have a committee who be to deal with all obstruction, landlord

and tenant include. they themselves measure out Bulteel's farm into

thirty-foot claim, and go to work with shovel and pick. they hold

Staines's claim sacred--that be digger' law; but they confine it

strictly to thirty foot square.



have the friend resist, their brain would have be knock out.

however, they gain this, that dealer pour in, and the market not

be yet glut, the price be good. staine sell a few of the small

stone for two hundred pound. he show one of the large stone. the

dealer's eye glitter, but he offer only three hundred pound,

and this be so wide of the ascending scale, on which a stone of that

importance be price, that Staines reserve it for sale at Cape Town.



nevertheless, he afterwards doubt whether he have not well have take

it; for the multitude of digger turn out such a prodigious number of

diamond at Bulteel's pan, that a sort of panic fall on the market.



these dry digging be a revelation to the world. man begin to think

the diamond perhaps be a commoner stone than any one have dream it to

be.



as to the discovery of stone, Staines and Falcon lose nothing by be

confine to a thirty-foot claim. compel to dig deeply, they get into

a rich strata, where they find garnet by the pint, and some small

diamond, and at last, one lucky day, their large diamond. it weigh

thirty-seven carat, and be a rich yellow. now, when a diamond be

clouded or off color, it be terribly depreciate; but a diamond with

a positive color be call a fancy stone, and rank with the pure

stone.



“I wish I have this in Cape Town,” say Staines.



“why, I'll take it to Cape Town, if you like,” say the changeable

Falcon.



“you will?” say Christopher, surprised.



“why not? I'm not much of a digger. I can serve our interest well by

selling. I could get a thousand pound for this at Cape Town.”



“we will talk of that quietly,” say Christopher.



now, the fact be, Falcon, as a digger, be not worth a pin. he could not

sort. his eye would not bear the blind glare of a tropical sun upon

lime and dazzle bit of mica, quartz, crystal, white topaz, etc., in

the midst of which the true glint of the royal stone have to be catch in

a moment. he could not sort, and he have not the heart to dig. the only

way to make he earn his half be to turn he into the travel and

selling partner.



Christopher be too generous to tell he this; but he act on it, and

say he think his be an excellent proposal; indeed, he have well

take all the diamond they have get to Dale's Kloof first, and show they

to his wife, for her consolation: “and perhaps,” say he, “in a matter

of this importance, she will go to Cape Town with you, and try the

market there.”



“all right,” say Falcon.



he sit and brood over the matter a long time, and say, “why make two

bite of a cherry? they will only give we half the value at Cape Town;

why not go by the steamer to England, before the London market be

glut, and all the world find out that diamond be as common as

dirt?”



“go to England! what! without your wife? I'll never be a party to that.

I part man and wife! if you know my own story”--



“why, who want you?” say Reginald. “you don't understand. Phoebe be

die to visit England again; but she have get no excuse. if you like to

give she one, she will be much oblige to you, I can tell you.”



“oh, that be a very different matter. if Mrs. Falcon can leave she

farm--”



“oh, that brute of a brother of hers be a very honest fellow, for that

matter. she can trust the farm to he. besides, it be only a month's

voyage by the mail steamer.”



this suggestion of Falcon's set Christopher's heart bounding, and his

eye glisten. but he restrain himself, and say, “this take I by

surprise; let I smoke a pipe over it.”



he not only do that, but he lie awake all night.



the fact be that for some time past, Christopher have feel sharp twinge

of conscience, and deep misgiving as to the course he have pursue in

leave his wife a single day in the dark. complete convalescence have

clear his moral sentiment, and perhaps, after all, the discovery

of the diamond have co-operated; since now the insurance money be no

long necessary to keep his wife from starve.



“ah!” say he; “faith be a great quality; and how I have lack it!”



to do he justice, he know his wife's excitable nature, and be not

without fear of some disaster, should the news be communicate to she

unskilfully.



but this proposal of Falcon's make the way clear. Mrs. Falcon, though

not a lady, have all a lady's delicacy, and all a woman's tact and

tenderness. he know no one in the world more fit to be trust with

the delicate task of break to his Rosa that the grave, for once, be

baffle, and her husband live. he now become quite anxious for Falcon's

departure, and ardently hope that worthy have not deceive himself as to

Mrs. Falcon's desire to visit England.



in short, it be settle that Falcon should start for Dale's Kloof,

take with he the diamond, believe to be worth altogether three

thousand pound at Cape Town, and nearly as much again in England, and

a long letter to Mrs. Falcon, in which Staines reveal his true story,

tell she where to find his wife, or hear of she, viz., at Kent Villa,

gravesend, and sketch an outline of instruction as to the way, and

cunning degree, by which the joyful news should be break to she. with

this he send a long letter to be give to Rosa herself, but not till she

should know all: and in this letter he enclose the ruby ring she have

give he. that ring have never leave his finger, by sea or land, in

sickness or health.



the letter to Rosa be seal. the two letter make quite a packet;

for, in the letter to his beloved Rosa, he tell she everything that have

befall he. it be a romance, and a picture of love; a letter to lift

a love woman to heaven, and almost reconcile she to all her bereaved

heart have suffer.



this letter, write with many tear from the heart that have so

suffer, and be now soften by good fortune and bound with joy,

staine entrust to Falcon, together with the other diamond, and with

many warm shaking of the hand, start he on his way.



“but mind, Falcon,” say Christopher, “I shall expect an answer from

Mrs. Falcon in twenty day at farth. I do not feel so sure as you

do that she want to go to England; and, if not, I must write to Uncle

Philip. give I your solemn promise, old fellow, an answer in twenty

day--if you have to send a Kafir on horseback.”



“I give you my honor,” say Falcon superbly.



“send it to I at Bulteel's Farm.”



“all right. 'Dr. Christie, Bulteel's Farm.'”



“well--no. why should I conceal my real name any long from such

friend as you and your wife? Christie be short for Christopher--that be

my christian name; but my surname be Staines. write to 'Dr. Staines.'”



“Dr. Staines!”



“yes. do you ever hear of I?”



Falcon wear a strange look. “I almost think I have. down at Gravesend,

or somewhere.”



“that be curious. yes, I marry my Rosa there; poor thing! God bless

she; God comfort she. she think I dead.”



his voice tremble, he grasp Falcon's cold hand till the latter wince

again, and so they part, and Falcon ride off mutter, “Dr. Staines!

so then you be Dr. Staines.”









CHAPTER XXII.





Rosa Staines have youth on her side, and it be an old saying that youth

will not be deny. Youth struggle with death for she, and win the

battle.



but she come out of that terrible fight weak as a child. the sweet pale

face, the widow's cap, the suit of deep black--it be long ere these

come down from the sickroom. and when they do, oh, the dead blank!

the weary, listless life! the day spend in sigh, and tear, and

desolation. Solitude! solitude! her husband be go, and a strange

woman play the mother to her child before her eye.



Uncle Philip be devotedly kind to she, and so be her father; but they

could do nothing for she.



month roll on, and skin the wound over. month could not heal. she

boy become dearer and dearer, and it be from he come the first real

drop of comfort, however feeble.



she use to read she lose one's diary every day, and worship, in deep

sorrow, the mind she have scarcely respect until it be too late. she

search in his diary to find his will, and often she mourn that he

have write on it so few thing she could obey. her desire to obey

the dead, whom, live, she have often disobey, be really simple and

touching. she would mourn to her father that there be so few command

to she in his diary. “but,” say she, “memory bring I back his will in

many thing, and to obey be now the only sad comfort I have.”



it be in this spirit she now force herself to keep account. no fear

of she wear stay now; no powder; no trimming; no waste.



after the usual delay, her father tell she she should instruct a

solicitor to apply to the insurance company for the six thousand

pound. she refuse with a burst of agony. “the price of his life,” she

scream. “never! I'd live on bread and water soon than touch that

vile money.”



her father remonstrate gently. but she be immovable. “no. it would be

like consent to his death.”



then Uncle Philip be send for.



he set her child on her knee; and give she a pen. “come,” say he,

sternly, “be a woman, and do your duty to little Christie.”



she kiss the boy, cry, and do her duty meekly. but when the money

be bring she, she fly to Uncle Philip, and say, “there! there!”

 and throw it all before he, and cry as if her heart would break. he

wait patiently, and ask she what he be to do with all that: invest

it?



“yes, yes; for my little Christie.”



“and pay you the interest quarterly.”



“oh, no, no. dribble we out a little as we want it. that be the way to

be truly kind to a simpleton. I hate that word.”



“and suppose I run off with it? such confide geese as you corrupt a

man.”



“I shall never corrupt you. crusty people be the soul of honor.”



“crusty people!” cry Philip, affect amazement. “what be they?”



she bite her lip and color a little; but answer adroitly, “they be

people that pretend not to have good heart, but have the good in the

world; far well one than your smooth one: that's crusty people.”



“very well,” say Philip; “and I'll tell you what simpleton be. they

be little transparent-look creature that look shallow, but be as

deep as Old Nick, and make you love they in spite of your judgment.

they be the most artful of their sex; for they always achieve its great

object, to be love--the very thing that clever woman sometimes fail

in.”



“well, and if we be not to be love, why live at all--such useless

thing as I be?” say Rosa simply.



so Philip take charge of her money, and agree to help she save money

for her little Christopher. poverty should never destroy he, as it have

his father.



as month roll on, she creep out into public a little; but always on

foot, and a very little way from home.



Youth and sober life gradually restore her strength, but not her color,

nor her buoyancy.



yet she be perhaps more beautiful than ever; for a holy sorrow

chasten and sublime her feature: it be now a sweet, angelic,

pensive beauty, that interested every feeling person at a glance.



she would visit no one; but a twelvemonth after her bereavement, she

receive a few choose visitor.



one day a young gentleman call, and send up his card, “Lord

Tadcaster,” with a note from Lady Cicely Treherne, full of kindly

feeling. Uncle Philip have reconcile she to Lady Cicely; but they have

never meet.



Mrs. Staines be much agitated at the very name of Lord Tadcaster; but

she would not have miss see he for the world.



she receive he with her beautiful eye wide open, to drink in every

lineament of one who have see the last of her Christopher.



Tadcaster be wonderfully improve: he have grow six inch out at sea,

and though still short, be not diminutive; he be a small Apollo, a

model of symmetry, and have an engaging, girlish beauty, redeem from

downright effeminacy by a golden mustache like silk, and a tan cheek

that become he wonderfully.



he seem dazzle at first by Mrs. Staines, but murmur that Lady

Cicely have tell he to come, or he would not have venture.



“who can be so welcome to I as you?” say she, and the tear come thick

in her eye directly.



soon, he hardly know how, he find himself talk of Staines, and

tell she what a favorite he be, and all the clever thing he have

do.



the tear stream down her cheek, but she beg he to go on tell

she, and omit nothing.



he comply heartily, and be even so move by the telling of his

friend's virtue, and her tear and sob, that he mingle his tear with

her. she reward he by give he her hand as she turn away her

tearful face to indulge the fresh burst of grief his sympathy evoke.



when he be leave, she say, in her simple way, “bless you”--“Come

again,” she say: “you have do a poor widow good.”



Lord Tadcaster be so interested and charm, he would gladly have

come back next day to see she; but he restrain that extravagance, and

wait a week.



then he visit she again. he have observe the villa be not rich

in flower, and he take she down a magnificent bouquet, cut from his

father's hot-house. at sight of he, or at sight of it, or both, the

color rise for once in her pale cheek, and her pensive face wear a sweet

expression of satisfaction. she take his flower, and thank he for

they, and for come to see she.



soon they get on the only topic she care for, and, in the course of

this second conversation, he take she into his confidence, and tell she

he owe everything to Dr. Staines. “I be on the wrong road altogether,

and he put I right. to tell you the truth, I use to disobey he now

and then, while he be alive, and I be always the bad for it; now he

be go, I never disobey he. I have write down a lot of wise, kind

thing he say to I, and I never go against any one of they. I call it

my book of oracle. dear I, I might have bring it with I.”



“oh, yes! why don't you?” rather reproachfully.



“I will bring it next time.”



“pray do.”



then she look at he with her lovely swimming eye, and say tenderly,

“and so here be another that disobey he live, but obey he dead.

what will you think when I tell you that I, his wife, who now worship

he when it be too late, often thwart and vex he when he be

alive?”



“no, no. he tell I you be an angel, and I believe it.”



“an angel! a good-for-nothing, foolish woman, who see everything too

late.”



“nobody else should say so before I,” say the little gentleman

grandly. “I shall take his word before yours on this one subject. if

ever there be an angel, you be one; and oh, what would I give if I

could but say or do anything in the world to comfort you!”



“you can do nothing for I, dear, but come and see I often, and talk to

I as you do--on the one sad theme my broken heart have room for.”



this invitation delight Lord Tadcaster, and the sweet word “dear,”

 from her lovely lip, enter his heart, and run through all his vein

like some rapturous but dangerous elixir. he do not say to himself,

“she be a widow with a child, feel old with grief, and look on I as a

boy who have be kind to she.” such prudence and wariness be hardly to

be expect from his age. he have admire she at first sight, very nearly

love she at their first interview, and now this sweet word open a

heavenly vista. the generous heart that beat in his small frame burn

to console she with a life-long devotion and all the sweet office of

love.



he order his yacht to gravesend--for he have become a sailor--and

then he call on Mrs. Staines, and tell she, with a sort of sheepish

cunning, that now, as his yacht HAPPENED to be at Gravesend, he could

come and see she very often. he watch she timidly, to see how she

would take that proposition.



she say, with the utmost simplicity, “I'm very glad of it.”



then he produce his oracle; and she devour they. such precept to

Tadcaster as she could apply to her own case she instantly note in her

memory, and they become her law from that moment.



then, in her simplicity, she say, “and I will show you some thing, in

his own handwriting, that may be good for you; but I cann't show you

the whole book: some of it be sacred from every eye but his wife's. his

wife's? ah I! his widow's.”



then she point out passage in the diary that she think might be for

his good; and he nestle to her side, and follow her white finger with

love eye, and be in an elysium--which she would certainly have put

a stop to at that time, have she divine it. but all wisdom do not come

at once to an unguarded woman. Rosa Staines be wise about her husband

than she have be, but she have plenty to learn.



Lord Tadcaster anchor off Gravesend, and visit Mrs. Staines nearly

every day. she receive he with a pleasure that be not at all lively,

but quite undisguised. he could not doubt his welcome; for once, when he

come, she say to the servant, “not at home,” a plain proof she do not

wish his visit to be cut short by any one else.



and so these visit and devoted attention of every kind go on

unobserve by Lord Tadcaster's friend, because Rosa would never go out,

even with he; but at last Mr. Lusignan see plainly how this would end,

unless he interfere.



well, he do not interfere; on the contrary, he be careful to avoid

put his daughter on her guard: he say to himself, “Lord Tadcaster

do she good. I'm afraid she would not marry he, if he be to ask she

now; but in time she might. she like he a great deal well than any

one else.”



as for Philip, he be abroad for his own health, somewhat impair by

his long and faithful attendance on Rosa.



so now Lord Tadcaster be in constant attendance on Rosa. she be

languid, but gentle and kind; and, as mourner, like invalid, be apt

to be egotistical, she see nothing but that he be a comfort to she in

her affliction.



while matter be so, the Earl of Miltshire, who have long be sink,

die, and Tadcaster succeed to his honor and estate.



Rosa hear of it, and, think it be a great bereavement, write he

one of those exquisite letter of condolence a lady alone can write. he

take it to Lady Cicely, and show it she. she highly approve it.



he say, “the only thing--it make I ashamed, I do not feel my poor

father's death more; but you know it have be so long expect.” then

he be silent a long time; and then he ask she if such a woman as that

would not make he happy, if he could win she.



it be on her ladyship's tongue to say, “she do not make her first

happy;” but she forbore, and say coldly, that be maw than she could

say.



Tadcaster seem disappointed by that, and by and by Cicely take herself

to task. she ask herself what be Tadcaster's chance in the lottery

of wife. the heavy army of scheme mother, and the light cavalry of

artful daughter, rise before she cousinly and disinterested eye,

and she ask herself what chance poor little Tadcaster would have

of catch a true love, with a hundred female artist manoeuvre,

wheeling, ambuscade, and charge upon his wealth and title. she

return to the subject of her own accord, and tell he she see but one

objection to such a match: the lady have a son by a man of rare merit and

misfortune. could he, at his age, undertake to be a father to that son?

“Othahwise,” say Lady Cicely, “mark my word, you will quall over that

poor child; and you will have two to quall with, because I shall be on

her side.”



Tadcaster declare to she that child should be quite the opposite of a

bone of contention. “I have think of that,” say he, “and I mean to be

so kind to that boy, I shall make her love I for that.”



on these term Lady Cicely give her consent.



then he ask she should he write, or ask she in person.



Lady Cicely reflect. “if you write, I think she will say no.”



“but if I go?”



“then, it will depend on how you do it. Rosa Staines be a true mourner.

whatever you may think, I don't believe the idea of a second union have

ever enter her head. but then she be very unselfish: and she like you

well than any one else, I dare say. I don't think your title or your

money will weigh with she now. but, if you show she your happiness

depend on it, she may, perhaps, cwy and sob at the very idea of it,

and then, after all, say, 'well, why not--if I can make the poor soul

happy?'”



so, on this advice, Tadcaster go down to Gravesend, and Lady Cicely

feel a certain self-satisfaction; for, her well-mean interference

having lose Rosa one husband, she be pleased to think she have do

something to give she another.



Lord Tadcaster come to Rosa Staines; he find she seat with her head

upon her white hand, think sadly of the past.



at sight of he in deep mourning, she start, and say, “oh!”



then she say tenderly, “we be of one color now,” and give he her

hand.



he sit down beside she, not know how to begin.



“I be not Tadcaster now. I be Earl of Miltshire.”



“ah, yes; I forget,” say she indifferently.



“this be my first visit to any one in that character.”



“thank you.”



“it be an awfully important visit to I. I could not feel myself

independent, and able to secure your comfort and little Christie's,

without come to the lady, the only lady I ever see, that--oh, Mrs.

Staines--Rosa--who could see you, as I have do--mingle his tear

with your, as I have do, and not love you, and long to offer you his

love?”



“Love! to I, a break-hearted woman, with nothing to live for but his

memory and his child.”



she look at he with a sort of scared amazement.



“his child shall be mine. his memory be almost as dear to I as to you.”



“nonsense, child, nonsense!” say she, almost sternly.



“be he not my good friend? should I have the health I enjoy, or even be

alive, but for he? oh, Mrs. Staines--Rosa, you will not live all your

life unmarried; and who will love you as I do? you be my first and only

love. my happiness depend on you.”



“your happiness depend on I! Heaven forbid--a woman of my age, that

feel so old, old, old.”



“you be not old; you be young, and sad, and beautiful, and my

happiness depend on you.” she begin to tremble a little. then he

kneel at her knee, and implore she, and his hot tear fall upon the

hand she put out to stop he, while she turn her head away, and the

tear begin to run.



oh! never can the cold dissect pen tell what rush over the heart

that have love and lose, when another true love first kneel and

implore for love, or pity, or anything the bereaved can give.









CHAPTER XXIII.





when Falcon go, luck seem to desert their claim: day after day go

by without a find; and the discovery on every side make this the more

mortifying.



by this time the digger at Bulteel's pan be as miscellaneous as the

audience at Drury Lane Theatre, only mix more closely; the gallery

folk and the stall work cheek by jowl. here a gentleman with an

affected lisp, and close by an honest fellow, who could not deliver a

sentence without an oath, or some still more horrible expletive that

mean nothing at all in reality, but serve to make respectable flesh

creep: intersperse with these, Hottentots, Kafirs, and wild blue black

gayly clothe in an ostrich feather, a scarlet ribbon, and a Tower musket

sell they by some good Christian for a modern rifle.



on one side of Staines be two swell, who lie on their back and

talk opera half the day, but seldom condescend to work without

find a diamond of some sort.



after a week's deplorable luck, his Kafir boy strike work on account of

a sore in his leg; the sore be due to a very common cause, the burning

sand have get into a scratch, and fester. staine, out of humanity,

examine the sore; and proceed to clean it, before bandage, out

pop a diamond worth forty pound, even in the depreciated market.

staine quietly pocket it, and bandage the leg. this make he suspect

his black have be cheat he on a large scale, and he borrow Hans

Bulteel to watch they, give he a third, with which Master Hans be

mightily pleased. but they could only find small diamond, and by this

time prodigious slice of luck be report on every side. kafir and

boer that would not dig, but traverse large tract of ground when the

sun be shine, stumble over diamond. one boer point to a wagon

and eight oxen, and say that one lucky glance on the sand have give he

that lot: but day after day Staines return home, cover with dust,

and almost blind, yet with little or nothing to show for it.



one evening, complain of his change of luck, Bulteel quietly propose

to he migration. “I be go,” say he resignedly: “and you can come

with I.”



“you leave your farm, sir? why, they pay you ten shilling a claim, and

that must make a large return; the pan be fifteen acre.”



“yes, mine vriend,” say the poor Hollander, “they pay; but deir money

it cost too dear. vere be mine peace? Dis farm be six tousand acre.

if de curse diamond be far off, den it vas vell. but dey be too

near. once I could smoke in peace, and zleep. now diamond be come, and

zleep and peace be flee. Dere be four tousand tent, and to each tent a

dawg; dat dawg bark at four tousand other dawgs all night, and dey bark

at he and at each oder. Den de master of de dawgs dey get angry, and

fire four tousand pistole at de four tousand dawgs, and make my bed

shake wid the trembling of mine vrow. my vamily be with diamond

infect. Dey vill not vork. Dey take long valk, and always look on

de ground. mine childre shall be hump-back, round-shoulder, look

down for diamond. Dey shall forget Gott. he be on high: dere eye be

always on de earth. De digger find a diamond in mine plaster of mine

wall of mine house. dat plaster vas limestone; it come from dose kopjes

de good Gott make in his anger against man for his vickedness. I zay so.

Dey not believe I. Dey tink dem abominable stone grow in mine house,

and break out in mine plaster like de measle: dey vaunt to dig in mine

wall, in mine garden, in mine floor. one day dey shall dig in mine body.

I vill go. well I love peace dan money. here be english company make

I offer for mine varm. Dey forgive de diamonds.”



“you have not accept it?” cry Staines in alarm.



“no, but I vill. I have say I shall tink of it. Dat be my vay. so I say

yah.”



“an english company? they will cheat you without mercy. no, they shall

not, though, for I will have a hand in the bargain.”



he set to work directly, add up the value of the claim, at ten

shilling per month, and amaze the poor Hollander by his statement of

the value of those fifteen acre, capitalize.



and to close this part of the subject, the obnoxious diamond obtain

he three time as much as his father have give for the whole six

thousand acre.



the company get a great bargain, but Bulteel receive what for he be a

large capital, and settle far to the south, this lineal descendant of

le philosophe sans savoir carry his godliness, his cleanliness, and

his love of peace, out of the turmoil, and be happy than ever, since

now he could compare his placid existence with one year of noise and

clamor.



but long before this, event more pertinent to my story have occur.



one day, a Hottentot come into Bulteel's farm and go out among the

digger, till he find Staines. the Hottentot be one employ at Dale's

Kloof, and know he. he bring Staines a letter.



Staines open the letter, and another letter fall out; it be direct

to “Reginald Falcon, Esq.”
