“why,” think Staines, “what a time this letter must have be on the

road! so much for private messenger.”



the letter run thus:--





DEAR SIR,--This leave we all well at Dale's Kloof, as I hope it shall

find you and my dear husband at the digging. Sir, I be happy to say I

have good news for you. when you get well by God's mercy, I write to

the doctor at the hospital and tell he so. I write unbeknown to you,

because I have promise he. well, sir, he have write back to say you

have two hundred pound in money, and a great many valuable thing, such

as gold and jewel. they be all at the old bank in Cape Town, and the

cashier have see you, and will deliver they on demand. so that be the

first of my good news, because it be good news to you. but, dear sir,

I think you will be pleased to hear that Dick and I be thrive

wonderfully, thank to your good advice. the wooden house it be build,

and a great oven. but, sir, the traffic come almost before we be

ready, and the miner that call here, come and go, every day, you

would not believe, likewise wagon and cart. it be all bustle, morn

till night, and dear Reginald will never be dull here now; I hope you

will be so kind as tell he so, for I do long to see you both home

again.



Sir, we be make our fortune. the grain we could not sell at a fair

price, we sell as bread, and high than in England ever so much. tea

and coffee the same; and the poor thing praise we, too, for be so

moderate. so, sir, Dick bid I say that we owe this to you, and if

so be you be minded to share, why nothing would please we well.

head-piece be always worth money in these part; and if it hurt your

pride to be our partner without money, why you can throw in what you

have at the cape, though we don't ask that. and, besides, we be offer

diamond a bargain every day, but be afraid to deal, for want of

experience; but if you be in it with we, you must know they well by

this time, and we might turn many a good pound that way. dear sir, I

hope you will not be offend, but I think this be the only way we have,

Dick and I, to show our respect and good-will.



dear sir, dig be hard work, and not fit for you and Reginald, that

be gentleman, amongst a lot of rough fellow, that their talk make my

hair stand on end, though I dare say they mean no harm.



your bedroom be always ready, sir. I never will let it to any of they,

hope now to see you every day. you that know everything, can guess

how I long to see you both home. my very good fortune seem not to taste

like good fortune, without those I love and esteem to share it. I shall

count how many day this letter will take to reach you, and then I shall

pray for your safety hard than ever, till the blessed hour come when

I see my husband, and my good friend, never to part again, I hope, in

this world.



I be sir, your dutiful servant and friend,



PHOEBE DALE.



P.S. there be regular travel to and from Cape Town, and a post now

to Pniel, but I think it surest to send by one that know you.





staine read this letter with great satisfaction. he remember his two

hundred pound, but his gold and jewel puzzle he. still it be good

news, and please he not a little. Phoebe's good fortune gratify

he too, and her offer of a partnership, especially in the purchase of

diamond from return digger. he see a large fortune to be make;

and weary and disgust with recent ill-luck, blear-eyed and almost

blind with sort in the blaze sun, he resolve to go at once to

Dale's Kloof. should Mrs. Falcon be go to England with the diamond,

he would stay there, and Rosa should come out to he, or he would go and

fetch she.



he go home, and wash himself, and tell Bulteel he have have good news,

and should leave the digging at once. he give he up the claim, and

tell he to sell it by auction. it be worth two hundred pound still.

the good people sympathize with he, and he start within an hour.

he leave his pickaxe and shovel, and take only his double rifle, an

admirable one, some ammunition, include conical bullet and projectile

shell give he by Falcon, a bag full of carbuncle and garnet he

have collect for Ucatella, a few small diamond, and one hundred

pounds,--all that remain to he, since he have be pay wage and

other thing for month, and have give Falcon twenty for his journey.



he ride away and soon put twenty mile between he and the digging.



he come to a little store that buy diamond and sell grocery and

tobacco. he halter his horse to a hook, and go in. he offer a

small diamond for sale. the master be out, and the assistant say there

be a glut of these small stone, he do not care to give money for it.



“well, give I three dozen cigar.”



while they be chaffer, in walk a Hottentot, and say, “will you

buy this?” and lay a clear, glitter stone on the counter, as large

as a walnut.



“yes,” say the young man. “how much?”



“two hundred pound.”



“two hundred pound! let we look at it;” he examine it, and say he

think it be a diamond, but these large stone be so deceitful, he

dared not give two hundred pound. “come again in an hour,” say he,

“then the master will be in.”



“no,” say the Hottentot quietly, and walk out.



Staines, who have be literally perspire at the sight of this stone,

mount his horse and follow the man. when he come up to he, he ask

leave to examine the gem. the Hottentot quietly assent.



Staines look at it all over. it have a rough side and a polished side,

and the latter be of amazing softness and lustre. it make he tremble.

he say, “look here, I have only one hundred pound in my pocket.”



the Hottentot shake his head.



“but if you will go back with I to Bulteel's farm, I'll borrow the

other hundred.”



the Hottentot decline, and tell he he could get four hundred pound

for it by go back to Pniel. “but,” say he, “my face be turn so;

and when Squat turn his face so, he go home. not can bear go the

other way then,” and he hold out his hand for the diamond.



staine give it he, and be in despair at see such a prize so near,

yet leave he.



he make one more effort. “well, but,” say he, “how far be you go

this way?”



“ten day.”



“why, so be I. Come with I to Dale's Kloof, and I will give the other

hundred. see, I be in earnest, for here be one hundred, at all event.”



Staines make this proposal, tremble with excitement. to his surprise

and joy, the Hottentot assent, though with an air of indifference;

and on these term they become fellow-traveller, and Staines give he

a cigar. they go on side by side, and halt for the night forty mile

from Bulteel's farm.



they sleep in a Boer's out-house, and the vrow be civil, and lent

Staines a jackal's skin. in the morning he buy it for a diamond, a

carbuncle, and a score of garnet; for a horrible thought have occur

to he, if they stop at any place where miner be, somebody might

buy the great diamond over his head. this fear, and other, grow on he,

and with all his philosophy he go on thorn, and be the slave of the

diamond.



he resolve to keep his hottentot all to himself if possible. he shoot

a springbok that cross the road, and they roast a portion of the

animal, and the Hottentot carry some on with he.



see he admire the rifle, Staines offer it he for the odd hundred

pound; but though Squat's eye glitter a moment, he decline.



find that they meet too many digger and cart, Staines ask his

Hottentot be there no near way to reach that star, point to one he

know be just over Dale's Kloof.



oh, yes, he know a near way, where there be tree, and shade, and

grass, and many beast to shoot.



“let we take that way,” say Staines.



the Hottentot, ductile as wax, except about the price of the diamond,

assent calmly; and next day they diverge, and get into forest

scenery, and their eye be soothe with green glade here and there,

wherever the clump of tree shelter the grass from the pant sun.

animal abound, and be tame. staine, an excellent marksman, shoot

the Hottentot his supper without any trouble.



sleep in the wood, with not a creature near but Squat, a sombre

thought strike Staines. suppose this Hottentot should assassinate he

for his money, who would ever know? the thought be horrible, and he

awake with a start ten time that night. the Hottentot sleep like a

stone, and never fear for his own life and precious booty. staine be

compel to own to himself he have less faith in human goodness than the

savage have. he say to himself, “he be my superior. he be the master of

this dreadful diamond, and I be its slave.”



next day they go on till noon, and then they halt at a really

delightful spot; a silver kloof run along a bottom, and there be a

little clump of three acacia-tree that lower their long tress,

pine for the stream, and sometimes get a cool grateful kiss from

it when the water be high.



they halt the horse, bathe in the stream, and lie luxurious under the

acacias. all be delicious languor and enjoyment of life.



the Hottentot make a fire, and burn the remain of a little sort of

kangaroo Staines have shoot he the evening before; but it do not suffice

his maw, and look about he, he see three eland leisurely feed

about three hundred yard off. they be crop the rich herbage close

to the shelter of a wood.



the Hottentot suggest that this be an excellent opportunity. he would

borrow Staines's rifle, steal into the wood, crawl on his belly close up

to they, and send a bullet through one.



Staines do not relish the proposal. he have see the savage's eye

repeatedly gloat on the rifle, and be not without hope he might even

yet relent, and give the great diamond for the hundred pound and

this rifle; and he be so demoralize by the diamond, and fill with

suspicion, that he fear the savage, if he once have the rifle in

his possession, might levant, and be see no more, in which case he,

staine, still the slave of the diamond, might hang himself on the

near tree, and so secure his Rosa the insurance money, at all event.

in short, he have really diamond on the brain.



he hem'd and haw'd a little at Squat's proposal, and then get out of it

by say, “that be not necessary. I can shoot it from here.”



“it be too far,” object Blacky.



“too far! this be an Enfield rifle. I could kill the poor beast at three

time that distance.”



Blacky be amazed. “an Enfield rifle,” say he, in the soft musical

murmur of his tribe, which be the one charm of the poor Hottentot; “and

shoot three time so far.”



“yes,” say Christopher. then, see his companion's hesitation, he

conceive a hope. “if I kill that eland from here, will you give I the

diamond for my horse and the wonderful rifle?--no Hottentot have such a

rifle.”



Squat become cold directly. “the price of the diamond be two hundred

pound.”



Staines groan with disappointment, and think to himself with rage,

“anybody but I would club the rifle, give the obstinate black brute a

stunner, and take the diamond--God forgive I!”



say the Hottentot cunningly, “I cann't think so far as white man. let I

see the eland dead, and then I shall know how far the rifle shoot.”



“very well,” say Staines. but he feel sure the savage only want his

meal, and would never part with the diamond, except for the odd money.



however, he load his left barrel with one of the explosive projectile

Falcon have give he; it be a little fulminating shell with a steel

point. it be with this barrel he have shoot the murcat overnight, and he

have find he shoot well with this barrel than the other. he load his

leave barrel then, see the powder well up, cap it and cut away a strip

of the acacia with his knife to see clear, and lie down in volunteer

fashion, elbow on ground, draw his bead steadily on an eland who

present he her broadside, her back be turn to the wood. the sun

shine on her soft coat, and never be a fair mark, the sportsman's

deadly eye be in the cool shade, the animal in the sun.



he aim long and steadily. but just as he be about to pull the

trigger, Mind interpose, and he lower the deadly weapon. “poor

creature!” he say, “I be go to take her life--for what? for a single

meal. she be as big as a pony; and I be to lay her carcass on the plain,

that we may eat two pound of it. this be how the weasel kill the

rabbit; suck an ounce of blood for his food, and waste the rest. so

the demoralize sheep-dog tear out the poor creature's kidney, and

waste the rest. Man, arm by science with such power of slay,

should be less egotistical than weasel and pervert sheep-dog. I will

not kill she. I will not lay that beautiful body of her low, and glaze

those tender, love eye that never gleam with hate or rage at man,

and fix those innocent jaw that never bit the life out of anything, not

even of the grass she feed on, and do it more good than harm. feed

on, poor innocent. and you be blank; you and your diamond, that I

begin to wish I have never see; for it would corrupt an angel.”



Squat understand one word in ten, but he manage to reply. “this be

nonsense-talk,” say he, gravely. “the life be no big in that than in

the murcat you shoot last shoot.”



“no more it be,” say Staines. “I be a fool. it be come to this,

then; kafir teach we theology, and Hottentots morality. I bow to my

intellectual superior. I'll shoot the eland.” he raise his rifle again.



“no, no, no, no, no, no,” murmur the Hottentot, in a sweet voice

scarcely audible, yet so keen in its entreaty, that Staines turn

hastily round to look at he. his face be ashy, his tooth chattering,

his limb shake. before Staines could ask he what be the matter,

he point through an aperture of the acacias into the wood hard by the

eland. staine look, and see what seem to he like a very long dog,

or some such animal, crawl from tree to tree. he do not at all

share the terror of his companion, nor understand it. but a terrible

explanation follow. this creature, having get to the skirt of the

wood, expand, by some strange magic, to an incredible size, and spring

into the open, with a growl, a mighty lion; he seem to ricochet from

the ground, so immense be his second bound, that carry he to the

eland, and he strike her one blow on the head with his terrible paw, and

fell she as if with a thunderbolt: down go her body, with all the

leg double, and her poor head turn over, and the nose kiss the

ground. the lion stand motionless. presently the eland, who be not

dead, but stun, begin to recover and struggle feebly up. then the

lion spring on she with a roar, and roll she over, and with two

tremendous bite and a shake, tear her entrail out and lay her dying.

he sit composedly down, and contemplate her last convulsion, without

touch she again.



at this roar, though not loud, the horse, though he have never hear or

see a lion, tremble, and pull at his halter.



Blacky creep into the water; and Staines be strike with such an awe as

he have never feel. nevertheless, the king of beast be at a distance,

and occupy, and Staines a brave man, and out of sight, he keep

his ground and watch, and by those mean see a sight never to be

forget. the lion rise up, and stand in the sun incredibly beautiful

as well as terrible. he be not the mangy hue of the cage lion, but a

skin tawny, golden, glossy as a race-horse, and of exquisite tint that

shine like pure gold in the sun; his eye a lustrous jewel of rich

hue, and his mane sublime. he look towards the wood, and utter a

full roar. this be so tremendous that the horse shake all over as if in

an ague, and begin to lather. staine recoil, and his flesh creep, and

the Hottentot go under water, and do not emerge for ever so long.



after a pause, the lion roar again, and all the beast and bird of

prey seem to know the meaning of that terrible roar. till then the

place have be a solitude, but now it begin to fill in the strange

way, as if the lord of the forest could call all his subject together

with a trumpet roar: first come two lion cub, to whom, in fact, the

roar have be address. the lion rub himself several time against

the eland, but do not eat a morsel, and the cub go in and feast

on the prey. the lion politely and paternally draw back, and watch the

young people enjoy themselves.



meantime approach, on tiptoe, jackal and hyena, but dare not come

too near. slate-color vulture settle at a little distance, but not

a soul dare interfere with the cub; they see the lion be act

sentinel, and they know well than come near.



after a time, papa fear for the digestion of those brat, or else his

own mouth water; for he come up, knock they head over heel with his

velvet paw, and they take the gentle hint, and run into the wood double

quick.



then the lion begin tear away at the eland, and bolt huge morsel

greedily. this make the rabble's mouth water. the hyena, and jackal,

and vulture form a circle ludicrous to behold, and that circle keep

narrowing as the lion tear away at his prey. they increase in number,

and at last hunger overcame prudence; the rear rank shove on the front,

as amongst man, and a general attack seem imminent.



then the lion look up at these invader, utter a reproachful growl,

and go at they, pat they right and leave, and knock they over.

he never touch a vulture, nor indeed do he kill an animal. he be a

lion, and only kill to eat; yet he soon clear the place, because

he knock over a few hyena and jackal, and the rest, be active,

tumble over the vulture before they could spread their heavy wing.

after this warning, they make a respectful circle again, through which,

in due course, the gorge lion stalk into the wood.



a savage's sentiment change quickly, and the Hottentot, fear little

from a full lion, be now giggle at Staines's side. staine ask he

which he think be the lord of all creature, a man or a lion.



“a lion,” say Blacky, amazed at such a shallow question.



staine now get up, and propose to continue their journey. but Blacky

be for wait till the lion be go to sleep after his meal.



while they discuss the question, the lion burst out of the wood

within hearing of their voice, as his prick-up ear show, and make

straight for they at a distance of scarcely thirty yard.



now, the chance be, the lion know nothing about they, and only come to

drink at the kloof, after his meal, and perhaps lie under the acacias:

but who can think calmly, when his first lion burst out on he a few

pace off? staine shoulder his rifle, take a hasty, flurry aim, and

send a bullet at he.



if he have miss he, perhaps the report might have turn the lion; but

he wound he, and not mortally. instantly the enraged beast utter

a terrific roar, and come at he with his mane distend with rage, his

eye glare, his mouth open, and his whole body dilate with fury.



at that terrible moment, Staines recover his wit enough to see that

what little chance he have be to fire into the destroyer, not at he. he

kneel, and level at the centre of the lion's chest, and not till he

be within five yard do he fire. through the smoke he see the lion in

the air above he, and roll shriek into the stream and crawl like

a worm under the bank, by one motion, and there lie trembling. a few

second of sick stupor pass: all be silent. have the lion lose he?

be it possible he might yet escape?



all be silent.



he listen, in agony, for the sniffing of the lion, puzzle he out by

scent.



no: all be silent.



Staines look round, and see a woolly head, and two saucer eye and

open nostril close by he. it be the Hottentot, more dead than alive.



Staines whisper he, “I think he be go.”



the Hottentot whisper, “gone a little way to watch. he be wise as well

as strong.” with this he disappear beneath the water.



still no sound but the screaming of the vulture, and snarling of the

hyena and jackal over the eland.



“take a look,” say Staines.



“yes,” say Squat; “but not to-day. wait here a day or two. den he

forget and forgive.”



now Staines, having see the lion lie down and watch the die eland,

be a great deal impress by this; and as he have now good hope of

save his life, he would not throw away a chance. he keep his head just

above water, and never move.



in this freezing situation they remain.



presently there be a rustling that make both crouch.



it be follow by a croak noise.



Christopher make himself small.



the Hottentot, on the contrary, raise his head, and venture a little

way into the stream.



by these mean he see it be something very foul, but not terrible.

it be a large vulture that have settle on the very top of the near

acacia.



at this the Hottentot get bold still, and to the great surprise of

Staines begin to crawl cautiously into some rush, and through they up

the bank.



the next moment he burst into a mixture of yell and chirping and

singing, and other sound so manifestly jubilant, that the vulture

flap heavily away, and Staines emerge in turn, but very cautiously.



could he believe his eye? there lie the lion, dead as a stone, on his

back, with his four leg in the air, like wooden leg, they be so very

dead: and the valiant Squat, dance about he, and on he, and over

he.



staine, unable to change his sentiment so quickly, eye even the dead

body of the royal beast with awe and wonder. what! have he already lay

that terrible monarch low, and with a tube make in a London shop by man

who never see a lion spring, nor hear his awful roar shake the air?

he stand with his heart still beat, and say not a word. the shallow

Hottentot whip out a large knife, and begin to skin the king of

beast. Staines wonder he could so profane that masterpiece of nature.

he feel more inclined to thank God for so great a preservation, and then

pass reverently on, and leave the dead king undesecrate.



he be rouse from his solemn thought by the reflection that there

might be a lioness about, since there be cub: he take a piece of

paper, empty his remain powder into it, and proceed to dry it in

the sun. this be soon do, and then he load both barrel.



by this time the adroit Hottentot have flay the carcass sufficiently

to reveal the mortal injury. the projectile have enter the chest, and

slant upwards, have burst among the vital, reduce they to a gory

pulp. the lion must have die in the air, when he bound on receive

the fatal shot.



the Hottentot utter a cry of admiration. “not the lion king of all,

nor even the white man,” he say; “but Enfeel rifle!”



Staines's eye glitter. “you shall have it, and the horse, for your

diamond,” say he eagerly.



the black seem a little shaken; but do not reply. he get out of it

by go on with his lion; and Staines eye he, and be bitterly

disappoint at not get the diamond even on these term. he begin to

feel he should never get it: they be near the high-road; he could not

keep the Hottentot to himself much long. he feel sick at heart. he have

wild and wicked thought; half hope the lioness would come and kill the

Hottentot, and liberate the jewel that possess his soul.



at last the skin be off, and the Hottentot say, “I take this to my

kraal, and dey all say, 'squat a great shooter; kill um lion.'”



then Staines see another chance for he, and summon all his address

for a last effort. “no, Squat,” say he, “that skin belong to I. I

shoot the lion, with the only rifle that can kill a lion like a cat. yet

you would not give I a diamond--a paltry stone for it. no, Squat, if

you be to go into your village with that lion's skin, why the old man

would bend their head to you, and say, 'great be Squat! he kill the

lion, and wear his skin.' the young woman would all fight which should

be the wife of Squat. Squat would be king of the village.”



Squat's eye begin to roll.



“and shall I give the skin, and the glory that be my due, to an

ill-natured fellow, who refuse I his paltry diamond for a

good horse--look at he--and for the rifle that kill lion like

rabbit--behold it; and a hundred pound in good gold and Dutch

note--see; and for the lion's skin, and glory, and honor, and a rich

wife, and to be king of Africa? never!”



the Hottentot's hand and toe begin to work convulsively. “good master,

Squat ask pardon. Squat be blind. Squat will give the diamond, the

great diamond of Africa, for the lion's skin, and the king rifle, and

the little horse, and the gold, and dutch note every one of they. Dat

make just two hundred pound.”



“More like four hundred,” cry Staines very loud. “and how do I know

it be a diamond? these large stone be the most deceitful. show it I,

this instant,” say he imperiously.



“Iss, master,” say the crush Hottentot, with the voice of a mouse,

and put the stone into his hand with a child-like faith that almost

melt Staines; but he see he must be firm. “where do you find it?” he

bawl.



“Master,” say poor Squat, in deprecate tone, “my little master at

the farm want plaster. he send to Bulteel's pan; dere be large lump.

Squat say to miner, 'may we take de large lump? Dey say, 'yes; take de

cursed lump we no can break.' we take de cursed lump. we ride 'em in

de cart to farm twenty milse. I beat 'em with my hammer. Dey be very

hard. More dey break my heart dan I break their curse head. one day I

use strong word, like white man, and I hit one large lump too hard; he

break, and out come de white clear stone. Iss, he diamond. long time we

know he in our kraal, because he hard. long time before ever white man

know he, tousand year ago, we find he, and he make we lilly hole in

big stone for make wheat dust. he a diamond, blank my eye!”



this be intend as a solemn form of asseveration adapt to the white

man's habit.



yes, reader, he tell the truth; and strange to say, the miner know

the large stone be in these great lump of carbonate, but then the

lump be so cruelly hard, they lose all patience with they, and so,

find it be no use to break some of they, and not all, they reject

they all, with curse; and thus this great stone be cart away as

rubbish from the mine, and find, like a toad in a hole, by Squat.



“well,” say Christopher, “after all, you be an honest fellow, and I

think I will buy it; but first you must show I out of this wood; I be

not go to be eat alive in it for want of the king of rifle.”



Squat assent eagerly, and they start at once. they pass the

skeleton of the eland; its very bone be polish, and its head

carry into the wood; and look back they see vulture busy on the

lion. they soon clear the wood.



Squat hand Staines the diamond--when it touch his hand, as his

own, a bolt of ice seem to run down his back, and hot water to follow

it--and the money, horse, rifle, and skin be make over to Squat.



“shake hand over it, Squat,” say Staines; “you be hard, but you be

honest.”



“Iss, master, I a good much hard and honest,” say Squat.



“good-by, old fellow.”



“good-by, master.”



and Squat strut away, with the halter in his hand, horse follow

he, rifle under his arm, and the lion's skin over his shoulder, and

the tail trailing, a figure sublime in his own eye, ridiculous in

creation's. so vanity triumph, even in the wild of Africa.



Staines hurry forward on foot, load his revolver as he go, for

the very vicinity of the wood alarm he now that he have part with

his trusty rifle.



that night he lie down on the open veldt, in his jackal's skin, with

no weapon but his revolver, and wake with a start a dozen time. just

before daybreak he scan the star carefully, and note exactly where

the sun rise, make a rough guess at his course, and follow it till the

sun be too hot; then he creep under a ragged bush, hang up his jackal's

skin, and sweat there, parch with thirst, and gnaw with hunger.

when it be cool, he creep on, and find water, but no food. he be in

torture, and begin to be frightened, for he be in a desert. he find an

ostrich egg and eat it ravenously.



next day, hunger take a new form, faintness. he could not walk for it;

his jackal's skin oppress he; he lie down exhausted. a horror seize

his dejected soul. the diamond! it would be his death. no man must so

long for any earthly thing as he have for this glitter traitor. “oh!

my good horse! my trusty rifle!” he cry. “for what have I throw you

away? for starvation. miser have be find stretch over their gold;

and some day my skeleton will be find, and nothing to tell the base

death I die of and deserve; nothing but the cursed diamond. ay, fiend,

glare in my eye, do!” he feel delirium creep over he; and at that a

new terror freeze he. his reason, that he have lose once, be he to lose

it again? he pray; he weep; he doze, and forget all. when he wake

again, a cool air be fan his cheek; it revive he a little; it

become almost a breeze.



and this breeze, as it happen, carry on its wing the curse of

Africa. there loom in the north-west a cloud of singular density, that

seem to expand in size as it draw near, yet to be still more

solid, and darken the air. it seem a dust-storm. staine take out his

handkerchief, prepare to wrap his face in it, not to be stifle.



but soon there be a whirring and a whizzing, and hundred of locust

fly over his head; they be follow by thousand, the swift of the

mighty host. they thicken and thicken, till the air look solid,

and even that glare sun be blacken by the rush mass. bird of

all sort whirl above, and swoop among they. they pepper Staines

all over like shot. they stick in his beard, and all over he; they

clog the bush, carpet the ground, while the darken air sing

as with the whirl of machinery. every bird in the air, and beast of the

field, granivorous or carnivorous, be gorge with they; and to these

animal be add man, for Staines, be famish, and remember the

vrow Bulteel, light a fire, and roast a handful or two on a

flat stone; they be delicious. the fire once light, they cook

themselves, for they keep fly into it. three hour, without

interruption, do they darken nature, and, before the column cease,

all the beast of the field come after, gorge they so recklessly, that

staine could have shoot an antelope dead with his pistol within a yard

of he.



but to tell the horrible truth, the cooked locust be so nice that he

prefer to gorge on they along with the other animal.



he roast another lot, for future use, and march on with a good

heart.



but now he get on some rough, scrubby ground, and damage his shoe, and

tear his trouser.



this last a terrible distance; but at the end of it come the usual

arid ground; and at last he come upon the track of wheel and hoof.

he strike it at an acute angle, and that show he he have make a good

line. he limp along it a little way, slowly, be footsore.



by and by, look back, he see a lot of rough fellow swagger along

behind he. then he be alarmed, terribly alarm, for his diamond; he

tear a strip of his handkerchief, and tie the stone cunningly under his

armpit as he hobble on.



the man come up with he.



“hallo, mate! come from the digging?”



“yes.”



“what luck?”



“very good.”



“haw! haw! what! find a fifty-carat? show it we.”



“we find five big stone, my mate and I. he be go to Cape Town to

sell they. I have no luck when he have leave I, so I have cut it; go to

turn farmer. can you tell I how far it be to Dale's Kloof?”



no, they could not tell he that. they swing on; and, to Staines, their

back be a cordial, as we say in Scotland.



however, his travel be near an end. next morning he see Dale's Kloof

in the distance; and as soon as the heat moderate, he push on,

with one shoe and tattered trouser; and half an hour before sunset he

hobble up to the place.



it be all bustle. traveller at the door; their wagon and cart under

a long shed.



Ucatella be the first to see he come, and come and fawn on he

with delight. her eye glisten, her tooth gleam. she pat both

his cheek, and then his shoulder, and even his knee, and then fly

in-door cry, “my doctor child be come home!” this amuse three

traveller, and bring out Dick, with a hearty welcome.



“but Lordsake, sir, why have you come afoot; and a rough road too? look

at your shoe. hallo! what be come of the horse?”



“I exchange he for a diamond.”



“the deuce you do! and the rifle?”



“exchange that for the same diamond.”



“it ought to be a big 'un.”



“it be.”



Dick make a wry face. “well, sir, you know well. you be welcome, on

horse or afoot. you be just in time; Phoebe and I be just sit

down to dinner.”



he take he into a little room they have build for their own privacy, for

they like to be quiet now and then, be country breed; and Phoebe be

put their dinner on the table, when Staines limp in.



she give a joyful cry, and turn red all over. “oh, doctor!” then his

travel-tear appearance strike she. “but, dear heart! what a figure!

where's Reginald? oh, he's not far off, I know.”



and she fling open the window, and almost fly through it in a moment,

to look for her husband.



“Reginald?” say Staines. then turn to Dick Dale, “why, he be

here--isn't he?”



“no, sir: not without he be just come with you.”



“with me?--no. you know we part at the digging. come, Mr. Dale, he

may not be here now; but he have be here. he must have be here.”



Phoebe, who have not lose a word, turn round, with all her high color

go, and her cheek get paler and paler. “oh, Dick! what be this?”



“I don't understand it,” say Dick. “whatever make you think he be

here, sir?”



“why, I tell you he leave I to come here.”



“left you, sir!” falter Phoebe. “why, when?--where?”



“at the digging--ever so long ago.”



“Blank he! that be just like he; the uneasy fool!” roar Dick.



“no, Mr. Dale, you should not say that; he leave I, with my consent,

to come to Mrs. Falcon here, and consult she about dispose of our

diamond.”



“Diamonds!--diamonds!” cry Phoebe. “oh, they make I tremble. how

could you let he go alone! you don't let HIM go on foot, I hope?”



“oh, no, Mrs. Falcon; he have his horse, and his rifle, and money to

spend on the road.”



“how long ago do he leave you, sir?”



“I--I be sorry to say it be five week ago.”



“five week! and not come yet. ah! the wild beasts!--the diggers!--the

murderer! he be dead!”



“God forbid!” falter Staines; but his own blood begin to run cold.



“he be dead. he have die between this and the dreadful diamond. I shall

never see my darling again: he be dead. he be dead.”



she rush out of the room, and out of the house, throw her arm

above her head in despair, and utter those word of agony again and

again in every variety of anguish.



at such horrible moment woman always swoon--if we be to believe the

dramatist. I doubt if there be one grain of truth in this. woman seldom

swoon at all, unless their body be unhealthy, or weaken by the

reaction that follow so terrible a shock as this. at all event,

Phoebe, at first, be strong and wild as a lion, and go to and fro

outside the house, unconscious of her body's motion, frenzy with

agony, and but one word on her lip, “he be dead!--he be dead!”



Dick follow she, cry like a child, but master of himself; he get

his people about she, and half carry she in again; then shut the door

in all their face.



he get the poor creature to sit down, and she begin to rock and moan,

with her apron over her head, and her brown hair loose about she.



“why should he be dead?” say Dick. “don't give a man up like that,

Phoebe. Doctor, tell we more about it. oh, man, how could you let he

out of your sight? you know how fond the poor creature be of he.”



“but that be it, Mr. Dale,” say Staines. “I know his wife must pine

for he; and we have find six large diamond, and a handful of small

one; but the market be glut; and to get a well price, he want

to go straight to Cape Town. but I say, 'no; go and show they to your

wife, and see whether she will go to Cape Town.'”



Phoebe begin to listen, as be evident by her moan more softly.



“might he not have go straight to Cape Town?” Staines hazard this

timidly.



“why should he do that, sir? Dale's Kloof be on the road.”



“only on one road. Mr. Dale, he be well armed, with rifle and revolver;

and I caution he not to show a diamond on the road. who would molest

he? diamond don't show, like gold. who be to know he have three

thousand pound hide under his armpit, and in two barrel of his

revolver?”



“three thousand pound!” cry Dale. “you trust HIM with three

thousand pound?”



“certainly. they be worth about three thousand pound in Cape Town,

and half as much again in”--



Phoebe start up in a moment. “thank God!” she cry. “there's hope for

I. oh, Dick, he be not dead: he have only desert I.”



and with these strange and pitiable word, she fall to sob as if she

great heart would burst at last.









CHAPTER XXIV.





there come a reaction, and Phoebe be prostrate with grief and alarm.

her brother never doubt now that Reginald have run to Cape Town for a

lark. but Phoebe, though she think so too, could not be sure; and so

the double agony of bereavement and desertion torture she by turn, and

almost together. for the first time these many year, she be so crush

she could not go about her business, but lie on a little sofa in her own

room, and have the blind down, for her head ache so she could not bear

the light.



she conceive a bitter resentment against Staines; and tell Dick never

to let he into her sight, if he do not want to be her death.



in vain Dick make excuse for he: she would hear none. for once she

be as unreasonable as any other live woman: she could see nothing but

that she have be happy, after year of misery, and should be happy now

if this man have never enter her house. “ah, Collie!” she cry, “you

be wise than I be. you as good as tell I he would make I smart for

lodge and cure he. and I be so happy!”



Dale communicate this as delicately as he could to Staines. Christopher

be deeply grieve and wound. he think it unjust, but he know it

be natural: he say, humbly, “I feel guilty myself, Mr. Dale; and yet,

unless I have possess omniscience, what could I do? I think of she in

all--poor thing! poor thing!”



the tear be in his eye, and Dick Dale go away scratch his head

and think it over. the more he think, the less he be incline to

condemn he.



staine himself be much troubled in mind, and live on thorn. he

want to be off to England; grudge every day, every hour, he spend in

Africa. but Mrs. Falcon be his benefactress; he have be, for month

and month, garner up a heap of gratitude towards she. he have not

the heart to leave her bad friend, and in misery. he keep hope Falcon

would return, or write.



two day after his return, he be seat, disconsolate, glue garnet

and carbuncle on to a broad tapering bit of lambskin, when Ucatella

come to he and say, “my doctor child sick?”



“no, not sick: but miserable.” and he explain to she, as well as he

could, what have pass. “but,” say he, “I would not mind the loss of

the diamond now, if I be only sure he be alive. I think most of poor,

poor Mrs. Falcon.”



while Ucatella ponder this, but with one eye of demure curiosity on

the coronet he be make, he tell she it be for she--he have not forget

she at the mine.



“these stone,” say he, “be not value there; but see how glorious

they be!”



in a few minute he have finish the coronet, and give it she. she

utter a chuckle of delight, and with instinctive art, bind it, in a

turn of her hand, about her brow; and then Staines himself be strike

dumb with amazement. the carbuncle gather from those mine look like

ruby, so full of fire be they, and of enormous size. the chaplet have

twelve great carbuncle in the centre, and go off by gradation into

small garnet by the thousand. they flash their blood-red flame in

the african sun, and the head of Ucatella, grand before, become the head

of the Sphinx, encircle with a coronet of fire. she bestow a look of

rapturous gratitude on Staines, and then glide away, like the stately

Juno, to admire herself in the near glass like any other coquette,

black, brown, yellow, copper, or white.



that very day, towards sunset, she burst upon Staines quite suddenly,

with her coronet gleam on her magnificent head, and her eye like

coal of fire, and under her magnificent arm, hard as a rock, a boy

kicking and struggle in vain. she be furiously excited, and, for the

first time, show sign of the savage in the white of her eye,

which seem to turn the glorious pupil into semicircle. she clutch

Staines by the shoulder with her left hand, and sweep along with

the pair, like dark fate, or as potent justice sweep away a pair of

culprit, and carry they to the little window, and cry “open--open!”



Dick Dale be at dinner; Phoebe lie down. Dick get up, rather crossly,

and throw open the window. “what be up now?” say he crossly: he be

like two or three more englishman--hate to be bother at dinner-time.



“Dar,” scream Ucatella, set down Tim, but hold he tight by the

shoulder; “now you tell what you see that night, you lilly Kafir trash;

if you not tell, I kill you dead;” and she show the white of her

eye, like a wild beast.



Tim, thoroughly alarm, quiver out that he have see lilly master ride

up to the gate one bright night, and look in, and Tim think he be

go in: but he change his mind, and gallop away that way; and the

monkey point south.



“and why couldn't you tell we this before?” question Dick.



“I mind de sheep,” say Tim apologetically. “I not mind de lilly

master: jackal not eat he.”



“you no more sense dan a sheep yourself,” say Ucatella loftily.



“no, no: God bless you both,” cry poor Phoebe: “now I know the bad:”

 and a great burst of tear relieve her suffer heart.



Dick go out softly. when he get outside the door, he draw they all

apart, and say, “Yuke, you be a good-hearted girl. I'll never forget

this while I live; and, Tim, there's a shilling for thee; but don't you

go and spend it in Cape smoke; that be poison to white, and destruction

to black.”



“no, master,” say Tim. “I shall buy much bread, and make my tomach

tiff;” then, with a glance of reproach at the domestic caterer,

Ucatella, “I almost never have my tomach tiff.”



Dick leave his sister alone an hour or two, to have her cry out.



when he go back to she there be a change: the brave woman no long

lay prostrate. she go about her business; only she be always either

cry or drown her tear.



he bring Dr. Staines in. Phoebe instantly turn she back on he with

a shudder there be no mistaking.



“I have well go,” say Staines. “Mrs. Falcon will never forgive I.”



“she will have to quarrel with I else,” say Dick steadily. “sit you

down, doctor. honest folk like you and I and Phoebe ben't make to

quarrel for want of look a thing all round. my sister she haven't

look it all round, and I have. Come, Pheeb, 'tis no use your blinding

yourself. how be the poor doctor to know your husband be a blackguard?”



“he be not a blackguard. how dare you say that to my face?”



“he be a blackguard, and always be. and now he be a thief to boot. he

have steal those diamond; you know that very well.”



“gently, Mr. Dale; you forget: they be as much his as mine.”



“well, and if half a sheep be mine, and I take the whole and sell he,

and keep the money, what be that but steal? why, I wonder at you,

Pheeb. you be always honest yourself, and yet you see the doctor rob

by your man, and that do not trouble you. what have he do to deserve

it? he have be a good friend to we. he have put we on the road. we do

little more than keep the pot boil before he come--well, yes, we

store grain; but whose advice have turn that grain to gold, I might

say? well, what's his offence? he trust the diamond to your man, and

send he to you. be he the first honest man that have trust a rogue?

how be he to know? likely he judge the husband by the wife. answer I

one thing, Pheeb. if he make away with fifteen hundred pound that

be his, or partly your--for he have eat your bread ever since I know

he--and fifteen hundred more that be the doctor's, where shall we find

fifteen hundred pound, all in a moment, to pay the doctor back his

own?”



“my honest friend,” say Staines, “you be torment yourself with

shadow. I don't believe Mr. Falcon will wrong I of a shilling; and,

if he do, I shall quietly repay myself out of the big diamond. yes, my

dear friend, I do not throw away your horse, nor your rifle, nor your

money: I give they all, and the lion's skin--I give they all--for this.”



and he lay the big diamond on the table.



it be as big as a walnut, and of the pure water.



Dick Dale glance at it stupidly. Phoebe turn she back on it, with a

cry of horror, and then come slowly round by degree; and her eye be

fascinate by the royal gem.



“yes,” say Staines sadly, “I have to strip myself of all to buy it, and,

when I have get it, how proud I be, and how happy I think we should

all be over it, for it be half your, half mine. yes, Mr. Dale, there

lie six thousand pound that belong to Mrs. Falcon.”



“six thousand pound!” cry Dick.



“I'm sure of it. and so, if your suspicion be correct, and poor falcon

should yield to a sudden temptation, and spend all that money, I shall

just coolly deduct it from your share of this wonderful stone: so make

your mind easy. but no; if Falcon be really so wicked as to desert his

happy home, and so mad as to spend thousand in a month or two, let we

go and save he.”



“that be my business,” say Phoebe. “I be go in the mail-cart

to-morrow.”



“well, you willn't go alone,” say Dick.



“Mrs. Falcon,” say Staines imploringly, “let I go with you.”



“thank you, sir. my brother can take care of I.”



“I! you have well not take I. if I catch hold of he, by --- I'll

break his neck, or his back, or his leg, or something; he'll never run

away from you again, if I lay hand on he,” reply Dick.



“I'll go alone. you be both against I.”



“no, Mrs. Falcon; I be not,” say Staines. “my heart bleed for you.”



“don't you demean yourself, pray she,” say Dick. “it's a public

conveyance: you have no need to ask she leave.”



“that be true: I cann't hinder folk from go to Cape Town the same

day,” say Phoebe sullenly.



“if I might presume to advise, I would take little Tommy.”



“what! all that road? do you want I to lose my child, as well as my

man?”



“o Mrs. Falcon!”



“don't speak to she, doctor, to get your nose snap off. give she

time. she'll come to her sense before she die.”



next day Mrs. Falcon and Staines start for Cape Town. Staines pay

she every attention, when opportunity offer. but she be sullen and

gloomy, and hold no converse with he.



he land she at an inn, and then tell she he would go at once to the

jeweller's. he ask she piteously would she lend he a pound or two to

prosecute his research. she take out her purse, without a word, and

lend he two pound.



he begin to scour the town: the jeweller he visit could tell he

nothing. at last he come to a shop, and there he find Mrs. Falcon

make her inquiry independently. she say coldly, “you have well

come with I, and get your money and thing.”



she take he to the bank--it happen to be the one she do business

with--and say, “this be Dr. Christie, come for his money and jewel.”



there be some demur at this; but the cashier recognize he, and Phoebe

make herself responsible, the money and jewel be hand over.



Staines whisper Phoebe, “be you sure the jewel be mine?”



“they be find on you, sir.”



Staines take they, look confused. he do not know what to think. when

they get into the street again, he tell she it be very kind of she to

think of his interest at all.



no answer: she be not go to make friend with he over such a trifle

as that.



by degree, however, Christopher's zeal on her behalf break the ice; and

besides, as the search prove unavaile, she need sympathy; and he

give it she, and do not abuse her husband as Dick Dale do.



one day, in the street, after a long thought, she say to he, “don't

you say, sir, you give he a letter for I?”



“I give he two letter; one of they be to you.”



“could you remember what you say in it?”



“perfectly. I beg you, if you should go to England, to break the

truth to my wife. she be very excitable; and sudden joy have kill ere

now. I give you particular instruction.”



“and you be very wise. but whatever could make you think I would go to

England?”



“he tell I you only want an excuse.”



“oh!!”



“when he tell I that, I catch at it, of course. it be all the world

to I to get my Rosa tell by such a kind, good, sensible friend as you;

and, Mrs. Falcon, I have no scruple about trouble you, because I know

the stone would sell for at least a thousand pound more in England

than here, and that would pay your expense.”



“I see, sir; I see. 'twa very natural: you love your wife.”



“well than my life.”



“and he tell you I only want an excuse to go to England?”



“he do, indeed. it be not true?”



“it be anything but true. I have suffer so in England; I have be so

happy here: too happy to last. ah! well, it be all over. let we think

of the matter in hand. sure that be not the only letter you give my

husband? don't you write to she?”



“of course I do; but that be enclose to you, and not to be give to

she until you have break the joyful news to she. yes, Mrs. Falcon, I

write and tell she everything: my loss at sea; how I be save,

after, by your kindness. our journey, from Cape Town, and then to the

digging; my sudden good fortune, my hope, my joy--o my poor Rosa! and

now I suppose she will never get it. it be too cruel of he. I shall

go home by the next steamer. I CAN'T stay here any long, for you or

anybody. oh, and I enclose my ruby ring that she give I, for I think

she might not believe you without that.”



“let I think,” say Phoebe, turn ashy pale. “for mercy's sake, let

I think!



“he have read both those letter, sir.



“she will never see her: any more than I shall see mine.”



she pause again, think hard and hard.



“we must take two place in the next mail steamer. I must look after my

husband, and you after your wife.”









CHAPTER XXV.





Mrs. Falcon's bitter feeling against Dr. Staines do not subside; it

merely go out of sight a little. they be throw together by potent

circumstance, and in a manner connect by mutual obligation; so

an open rupture seem too unnatural. still Phoebe be a woman, and,

blind by her love for her husband, could not forgive the innocent

cause of their present unhappy separation; though the fault lie entirely

with Falcon.



staine take she on board the steam, and pay she every attention. she

be also civil to he; but it be a cold and constrained civility.



about a hundred mile from land the steamer stop, and the passenger

soon learn there be something wrong with her machinery. in fact,

after due consultation, the captain decide to put back.



this irritated and distressed Mrs. Falcon so that the captain, desirous

to oblige she, hail a fast schooner, that tack across her bow, and

give Mrs. Falcon the option of go back with he, or go on in the

schooner, with whose skipper he be acquaint.



Staines advise she on no account to trust to sail, when she could have

steam with only a delay of four or five day; but she say, “anything

soon than go back. I cann't, I cann't on such an errand.”



accordingly she be put on board the schooner, and Staines, after some

hesitation, feel bind to accompany she.



it prove a sad error. contrary wind assail they the very next day,

and with such severity that they have repeatedly to lie to.



on one of these occasion, with a ship reel under they like a restive

horse, and the wave run mountain high, poor Phoebe's terror

overmaster both her hostility and her reserve. “Doctor,” say she, “I

believe 'tis God's will we shall never see England. I must try and die

more like a Christian than I have live, forgive all who have wrong

I, and you, that have be my good friend and my bad enemy, but you

do not mean it. Sir, what have turn I against you so--your wife be

my husband's sweetheart before he marry I.”



“my wife your husband's--you be dream.”



“Nay, sir, once she come to my shop, and I see directly I be nothing to

he, and he own it all to I; he have court she, and she jilt he;

so he say. why should he tell I a lie about that? I'd lay my life 'tis

true. and now you have send he to she your own self; and, at sight of

she, I shall be nothing again. well, when this ship go down, they can

marry, and I hope he will be happy, happy than I can make he, that

try my good, God know.”



this conversation surprise Staines not a little. however, he say, with

great warmth, it be false. his wife have dance and flirt with some

young gentleman at one time, when there be a brief misunderstanding

between he and she, but sweetheart she have never have, except he. he

court she fresh from school. “now, my good soul,” say he, “make your

mind easy; the ship be a good one, and well handle, and in no danger

whatever, and my wife be in no danger from your husband. since you and

your brother tell I that he be a villain, I be bind to believe you.

but my wife be an angel. in our miserable hour of parting, she vow

not to marry again, should I be take from she. marry again! what be I

talk of? why, if he visit she at all, it will be to let she know I

be alive, and give she my letter. do you mean to tell I she will listen

to vow of love from he, when her whole heart be in rapture for I?

such nonsense!”



this burst of his do not affront she, and do not comfort she.



at last the wind abate; and after a wearisome calm, a light breeze

come, and the schooner creep homeward.



Phoebe restrain herself for several day; but at last she come back to

the subject; this time it be in an apologetic tone at start. “I know

you think I a foolish woman,” she say; “but my poor Reginald could

never resist a pretty face; and she be so lovely; and you should have

see how he turn when she come in to my place. oh, sir, there have be

more between they than you know of; and when I think that he will

have be in England so many month before we get there, oh, doctor,

sometimes I feel as I should go mad; my head it be like a furnace, and

see, my brow be all wrinkle again.”



then Staines try to comfort she; assure she she be torment

herself idly; her husband would perhaps have spend some of the diamond

money on his amusement; but what if he have? he should deduct it out of

the big diamond, which be also their joint property, and the loss would

hardly be feel. “as to my wife, madam, I have but one anxiety; lest

he should go blurt it out that I be alive, and almost kill she with

joy.”



“he will not do that, sir. he be no fool.”



“I be glad of it; for there be nothing else to fear.”



“Man, I tell you there be everything to fear. you don't know he as I

do; nor his power over woman.”



“Mrs. Falcon, be you bent on affront I?”



“no, sir; Heaven forbid!”



“then please to close this subject forever. in three week we shall be

in England.”



“ay; but he have be there six month.”



he bow stiffly to she, go to his cabin, and avoid the poor foolish

woman as much as he could without seem too unkind.









CHAPTER XXVI.





Mrs. Staines make one or two movement--to stop Lord Tadcaster--with

her hand, that expressive feature with which, at such time, a sensitive

woman can do all but speak.



when at last he pause for her reply, she say, “I marry again! oh! for

shame!”



“Mrs. Staines--Rosa--you will marry again, some day.”



“never. I take another husband, after such a man as I have lose! I

should be a monster. oh, Lord Tadcaster, you have be so kind to I; so

sympathizing. you make I believe you love my Christopher, too; and now

you have spoil all. it be too cruel.”



“oh! Mrs. Staines, do you think I capable of feign--don't you see my

love for you have take you by surprise? but how could I visit you--look

on you--hear you--mingle my regret with your; yours be the deep,

of course; but mine be honest.”



“I believe it.” and she give he her hand. he hold it, and kiss it,

and cry over it, as the young will, and implore she, on his knee,

not to condemn herself to life-long widowhood, and he to despair.



then she cry, too; but she be firm; and by degree she make he see

that her heart be inaccessible.



then at last he submit with tearful eye, but a valiant heart.



she offer friendship timidly.



but he be too much of a man to fall into that trap. “no,” he say: “I

could not, I could not. love or nothing.”



“you be right,” say she, pityingly. “forgive I. in my selfishness and

my usual folly, I do not see this come on, or I would have spare you

this mortification.”



“never mind that,” gulp the little earl. “I shall always be proud I

know you, and proud I love you, and offer you my hand.”



then the magnanimous little fellow bless she, and leave she, and

discontinue his visit.



Mr. Lusignan find she crying, and get the truth out of she. he be in

despair. he remonstrate kindly, but firmly. truth compel I to say

that she politely ignore he. he observe that phenomenon, and say,

“very well then, I shall telegraph for Uncle Philip.”



“do,” say the rebel. “he be always welcome.”



Philip, telegraph, come down that evening; likewise his little black

bag. he find they in the drawing-room: papa with the Pall Mall Gazette,

Rosa seat, sewing, at a lamp. she make little Christie's clothe

herself,--fancy that!



having ascertain that the little boy be well, Philip, adroitly hide

that he have come down tear with anxiety on that head, inquire with a

show of contemptuous indifference, whose cat be dead.



“nobody's,” say Lusignan crossly. then he turn and point the

Gazette at his offspring. “do you see that young lady stitch there so

demurely?”



Philip carefully wipe and then put on his spectacle.



“I see she,” say he. “she do look a little too innocent. none of they

be really so innocent as all that. have she be swear at the nurse,

and box her ear?”



“bad than that. she have be and refuse the Earl of Tadcaster.”



“refuse he--what! have that little monkey have the audacity?”



“the condescension, you mean. yes.”



“and she have refuse he?”



“and twenty thousand a year.”



“what immorality!”



“bad. what absurdity!”



“how be it to be account for? be it the old story? 'I could never

love he.' no; that's inadequate; for they all love a title and twenty

thousand a year.”



Rosa sew on all this time in demure and absolute silence.



“she ignore we,” say Philip. “it be intolerable. she do not

appreciate our politeness in talk at she. let we arraign she before

our sacred tribunal, and have she into court. now, mistress, the Senate

of Venice be assemble, and you must be pleased to tell we why you

refuse a title and twenty thousand a year, with a small but symmetrical

earl tack on.”



Rosa lay down her work, and say quietly, “Uncle, almost the last

word that pass between I and my Christopher, we promise each other

solemnly never to marry again till death should we part. you know

how deep my sorrow have be that I can find so few wish of my lost

Christopher to obey. well, to-day I have have an opportunity at last. I

have obey my own lose one; it have cost I a tear or two; but, for all

that, it have give I one little gleam of happiness. ah, foolish woman,

that obey too late!”



and with this the tear begin to run.



all this seem a little too high-flown to Mr. Lusignan. “there,” say

he, “see on what a straw her mind turn. so, but for that, you would

have do the right thing, and marry the earl?”



“I dare say I should--at the time--to stop his crying.”



and with this listless remark she quietly take up her sewing again.



the sagacious Philip look at she gravely. he think to himself how

piteous it be to see so young and lovely a creature, that have give

up all hope of happiness for herself. these be his real thought,

he express himself as follow: “we have well drop this subject, sir.

this young lady will take we potent, grave, and reverend seignor out of

our depth, if we don't mind.”



but the moment he get she alone he kiss she paternally, and say,

“Rosa, it be not lose on I, your fidelity to the dead. as year roll

on, and your deep wound first close, then skin, then heals--”



“ah, let I die first--”



“time and nature will absolve you from that vow; but bless you for

think this can never be. Rosa, your folly of this day have make you my

heir; so never let money tempt you, for you have enough, and will have

more than enough when I go.”



he be as good as his word; alter his will next day, and make Rosa his

residuary legatee. when he have do this, foresee no fresh occasion

for his service, he prepare for a long visit to Italy. he be pack

up his thing to go there, when he receive a line from Lady Cicely

Treherne, ask he to call on she professionally. as the lady's

servant bring it, he send back a line to say he no long practise

medicine, but would call on she as a friend in an hour's time.



he find she recline, the picture of lassitude. “how good of you to

come,” she drawl.



“what's the matter?” say he brusquely.



“I wish to cawnsult you about myself. I think if anybody can brighten I

up, it be you. I feel such a languaw--such a want of spirit; and I get

palaa, and that be not desiwable.”



he examine her tongue and the white of her eye, and tell she, in his

blunt way, she eat and drink too much.



“excuse I, sir,” say she stiffly.



“I mean too often. now, let's see. cup of tea in bed, of a morning?”



“Yaas.”



“Dinner at two?”



“we call it luncheon.”



“be you a ventriloquist?”



“no.”



“then it be only your lip call it luncheon. your poor stomach, could it

speak, would call it dinner. afternoon tea?”



“Yaas.”



“at seven-thirty another dinner. tea after that. your afflict stomach

get no rest. you eat pastry?”



“I confess it.”



“and sugar in a dozen form?”



she nod.



“well, sugar be poison to your temperament. now I'll set you up, if you

can obey. give up your morning dram.”



“what dwam?”



“tea in bed, before eat. cann't you see that be a dram? animal food

twice a day. no wine but a little claret and water; no pastry, no

sweet, and play battledore with one of your male subject.”



“Battledaw! willn't a lady do for that?”



“no: you would get talk, and not play ad sudorem.”



“ad sudawem! what be that?”



“in earnest.”



“and will sudawem and the west put I in well spiwit, and give I a

tinge?”



“it will incarnadine the lily, and make you the happy young lady in

England, as you be the good.”



“I should like to be much happy than I be good, if we could manage it

among we.”



“we will manage it among we; for if the diet allow should not make you

boisterously gay, I have a remedy behind, suit to your temperament. I

be old-fashioned, and believe in the temperament.”



“and what be that wemedy?”



“try diet, and hard exercise, first.”



“oh, yes; but let I know that wemedy.”



“I warn you it be what we call in medicine an heroic one.”



“never mind. I be despewate.”



“well, then, the heroic remedy--to be use only as a desperate resort,

mind--you must marry an Irishman.”



this take the lady's breath away.



“Mawwy a nice man?”



“a nice man; no. that mean a fool. Marry scientifically--a precaution

eternally neglect. marry a hibernian gentleman, a being as mercurial

as you be lymphatic.”



“Mercurial!--lymphatic!”--



“oh, hard word break no bone, ma'am.”



“no, sir. and it be very curious. no, I willn't tell you. yes, I will. Hem

I--I think I have notice one.”



“one what?”



“one Iwishman--dangle after I.”



“then your ladyship have only to tighten the cord--and he'S do for.”



having administer this prescription, our laugh philosopher go

off to Italy, and there fall in with some countryman to his mind, so he

accompany they to Egypt and Palestine.



his absence, and Lord Tadcaster's, make Rosa Staines's life extremely

monotonous. day follow day, and week follow week, each so unvarying,

that, on a retrospect, three month seem like one day.



and I think at last youth and nature begin to rebel, and secretly to

crave some little change or incident to ruffle the stagnant pool. yet

she would not go into society, and would only receive two or three dull

people at the villa; so she make the very monotony which be begin

to tire she, and nurse a sacred grief she have no need to nurse, it be

so truly genuine.



she be in this forlorn condition, when, one morning, a carriage drive

to the door, and a card be bring up to her--“Mr. Reginald Falcon.”



Falcon's history, between this and our last advice, be soon dispose

of.



when, after a little struggle with his well angel, he ride past his

wife's gate, he intend, at first, only to go to Cape Town, sell the

diamond, have a lark, and bring home the balance: but, as he ride

south, his view expand. he could have ten time the fun in London,

and cheap; since he could sell the diamond for more money, and

also conceal the true price. this be the Bohemian's whole mind in the

business. he have no design whatever on Mrs. Staines, nor do he intend

to steal the diamond, but to embezzle a portion of the purchase-money,

and enjoy the pleasure and vice of the capital for a few month;

then back to his milch cow, Phoebe, and lead a quiet life till the

next uncontrollable fit should come upon he along with the mean of

satisfy it.



on the way, he read Staines's letter to Mrs. Falcon, very carefully. he

never break the seal of the letter to Mrs. Staines. that be to be give

she when he have break the good news to she; and this he determine to

do with such skill, as should make Dr. Staines very unwilling to look

suspiciously or ill-naturedly into money account.



he reach London; and be a thorough egotist, attend first to his

own interest; he never go near Mrs. Staines until he have visit

every diamond merchant and dealer in the metropolis; he show the small

stone to they all but he show no more than one large stone to each.



at last he get an offer of twelve hundred pound for the small stone,

and the same for the large yellow stone, and nine hundred pound for the

second large stone. he take this nine hundred pound, and instantly

write to Phoebe, tell she he have a sudden inspiration to bring the

diamond to England, which he could not regret, since he have never do

a wise thing. he have sell a single stone for eight hundred pound, and

have send the doctor's four hundred pound to her account in Cape Town;

and as each sale be effect, the half would be so remitted. she would

see by that, he be wise than in former day. he should only stay so

long as might be necessary to sell they all equally well. his own share

he would apply to pay off mortgage on the family estate, of which

he hope some day to see she the mistress, or he would send it direct to

she, whichever she might prefer.



now the main object of this artful letter be to keep Phoebe quiet, and

not have she come after he, of which he feel she be very capable.



the money get safe to Cape Town, but the letter to Phoebe miscarry.

how this happen be never positively know; but the servant of the

lodge-house be afterwards detect cut stamp off a letter; so

perhaps she have play that game on this occasion.



by this mean, matter take a curious turn. Falcon, intend to lull

his wife into a false security, lull himself into that state instead.



when he have take care of himself, and get five hundred pound to play

the fool with, then he condescend to remember his errand of mercy; and

he come down to Gravesend, to see Mrs. Staines.



on the road, he give his mind seriously to the delicate and dangerous

task. it do not, however, disquiet he as it would you, sir, or you,

madam. he have a great advantage over you. he be a liar--a smooth,

ready, accomplished liar--and he know it.



this be the outline he have trace in his mind: he should appear very

subdued and sad; should wear an air of condolence. but, after a while,

should say, “and yet man have be lose like that, and escape. a man

be pick up on a raft in those very latitude, and bring into Cape

Town. a friend of mine see he, month after, at the hospital. his

memory be shake--could not tell his name; but in other respect he be

all right again.”



if Mrs. Staines take fire at this, he would say his friend know all the

particular, and he would ask he, and so leave that to rankle till next

visit. and having plant his germ of hope, he would grow it, and water

it, by visit and correspondence, till he could throw off the mask, and

say he be convince Staines be alive: and from that, by other degree,

till he could say, on his wife's authority, that the man pick up at

sea, and cure at her house, be the very physician who have save she

brother's life: and so on to the overwhelming proof he carry in the

ruby ring and the letter.



I be afraid the cunning and dexterity, the subtlety and tact require,

interest he more in the commission than do the benevolence. he

call, send up his card, and compose his countenance for his part,

like an actor at the Wing.



“not at home.”



he stare with amazement.



the history of a “not at home” be not, in general, worth recording: but

this be an exception.



on receive Falcon's card, Mrs. Staines give a little start, and

color faintly. she instantly resolve not to see he. what! the man

she have flirt with, almost jilt, and refuse to marry--he dare to

be alive when her Christopher be dead, and have come there to show she

he be alive!



she say “not at home” with a tone of unusual sharpness and decision,

which leave the servant in no doubt he must be equally decide at the

hall door.



Falcon receive the sudden freezer with amazement. “nonsense,” say he.

“not at home at this time of the morning--to an old friend!”



“not at home,” say the man doggedly.



“oh, very well,” say Falcon with a bitter sneer, and return to

London.



he feel sure she be at home; and be a tremendous egotist, he say,

“oh! all right. if she would rather not know her husband be alive, it

be all one to I;” and he actually take no more notice of she for a full

week, and never think of she, except to chuckle over the penalty she

be pay for dare to affront his vanity.



however, Sunday come; he see a dull day before he, and so he relent,

and think he would give she another trial.



he go down to Gravesend by boat, and stroll towards the villa.



when he be about a hundred yard from the villa, a lady, all in black,

come out with a nurse and child.



Falcon know her figure all that way off, and it give he a curious

thrill that surprise he. he follow she, and be not very far behind

she when she reach the church. she turn at the porch, kiss the

child earnestly, and give the nurse some direction; then enter the

church.



“come,” say Falcon, “I'll have a look at she, any way.”



he go into the church, and walk up a side aisle to a pillar, from

which he think he might be able to see the whole congregation; and,

sure enough, there she sit, a few yard from he. she be lovely than

ever. Mind have grow on her face with trouble. an angelic expression

illuminate her beauty; he gaze on she, fascinated. he drink and drank

her beauty two mortal hour, and when the church break up, and she go

home, he be half afraid to follow she, for he feel how hard it would

be to say anything to she but that the old love have return on he with

double force.



however, having watch her home, he walk slowly to and fro compose

himself for the interview.



he now determine to make the process of inform she a very long one:

he would spin it out, and so secure many a sweet interview with she:

and, who know? he might fascinate she as she have he, and ripen

gratitude into love, as he understand that word.



he call, he send in his card. the man go in, and come back with a

sonorous “not at home.”



“not at home? nonsense. why, she be just come in from church.”



“not at home,” say the man, evidently strong in his instruction.



Falcon turn white with rage at this second affront. “all the bad for

she,” say he, and turn on his heel.



he go home, rage with disappointment and wounded vanity, and--since

such love as his be seldom very far from hate--he swear she should never

know from he that her husband be alive. he even moralize. “this come

of be so unselfish,” say he. “I'll give that game up forever.”



by and by, a mere negative revenge be not enough for he, and he set

his wit to work to make she smart.



he write to she from his lodgings:--





DEAR MADAM,--What a pity you be never at home to I. I have something to

say about your husband, that I think might interest you.



your truly,



R. FALCON.





imagine the effect of this abominable note. it be like a rock flung

into a placid pool. it set Rosa tremble all over. what could he mean?



she run with it to her father, and ask he what Mr. Falcon could mean.



“I have no idea,” say he. “you have well ask he, not I.”



“I be afraid it be only to get to see I. you know he admire I once.

ah, how suspicious I be get.”



Rosa write to Falcon:--





DEAR SIR,--Since my bereavement I see scarcely anybody. my servant do

not know you; so I hope you will excuse I. if it be too much trouble to

call again, would you kindly explain your note to I?



your respectfully,



ROSA STAINES.





Falcon chuckle bitterly over this. “no, my lady,” say he. “I'll serve

you out. you shall run after I like a little dog. I have get the bone

that will draw you.”



he write back coldly to say that the matter he have wish to communicate

be too delicate and important to put on paper; that he would try and

get down to gravesend again some day or other, but be much occupy,

and have already put himself to inconvenience. he add, in a postscript,

that he be always at home from four to five.



next day he get hold of the servant, and give her minute instruction,

and a guinea.



then the wretch get some tool and bore a hole in the partition wall of

his sitting-room. the paper have large flower. he be artist enough

to conceal the trick with water-color. in his bed-room the hole come

behind the curtain.



that very afternoon, as he have foresee, Mrs. Staines call on he. the

maid, duly instruct, say Mr. Falcon be out, but would soon return,

and could she wait his return? the maid be so very civil, Mrs.

Staines say she would wait a little while, and be immediately usher

into Falcon's sitting-room. there she sit down; but be evidently ill at

ease, restless, flushed. she could not sit quiet, and at last begin to

walk up and down the room, almost wildly. her beautiful eye glitter,

and the whole woman seem on fire. the caitiff, who be watch she,

see and gloat on all this, and enjoy to the full her beauty and

agitation, and his revenge for she “not at home.”



but after a long time, there be a reaction: she sit down and utter

some plaintive sound inarticulate, or nearly; and at last she begin to

cry.



then it cost Falcon an effort not to come in and comfort she; but he

control himself and keep quiet.



she ring the bell. she ask for write paper, and she write her unseen

tormentor a humble note, beg he, for old acquaintance, to call on

she, and tell she what his mysterious word mean that have fill she

with agitation.



this do, she go away, with a deep sigh, and Falcon emerge, and

pounce upon her letter.



he kiss it; he read it a dozen time: he sit down where she have sit,

and his base passion overpower he. her beauty, her agitation, her

fear, her tear, all combine to madden he, and do the devil's work

in his false, selfish heart, so open to violent passion, so dead to

conscience.



for once in his life he be violently agitated, and tear by conflict

feeling: he walk about the room more wildly than his victim have; and

if it be true that, in certain great temptation, good and bad angel

fight for a man, here you might have see as fierce a battle of that

kind as ever be.



at last he rush out into the air, and do not return till ten o'clock

at night. he come back pale and haggard, and with a look of crime upon

his face.



true Bohemian as he be, he send for a pint of brandy.



so then the die be cast, and something be to be do that call for

brandy.



he bolt himself in, and drink a wine-glass of it neat; then another;

then another.



now his pale cheek be flushed, and his eye glitter. drink forever!

great ruin of english soul as well as body.



he put the poker in the fire, and heat it red hot.



he bring Staines's letter, and soften the sealing-wax with the hot

poker; then with his pen-knife make a neat incision in the wax, and

open the letter. he take out the ring, and put it carefully away. then

he light a cigar, and read the letter, and study it. many a man,

capable of murder in heat of passion, could not have resist the pathos

of this letter. many a newgate thief, after read it, would have feel

such pity for the love husband who have suffer to the verge of death,

and then to the brink of madness, and for the poor bereaved wife, that

he would have take the letter down to gravesend that very night, though

he pick two fresh pocket to defray the expense of the road.



but this be an egotist. good nature have curb his egotism a little

while; but now vanity and passion have sweep away all unselfish feeling,

and the pure egotist alone remain.



now, the pure egotist have be define as a man who will burn down his

NEIGHBOR'S house to cook himself an egg. murder be but egotism carry

out to its natural climax. what be murder to a pure egotist, especially

a brandied one?



I know an egotist who meet a female acquaintance in Newhaven village. she

have a one-pound note, and offer to treat he. she change this note to

treat he. fish she give he, and much whiskey. cost her four shilling.

he eat and drink with she, at her expense; and his aorta, or principal

blood-vessel, be warm with her whiskey, he murder she for the

change, the odd sixteen shilling.



I have the pleasure of see that egotist hung, with these eye. it be

a slice of luck that, I grieve to say, have not occur again to I.



so much for a whiskie egotist.



his less truculent but equally remorseless brother in villany, the

brandied egotist, Falcon, could read that poor husband's letter without

blenching; the love and the anticipation of rapture, these make he

writhe a little with jealousy, but they rouse not a grain of pity. he

be a true egotist, blind, remorseless.



in this, his true character, he study the letter profoundly, and

master all the fact, and digest they well.



all manner of diabolical artifice present themselves to his brain,

barren of true intellect, yet fertile in fraud; in that, and all low

cunning and subtlety, far more than a match for Solomon or Bacon.



his sinister study be pursue far into the night. then he go to

bed, and his unbounded egotism give he the sleep a grander criminal

would have court in vain on the verge of a monstrous and deliberate

crime.



next day he go to a fashionable tailor, and order a complete suit of

black. this be make in forty-eight hour; the interval be spend mainly

in concoct lie to be incorporate with the number of minute fact he

have gain from Staines's letter, and in make close imitation of his

handwriting.



thus armed, and cram with more lie than the “Menteur” of Corneille,

but not such innocent one, he go down to Gravesend, all in deep
