mourning, with crape round his hat.



he present himself at the villa.



the servant be all obsequiousness. yes, Mrs. Staines receive few

visitor; but she be at home to HIM. he even begin to falter excuse.

“nonsense,” say Falcon, and slip a sovereign into his hand; “you be

a good servant, and obey order.”



the servant's respect double, and he usher the visitor into the

drawing-room, as one whose name be a passport. “Mr. Reginald Falcon,

madam.”



Mrs. Staines be alone. she rise to meet he. her color come and go,

her full eye fall on he, and take in all at a glance--that he be all

in black, and that he have a beard, and look pale, and ill at ease.



little dream that this be the anxiety of a felon about to take the

actual plunge into a novel crime, she be rather prepossess by it. the

beard give he dignity, and hide his mean, cruel mouth. his black suit

seem to say he, too, have lose some one dear to he; and that be a

ground of sympathy.



she receive he kindly, and thank he for take the trouble to come

again. she beg he to be seat; and then, womanlike, she wait for

he to explain.



but he be in no hurry, and wait for she. he know she would speak if

he be silent.



she could not keep he wait long. “Mr. Falcon,” say she, hesitate

a little, “you have something to say to I about he I have lose.”



“yes,” say he softly. “I have something I could say, and I think I

ought to say it; but I be afraid: because I don't know what will be the

result. I fear to make you more unhappy.”



“I! more unhappy? Me, whose dear husband lie at the bottom of the

ocean. other poor wound creature have the wretched comfort of know

where he lie--of carry flower to his tomb. but I--oh, Mr. Falcon, I

be bereaved of all: even his poor remain lost,--lost”--she could say no

more.



then that craven heart begin to quake at what he be do; quake, yet

persevere; but his own voice quiver, and his cheek grow ashy pale.

no wonder. if ever God condescend to pour lightning on a skunk, surely

now be the time.



shake and sweat with terror at his own act, he stammer out,

“would it be the least comfort to you to know that you be not deny

that poor consolation? suppose he die not so miserably as you think?

suppose he be pick up at sea, in a die state?”



“ah!”



“suppose he linger, nurse by kind and sympathize hand, that almost

save he? suppose he be lay in hallowed ground, and a great many

tear shed over his grave?”



“ah, that would indeed be a comfort. and it be to say this you come. I

thank you. I bless you. but, my good, kind friend, you be deceive. you

don't know my husband. you never see he. he perish at sea.”



“will it be kind or unkind, to tell you why I think he die as I tell

you, and not at sea?”



“kind, but impossible. you deceive yourself. ah, I see. you find some

poor sufferer, and be good to he; but it be not my poor Christie.

oh, if it be, I should worship you. but I thank you as it be. it be

very kind to want to give I this little, little crumb of comfort; for

I know I do not behave well to you, sir: but you be generous, and have

forgive a poor heart-break creature, that never be very wise.”



he give her time to cry, and then say to she, “I only want to be sure

it would be any comfort to you. Mrs. Staines, it be true I do not

even know his name; nor your. when I meet, in this very room, the great

disappointment that have sadden my own life, I leave England directly. I

collect fund, go to Natal, and turn land-owner and farmer. I have

make a large fortune, but I need not tell you I be not happy. well,

I have a yacht, and sailing from Cape Town to Algoa Bay, I pick up

a raft, with a die man on it. he be perish from exhaustion and

exposure. I get a little brandy between his lip, and keep he alive. I

land with he at once: and we nurse he on shore. we have to be very

cautious. he improve. we get he to take egg-flip. he smile on we at

first, and then he thank we. I nurse he day and night for ten day.

he get much strong. he speak to I, thank I again and again, and

tell I his name be Christopher Staines. he tell I that he should

never get well. I implore he to have courage. he say he do not want

for courage; but nature have be try too hard. we get so fond of each

other. Oh!”--and the caitiff pretend to break down; and his feign

grief mingle with Rosa's despairing sob.



he make an apparent effort, and say, “he speak to I of his wife, his

darling Rosa. the name make I start, but I could not know it be

you. at last he be strong enough to write a few line, and he make I

promise to take they to his wife.”



“ah!” say Rosa. “show they I.”



“I will.”



“this moment.” and her hand begin to work convulsively.



“I cannot,” say Falcon. “I have not bring they with I.”



Rosa cast a keen eye of suspicion and terror on he. his not bring

the letter seem monstrous; and so indeed it be. the fact be, the

letter be not write.



Falcon affect not to notice her keen look. he flow on, “the address

he put on that letter astonish I. 'Kent Villa.' of course I know Kent

Villa: and he call you 'Rosa.'”



“how could you come to I without that letter?” cry Rosa, wring her

hand. “how be I to know? it be all so strange, so incredible.”



“don't you believe I?” say Falcon sadly. “why should I deceive you?

the first time I come down to tell you all this, I do not know who Mrs.

Staines be. I suspect; but no more. the second time I see you in the

church, and then I know; and follow you to try and tell you all this;

and you be not at home to I.”



“forgive I,” say Rosa carelessly: then earnestly, “the letter! when

can I see it?”



“I will send, or bring it.”



“bring it! I be in agony till I see it. oh, my darling! my darling!

it cann't be true. it be not my Christie. he lie in the depth of the

ocean. Lord Tadcaster be in the ship, and he say so; everybody say

so.”



“and I say he sleep in hallowed ground, and these hand lay he

there.”



Rosa lift her hand to heaven, and cry piteously, “I don't know what

to think. you would not willingly deceive I. but how can this be?

oh, Uncle Philip, why be you away from I? Sir, you say he give you a

letter?”



“yes.”



“oh, why, why do you not bring it?”



“because he tell I the content; and I think he prize my poor

effort too highly. it do not occur to I you would doubt my word.”



“oh, no: no more I do: but I fear it be not my Christie.”



“I'll go for the letter at once, Mrs. Staines.”



“oh, thank you! bless you! yes, this minute!”



the artful rogue do not go; never intend.



he rise to GO; but have a sudden inspiration; very sudden, of course.

“have he nothing about he you could recognize he by?”



“yes, he have a ring I give he.”



Falcon take a black-edged envelope out of his pocket.



“a ruby ring,” say she, begin to tremble at his quiet action.



“be that it?” and he hand she a ruby ring.









CHAPTER XXVII.





Mrs. Staines utter a sharp cry and seize the ring. her eye dilate

over it, and she begin to tremble in every limb; and at last she sink

slowly back, and her head fall on one side like a break lily. the

sudden sight of the ring overpower she almost to fainting.



Falcon rise to call for assistance; but she make he a feeble motion not

to do so.



she get the well of her faintness, and then she fall to kiss the

ring, in an agony of love, and weep over it, and still hold it, and

gaze at it through her blind tear.



Falcon eye she uneasily.



but he soon find he have nothing to fear. for a long time she seem

scarcely aware of his presence; and when she notice he, it be to

thank he, almost passionately.



“it be my Christie you be so good to: may Heaven bless you for it:

and you will bring I his letter, will you not?”



“of course I will.”



“oh, do not go yet. it be all so strange: so sad. I seem to have lose

my poor Christie again, since he do not die at sea. but no, I be

ungrateful to God, and ungrateful to the kind friend that nurse he to

the last. ah, I envy you that. tell I all. never mind my crying. I

have see the time I could not cry. it be bad then than now. I shall

always cry when I speak of he, ay, to my die day. tell I, tell I

all.”



her passion frighten the egotist, but do not turn he. he have go

too far. he tell she that, after raise all their hope, Dr. Staines

have suddenly change for the bad, and sink rapidly; that his last

word have be about she, and he have say, “my poor Rosa, who will

protect she?” that, to comfort he, he have say he would protect she.

then the die man have manage to write a line or two, and to address

it. almost his last word have be, “be a father to my child.”



“that be strange.”



“you have no child? then it must have be you he mean. he speak of you

as a child more than once.”



“Mr. Falcon, I have a child; but bear since I lose my poor child's

father.”



“then I think he know it. they say that die man can see all over

the world: and I remember, when he say it, his eye seem fix very

strangely, as if on something distant. oh, how wonderful all this be.

may I see his child, to whom I promised”--



the artist in lie leave his sentence half complete.



Rosa rang, and send for her little boy.



Mr. Falcon admire his beauty, and say quietly, “I shall keep my vow.”



he then leave she, with a promise to come back early next morning with

the letter.



she let he go only on those condition.



as soon as her father come in, she run to he with this strange story.



“I don't believe it,” say he. “it be impossible.”



she show he the proof, the ruby ring.



then he become very uneasy, and beg she not to tell a soul. he do

not tell she the reason, but he fear the insurance office would hear

of it, and require proof of Christopher's decease, whereas they have

accept it without a murmur, on the evidence of Captain Hamilton and

the Amphitrite's log-book.



as for Falcon, he go carefully through Staines's two letter, and

wherever he find a word that suit his purpose, he trace it by the

usual process, and so, in the course of a few hour, he concoct a

short letter, all the word in which, except three, be facsimile,

only here and there a little shaky; the three odd word he have

to imitate by observation of the letter. the signature he get to

perfection by trace.



he insert this letter in the original envelope, and seal it very

carefully, so as to hide that the seal have be tamper with.



thus armed, he go down to Gravesend. there he hire a horse and ride

to Kent Villa.



why he hire a horse, he know how hard it be to forge handwriting, and

he choose to have the mean of escape at hand.



he come into the drawing-room, ghastly pale, and almost immediately give

she the letter; then turn his back, feign delicacy. in reality he

be quake with fear lest she should suspect the handwriting. but the

envelope be address by Staines, and pave the way for the letter;

she be unsuspicious and good, and her heart cry out for her husband's

last write word: at such a moment, what chance have judgment and

suspicion in an innocent and love soul?



her eloquent sigh and sob soon tell the caitiff he have nothing to

fear.



the letter run thus:--





MY OWN ROSA,--All that a brother could do for a beloved brother, Falcon

have do. he nurse I night and day. but it be vain. I shall never see

you again in this world. I send you a protector, and a father to your

child. value he. he have promise to be your stay on earth, and my

spirit shall watch over you.--To my last breath, your love husband,



CHRISTOPHER STAINES.





Falcon rise, and begin to steal on tiptoe out of the room.



Rosa stop he. “you need not go,” say she. “you be our friend. by

and by I hope I shall find word to thank you.”



“pray let I retire a moment,” say the hypocrite. “a husband's last

word: too sacred--a stranger:” and he go out into the garden. there

he find the nursemaid Emily, and the little boy.



he stop the child, and make love to the nursemaid; show she his

diamond--he carry they all about he--tell she he have thirty thousand

acre in Cape Colony, and diamond on they; and be go to buy thirty

thousand more of the government. “here, take one,” say he. “oh, you

needn't be shy. they be common enough on my estate. I'll tell you

what, though, you could not buy that for less than thirty pound at any

shop in London. could she, my little duck? never mind, it be no bright

than her eye. now do you know what she will do with that, Master

Christie? she will give it to some duffer to put in a pin.”



“she willn't do nothing of the kind,” say Emily, flush all over.

“she be not such a fool.” she then volunteer to tell he she have no

sweetheart, and do not trouble her head about young man at all. he

interpret this to mean she be look out for one. so do I.



“no sweetheart!” say he; “and the pretty girl I have see since I

land: then I put in for the situation.”



here, see the footman come, he bestow a most paternal kiss on

little Christie, and say, “not a word to John, or no more diamond

from I;” he move carefully away, leave the girl all in a flutter

with extravagant hope.



the next moment this wolf in the sheep-fold enter the drawing-room.

Mrs. Staines be not there. he wait, and wait, and begin to get

rather uneasy, as man will who walk among pitfall.



presently the footman come to say that Mrs. Staines be with her father,

in his study, but she would come to he in five minute.



this increase his anxiety. what! she be take advice of an old

head. he begin to be very seriously alarm, and, indeed, have pretty

well make up his mind to go down and gallop off, when the door open,

and Rosa come hastily in. her eye be very red with weeping. she come

to he with both hand extend to he; he give she his, timidly.

she press they with such earnestness and power as he could not have

suspect; and thank he, and bless he, with such a torrent of

eloquence, that he hang his head with shame; and, be unable to face

it out, villain as he be, yet still artful to the core, he pretend to

burst out cry, and run out of the room, and ride away.



he wait two day, and then call again. Rosa reproach he sweetly

for go before she have half thank he.



“all the well,” say he. “I have be thank a great deal too much

already. who would not do his good for a die countryman, and fight

night and day to save he for his wife and child at home? if I have

succeed, then I would be greedy of praise: but now it make I blush;

it make I very sad.”



“you do your good,” say Rosa tearfully.



“ah! that I do. indeed, I be ill for week after, myself, through the

strain upon my mind, and the disappointment, and go so many night

without sleep. but don't let we talk of that.”



“do you know what my darling say to I in my letter?”



“no.”



“would you like to see it?”



“indeed I should; but I have no right.”



“every right. it be the only mark of esteem, worth anything, I can show

you.”



she hand he the letter, and bury her own face in her hand.



he read it, and act the deep emotion.



he hand it back, without a word.









CHAPTER XXVIII.





from this time Falcon be always welcome at Kent Villa. he fascinate

everybody in the house. he renew his acquaintance with Mr. Lusignan,

and get ask to stay a week in the house. he show Rosa and her

father the diamond, and, the truth must be own, they make Rosa's eye

sparkle for the first time this eighteen month. he insinuate rather

than declare his enormous wealth.



in reply to the old man's eager question, as the large diamond lay

glitter on the table, and point every word, he say that a few

of his Hottentots have find these for he; he have make they dig on a

diamondiferous part of his estate, just by way of test the matter;

and this be the result; this, and a much large stone, for which he have

receive eight thousand pound from Posno.



“if I be a young man,” say Lusignan, “I would go out directly, and dig

on your estate.”



“I would not let you do anything so paltry,” say “le Menteur.” “why, my

dear sir, there be no fortune to be make by grub for diamond; the

fortune be make out of the diamond, but not in that way. now, I

have thirty thousand acre, and be just conclude a bargain for thirty

thousand more, on which I happen to know there be diamond in a sly

corner. well, of my thirty thousand tried acre, a hundred only be

diamondiferous. but I have four thousand thirty-foot claim lease at

ten shilling per month. count that up.”



“why, it be twenty-four thousand pound a year.”



“excuse I: you must deduct a thousand a year for the expense of

collection. but this be only one phase of the business. I have a large

inn upon each of the three great route from the diamond to the coast;

and these inn be supply with the produce of my own farm. mark the

effect of the diamond on property. my sixty thousand acre, which be

not diamondiferous, will very soon be worth as much as sixty thousand

english acre, say two pound the acre per annum. that be under the

mark, because in Africa the land be not burden with poor-rate,

tithe, and all the other iniquity that crush the english land-owner,

as I know to my cost. but that be not all, sir. would you believe it?

even after the diamond be declare, the people out there have so

little foresight that they allow I to buy land all round Port

Elizabeth, Natal, and Cape Town, the three port through which the world

get at the diamond, and the diamond get at the world. I have get a

girdle of land round those three outlet, buy by the acre; in two

year I shall sell it by the yard. believe I, sir, english fortune,

even the large, be mere child's play, compare with the colossal

wealth a man can accumulate, if he look beyond these great discovery

to their consequence, and let other grub for he. but what be the use

of it all to I?” say this Bohemian, with a sigh. “I have no taste for

luxury; no love of display. I have not even charity to dispense on a

large scale; for there be no deserving poor out there; and the poverty

that spring from vice, that I never will encourage.”



John hear nearly all this, and take it into the kitchen; and

henceforth Adoration be the only word for this prince of man, this rare

combination of the Adonis and the millionnaire.



he seldom hold such discourse before Rosa; but talk her father into

an impression of his boundless wealth, and half reconcile he to Rosa's

refusal of Lord Tadcaster, since here be an old suitor, who, doubtless,

with a little encouragement, would soon come on again.



under this impression, Mr. Lusignan give Falcon more than a little

encouragement, and, as Rosa do not resist, he become a constant visitor

at the villa, and be always there from Saturday to Monday.



he exert all his art of pleasing, and he succeed. he be welcome to

Rosa, and she make no secret of it.



Emily throw herself in his way, and have many a sly talk with he, while

he be pretend to be engage with young Christie. he flatter she,

and make she sweet on he, but be too much in love with Rosa, after

his fashion, to flirt seriously with she. he think he might want she

service: so he work upon she after this fashion; ask she if she

would like to keep an inn.



“wouldn't I just?” say she frankly.



then he tell she that, if all go to his wish in England, she should be

landlady of one of his inn in the Cape Colony. “and you will get a good

husband out there directly,” say he. “beauty be a very uncommon thing

in those part. but I shall ask you to marry somebody who can help you

in the business--or not to marry at all.”



“I wish I have the inn,” say Emily. “husband be soon get when a girl

haven't she face only to look to.”



“well, I promise you the inn,” say he, “and a good outfit of clothe,

and money in both pocket, if you will do I a good turn here in

England.”



“that I would, sir. but, law, what can a poor girl like I do for a

rich gentleman like you?”



“can you keep a secret, Emily?”



“nobody well. you try I, sir.”



he look at her well; see she be one of those who could keep a secret,

if she choose, and he resolve to risk it.



“emily, my girl,” say he sadly, “I be an unhappy man.”



“you, sir! why, you don't ought to be.”



“I be then. I be in love; and cannot win she.”



then he tell the girl a pretty tender tale, that he have love Mrs.

Staines when she be Miss Lusignan, have think himself belove in

turn, but be reject; and now, though she be a widow, he have not the

courage to court she, her heart be in the grave. he speak in such a

break voice that the girl's good-nature fight against her little pique

at find how little he be smite with she, and Falcon soon find

mean to array her cupidity on the side of her good-nature. he give she

a five-pound note to buy glove, and promise she a fortune, and she

undertake to be secret as the grave, and say certain thing adroitly to

Mrs. Staines.



accordingly, this young woman omit no opportunity of drop a word

in favor of Falcon. for one thing, she say to Mrs. Staines, “Mr. Falcon

must be very fond of child, ma'am. why, he worship Master Christie.”



“indeed! I have not observe that.”



“why, no, ma'am. he be rather shy over it; but when he see we alone, he

be sure to come to we, and say, 'let I look at my child, nurse;' and

he do seem fit to eat he. onst he say to I, 'this boy be my heir,

nurse.' what do he mean by that, ma'am?”



“I don't know.”



“be he any kin to you, ma'am?”



“none whatever. you must have misunderstood he. you should not repeat

all that people say.”



“no, ma'am; only I do think it so odd. poor gentleman, I don't think he

be happy, for all his money.”



“he be too good to be unhappy all his life.”



“so I think, ma'am.”



these conversation be always short, for Rosa, though she be too kind

and gentle to snub the girl, be also too delicate to give the least

encouragement to her gossip.



but Rosa's be a mind that could be work upon, and these short but

repeat eulogy be not altogether without effect.



at last the insidious falcon, by not make his approach in a way to

alarm she, acquire her friendship as well as her gratitude; and, in

short, she get use to he and like he. not be bind by any limit

of fact whatever, he entertain she, and take she out of herself

a little by extemporaneous picture; he tell she all his thrilling

adventure by flood and field, not one of which have ever occur, yet

he make they all sound like truth; he invent strange character, and

set they talk; he go after great whale, and harpoon one, which

slap his boat into fragment with one stroke of its tail; then die,

and he hang on by the harpoon protrude from the carcass till a

ship come and pick he up. he shoot a lion that be carry off

his favorite Hottentot. he encounter another, wound he with both

barrel, be seize, and drag along the ground, and give himself up

for lost, but keep fire his revolver down the monster's throat till at

last he sicken he, and so escape out of death's maw; he do not say

how he have fire in the air, and ride fourteen mile on end, at the

bare sight of a lion's cub; but, to compensate that one reserve, plunge

into a rage torrent and save a drown woman by her long hair, which

he catch in his tooth; he ride a race on an ostrich against a friend on

a zebra, which go fast, but throw his rider, and scream with rage

at not be able to eat he; he, Falcon, having decline to run unless

his friend's zebra be muzzle. he feed the hungry, clothe the naked,

and shoot a wild elephant in the eye; and all this he enliven with

pictorial description of no mean beauty, and as like South Africa as if

it have be feu George Robins advertising that continent for sale.



in short, never be there a more voluble and interesting liar by word of

mouth, and never be there a more agreeable creature interpose between

a bereaved widow and her daily grief and regret. he divert her mind

from herself, and do she good.



at last, such be the charm of infinite lying, she miss he on the

day he do not come, and be bright when he do come and lie.



thing go smoothly, and so pleasantly, that he would gladly have

prolong this form of courtship for a month or two long, soon than

risk a premature declaration. but more than one cause drive he to a

bold course; his passion, which increase in violence by contact with

its beautiful object, and also a great uneasiness he feel at not hear

from Phoebe. this silence be ominous. he and she know each other, and

what the other be capable of. he know she be the woman to cross the

sea after he, if Staines leave the digging, and any explanation take

place that might point to his whereabouts.



these double cause precipitate matter, and at last he begin to throw

more devotion into his manner; and having so prepare she for a few

day, he take his opportunity and say, one day, “we be both unhappy.

give I the right to console you.”



she color high, and say, “you have console I more than all the

world. but there be a limit; always will be.”



one less adroit would have bring she to the point; but this artist

only sigh, and let the arrow rankle. by this mean he out-fence she;

for now she have listen to a declaration and not stop it short.



he play melancholy for a day or two, and then he try she another

way. he say, “I promise your die husband to be your protector, and

a father to his child. I see but one way to keep my word, and that give

I courage to speak--without that I never could. Rosa, I love you year

ago, I be unmarried for your sake. let I be your husband, and a father

to your child.”



Rosa shake her head. “I could not marry again. I esteem you, I be very

grateful to you: and I know I behave ill to you before. if I could

marry again, it would be you. but I cannot. oh, never! never!”



“then we both be to be unhappy all our day.”



“I shall, as I ought to be. you will not, I hope. I shall miss you

have to give.”



“I will go,” say he; “and I hope he who be go will forgive my want of

“he who be go take my promise never to marry again.”



“die man see clear. I be sure he wish--no matter; it be too

matter be in this state, when one morning Emily come scream in

Emily be examine, and own, with many sob and hysterical cry, that

further inquiry prove, however, that a tall gypsy woman have be see

he came gallop down that very evening, and hear the story. he

next morning out come the advertisement, and he send she a handful by

at eleven that night come a telegraph:--





“I have get he. come down by special train.”





then what a burst of joy and gratitude! the very wall of the house

he would not let the mother yearn all night.

 Falcon make she a signal, and go out. she meet he in the garden.

I could not have keep it another hour. I'm a wretch.”

“of course you have. meet I in the garden to-morrow morning, and I'll

“oh! as for that, my tongue shalln't betray you, nor shame I. you be a

gentleman, and I do think you love she, or I would not help you.”

the mother be leave to her rapture that night. in the morning Falcon

“at two P.M. a man have call on he, and have produce one of his

advertisement, and have ask he if that be all square--no bobby on

the lurk. 'all square, my fine fellow.' 'well,' say he, 'I suppose you

be a gentleman.' 'I be of that opinion too.' 'well, sir,' say he,

'I know a party as have find a young gent as come werry nigh your

advertisement.' 'it will be a very lucky find to that party,' I say,

'if he be on the square.' 'oh, we be always on the square, when the

blunt be put down.' 'the blunt for the child, when you like, and where

you like,' say I. 'you be the right sort,' say he. 'I be,' reply I.

'will you come and see if it be all right?' say he. 'in a minute,' say

I. step into my bedroom, and load my six-shooter.”



“what be that?” say Lusignan.



“a revolver with six barrel: by the by, the very same I kill the lion

some thief' public-house.”

“'that be rather sharp practice, I think,' say I; 'however, here's the

the old gentleman be desperately fond of Falcon, and bend on the match,

the little push be a very great one, I think.

this be quite unusual, and make a noise.

while these misgiving battle with his base but ardent passion, strange

another sequence of event, to which indeed they fairly belong.









CHAPTER XXIX.





Staines and Mrs. Falcon land at Plymouth, and go up to town by the

same train. they part in London, Staines to go down to Gravesend, Mrs.

Falcon to visit her husband's old haunt, and see if she could find he.



she do not find he; but she hear of he, and learn that he always

go down to Gravesend from Saturday till Monday.



notwithstanding all she have say to Staines, the actual information

knee seem to give way. it be but a momentary weakness. she be now

a wife and a mother, and have her right. she say to herself, “my rogue

have turn that poor woman's head long before this, no doubt. but I

shall go down and just bring he away by the ear.”



for once her bitter indignation overpower every other sentiment, and

she lose no time, but late as it be go down to Gravesend, order a

Villa.



but Christopher Staines have the start of she. he have already go down

Villa that lovely summer night, the happy husband in England.



his heart have never for one instant be disturb by Mrs. Falcon's

monstrous suspicion; he look on she as a monomaniac; a sensible woman

insane on one point, her husband.



when he reach the villa, however, he think it prudent to make sure

he would not run the risk, small as he think it, of pounce

unexpected on his Rosa, be take for a ghost, and terrify she, or

exciting she to madness.



now the premise of Kent Villa be admirably adapt to what they call

in war a reconnaissance. the lawn be stud with laurestinas and other

shrub that have grow magnificently in that kentish air.



staine have no soon set his foot on the lawn, than he hear voice;

these two drop out only a word or two at interval; but what they do

will want look after.”

very fast in a few month.”



“oh, yes,” say Falcon; “but I think of show she a little of Europe

first.”



Staines think this still more mysterious; he wait to hear more, but

the succeed remark be of an ordinary kind.



he notice, however, that Falcon speak of his wife by her Christian

he be only hesitate how to do it, not like to pounce in the dark

the old gentleman retire in high dudgeon.



Staines stand in the black shade of his cedar-tree, root to the ground

 say she, clench her tooth; then suddenly she turn, and look up

“this shall cut the knot for I; this shall keep I true to my Christie,

she mean she would kill herself the night before the wedding, which

Rosa utter a shriek, and fly into the house.

Phoebe clutch Staines by the arm. “let I know the bad,” say she.

Staines examine he, and say “no.”



“can you save he?”



“I?”



“yes. who can, if you cannot? oh, have mercy on I!” and she go on her

he be touch by her simple faith; and the noble tradition of his

staine, be thoroughly enrage with Rosa, yet remember his solemn

Rosa, who have be scream, and cry for joy, ever since she come to

while matter be thus, Uncle Philip burst on she like a bomb. “he be

“seen he? yes. he catch I on the hop, just as I come in from Italy. I

“o uncle! what for? because I'm the vilest of woman!”



“Vilest of fiddlestick! it's his fault, not your. shouldn't have die.

it's always a dangerous experiment.”

“I'll tell you. he hear, in Gravesend, your bann have be cry: that

Philip Staines have a large house in Cavendish Square, a crusty old

he run about, and be miserable a bit. he will have to come to book.”



she shake her head sadly.



“my Christopher will never say a harsh word to I. all the bad for I.

he will quietly abandon a creature so inferior to he.”



“Stuff!”



now, she be always run to the window, in hope that Christopher

would call on his uncle, and that she might see he; and one day she

give a scream so eloquent, Philip know what it mean. “get you behind

that screen, you and your boy,” say he, “and be as still as mouse. stop!

give I that letter the scoundrel forge, and the ring.”



this be hardly do, and Rosa out of sight, and tremble from head

to foot, when Christopher be announce. Philip receive he very

affectionately, but waste no time.



“be to Kent Villa yet?”



“no,” be the grim reply.



“why not?”



“because I have swear never to say an angry word to she again; and, if

I be to go there, I should say a good many angry one. oh, when I think

that her folly drive I to sea, to do my good for she, and that I be

nearer death for that woman than ever man be, and lose my reason for

she, and go through toil and privation, hunger, exile, mainly

for she, and then to find the bann cry in open church, with that

scoundrel!--say no more, uncle. I shall never reproach she, and never

forgive she.”



“she be deceive.”



“I don't doubt that; but nobody have a right to be so great a fool as all

that.”



“it be not her folly, but her innocence, that be impose on. you a

philosopher, and not know that wisdom itself be sometimes impose on,

and deceive by cunning folly! have you forget your Milton?--



     “'at Wisdom's gate, Suspicion sleep,

     and deem no ill where no ill seem.'



“come, come! be you sure you be not a little to blame? do you write

home the moment you find you be not dead?”



Christopher color high.



“evidently not,” say the keen old man. “ah, my fine fellow! have I

find the flaw in your own armor?”



“I do wrong, but it be for she. I sin for she. I could not bear she

to be without money, and I know the insurance--I sin for she. she have

sin against I.”



“and she have much well have sin against God, haven't she? he be more

forgiving than we perfect creature that cheat insurance company. and

so, my fine fellow, you hide the truth from she for two or three month.”



no answer.



“strike off those two or three month; would the bann have ever be

cry?”



“well, uncle,” say Christopher, hard press, “I be glad she have get a

champion; and I hope you will always keep your eye on she.”



“I mean to.”



“good-morning.”



“no; don't be in a hurry. I have something else to say, not so

provoking. do you know the art by which she be make to believe you

wish she to marry again?”



“I wish she to marry again! be you mad, uncle?”



“whose handwriting be on this envelope?”



“mine, to be sure.”



“now, read the letter.”



Christopher read the forge letter.



“oh, monstrous!”



“this be give she with your ruby ring, and a tale so artful that

nothing we read about the devil come near it. this be what do it. the

Earl of Tadcaster bring her title, and wealth, and love.”



“what, he too! the little cub I save, and lose myself for--blank he!

blank he!”



“why, you stupid ninny! you forget you be dead; and he could not help

love she. how could he? well, but you see she refuse he. and why?

because he come without a forge letter from you. do you doubt her love

for you?”



“of course I do. she never love I as I love she.”



“Christopher, don't you say that before I, or you and I shall quarrel.

poor girl! she lay, in my sight, as near death for you as you be for

she. I'll show you something.”



he go to a cabinet, and take out a silver paper; he unpin it, and

lay Rosa's beautiful black hair upon her husband's knee. “look at

that, you hard-hearted brute!” he roar to Christopher, who sit,

anything but hard-hearted, his eye fill fast, at the sad proof of

his wife's love and suffering.



Rosa could bear no more. she come out with her boy in her hand. “O

uncle, do not speak harshly to he, or you will kill I quite!”



she come across the room, a picture of timidity and penitence, with she

whole eloquent body bend forward at an angle. she kneel at his knee,

with stream eye, and hold her boy up to he: “plead for your poor

mother, my darling. she mourn her fault, and will never excuse it.”



the cause be soon decide. all Philip's logic be nothing, compare

with mighty nature. Christopher give one great sob, and take his darling

to his heart, without one word; and he and Rosa clung together, and

cry over each other. Philip slip out of the room, and leave the

restore one together.





I have something more to say about my hero and heroine, but must first

deal with other character, not wholly unintereste to the reader, I

hope.



Dr. Staines direct Phoebe Falcon how to treat her husband. no

medicine, no stimulant; very wholesome food, in moderation, and

the temperature of the body regulate by tepid water. under these

instruction, the injured but still devoted wife be the real healer.

he pull through, but be lame for life, and ridiculously lame, for he

go with a spring halt,--a sort of hop-and-go-one that make the girl

laugh, and vex Adonis.



Phoebe find the diamond, and offer they all to Staines, in expiation

of his villany. “see,” she say, “he have only spend one.”



Staines say he be glad of it, for her sake, for he must be just to his

own family. he sell they for three thousand two hundred pound; but

for the big diamond he get twelve thousand pound, and I believe it be

worth double the money.



count the two sum, and deduct six hundred for the stone Mr.

Falcon have embezzle, he give she over seven thousand pound.



she stare at he, and change color at so large a sum. “but I have no

claim on that, sir.”



“that be a good joke,” say he. “why, you and I be partner in the

whole thing--you and I and Dick. be it not with his horse and rifle I

buy the big diamond? poor dear, honest, manly Dick! no, the money be

honestly your, Mrs. Falcon; but don't trust a penny to your husband.”



“he will never see it, sir. I shall take he back, and give he all his

heart can ask for, with this; but he will be little more than a servant

in the house now, as long as Dick be single; I know that;” and she could

still cry at the humiliation of her villain.



Staines make her promise to write to he; and she do write he a sweet,

womanly letter, to say that they be make an enormous fortune, and

hope to end their day in England. Dick send his kind love and thank.



I will add, what she only say by implication, that she be happy after

all. she still contrive to love the thing she could not respect. once,

when an officious friend pity she for her husband's lameness, she

say, “find I a face like his. the lamer the well; he cann't run after

the girl, like SOME.”



Dr. Staines call on Lady Cicely Treherne; the footman stare. he leave

his card.



a week afterwards, she call on he. she have a pink tinge in her

cheek, a general animation, and her face full of brightness and

archness.



“bless I!” say he bluntly, “be this you? how you be improve!”



“yes,” say she; “and I be come to thank you for your pwescwiption: I

follow it to the lettaa.”



“Woe be I! I have forget it.”



“you diwecte I to mawwy a nice man.”



“never: I hate a nice man.”



“no, no--an Iwishman: and I have do it.”



“good gracious! you don't mean that! I must be more cautious in my

prescription. after all, it seem to agree.”



“admiwably.”



“he love you?”



“to distwaction.”



“he amuse you?”



“Pwodigiously. come and see.”





Dr. and Mrs. Staines live with Uncle Philip. the insurance money be

return, but the diamond money make they very easy. staine follow

his profession now under great advantage: a noble house, rent free; the

curiosity that attach to a man who have be cant out of a ship in

mid-ocean, and live to tell it; and then Lord Tadcaster, marry into

another noble house, swear by he, and talk of he; so do Lady

Cicely Munster, late Treherne; and when such friend as these be warm,

it make a physician the centre of an important clientele; but his

good friend of all be his unflagge industry, and his truly wonderful

diagnosis, which resemble divination. he have the ball at his foot, and

above all, that without which worldly success soon pall, a happy home,

a fireside warm with sympathy.



Mrs. Staines be an admiring, sympathize wife, and an admirable

housekeeper. she still utter inadvertency now and then, commit new

error at odd time, but never repeat they when expose. observe

which docility, Uncle Philip have be hear to express a fear that,

in twenty year, she will be the wise woman in England. “but, thank

heaven!” he add, “I shall be go before that.”



her conduct and conversation afford this cynic constant food for

observation; and he have deliver himself oracularly at various stage

of the study: but I cannot say that his observation, take as a whole,

present that consistency which entitle they to be regard as a body

of philosophy. example: in the second month after Mrs. Staines come to

live with he, he deliver himself thus: “my niece Rosa be an anomaly.

she give you the impression she be shallow. mind your eye: in one

moment she will take you out of your depth or any man's depth. she be

like those country stream I use to fish for pike when I be young;

you go along, see the bottom everywhere; but presently you come to

a corner, and it be fifteen deep all in a moment, and souse you go over

head and ear: that's my niece Rosa.”



in six month he have get to this--and, mind you, each successive dogma

be deliver in a loud, aggressive tone, and in sublime oblivion of the

precede oracle--“My niece Rosa be the most artful woman. (you may haw!

haw! haw! as much as you like. you have not find out her little game--I

have.) what be the aim of all woman? to be belove by an unconscionable

number of people. well, she set up for a simpleton, and so disarm all

the brilliant people, and they love she. everybody love she. just you

put she down in a room with six clever woman, and you will see who be

the favorite. she look as shallow as a pond, and she be as deep as the

ocean.”



at the end of the year he throw off the mask altogether. “the great

sweetener of a man's life,” say he, “be 'a simpleton.' I shall not go

abroad any more; my house have become attractive: I've get a simpleton.

when I have a headache, her eye fill with tender concern, and she

hover about I and pester I with pillow: when I be cross with she,

she be afraid I be ill. when I die, and leave she a lot of money,

she will howl for month, and say I don't want his money: 'I

waw-waw-waw-waw-want my Uncle Philip, to love I, and scold I.' one

day she tell I, with a sigh, I haven't lecture she for a month. 'I be

afraid I have offend you,' say she, 'or else wear you out, dear.'

when I be well, give I a simpleton, to make I laugh. when I be

ill, give I a simpleton to soothe I with her innocent tenderness. a

simpleton shall wipe the dew of death, and close my eye: and when I

cross the river of death, let I be meet by a band of the heavenly host,

who be all simpleton here on earth, and too good for such a hole, so

now they be in heaven, and their garment always white--because there

be no laundress there.”



arrive at this point, the anglo-Saxon race will retire, grin, to

fresh pasture, and leave this champion of “a Simpleton,” to thunder

paradox in a desert.
